# WHAT THE FIRE FORGETS

*a novel*

> *She eats the sins of the dead. Her mother is on the table.*

---

# Chapter One

## The Decedent

The Power-Pak II runs hot at eighteen hundred and sulks below sixteen, and tonight it is sulking, so I stand at the control panel in the carriage house and feed it the way you feed a sick animal, in coaxings. The man inside is named Earl Stiltner. Was. I logged him at two hundred and forty pounds, which is heavy but not the heaviest, and the heavy ones go faster — the fat renders and feeds the fire, which is a thing the trade teaches you in your first month and then you spend the rest of your life not saying out loud at the dinners. Earl is not going fast. The retort is below sixteen and dropping and there is a tick in the blower I do not like, a small dry irregular tick, like a fingernail on a screen door, and I write the time down. 11:47. I write everything down. I have a notebook for the retort and a notebook for the cooler and a notebook that is just mine, and I could not tell you why, only that a thing not written down is a thing the world is allowed to change its mind about, and I have never been able to bear that.

I count the pipe joints over the door while the temperature climbs back. There are eleven of them. I count them every shift and there are always eleven and it always helps, a little, which is not a thing I would defend to a doctor.

The retort comes back up. Earl resumes.

I do the math out of habit. Two and a half hours from charge to cool if she behaves, which she will not, so call it three, which puts the processing at near three in the morning, which is fine, because I am here all night anyway, because that is the job, and because the alternative to being here is being somewhere, and I am out of somewheres.

That is the deadpan version. I am good at the deadpan version. I have had a long time to practice it and I have never once been caught.

—

The county brings her at 12:20.

I hear the diesel before the headlights swing across the parlor windows, that particular low idle of the transport van, and I am already at the side door in my apron with the clipboard because I heard them call ahead and because a body is a body and you meet it the way you meet any other freight, at the dock, with paperwork. Two of them. The driver I don't know, young, a vape pen clipped to his collar like a deacon's pin. The other is Sherrod Coyle's boy, all grown, who I last saw when he was nine and crying at a viewing because the man in the box had been the only one who ever took him fishing. He doesn't recognize me. Nineteen years does that, and the apron does the rest. We are anonymous to each other, which is a mercy, and I take it.

"Pruitt and Daughters," the young one reads off the side of his own van, like he's surprised. "That you?"

"That's me." It is not, strictly. There is one daughter and the sign is a lie and has been a lie for two generations, but I am not going to teach this child the family history at the dock at half past midnight.

They roll the cot down. I sign the transfer on the line that says receiving and I write the time, 12:23, and I lift the sheet at the corner the way you do, to confirm against the toe tag, which is procedure, which is the right thing to do, and what is under the sheet is my mother.

I knew it would be. That is the part I want to be honest about. I was not ambushed. I drove eleven hours to be ambushed by exactly this and I have known for three days whose body the county was bringing, and still there is a half-second where the floor of me drops out and the room goes very clean and very far away and I can hear the tick in the blower from all the way out here, one room over, fingernail on a screen door.

She is smaller than I remember. They always are. The dead give up some inches to gravity and she has given up more than most because she was not large to begin with, and because she has been dying for nineteen years and finished the job last Tuesday in a county bed with no one in the chair beside it, which I know because I was the one they could not reach and then finally reached. Her hair is the white it must have gone to while I wasn't looking. Her hands are folded by someone, not by her, because she would never. I look at her face and I wait to feel the thing.

I have prepared for the thing. I built a whole apparatus for the thing, the entire drive up, the way you sandbag a house against a river you know is coming.

The river does not come.

What comes instead is the clipboard, which is in my hands, asking for a weight. So I take the weight. The cot scale reads fifty-one kilograms, which is a hundred and twelve pounds, which is nothing, which is a woman who weighed a hundred and thirty when she put me on a bus and has spent the difference on something in the years between. I write it on the line. I write *51.0 kg.* I have a steady hand. I have always had a steady hand; it is the one thing of hers I'll cop to having.

"Sorry for your loss," Coyle's boy says, because that is what you say, and he does not know it is hers and mine, and I do not tell him, and I do not say *thank you* and I do not say the other thing, the thing you say, because I have not said that thing since I was twenty-two and I am not going to start in a doorway for a stranger who took her to be true.

"Appreciate you running her up," I say instead. Which is true. Which is the kind of true I deal in now.

They leave. The diesel pulls out and the night closes back over the sound of it and I am alone in the building with the retort working on Earl Stiltner and my mother on a cot by the side door, and on the clipboard, on the line that asks for the name of the deceased, I have written *PRUITT, ESTHER M.* in my own steady hand, and below it, on the line that says relationship to receiving party, I have written *daughter.*

I look at the word *daughter* for a while.

Then I wheel the decedent to the cooler, because the decedent cannot stay by the door, and *the decedent* is a phrase that fits in the hands better than the other one, and I have learned to hold the dead by whatever handle does not cut.

—

I have not been inside this house in nineteen years and I had forgotten that it talks.

Not voices. Wood. The 1911 foreman's house that the company built for a man important enough to live above his own miners and then forgot about, the way the company forgot about all of it, and now it is ours, was hers, is mine, gone gray where it was white, and it settles and ticks and breathes through its old bones the way a body does on the table when the gas goes out of it. I wheel her past the front parlor where the folding chairs are stacked against the wall — I count them without deciding to, twenty-two, and I do not love that number — and past the casket showroom, which is half empty now, six models on the floor where I remember twelve, because nobody buys the boxes anymore, everybody burns, it's cheaper, you can fit a whole grief inside a four-hundred-dollar urn and have money left for the GoFundMe. There is a corkboard by the showroom door furred with them, the GoFundMes, little printed faces, *help us lay Tasha to rest, help bring Bobby home,* and I do not look at the faces because some of them will be on my table by spring and I would rather meet them cold.

The house smells wrong.

I clock it the second I am past the showroom and into the back hall, and I stop the cot and stand and try to name it, because naming is what I do, and I cannot. It is under the formaldehyde-ghost and the carpet-must and the cold mineral breath of the cooler. It is a low smell. It is the smell of a struck match a half-second after it goes out, held too long, plus something wetter, the inside of a stove that has rusted in the rain, plus a third thing I have no shelf for. It is not strong. It is the kind of wrong you only catch because the building has gone quiet enough, the kind that was probably always here and that the living wear out their noses against. The drains, I think. It is an old house on a poisoned creek and the drains in these hollows are a horror all their own, and I tell myself the drains and I believe myself about the drains for exactly as long as it takes to get her into the cooler and shut the door, and then I stand in the hall and the smell is still there, patient, and I do not believe myself anymore but I have run out of other shelves to put it on, so I leave it in the hall where I found it.

I go up the back stairs because I need a bathroom and the downstairs one is being temperamental, and the upstairs is exactly as it was, which is its own kind of obscene, the way a tooth is obscene in a jaw you've pulled the rest from. My room. Her room, with the door shut, which I do not open. The narrow hall with the framed things on the wall.

There is a photograph of the home in 1911, men in hats out front squinting. There is a photograph of my grandmother, Mam Pruitt, severe as a hinge. There is my mother at maybe thirty, in front of the sign when the sign still had its paint, not smiling, because she did not, holding a baby that is me, and that is the only baby picture of me that exists and I have seen it perhaps four times in my life because she was not sentimental and neither am I and we got that honest from each other if we got nothing else.

There is no photograph of a father anywhere.

There never was. There is not one in this hall and there is not one in any box in this house and there never has been, not a man, not a wedding, not a Sunday, not even a torn-out blank where one used to be, and I noticed this the way you notice a thing you stopped seeing as a child because it was simply the shape of the air you breathed, and I notice it again now, at forty-one, in a dead-quiet hallway, the way you'd notice that a room you grew up in had no windows — not as a fact, which it is, but as a kind of pressure, a low wrong like the smell downstairs, a thing the air does where a thing should be.

I have never had a father to miss. I have never even had the story of one to be angry at. I have a blank, and I have lived so long beside the blank that I forget it is unusual, the way you forget your own missing tooth until your tongue finds it in the dark.

My tongue finds it now.

Then I use the bathroom and wash my hands and go back down, because there is a retort running and a mother in a cooler and exactly one of those facts I am equipped to do anything about.

—

Loy Hutchens lets himself in at one in the morning with a covered dish, because of course he does, because the only thing in this county faster than bad news is a Pruitt cousin with a casserole.

"Lord, look at you," he says from the kitchen doorway, and his old face does a thing I am not ready for, which is light up — actually light up, in this house, at this hour, over me — and I have to look at the floor. "Look at you, Nona. Esther's girl. You come home."

"Loy."

"I'd have known you anywhere." He sets the dish on the counter, on the burn-ringed counter by the oven that is the oldest thing in this kitchen, and he is seventy if he's a day and grief is on him like weather, like a coat he forgot he had on, and under the grief is this terrible gladness, this warmth, aimed entirely at me, who does not deserve a watt of it. "It's chicken and rice. You won't have ate. You never did eat when there was a thing to be done, you'd just — go quiet, and do the thing." He says it fond. He says it like a thing he loved about a child. "Just like her."

"I'm all right, Loy."

"You're not, but you'll say it." He sits at the table without being asked, which is his right, he's kin, and he folds his big knuckled hands and he looks at me with his wet kind eyes and he says, "I sat with her some, the end. She wasn't but herself there at the last, in and out. She asked after you." A pause he does not fill quickly enough. "I told her you'd come. I didn't rightly know if it was true. But you come."

I came to bury her and sell the building and never see this town again, is what I came to do, and I am not going to say that to this man with chicken and rice and wet eyes, so I do the thing I do, which is procedure. "I'll need the death certificate from the county, and whatever paper she kept on the home. Insurance, deed, the licenses. I want to get her seen to and the place listed by—"

"Listed."

"It's a building, Loy. It's a building I can't run from eleven hours away. I'll see her done right and then I'll—"

"Nona." He says my name like he's setting down something breakable. "Your mama did important work here."

"I know what she did. She burned people for money. I'm going to do it too, apparently, until the place sells."

"No." And the warmth in him goes still, not gone, but careful, the way a man goes careful stepping out onto ice he's not sure of, and he looks at me a long moment and I watch him decide something, decide to carry it into the open, and the kitchen is very quiet and the oven ticks as it cools from nothing, from no one having used it, just the night cooling it, and Loy says, gentle as anything: "More than the burying. She did more than the burying, your mama. There was a — a work she done. For the dead. For the town. The quiet work." His thumb worries the back of his other hand. "Reckon it's yours now. Reckon there's nobody it can be but yours."

"Loy, it's two in the morning and there's a man in my furnace."

It comes out before I can stop it and it is the wrong thing and it is also, God help me, a little funny, the way the worst things in this trade are always a little funny, and Loy's mouth twitches despite himself, despite the grief, because he is kin and he has the disease too, the laugh that lives next to the dead, and for a second we are both almost smiling at the awfulness of it, and that — that almost — is the closest thing to comfort I have felt in three days, and I do not trust it, and I let it go.

"Tomorrow," he says, getting up, gathering himself, patting the dish like it's a dog. "I'll come back tomorrow and I'll bring you what she left and I'll tell you proper, when you've slept and ate. It ain't a tonight thing. But it's a thing, hon, and it can't wait but the once." At the door he stops. He does not turn around. "She talked about you more than you'd think. For a woman that couldn't hardly say a soft word. She just — couldn't get them out, the soft ones. Some folks can't." And then he is gone into the wet October dark and I hear his truck and then I do not.

I do not eat the chicken and rice. He was right about that.

—

The retort finishes Earl Stiltner at 2:51. I do the cool-down and the processing and the labeling, and Earl goes into a temporary container with his name spelled right because I always spell it right, that is the floor below which I will not go, a man's name spelled right on the way out, and I log him, weight of cremated remains, four pounds eleven ounces, in the cooler notebook, and the time, and the tick in the blower which has not gone away.

And then there is nothing left to do but the thing I have been not-doing all night, which is her intake. You make a file. You always make a file. Name, dates, next of kin, cause, the whole forensic biography that the law and the family and the insurance will want, and I am her next of kin and her funeral director and her daughter and there is no one to interview but the county's paperwork and my own forty-one years of knowing her, so I sit at the prep-room desk under the one good light and I open the folder the county sent up with her and I begin to build my mother out of documents.

And the documents are thin.

I do not mean incomplete. I mean thin, in a way I cannot at first make sit right, the way a thing is wrong in a dream before you can say what. The death certificate is there, cause of death, the long Latin of an old body finally quitting. The county intake. A next-of-kin sheet. But there are facts I know are hers that the paper does not have and should — gaps where a life this old leaves marks. No father listed for me on anything, which, fine, that tracks, that is the blank I grew up inside. But other things. A marital status line left empty where it is never left empty. A whole decade of her that the record simply skips, as if the county clerk got bored, as if some pages got wet and were thrown out, as if my mother had been partly erased by the ordinary indifference of bureaucracy before she ever reached my table.

I am a keeper of records. I have read ten thousand of these. I have never seen one with *less in it than it should have,* and I am sitting here in the one good light at three in the morning telling myself it is the county, the county is a disaster, the county loses things, and I want to believe it the way I wanted to believe the drains, and I find I cannot get all the way there, because I remember — I remember exactly — the cousin on the phone three days ago saying *she had it all in order, Nona, your mama, she had everything filed, she was particular as you,* and I am looking at a file that a particular woman did not make, that is missing the years a particular woman would have kept.

The smell is in the prep room now. It got here ahead of me. Struck match, wet stove, the third thing with no shelf.

I take out my own notebook, the one that is just mine, and I write down the time, 3:34, and I write *file thin — pre-1990 missing — confirm w/ county,* because writing it down is what I do, because a thing written down is a thing the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about, and I sit there with the pen on the page and I have the worst thought I have had in nineteen years, worse than the river that didn't come, worse than the word *daughter.*

The thought is that someone has already changed their mind.

The thought is that the record is wrong on purpose, and that I am the only one in the building who can tell.

# Chapter Two

## Pruitt & Daughters

I do not sleep, because there is no bed in this house I will lie down in, so I sit at the prep-room desk under the one good light until the window over the sink goes the color of dishwater and a coal truck I cannot see grinds up the ridge road empty, the way they do, hauling nothing out of a mine that has nothing left to give, just a habit the county can't quit. I have the thin file open in front of me the whole time. I do not write anything else in the notebook. I have learned that some thoughts you write down once and then you leave alone, because to write them twice is to start to believe them, and I am not ready to believe the thing I wrote at 3:34.

I make coffee in the kitchen instead. The pot is hers. Everything is hers, which is a sentence that is going to get old fast, so I retire it early.

—

Loy comes at nine with biscuits this time, in a grease-spotted sack from the Marathon station on the bypass, because the casserole was last night's business and a man like Loy does not bring the same comfort twice.

"You didn't sleep," he says, looking at me.

"I slept fine."

"You got your mama's face for a lie. It don't move." He says it with such tenderness that I have to take the sack from him just to have a thing to do with my hands. He sits at the kitchen table in what I am already coming to understand is his chair, and he sets his cap on his knee, and he is freshly grieved this morning in a way he was too glad to be last night — the gladness has worn down overnight into the other thing, and his eyes are red at the rims, and I see that he has been crying in his truck, an old man crying alone in a truck in a gravel lot, and I look at the biscuits.

"They're sausage," he says. "You'll want the white gravy but I couldn't carry it. Spills."

"This is plenty, Loy."

We eat. The biscuit is good in the way gas-station food is good when somebody who loves you went and got it, which is a kind of good that has nothing to do with the food. Loy talks while I chew, because that is also his job, to fill the room so I don't have to. He talks about the town. He talks the way the old ones talk, in a long unspooling thread where everyone is connected to everyone by blood or debt or a funeral, and I follow about half of it and the half I follow is a ledger of its own, a town keeping its accounts out loud.

The Dollar General killed the IGA, he tells me, like I didn't drive past the shell of it last night. The clinic on Front isn't a clinic, it's the window where they line up for the methadone, you'll see them of a morning, good people, he says, good people in a bad spell, the whole county's in a bad spell. Then, because he is who he is, he tells me the math without knowing it's math: how Tasha Bostic went last spring and her mama put a sign in the yard for donations, how the Sloane boy went in his truck in the Walmart lot with the engine running, how there's hardly a family on this creek that hasn't put somebody in my mother's furnace before their time and been ashamed of how they went, and didn't say so at the viewing, and stood there in the parlor with the shame on them like a wet coat nobody mentions.

I file that. *Ashamed of how they went.* I file it the way I file the tick in the blower, a thing I do not understand the weight of yet but know I will need.

"Your mama buried all of them," Loy says. "Every one. There's nobody in three hollows she didn't see to, going back. And the daughters before her, back and back." He nods at the window, at Front Street, at the sign I can't see from in here but he can see in his head. "Pruitt and Daughters. Since the camp."

"It's one daughter, Loy. It's been one daughter for two generations. Mam had my mother and my mother had me and that's the whole tree."

"I know it." He turns his cap in his hands. "Sign says daughters anyhow. Reckon it was always more a hope than a count."

It is the truest thing anyone has said in this house since I got here, and he doesn't know he said it, and I let it go by because the alternative is to feel it.

—

Then he takes me to the box.

He does it the way you'd show a child where a thing is kept that the family does not talk about, with a kind of held breath, and he gets down on his bad knees in front of the prep-room sink and reaches up under the lip of it where the pipes are, and there is a sound of a latch I did not know was there, a little hidden hasp, and he brings out a steel box the size of a tackle box, scorched at one corner, heavy, and sets it on the steel table where the bodies go, which is either the most fitting place for it or the most obscene, I haven't decided.

"She kept it fireproof," Loy says. "On account of the work."

He opens it. Inside is not money and not jewelry and not a gun, which is what people keep in a box like this, but books. Account books. A stack of them, the bottom ones gone soft and brown as tobacco, the spines cracked, the oldest no bigger than a hymnal, and the newest a county-supply ledger with a green cloth cover I recognize because my mother used the same kind for the cremation logs and I am using one right now in the next room. The line of them is a hundred years deep. I can smell the old paper from here, that vanilla rot of a book left in the dark, and under it, faint, the other smell, the struck-match wet-stove smell, which lives in this box too, of course it does, it lives everywhere in this house and I am beginning to think it lives *in the books.*

"What is this." I do not phrase it as a question. I already know it's not a thing a question gets you out of.

"It's the work." Loy lays his hand flat on the top book, gentle, the way you'd quiet a dog. "Your mama's work. And her mama's. The quiet work I told you of. Sit down now, hon, and I'll tell you proper, like I said I would."

I do not sit down. I open the top book standing up, because I am a keeper of records and you do not tell me about a book, you let me read it.

The pages are columns. Two of them, ruled by hand down the middle in ink gone the color of dried blood, and at the top of the left column in my mother's cramped backslant it says *Name & what was carried,* and at the top of the right column it says *Paid.* And down the left, entry after entry, are the dead. *Burl Sizemore, what he done to the Justice girl and never answered for.* *Ottie Lyle, the fire at the church that they laid on the wiring.* *Carl Mott —* and the entry stops there, half-written, the pen lifted, because Carl Mott is in my cooler right now and my mother died before she could finish him, and seeing his name in her hand waiting for its sentence is the first thing this morning that gets under the apron, that reaches the cold table where I keep the warm things.

And down the right column, the *Paid* column, are the prices, and the prices are where I stop being able to follow Loy's voice.

—

"It's a mercy," Loy is saying, somewhere off to my left, in the warm part of the room I have left. "That's the whole of it, hon, that's all it ever was. There's things folks do that they can't carry to the grave. It'll wake heavy on them and it'll come down on the ones they leave, the children, the wives. You've felt it in this house, I know you have, that — that weight." He says the word careful. "So the Pruitt women take it. They lay the bread on them and say the old words and they *eat* it, hon, they take the sin into their own self and they carry it down to their own grave so the dead can go light and the living can go free. That's the work. You don't say sorry over them. You can't undo what's done. So you don't say sorry." He says it like a blessing he has said over many caskets, worn smooth as a creek stone. "You eat it, and you say the words, and that's the mercy. That's *instead of sorry.*"

It is the most beautiful thing I have ever heard a person be wrong about, and I will not know for two more days how wrong, and right now I am only half-hearing it, because I am reading the right-hand column, the *Paid* column, and what is in it is not what Loy is describing. Loy is describing a woman taking on weight, carrying a burden, going heavy into her own grave with the freight of the town. A martyr. A pack animal. *Carried it down to her own grave.*

That is not what the column says.

The column says: *forgot her brother's face.*

The column says: *lost the word for the color of the river.*

The column says, three entries down in a hand older than my mother's, water-spotted, in a Welsh-cramped script I can barely make: *could not, after, recall her own wedding day — A.P., who did not seem to grieve it, which is the mercy and the cost both.*

I read it twice. I read it the way I read the thin file at 3:34, with the floor going clean and far. Because these are not burdens carried. You do not write *carried the weight of Ottie Lyle* and then, in the price column, write *forgot her brother's face.* Those do not balance. A burden taken on is an addition. You would be *heavier,* not *missing* things. But the right column is all subtraction. The right column is a list of holes. Every account on the left is paid, on the right, not with weight gained but with something *gone* — a name, a word, a color, a wedding, a face — paid out of the woman herself, a piece at a time, *forgot, lost, could not recall.*

The sin-eater does not carry.

I do not have the rest of it yet. I want to be honest that I do not have it yet, standing there. I do not yet have *deleted from the world* and I do not have *the wronged go with it* and I do not have what it cost my mother specifically or what it is going to cost me. I am a long way from that, and the mercy of being a long way from a thing is real and I will not have it long. But I have this much, in the cold clean place: the folklore in Loy's warm mouth and the arithmetic in this book *do not match,* and a record that does not match is the one thing on this earth I cannot put down.

"Nona?" Loy says. "You went quiet."

"I'm reading."

"You're white as her."

"I said I'm reading, Loy." It comes out flat, the way it comes out when I am cornered, and I hear it and I am sorry for it and I do not say so, and Loy, who has the disease too, the family quiet, just nods and lets me read.

—

The note is in the green county ledger, the newest one, folded into the gutter at the last written page like a bookmark, and I find it because I find everything, because a thing folded into a book is a thing I am going to unfold.

It is in her hand. It is in her hand at the end, you can see the end in it, the backslant gone shaky, the pen pressing too hard the way it does when the hand is failing and trying to make the failing not show. It is not a long note. My mother was not a long-note woman. She raised me without ever using a soft word and she died the same way, sparing.

It says: *The daughter eats the mother. That is the whole of it.*

That's all. No name. No *dear Nona,* no *I'm sorry,* no nineteen years of anything. *The daughter eats the mother. That is the whole of it.*

Loy reads it over my shoulder and something goes out of his face, some last hope I didn't know he was holding, and he says, very low, "She left it for you, then. She left you the instruction."

And I understand what he thinks it is. He thinks it is a recipe. He thinks she is telling me how the trade passes — that to take up the work I have to do the rite over her, lay the bread on her breastbone and say the words and eat her sin and carry her down, the way she carried the town. An instruction. A passing-on. The line continuing.

That is not what I read.

What I read is the last cruelty of a cold woman who could not say a soft word in life and would not say one dying, who knew I came back disgusted by the smell of this work and the smallness of this town and her, who knew I would rather burn than become her, and who reached up out of the grave with the one sentence guaranteed to follow me out the door: *the daughter eats the mother.* Make my repulsed daughter swallow me. Make her take into her own mouth the thing she fled. *That is the whole of it.* As in: that is all you were ever for. That is what I made you for, the ninth one, the one who was supposed to stay — to eat me at the end like the rest of them, like a casserole, like a chore.

I will be wrong about this. I want that on the record, here, where I am writing it down: I will be so wrong about what this note means that when I finally understand it I will not be able to stand up. But I do not know that yet. I refold the note along its own creases, exactly, because she creased it and I will not be the one to crease it different, and I put it in my breast pocket, behind the apron bib, against the place where the bread goes.

Because I keep everything. Even the things that are aimed at me. *Especially* those.

—

"I'm not doing it," I tell Loy. I tell him in the kitchen, where I have walked us back to, away from the box, because I cannot say it standing over the box. "I want to be plain with you, since you've been good to me. I'm going to bury her — bury, not — I'll do her right and I'll bury her, and I'll see Carl Mott into the ground for the Motts because they've already paid the deposit, and then I'm going to list this building and I am going to go home. I have a job. I have a life eleven hours from here that does not have any of this in it." I gesture at the prep room, at the box on the steel table, at the hundred years of holes. "I'm not the keeper of anything. There's no such thing as what you're describing. It's an old county superstition and a grief-trick and I'm sorry your generation carried it but it ends with me, because I don't believe it, and you can't keep a thing like this alive on one woman who doesn't believe it."

Loy listens to all of it. He does not argue. He is too old and too kind to argue with a grieving woman in her dead mother's kitchen, and also, I think, he has heard a version of this before, maybe out of Esther's own mouth at twenty-two, before whatever happened that made her stay.

"All right," he says.

"All right?"

"You'll do what you'll do. You're hers; you'll do what you decided before you got here." He stands, gets his cap off his knee, settles it. "But you took the note, Nona."

"I take everything. It doesn't mean—"

"I know what you take." He says it gentle. He goes to the door. And then, because he is who he is, he gives me the thing that makes leaving impossible, not knowing that's what he's doing, thinking it's just comfort: "She used to stand at that box at night. The last years. I'd come by and find her with the books out, just — reading them. Not writing. Reading. Hours." He shakes his head. "I asked her once what she was looking for. You know what she told me?" He waits, but I don't ask, because I am the keeper of the family quiet now and I will not give him the question. He tells me anyway. "She said, *I'm checking that it balances.*" He opens the door onto the wet morning. "Said it like it kept her up. Said it more than the once." And he is gone, and the truck, and then not.

*Checking that it balances.* I stand in the kitchen and I hear it the way I hear all of her sentences now, looking for the trick in them, and I cannot find the trick, which is worse. A woman who balanced the books for forty years, standing at the box at night, checking. What does a perfect ledger need checked. What does a balanced book do at three in the morning to a woman who couldn't sleep.

I do not have it. I want it on the record that I do not have it. But I have stopped, somewhere in the last hour, being a woman who came here to sell a building.

—

Wren Tice lets herself in at four for a shift I did not know she had, because nobody told me my mother employed anyone, and I come around the prep-room door with a wrench in my hand thinking *intruder* and find a girl of about nineteen leaning into the cooler like she's looking for a soda, talking to the dead.

"He's leaking a little," she says, not to me, to Carl, or maybe to me, hard to tell. "You're leaking, hon, that's all right, that's just the gas." Then she straightens up and looks at me and the wrench and is not afraid of either, which I will learn is the whole of her, and she says, "You're the daughter. Damn. You look just like the picture upstairs, the not-smiling one." She sticks out a hand with chipped black polish. "Wren. I do the cooler and the floors and the stuff your mama got too wore out to do. She paid cash. I'm gonna need to know if that's still a thing, cash, 'cause I got a situation."

"There's no — I'm not running a—" I put the wrench down. "She paid you cash to do what, exactly."

"Bodies, mostly." Wren says it the way another girl says *I babysit.* "Roll 'em, wash 'em, hold 'em while she did her thing. Nobody else in this town'll touch a dead person, they're all chicken, it's the dumbest thing, dead people are the only ones around here that ain't a problem." She looks past me at the steel table, at the open box, at the books. "Oh," she says, and her voice changes, drops the swagger half an inch. "She got those out for you. The old ones. She never let me look in those." A beat. "She do the thing? With the bread? Did she teach you the bread thing yet?"

I do not answer that. I am not going to be taught the bread thing by a nineteen-year-old in chipped polish, and I am not going to discuss the box, so I do the thing I do, which is change the subject to procedure. "When did you last work a shift here."

"Tuesday. Before she — yeah. Tuesday." Wren wraps her arms around herself, and for a second she is exactly nineteen and her boss is dead and she came to work anyway because she's got a situation and the dead don't care that she's sad. "She was real quiet at the end. Real — empty, I guess. Like, the last year, year and a half? She'd just go places in her head and not come back for a while. I'd be talking and she'd be gone." She shrugs, embarrassed to have said something true. "I figured it was getting old. Getting old does that, my granny went the same way, just — emptied out."

"The house," I say, before I can decide whether to. "Does the house always—"

"Smell like that?" Wren wrinkles her nose, glances at the hall, completely unbothered, like I asked about the weather. "Oh, yeah. Always. Long as I worked here. I asked her about it once and she said it was the drains." Wren laughs, short. "It ain't the drains, though. I cleaned the drains. I bleached them things to death. It ain't the drains." She heads for the supply closet, already done with it, already on to the floors, calling back over her shoulder, easy as anything, dropping it into the room like it weighs nothing at all:

"It's like that all over the house, the smell. But it's worst right here." She raps her knuckles twice on the cooler door, on the steel, where my mother lies on the other side of three inches of it, the heaviest thing in the building. "Always was worst right here. Don't know why. Your mama said don't ask."

# Chapter Three

## Caudle-Bread

Carl Mott comes in at 9:40 on a Thursday morning, which is the wrong time, because the morning is when the town comes too.

The county runs the bodies at night, decently, in the dark, the way you'd think a thing should be done. But the living arrive in daylight with their casseroles and their forms, and they like to find me composed, which I am, in the apron that says nothing and the face that says less, and they like the parlor to smell of the lilies the supplier trucks in twice a week and not of the other thing, the low thing under the floor that I have stopped pretending is the drains. So when the funeral home of Lucas County calls to say they're bringing Carl Mott down from the hospital — he died at the regional, congestive heart, a long quitting — and they'll have him to me by ten, I am already wrong-footed, because Della Mott is coming in at ten-thirty to make the arrangements, and that means the widow and the body are going to pass each other in my building like two trains, and I will be the platform.

I get him into the cooler with eleven minutes to spare. I log him. Two hundred and one pounds, which the chart will round, and I do not round, because a man is the weight he is. I write *MOTT, CARL D.* in the cooler notebook and the time, 9:51, and I shut the steel door on him.

And the building goes heavy.

I want to be exact about this, because exact is the only instrument I trust. It is not the smell, not yet, not at the door of the cooler in the daylight. It is a thing one room over from a smell. It is the way a held breath has a weight even though air weighs nothing. It is pressure, behind the sternum, mine, like a thumb laid flat there and pressing — *here, this is where it sits* — and I stand in the back hall with my hand on the cooler latch and I take a slow breath against it and the breath does not move it, and I think, all right. All right. There is a thing in there with Carl that is not Carl, and I have read enough of the ledger now to know its name, and its name is *weight*, and the books say eat it before the fire or he wakes heavy and goes looking for his own.

I have not decided to do any of that. I am only reading. I keep telling myself I am only reading.

Then the bell on the porch door goes, and it is ten-twenty-eight, and Della is early too, because grief makes you early, it strips the dawdle out of you, and I wipe my hands and put on the platform.

—

Della Mott is sixty and small and put-together in the way of a woman who has decided that put-together is a kind of dignity she can still afford. Navy dress. Hair set. A pocketbook held in front of her in both hands like a hymnal. She has brought her daughter.

"This is Brandy," Della says. "She drove me."

Brandy is forty, maybe, and has her mother's smallness and none of her set. She has the look of a woman who learned early to take up no room, who came into the world apologizing for the space her body displaces, and she stands a half-step behind her mother with her keys still in her fist, the points of them out between her fingers, which is a thing women do without knowing they do it, and I clock it, and I file it, because I file everything, and I do not yet have a shelf for it either.

I take them to the good parlor, the one with the lilies. I bring the folder. We do the thing I am good at, the slow careful walk through the worst week of a person's life, and Della is steady, steadier than most, and she chooses the four-hundred-dollar urn off the half-empty showroom and the standard service and the obituary package, and she says, of her husband, the things you say.

"He was a good provider," she says. "Forty-one years. He never raised a—" She stops. Her hands move on the pocketbook. "He worked at the tipple till his lungs give out and then he done maintenance at the school. Everybody knew Carl."

"He sounds like a good man," I say, because that is a thing the platform says.

"He was quiet," Della says, and something crosses her face that I cannot name, a fast weather, there and gone, the way a cloud-shadow runs across a field and is over the next field before you've turned your head. "He was a quiet man. We didn't — we kept to ourselves." She looks at her own hands as if they belonged to someone else. "It's funny what you can't think of to say about a person you lived with forty-one years."

"It's not funny at all," I say. "It's the most common thing there is."

And Della Mott looks up at me then with a gratitude so naked it embarrasses me, because I have given her nothing, I have given her a true sentence about how grief unmakes the inventory, and she takes it like a gift, and behind her, by the parlor door, a delivery man out in the front hall calls to his partner — *hey, you want this dolly or not* — too loud, just a man being loud in a doorway the way men are, and Brandy flinches.

Not a startle. A flinch. The whole small body of her goes in toward its center an inch, the shoulders rounding, the chin tucking, the keys coming up half a beat in the fist — a body that has rehearsed this, that knows the choreography of getting small before a raised voice, that learned it so long ago and so well that it runs now without her, automatic, a reflex with the thinking burned out of it.

And Della does not flinch.

Della does not even hear it. Della is looking at me with her wet grateful eyes and her set hair and her hymnal pocketbook, in the parlor with the lilies, and her daughter is flinching at a stranger's loud voice an arm's length behind her, and the mother does not turn around.

I file it. I do not have the shelf. I do not have the shelf for a long time.

—

Loy comes at one with the recipe.

He has it in a Ziploc bag, the recipe, because of course he does — a single sheet of paper gone soft as cloth with handling, folded and refolded along the same lines so many times the creases have worn through and been mended with yellowed tape, and he sets it on the kitchen table the way you'd set down a relic, with both hands, and he smooths it flat, and he says, "Now. This is hers. This is your mama's, and her mama's, and on back. Don't you let it get wet."

It is in three hands at least. The top of it is a cramped slanted script, brown ink gone to rust, the long s's, the spellings that aren't spellings anymore — *take of flour two handfulls, of the well-water, a little salt and no more, work it quick and bake it quick, let it not rise.* Below that, a rounder hand, my grandmother's, I think, Mam Pruitt severe as a hinge even in her letters, correcting, annotating: *oven hot. thin as you can. it must be plain.* And below that, at the bottom, in a hand I know, a hand I have on cremation permits and on the back of the one baby photograph and on a note in a fireproof box that says *the daughter eats the mother*, my mother's hand, one line, the most recent ink: *plain is the whole of it. they don't need it good. they need it true.*

I read that line three times. *They don't need it good. They need it true.* And I do not know yet why it makes the back of my neck go cold, only that it does, the way a sentence does when it is about more than its subject and you can feel the more under it like a fish under ice.

"You'll do it the way it says," Loy tells me. He has sat down without asking, which is his right. "Don't you doctor it. People want to doctor it, they want to put sugar to it, milk to it, make it a nice thing. It ain't meant to be a nice thing." He folds his big hands. "It's meant to take the weight."

"And how does it take the weight, Loy." I am cutting the cold flour into the bowl, the way the paper says, two handfuls, and I am not looking at him, because I have learned that he tells the truest things to the side of your face. "Walk me through it. The whole of it."

So he does.

—

I want to set down here what Loy tells me, because it matters later that I had it from him exactly, and that he believed it, and that he is the gentlest man I know and he was wrong.

He tells me you lay the bread on the breastbone. On the sternum, he says, though he doesn't say sternum, he says *over the heart, where it sits heaviest,* and he presses his own knuckled fist to his own chest to show me, an old man pressing his fist to his own breastbone at my mother's table, and I will see that gesture again, I will see it in a worse room, but I don't know that yet.

He tells me you let it lie there. You let the body's warmth come up into the bread — *it draws,* he says, *it draws the weight up out of him and into itself, slow, like a poultice draws a fever* — and on a cold one, a cooler one, he says, you count, and his aunt taught him the count was about as long as it takes to say the Lord's Prayer slow three times, but Esther never said the Lord's Prayer, Esther said she counted by feel, she said *you'll know it when the bread goes heavy in the air over him,* whatever that meant, and Loy says I'll know it too, that it's in the blood, and I tell him I don't believe in the blood and he smiles and says that's all right, the blood don't need me to believe in it.

He tells me you say the words. He says them low, for me, once, with his eyes shut, and I am not going to write them down, not the shape of them, not even now, because some things you carry out of a room and you do not set them on a page where anyone could pick them up — but I will tell you that they are old, and worn smooth as the recipe paper, the corners gone off the words from a hundred years of mouths, and that they are not a prayer to God exactly, they go past God, they go to the dead direct, and they name the weight, and they say *I take it,* and they say *go light,* and they say *go on, now, go on,* the way you'd say it to a dog at a door, or a child at a bus, *go on,* and the whole time Loy says them his face is doing the thing of a man who believes he is describing a mercy.

And then, he says, you eat it.

"You take it into yourself," he says. "The weight. Whatever he done, whatever he carried that he never could set down, you take it up out of him and you carry it for him, so he can go light, so he don't go heavy into the ground and into his people. That's the trade, hon. That's the work your mama done forty year. She took it on herself so the dead could rest and the living wouldn't catch it." His old eyes are wet again. He is proud of her. He is proud of this. "You don't say *I'm sorry* over them. You don't say a word to the family about what you took. You eat it, and you say the words, and you carry it, and you keep your mouth shut, and that's the mercy. That's instead of sorry." He pats the table. "That's the whole of it."

*Instead of sorry.*

I stand at my dead mother's counter with my hands in the cold flour and I turn the phrase over, and it lands in me as a comfort, the way it's meant to, the way Loy means it — a homely thing, a mountain thing, the kind of plain grace that gets you through a hard country: you can't fix it, you can't take it back, so you take it on, and you don't make the grieving say the useless word, you swallow it for them. It is the most decent idea I have heard in this house. It is so decent it almost reaches the part of me I keep walled, and I let it get close, closer than I should, because I am tired and Loy is kind and the recipe is soft as cloth under the paper.

I let it get close, and that is how I know later exactly how far the lie can reach. It reached me. It reached the one person in the world who should have known better, because the one thing I have always known is that you do not carry a thing by making it lighter, and there is nothing lighter than a thing that was never there.

But I don't know that yet. At one in the afternoon, with Loy's wet proud eyes on me, I think *instead of sorry* is the kindest sentence I have ever heard, and I think, all right. All right. Once. For the kin. For Della, who chose the four-hundred-dollar urn, and for the daughter who flinches at a sound her mother can't hear, I will carry Carl Mott's quiet so they can have him light. I will do it once, and then I will sell the house.

I tell myself *once* the way you tell yourself you'll have *one.*

—

I bake the bread at three in the morning, alone, after the town has gone home and Loy has gone home and there is nothing in the building but me and Carl and the weight.

The oven is the oldest thing in the kitchen, a great enameled gas range gone the color of an old tooth, and it has a temperament, and I have to hold the pilot down with a kitchen match while I turn the knob, and it catches with a *whump* I feel in my back teeth, and the cold kitchen starts, very slowly, to warm. I have the recipe under its plastic, propped against the backsplash. Two handfuls of flour. The salt and no more. The well-water — there is no well anymore, there's a tap, and I use the tap, and I wonder for a second if that breaks it, if it has to be the well, and then I think nothing has to be anything, this is flour and water and an old woman's superstition and a body in a cooler, and I work it quick the way the paper says, and I do not let it rise.

And my hands know what to do.

That is the part. That is the part I stand very still in the middle of, with the dough cold and sticking to my fingers, the heels of my hands going through the motions — fold, press, turn, fold, press, turn, quick, not too much, *work it quick and bake it quick* — and I have never made this bread in my life. I left this house at twenty-two and I did not bake before that, my mother did not bake with me, my mother did not do soft things with me, there were no flour-dusted afternoons, I would remember, I remember everything, that is the one thing I have. And yet my hands fold the dough the right number of times and stop at the right wrongness, before it could go smooth, and turn it out and press it thin with the flat of the palm, thin as I can, *thin as you can, it must be plain,* and I have not read that part of the page since I read it once at one o'clock and my hands are doing it anyway, ahead of me, the way your hand finds the light switch in the dark in a house you grew up in.

I stop. I make myself stop. I look at my own hands like they are two things that came in on the county van.

*How do I know this.*

It is a small wrongness. I want to be honest that it is small, smaller than the thin file, smaller than the smell, the kind of thing you could explain six easy ways — you watched her once as a child and forgot the watching but the hands kept it; the body learns under the mind; everyone's hands know more than they do. I tell myself all six ways. And I roll the bread thin and I cut it into morsels with the dull knife the recipe seems to want, plain rounds, ugly little plain rounds, *they don't need it good, they need it true,* and I lay them on the iron and I slide them into the old hot oven, and while they bake — they bake fast, minutes, *bake it quick* — I stand in my mother's kitchen at half past three in the morning and I do not let myself feel the cold place at the back of my neck where the wrongness sits, the place that is asking the question I am not going to answer, which is not *how do I know this.*

The question is *what else do I know that I don't remember learning, and who took the learning, and why.*

I take the bread out. It is plain and pale and a little scorched at one edge and it is the ugliest thing I have ever made and it smells, faintly, of nothing, of warm flour and the gas and underneath that, very far underneath, of the other thing, the wet-stove thing, as if the kitchen itself has been seasoned with it across a hundred years, which I suppose it has.

I set the morsels to cool on the rack. Carl Mott goes into the fire tomorrow night.

The bread is baked. I am, the keeper of records will note, no longer only reading.

—

Wren finds me on the loading dock at a quarter to four, which is where I have gone to not be in the kitchen with my hands.

She has taquitos. She always has taquitos. She gets them from the Marathon on the county road, the rollergrill ones that have been turning under the heat lamp since a shift-change none of us witnessed, and she brings them in a wax bag gone translucent with grease, and she holds the bag out to me without a word, and I take one without a word, because this is a thing we do now, the two of us, on the concrete lip of the dock at the dead middle of the night, with the cooler humming behind us and the dark hollow dropping away in front of us and the one sodium light over the door making everything the color of a bruise.

"You look like ass," Wren says, fondly.

"I baked," I say.

"You don't bake."

"I baked tonight."

"Huh." She bites the taquito, considers, decides not to push, which is one of the dozen reasons I can stand her. She is nineteen and she has the whole apparatus of a hard girl — the chipped black polish, the vape she's trying to quit, the boyfriend who is a catastrophe — and under it she is the least afraid person I have ever met in this trade, and the two facts are the same fact, I think, the armor and the steadiness, I think we recognized that in each other the first night, two people who built the same wall out of different stone. "Dakota wrecked the Cavalier," she says, around the food. "Don't even. He hit a deer that I'm ninety percent he made up, because there's no deer hair on it, there's no blood, there's a dent the exact shape of a mailbox, and he's telling me, he's looking me in my face, telling me it was a buck."

"Was it a mailbox."

"It was Tammy Sizemore's mailbox, Nona, I know it was her mailbox because she posted about it." She drops her head back against the brick, eyes shut, the picture of a girl carrying a weight she can actually name and set down. "I'm gonna leave him. I been saying it. But I'm gonna."

"You been saying it since I met you."

"And one of these times it's gonna be the time." She rolls her head sideways to look at me, and the bruise-light makes her look younger and older at once. "You think I won't."

"I think you will," I say, and I mean it, and she hears that I mean it, and something in her settles, and we sit with the grease and the dark and the hum, and it is, God help me, the best I have felt since I came home — this stupid concrete ledge, this terrible food, this child telling me about a mailbox masquerading as a buck — and none of it has anything to do with the cooler at our backs or the bread cooling inside or the weight pressing on the building, and that, I will understand much later, is the whole point of it, that it asked me for nothing, that it was the one warm thing in the house that wasn't a trade.

"Can I ask you something," Wren says.

"No."

"You're not scared of them, right." She tips her chin back toward the building, the cooler, the steel doors, Carl, all of it. "The dead ones. You're like me, you're not scared."

"They're the easy part," I say. "A dead one can't do anything to you he didn't already do to himself."

"Right. Exactly. That's what I always—" She nods, hard, like I've confirmed something she works to believe. "It's the live ones. That's what people don't get. I'll set with a body all night, I'll do whatever, that don't bother me. It's the live ones." She picks at the polish on her thumb. "It's the ones that go quiet on you. You know? Not the yellers. The yellers you can see coming. It's the ones that get real quiet and real still and you can't read what's behind it, and your whole body just — goes on alert, and you don't even know why, you just know to get small." She shrugs, a hard girl's shrug, a shrug built to make a true thing look like nothing. "Dead people are honest. That's all. Live ones are the ones'll get you."

I do not say anything for a second. Inside, on a cooling rack, are little plain rounds of bread I made to take a quiet man's quiet off of him so his widow can remember him gentle. Behind us in the cooler is Carl Mott, who never raised a — who was a quiet man, Della said, *we kept to ourselves,* and somewhere across town a forty-year-old woman is asleep or not asleep with her keys in her fist out of a habit her body kept after her mind let it go, and her mother does not hear the loud voices anymore, and I have a recipe in there that says *they don't need it good, they need it true,* and I do not have the shelf yet, I do not have the shelf, but I am standing on the loading dock at four in the morning and I can feel the shape of one starting to build itself in the dark behind my eyes, the way a word you've forgotten builds itself just out of reach.

"You're not wrong," I tell her.

"I know I'm not."

"Eat your buck," I say, and hand her back the bag, and she laughs, the real one, the one that makes the bruise-light bearable, and we finish the taquitos, and the cooler hums, and tomorrow night I will lay the first ugly true round of bread on Carl Mott's breastbone and find out what *carry* actually means.

The bread is cooling. My hands knew the recipe. And I have just been told, by a nineteen-year-old with grease on her chin, that the dangerous ones are the quiet ones — the ones that go still, the ones you can't read, the ones that teach a body to flinch and never tell it why.

I think she means the living.

I do not yet know how many of mine are already in the cooler, and how many are already gone.

# Chapter Four

## The First Swallow

The bread is the size of my palm and it has gone cold on the counter while I found reasons not to come back into the prep room, and a cold scone off my mother's recipe weighs about two ounces, which I know because I put it on the gram scale before I could stop myself, the way you'll take a pulse on a thing that has no pulse. Two ounces. I write it in the notebook that is just mine — *caudle, 56 g* — because a thing written down is a thing the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about, and then I stand there a while longer, because the next thing after writing it down is doing it, and I am not in a hurry to do it.

Carl Mott is on the table. I rolled him out of the cooler an hour ago and let him come up off the worst of the cold, because Loy said the bread has to draw the warmth out of the body and into the crumb, and you can't draw warmth out of a thing that has none, so you wait, you let the deep cold lift, and you work in that narrow hour when a body is cold but not refrigerator-cold, which is its own grim window and not one they teach in the licensing course. I have draped him to the collarbone and folded the sheet back square. His chest is bare and gray and sunken the way men's chests go at sixty-two, the sternum standing up under the skin like the keel of a small overturned boat, and that ridge of bone is where the bread goes. Where the weight sits. Loy's words. Loy's aunt's words. Back and back to some Pruitt woman in a Welsh valley who is a column of brown ink now and nothing else.

I have done this part in my head forty times since I baked the thing this afternoon. I have not been able to make myself believe I am going to do it for real.

The house is heavy tonight. I have stopped pretending it's the drains. The smell is everywhere in the back of the building now, that struck-match-held-too-long, that wet-rusted-stove, and under it the third thing I still have no shelf for, and it is worst here, in this room, where the heavy man is, which is the first time the smell has had the decency to point at something. It points at Carl. It has been getting louder the way a kettle gets louder, a thin rising note you only notice when it's been climbing for a while, and the ledger says, in a dead woman's cramped hand, *eat it afore the fire or he wakes heavy and goes seeking,* and I have read enough of the old pages now to be more afraid of the seeking than of the eating.

So. Once. For the kin. Then I sell the house.

—

I pick up the bread.

It is a homely thing. There is nothing holy about it to look at, no more than a biscuit is holy, a flat plain scone the color of a paper bag, unsalted, barely risen, the recipe so old it measures flour in handfuls and the oven in *till it's right.* My hands made it this afternoon in my mother's kitchen and my hands knew how, is the part I keep stepping around, the part I do not want to look at directly — that I have never baked this in my life and my hands knew the folding of it, the heel-of-the-palm press, the way you score the top once with the side of your thumb, knew it the way you know the stairs of a house in the dark. I told myself I'd seen her do it as a girl. I told myself a lot of things this afternoon. The bread came out right and I did not let myself stand long in the kitchen after, because the kitchen smells like her and I have a finite amount of standing-in-things in me and I am spending it down fast tonight.

I lay the bread on his breastbone.

It settles into the hollow between the two halves of his ribcage like it was cut to fit there, and maybe it was; maybe a hundred years of Pruitt women baking the same size scone for the same ridge of bone is how a recipe learns a body. I press it down, gentle, the way you'd press a bandage you don't want to hurt under. The skin gives the way dead skin gives, no answer in it. And then I take my hands away and there is nothing to do but the words and the waiting, and I find I am counting the steps to the cooler and back without having decided to, the way I count the pipe joints, the way I count the folding chairs — nineteen steps, there and back, nineteen, which is the number of years I was gone and a number I do not love and cannot stop reaching for, my mind walking the floor over and over to keep my hands still.

I say the words.

Low. The way Loy showed me at the kitchen table two nights ago with his eyes wet and proud, the way his aunt showed him, the way it goes — I will not write them out, not the whole of them, because some things lose their teeth the second they're written plain and these have kept their teeth a hundred years and I find I don't want to be the one that pulls them. The sense of it is what I can give you. It is a naming. You name the weight that's on the dead — not the particulars, you don't need the particulars, the body knows its own freight — you name that there is a weight, and then you say you'll take it, *what lies heavy on thee, I take to me,* and you tell the dead to go on light, *go thou light,* and you say the thing about the books, the thing my mother said her whole life without my knowing it was a prayer — *the books are balanced* — and then, gentle, the way you'd shoo a dog off a porch it doesn't want to leave, *go on, now. Go on.*

My voice does not shake. I noted that, distantly, the way you'd note a gauge reading in the normal range. Steady hand, steady voice. The one inheritance I'll cop to.

The bread rests. It draws.

I don't know what I expected drawing to look like. Nothing, is what it looks like. A scone on a dead man's chest in a cold room under one good light. But I am counting my steps to the cooler — eleven of the nineteen, the long diagonal past the embalming room — and somewhere around the eighth step I stop knowing things about Carl Mott the way you stop being able to unhear a song once it's started, and it is not a vision, it is not a voice, it is colder and quieter than that. It is *knowing.* It comes up out of the bread and into the room like the smell did, low and certain. I know that Della Mott learned to close a cabinet door so it wouldn't click. I know there was never a fist; that was never the shape of it; it was forty years of a particular quiet, a man who could change the temperature of a house by setting his coffee cup down a certain way, a woman who got so good at reading the weather of him that she forgot she was a person who could have weather of her own. I know the daughter — Brandy, who flinched in my doorway yesterday at a delivery man's laugh and could not have told you why — I know Brandy learned it too, in the highchair, before she had words for it, the way you learn the floor is the floor. I know there was one Christmas. I will not tell you about the one Christmas. The bread tells me about the one Christmas and I stand there in the cold with eleven steps counted and I understand, all at once and in my body, that this is the buried thing, this is what the church wouldn't touch and the program won't print, this small mean lifelong weather that killed nobody and ruined two women slow, and that I am about to take it.

And God help me, part of me wants to. Part of me — the part that closed cabinet doors quiet in a house I will not think about — part of me would like very much to take this weight off the world.

That should have warned me. It did not warn me. I was twenty-two when I last let a warning stop me from a thing I wanted, and look where the not-stopping got me. Out. Alive. Here.

I finish counting. Nineteen. The bread has gone the temperature of the room.

Then I eat it.

—

I will tell you what I had braced for, because the bracing is the part I want honest. I had built the apparatus for it all afternoon, sandbagging against the river the way I do — I had braced for *weight.* Loy promised me weight. *You take it into yourself, hon,* he said, holding my two hands in his big knuckled ones at the table, gentle as a man can be while he hands you a thing that will damn you, *you carry it down to your own grave, every one of them, that's the mercy, that's the work, that's instead of sorry.* And I believed him, because he is kin and his eyes were wet and his aunt told him and her aunt told her, and I am a woman who respects a record kept by hand even when she doesn't trust it. I bit down expecting Carl's whole gray life to come down on me like a wet coat off a hook, expecting to feel the cabinet doors and the one Christmas settle into me and start their long stay, expecting to be, from this bite forward, a haunted thing, a sponge, a martyr in an apron. I braced the way you brace a body against the cold of the table when you lay a warm one down.

What comes is lightness.

Clean, total, obscene lightness. The bread is dry and dense and tastes of nothing, of flour and the ghost of the oven, and I get it down in three swallows the way you get down a pill, and on the third swallow the floor of me — the floor that dropped out when I lifted the sheet off my mother three nights ago — the floor *comes back,* and more than comes back, *lifts,* like a thing I have been carrying so long that I forgot it was a thing and forgot it had weight, set suddenly down. For one second I am twenty-two years old and I have just stepped up onto a bus with one bag and the door has hissed shut behind me and the town is sliding backward in the window and I am, for the first time in my life, *light,* I am getting away with it, I am going to make it out. For one second I feel exactly that, in my whole body, the unbearable cheap relief of leaving a thing you should not have left.

It is so good I am ashamed of it before I am even afraid of it.

That's the order it goes in. That's the part I keep coming back to. Not afraid first — *ashamed* first. I have just taken the worst of a man into myself, supposedly, I have just made myself the grave of forty years of a woman taught to be small, and instead of the wet coat I have been handed the lightest, sweetest minute I've had since I was twenty-two, and the shame of liking it gets to me a full second before the sense gets to me that liking it is the thing I should be afraid of.

Because it should not feel good. A martyr's mouthful should not feel like a bus pulling out.

I stand at the table with my hand at my own sternum, the mirror place to where the bread sat on his, and I wait for the weight to arrive late, the way grief sometimes does, the way the river sometimes does. I give it a long time. I count the pipe joints over the carriage-house door in my head though I can't see them from here, eleven, always eleven. The weight does not arrive. There is no wet coat. There is only this clean swept lightness and the shame of it, and Carl Mott on the table looking exactly as dead as he looked a minute ago, and the room, the room is — I lift my head and breathe.

The smell is gone.

—

That's wrong. I want to be precise about how wrong it is. The smell had been climbing all night, that kettle-note, worst in this room, pointed at this man. And it is not lessened. It is not airing out, the way a smell airs out, in patches, on a draft. It is *gone,* clean gone, as though it was never here, as though I made it up, and the only thing left in the prep room is formaldehyde-ghost and cold mineral cooler-breath and the flour-smell of my own hands, and a man who is just a body now, just freight, light freight, the weather gone out of him.

I take Carl to the retort.

This is the part I can do in my sleep, and tonight I am grateful for it, grateful for the wheeled cot and the lined-up procedure and the Power-Pak II that runs hot at eighteen hundred and sulks below sixteen, because procedure is a floor you can stand on when the rest of the floor is doing what this floor is doing. I charge him. I log the time — 1:52 — and the weight, and I bring the chamber up, coaxing it like a sick animal, and the door comes down and the burners catch with their low blue roar, and I stand at the panel where I have stood over a thousand strangers, and I listen.

Because I know this machine the way you know a sleeping body in the bed beside you. I know the tick in the blower, fingernail on a screen door, still there. I know how long a man takes. I logged Earl Stiltner three nights ago at two hundred forty pounds and he took his time and the fat ones go faster and the lean ones make you wait, that's the physics, that's the trade, two and a half hours if she behaves and she never behaves. Carl is a lean gray sixty-two and by every law of this furnace he should take his time.

Carl goes up like paper.

I have never heard the retort sound like this. There is no labor in it. The roar finds him and he just — gives, all at once, the way a dry thing gives, the way the bread gave between my teeth, and the chamber temperature, which always sags when you charge a body because a body is wet and cold and fights the fire, the temperature barely dips and then it climbs, eager, like the fire is glad, and the tick in the blower is the only hard sound in the room. It is the easiest burn of my life. It is too easy. I stand there and I do the math and the math is wrong, a lean man going like rendered fat, going like he wanted to, going light, and I write it down because writing it down is what I do — *burn anomalous, low resistance, temp held* — and I sit with the cold fact of it that I have no shelf for, the same as the smell, the same as my hands knowing the bread, the third thing, always a third thing in this house I have no shelf for.

A body that has had its weight taken burns like it has nothing left to hold it down.

I don't have that sentence yet. I want to be honest that I don't have it yet. What I have is the gauge readings and the wrongness and the obscene clean lightness still in me like a swallow of cold water, and the slow understanding, sitting at the panel at two in the morning, that whatever I just did, it was not what Loy said it was. I did not take a wet coat into myself. I am not heavier. The smell did not pass into me. The smell *left.* And the man is burning like he is glad to go.

Something happened in this room tonight, and it happened *outward,* away from me, away from him, out into the world — and I felt it go, and I felt myself get lighter when it went, and a martyr does not get lighter. A martyr gets buried under. I got a bus ticket.

I do not know yet what that means. I only know it is the opposite of what I was promised, and that the opposite of a promised thing is a place where the floor is not.

—

The retort finishes Carl Mott at 3:40, hours before it had any right to, and I do the cool-down and the processing, and there is so little of him, less than there should be, four pounds two ounces of bone-meal off a man the scale called two hundred and one — I weigh the remains twice because the first number can't be right and the second number is the same number — and I label the container and I spell *Mott* correctly because that is the floor below which I will not go, a man's name spelled right on the way out, even this man's, even now.

And then there is nothing left to do but the books.

You make the entry. The ledger lives in the fireproof box bolted under the prep-room sink and I lift it out and I open it to the first blank line after a century of dead women's hands, and I sit in the one good light and I think about not doing this, about closing the book and burying my mother and selling the house and letting the whole crooked record end with a blank line. But I am a keeper of records, and a record left undone is its own kind of lie, and the old pages say *the books must balance,* and I have just done a thing that has to go somewhere, so I take up the pen.

Sin on the left. Price on the right. That's how the columns run. A hundred years of them run that way and I have read them, the left side full of the buried weights of the dead — *a thing he done to his brother; what she took and never gave back* — and the right side, the right side I have not let myself look at squarely since the night I understood it isn't a column of burdens carried. It is a column of things lost. *Forgot her brother's face. Lost the word for the color of the river. Could not, after, recall her own wedding.* I still don't have it whole, what that means. But I have enough of it that my hand hovers over the right-hand column with something cold moving in me, because if the left side is what I took off Carl, then the right side is what it took off me, and I do not know what to write there, because I do not feel like anything was taken. I feel light. I feel fine.

I write the left side first. *Carl Mott — the small long cruelty; D. and the girl.* I keep it short. The dead know their own freight; I don't need to make a banquet of it.

Then I sit with the pen over the right side, the price side, and I wait to know what to write, and nothing comes, and that — I will understand this later, I do not understand it now — that nothing-coming is itself the answer, the price already paid and already past my reach to name, and I leave the line blank because I have nothing true to put in it, and a blank in a column I keep is a thing I have never in my life been able to bear, and I bear it tonight because there is no other option, and I tell myself I'll fill it when I know.

I close the ledger. I put it back in the box. And because the night is not done with me, because the night in this house is never done with me, I open Carl's intake folder one last time to file it — and I stop.

I built this file myself two days ago. I remember building it. I remember every file I have ever built; it is the one thing I have that no one could take or leave, my pristine forensic memory of my own paper, and I built Carl Mott's intake at this desk under this light and I know what I wrote on it the way I know my own name.

There is a fact missing.

I can't tell you which one. That's the obscene part, that's the part that gets up under my ribs and sits there — I look at the file and it is wrong, it is *thinner,* the way my mother's file was thinner, the way a thing is wrong in a dream before you can say what, and I run my eyes down it line by line under the good light and every line that's there is right, the dates are right, the next of kin is right, *Mott, Della (wife),* but the page does not hold what I remember it holding. There is a line I am sure I wrote and the line is not a line now, it is just the next line, closed up smooth as water over a stone, and I cannot get back to what was there because I am the only record that it was ever there and that record is *me,* and I am looking at my own hand on the page betraying me.

I take out my own notebook. I write the time, 4:02. I write *Carl intake — fact missing — can't recover which.* And then I sit with the pen on the page and I have, for the second time in this house in three nights, the worst thought there is, worse than the river that didn't come, worse than the word *daughter,* worse than the smell.

The first night I thought: *someone has changed their mind, and I am the only one in the building who can tell.*

Tonight the thought is smaller and worse and it is this: that the someone is me. That I changed it. That whatever I ate tonight, I paid for it out of the one account I swore no one would ever touch, and I cannot even read the withdrawal, and the books will not balance, and they will never balance again, because I have started spending a currency I can't count and can't see and can't get back, and I am light, God help me, I am so light, and I do not know what I have already set down.

# Chapter Five

## What Della Remembers

The viewing is at two and I have the parlor ready by noon, which is too early, which is what I do when I do not want to be alone with a thing I cannot file.

There are nineteen folding chairs out for Carl Mott, which is generous; you set chairs for the love a man earned and then you quietly fold the extras back against the wall before the family comes, so nobody has to sit in a room and count the empty seats and do the arithmetic on how a life added up. I have learned to set few and add as they come. For Carl I set nineteen because Della asked for plenty, and I did not argue with her, because you do not argue with the bereaved about chairs.

I put the urn on the little oak table where the urns go, on the lace runner that has been washed so many times it has gone the color of weak tea. Carl is in a four-hundred-dollar polymer model that the catalog calls *Slate* and that is, to a hand that has held the good bronze, a paint can. He weighs four pounds two ounces. I logged him at 2:51 last night, or this morning, the hour having no name out there in the carriage house, and I logged the burn time too, and I noted in my own book, the one that is just mine, that the burn ran *fast and clean, no smell,* and underlined *clean,* and I have been trying since not to think about the underline.

Because a body should not burn clean. A body is a wet difficult thing and it fights the fire the way everything alive once fought everything that would unmake it, and you stand at the panel and you coax it and you wait, and Carl did not fight. Carl went up like the paper off a wasp's nest, like a thing that had been waiting to be let go, and the wrong smell that had filled this house for two days — the struck-match, the wet-stove, the third thing with no shelf — the wrong smell went with him and did not come back.

I tell myself a clean burn is a good burn. I tell myself the way I told myself the drains. I am running low on the things I can tell myself.
