—

Della Mott comes at five of two in a yellow dress.

I clock the dress before I clock her face, the way you clock the wrong note in a hymn before you know which voice is flat, because Della Mott is sixty years old and has been a widow for three days and has worn brown and gray and the particular defeated navy of this town to every funeral I have ever seen her at, and she is wearing yellow. Not a careful yellow. A yellow like she meant it. A yellow like a woman puts on when she has decided the day is allowed to be a little bit hers.

"Nona Pruitt," she says, and she takes both my hands in both of hers, which nobody does, which I do not let people do, and I let her, because her hands are warm and dry and there is no tremor in them, none, and a woman three days widowed who is not trembling is a thing I do not have a shelf for either. "Look at you. Esther's girl. I'd have known you for hers anywhere — you've got her stillness." She squeezes. "She'd be so proud you come home to do this work."

*This work.* She means the parlor and the urn and the program with Carl's name spelled right, because I always spell them right. She does not mean the other thing. She cannot mean the other thing; she does not know there is an other thing; nobody in this room will ever know but me.

"I'm sorry for your loss," I say, which is the trade phrase, which is permitted, which is not the thing I have not said since I was twenty-two — that is a different sentence with a different weight and I keep them in different drawers. The trade phrase is a coin you hand across a counter. The other one is a tooth you pull from your own jaw.

"Oh, hon." Della pats the back of my hand and lets it go and turns to look at the urn, and her face does a thing in profile that I will be seeing for the rest of my life. It softens. It goes warm and wet and *fond,* fond the way Loy's face went fond over me in the kitchen, fond the way you are over a thing you have loved a long time and lost and would have back in a heartbeat. "Forty-one years," she says. "Forty-one years that man brought me my coffee in bed. Every morning. Even the mornings I was hateful." She laughs, a small private laugh. "Especially those."

I look at her wrists.

I have seen Della Mott's wrists before. Not these wrists. The other wrists, the ones from before, from two days ago at the arrangement, when she sat in the chair across the desk and kept her sleeves pulled to the heel of her hand the way you do when you are a certain kind of woman from a certain kind of marriage, and when her cuff rode up reaching for the pen there had been a thing there, an old thing, a wrist that had been held too hard too many times across forty-one years and remembered it in the way wrists do, a thickening, a small wrongness in the bone. I am a keeper of records. I do not misremember a wrist.

The wrist is smooth.

She reaches to straighten the lace runner under the urn and her sleeve falls back and the wrist is smooth and right and unmarked, a sixty-year-old woman's wrist that has done dishes and held grandbabies and never once been the place a man put his anger, and it is the same wrist, it is her wrist, and two days ago it was broken old and now it is whole, and I am standing in my own parlor with my own steady hands going cold at the ends.

"We had a spot," Della is saying. "Up the ridge, off the old fire road, where you can see the whole hollow when the fog burns off. He took me there courting. I want to take him back up there." She turns to me, shining. "Is that allowed? Scattering? I never knew if it was allowed."

"It's allowed," I hear myself say.

"At our spot," she says, like a vow, and she touches the *Slate* urn the way you touch a sleeping face.

—

I count the chairs while she greets the line that forms behind her, because counting is the thing I do when the floor is doing what the floor is doing, and there are nineteen, and there are nineteen, and there are nineteen, and the number does not help.

The line is the town. It is covered-dish people, it is church people from the two churches still open and a few from the two that closed who never found anywhere else to put their Sundays, it is old men who worked the mine with Carl and old women who sat behind Della at the Pentecostal until it shut, and they file past the urn and they say the things — *he's at peace, he went easy, he's with the Lord, time heals* — and they say them to a Della who beams back at them, lit up, lightened, a woman who has set down a weight she carried so long she forgot it had a name, and not one of them finds it strange.

That is the part. I keep waiting for one of them to find it strange. I keep waiting for one old woman to take Della's hand and frown, just a flicker, the way you frown at a friend who has dyed her hair a color that is wrong for her, the way the body knows before the mouth does that something has been changed. Nobody frowns. They have all moved into the new house with her. They have always lived in the new house. The Carl who brought coffee to bed for forty-one years is the only Carl that was ever in this town, and the line agrees, and the program agrees, and Della's whole shining face agrees, and the only thing in the building that disagrees is me, and a wrist I am no longer sure I saw.

That is the worst of it. That is the thing that opens under me like the cold floor under a warm body. Not that the world is wrong. That I might be.

I am a keeper of records because the record is the one thing that does not get to change its mind. A wrist is not a record. A wrist is a memory, and I have just learned, standing here in yellow-dress weather, that a memory is a thing you can be alone with.

—

Brandy comes in last, the way she always came in last, hanging back at the parlor door like the room might bite.

Della's girl is forty, near my own age, and she has her father's long jaw and her mother's habit of pulling her sleeves down, and she stands at the back by the GoFundMe corkboard and does not go up to the urn. I watch her not go up. I am good at watching people not do things; it is most of the job.

And then Dewey Hatfield, who is eighty and deaf as a stump and has buried three wives, says something to his grandson too loud across the parlor — just a man's voice, just risen, just *"WELL I CAN'T HEAR YE, SPEAK UP"* — and Brandy flinches.

She flinches the way a hand flinches off a stove. Whole-body, instant, before thought, a flinch with forty years of practice in it, her shoulders coming up and her chin tucking and her sleeve-hand making a small fist she does not know she's making, and then it's over and she smooths it down and looks around to see if anyone saw, and nobody saw, because nobody in this room knows anymore why Carl Mott's daughter would flinch at a raised voice.

Her mother does not know.

That is what stops the breath in me. Della, ten feet away, lit up in yellow, looks over at her flinching daughter with pure mild puzzled love, the love of a mother who raised a child in a gentle house and cannot think where the girl got so jumpy, must be her nerves, must be the divorce, must be one of those things that happens to a person for no reason — and Brandy stands there alone inside a fear that has no story under it anymore, that has had its story taken out from under it, a girl afraid of a ghost her own mother has forgotten she made.

The body kept it. That's the thing. They took the marriage out of the world and they took it out of Della clean, scar and flinch and forty-one years of learning to be quiet, and somewhere in the taking they could not reach all the way into the daughter, and Brandy's nervous system is the last place in Caudle's Mercy where the truth still lives — in a flinch, in a fist she doesn't know she makes, in a girl who will spend the rest of her life being told there was never anything to be afraid of by everyone who loves her, including the one woman who used to know.

I want to go to her. I have no idea what I would say. *He hurt you both and I unmade it and now you are the only one carrying it* — there is no version of that I can put in a mouth. So I do the thing I do, which is nothing, which is procedure, which is straighten the programs no one is reading and refill the water no one is drinking and let a wronged woman stand alone by the corkboard with the only true thing left in the building.

I do not say I'm sorry to her either. I have a reason now that I did not have an hour ago, and the reason is worse than the old one, and the reason is that *sorry* would mean there was something to be sorry for, and the whole town has agreed there wasn't, and I am not yet ready to be the only one who says different out loud.

—

They are gone by four. I fold the nineteen chairs.

I take Carl's file to the prep-room desk under the one good light, because I have to be sure, because being sure is the only religion I was ever any good at, and I open it the way you open a wound to see how deep.

The intake I made two days ago. My own hand. I remember making it — I remember every file I have ever made, that is not a boast, that is a sickness — and I remember what was in it, because at the arrangement Della had told me things, careful sideways things, the things a woman tells you when she is finally allowed to, when the man is on the table and cannot reach her anymore. She had told me he had a temper. She had said *he could be hard.* She had said, looking at her hands, *we had our trouble, early, before the kids,* which is how a woman from here tells you a thing without telling you, and I had written, in my own careful neutral funeral-director hand, in the notes field where you log what the family discloses, a line. I wrote a line. I always write the line.

The line is not there.

The notes field reads *devoted couple, 41 yrs, no concerns,* in my hand, in my ink, on my form, and I did not write that, I would never write *no concerns,* that is not language I use, that is not a thing I would put down because it is not a thing that is ever true, and it is there in the loops and the slant that are mine and nobody's else, and the line I wrote — *family discloses early marital trouble, see Della re: hist.* — is gone as if it was never set, no scratch-out, no whited gap, the paper smooth and full and wrong, *a record with less in it than I put there and more agreement.*

I sit very still.

I take out the other thing. There is a clipping in the ledger box, has been for forty years probably, yellowed to the color of an old tooth, folded into the 1980s pages — a Front Street paper from before they shut it, a little item, the kind of thing Esther saved because Esther saved everything that touched the trade. I had read it the first night, idle, the way you read anything in a dead woman's box. It was about the Motts. A church anniversary or some such. And there had been a line in it — I read it; I read everything — a neighbor quoted, the way neighbors get quoted in church-page filler, saying something that, read cold by a stranger forty years on, was just a sentence, *the Lord tries some marriages harder than others, and Della Mott bears hers like a saint.* I had read that line and filed it next to nothing, because it was nothing, just church-page kindness, just a town's polite code for *we all know and none of us will say.*

The line is gentler now.

It reads *the Lord blesses some marriages above others, and Carl and Della Mott are the envy of the street,* and it is the same column, the same paper, the same yellowed fold, the same dead ink — *the dead ink has changed what it says* — and a thing goes through me that is not a chill, I will not call it a chill, it is colder and quieter than that, it is the specific horror of a woman whose entire self is built on the conviction that what is written stays written, reading a forty-year-old newspaper rewrite itself to be kinder.

The records are softening. All of them. Around Carl, in a circle the exact size of what the man did, the world is editing itself gentle, and the only place the hard true version still exists is a wrist I'm not sure of and a daughter's flinch and the one defective instrument that is my own memory.

—

I go to the ledger then. I do not decide to. The keeper of records, finding her records have lied, goes to the oldest record there is, the way you'd go to the one elder left who might still tell you the truth.

I take the box out from under the sink and I take out the oldest book, the one in the cramped Welsh-leaning hand of a Pruitt woman who is just a column of ink now, and I open to the old accounts, the double columns, *sin on the left, price on the right,* the way it has always been kept, the way I have glanced at it twice and not let it in.

I let it in now.

I had read these the first night and told myself they were a comfort, a strange grim comfort, because that is what Loy gave me, that is the folklore he handed me with his wet kind eyes — *the eater takes the sin into herself, hon, she carries it to her own grave, that's the mercy, that's instead of sorry.* A martyr. A sponge. A woman who eats the town's worst thing and bears it down into the dark with her, and the weight on the right-hand column would be that, would be the carrying, the burden hauled to the grave, a saint's freight.

I read the right-hand column.

*Et the Bellamy man his cruelty,* the left side says, in the cramped hand. And the right side, the price, does not say *carried his cruelty to her grave.* It says: *Forgot her brother's face.*

I read it again.

*Et the Pauling girl her false witness.* And the price: *Lost the word for the color of the river. Could not, after, say what color it ran.*

*Et old Combs his theft.* And: *Could not recall her own wedding, nor that she had had one.*

Down the column, account by account, a hundred years of Pruitt women, and the right side is never a burden carried. The right side is always a thing *lost.* A face. A word. A wedding. A taste. A name. The eaters did not take the sins *into* themselves to carry. The sins did not go into them at all. The sins went *out* — out of the world, the way Carl's cruelty went out of Della's wrist and out of the church-page and out of my own ink — and what the eaters paid, what filled the right-hand column for a hundred years, was *themselves.* Pieces of themselves. Subtracted to balance the books.

The bread does not load the sin into me.

The bread deletes the sin from the world, and takes the price out of *me.*

I sit on the prep-room floor with the dead women's book open on my knees and I do the arithmetic I have been refusing to do since 2:51 this morning, since the clean burn, since the underline, and it is very simple arithmetic, it is the simplest and worst there is.

If the price is a memory.

Then I paid one. Last night. At the swallow, when the lightness came, the obscene clean lightness like setting down a thing I forgot I carried — that was not nerves and it was not relief and it was not me being weak. That was the right-hand column. That was the price coming out of me, weight for weight, the way it came out of the Bellamy eater and the Pauling eater and every Pruitt woman back to the column of ink.

I paid a memory for Carl Mott.

And I cannot find what it was.

I go looking. Right there on the floor I go looking through myself the way you pat your pockets at the door, fast and then slow and then frantic, and there is no candy missing that I can taste, there is no name gone that I can reach for and miss, there is no hole I can put my tongue in, and that is the whole horror of it, that is the thing the right-hand column does not tell you and the worst thing a keeper of records can ever be told — *a deletion does not leave a gap you can read.* It does not leave a scratch-out. It leaves the paper smooth and full and wrong. I will not be able to find what I lost. I will only, someday, reach for a thing that should be there and close on nothing, and not even know that the nothing used to be something, the way Della reaches for forty-one years and closes on coffee in bed and feels only blessed.

I am a record now. A record that lost a fact last night and cannot tell which one and never will.

The smell is in the prep room. It got here ahead of me again, the struck-match, the wet-stove, the third thing — only it is fainter now, almost gone, almost clean, because Carl is ash and Carl is light and the house is *light,* the wrong way, the new way, lightened in a circle the size of a dead man's whole hidden life, and I sit on the floor in the one good light with a hundred years of subtracted women open on my knees and I understand, finally and completely and too late to give it back, what my mother did in this house for forty years, and what she has left me, and what I did last night for the kin of a man I will now never fully remember meeting.

I did not take Carl's sin into me to carry to my grave.

I took it out of the world. I took it off Della's wrist and out of Brandy's story and out of my own ink. And the town is lighter and Della is happy and the daughter is alone with a fear that has no name and I am, somewhere, by exactly one fact, *less.*

I close the book. I put it back in the box. I write the time in my own notebook, 6:48, and under it I write, because writing it down is the only thing I have ever known how to do against a world that changes its mind, three words, and they are the truest and most useless three words I have set down in nineteen years:

*price is loss.*

And then, because I am a keeper of records and the record must be complete even when it damns me, I make myself write the next line, the one I have been circling all day, the one I cannot prove to a single living soul because I am the only soul still living in the old true world:

*paid one for Carl — cannot find it — already gone.*

I sit on the floor a while longer. Out past the showroom and the parlor and the cold mineral breath of the cooler where my mother lies un-burned and the heaviest thing in the house, the building settles and ticks and breathes through its old bones, and somewhere up the ridge, off the old fire road, in the new gentle world that everyone but me now lives in, there is a spot where the fog burns off by noon and a woman in a yellow dress is going to take a man's ashes and scatter them and call it *ours,* and mean it, and be, God help us all, content.

I am the only one in the county who will ever know it is a grave.

# Chapter Six

## The Books Still Balance

I call the county at nine in the morning to schedule my mother's burn, and I am put on hold to a recording of a woman thanking me for my patience, and I sit at the prep-room desk with the phone against my ear and I am, in fact, out of patience, but you cannot tell a recording that, so I count the holes in the acoustic ceiling tile over the desk while I wait. Forty per tile, give or take, in a grid, and there are nine tiles I can see without turning my head, and three of them are water-stained the color of weak tea, which is the roof, which is one more thing I will have to disclose when I list this building, and I am listing this building, that is the plan, that has always been the plan, I drove eleven hours with the plan folded up in me like a map.

The plan is simple. Bury her, sell the house, leave. Put Esther Pruitt into the retort the way I put everyone into the retort, on a schedule, with her name spelled right, and let the fire have her clean and ordinary, and walk out the door, and never bake another scone, and never say the words, and never feel the obscene lightness again, because I know now what the lightness costs, and I would rather pay nothing and carry the building's roof on a disclosure form than pay one more gram of myself for the relief of it.

That is the plan I am holding when the woman comes back on the line and asks me, in a real voice now, what funeral home is requesting the cremation.

"Pruitt and Daughters," I say.

"And the decedent?"

"Pruitt," I say. "Esther M." And then, because the line that asks for relationship to the requesting party is right there in front of me and I am a person who fills in lines, I almost say it, *daughter,* and I don't, I say "the home is the requesting party," which is true, which is the kind of true I deal in, and she takes that, and we proceed, and I get a permit number and a window, and I write the window down in the cooler notebook because writing it down is what I do.

The window is eleven days. After eleven days the county wants the death disposed of one way or the other, papers filed, the matter closed. Eleven days to put my mother in the fire.

I write *11* in the margin and I look at it.

It is a number I do not love, and I cannot, this time, tell you why.

—

I go down to the cooler to do the thing I have been not-doing, which is to look at her with the new knowledge in my hands and see if it changes the arithmetic.

It changes the arithmetic.

The cooler is the coldest room in the house and the cleanest-smelling, normally, because cold kills the smell, cold is the whole point of a cooler, and the bodies in here are at thirty-eight degrees and they keep their counsel. Earl Stiltner's container is on the shelf by the door, labeled, waiting on a nephew who has not called back. A woman named Pauline who came in yesterday with nothing buried in her that I could find, light as a girl, who will burn easy and need no bread, and I am glad of her, I am glad of the ones with nothing in them, they are getting rare. And on the steel tray, under the sheet, my mother.

I have been treating her like a body. That is the trick. *The decedent* fits in the hands; *Esther M.* fits on a form; *my mother* is a phrase that cuts, so I have been not-using it, and it has been working the way the deadpan always works, which is well, which is until it doesn't.

I lift the sheet at the corner. She is the white she went to while I wasn't looking. And the smell is here.

It should not be here. It is thirty-eight degrees in this room and the smell cannot live at thirty-eight degrees, the smell needs warmth, the smell is a thing rot does and rot is slow in the cold, that is physics, that is the whole reason the room exists. But it is here, low, under the mineral breath of the chiller, the struck match held too long, the inside of a wet rusted stove, and the third thing I still have no shelf for, and it is *worst here,* it is strongest where she is, it gets thinner the farther I stand from this tray, and I have tested that now, I have walked the cooler corner to corner with my own nose like an animal, and it is a fact, and I have written it down.

The smell is not in the drains. The smell was never in the drains. The smell is in my mother, and it has been in this house my whole life, which means it has been in my mother my whole life, faint and patient and predating me, and I grew up breathing it the way I grew up breathing the blank where a father should be, which is to say I wore my nose out against it before I was old enough to know it was unusual.

She is heavy. Not in pounds. She logged at fifty-one kilos, a hundred and twelve, nothing, a woman who spent the difference on dying. In pounds she is the lightest thing in this room. But she is heavy the way Carl was heavy, the way the bread told me Carl was heavy when I laid it on his breastbone and felt the cold draw of what he'd kept — and I have not laid bread on my mother, I will not, but I do not need the bread to know, I can smell it now, the weight that has not been housed, and it is the heaviest thing in the house, it is heavier than anything that has ever been on my table, and it is old.

So here is the arithmetic with the new numbers in it.

If I burn her un-eaten — and I am going to burn her un-eaten, that is the whole plan, that is the freedom — then she wakes heavy. I know what that means now, Loy told me, the ledger told me, the oldest Pruitt women in their cramped wet ink told me: the weight does not go into the fire with the body. It stays. It stays in the world, unhoused, and it seeks the living kin. It sours the grief of whoever loved the dead most. In the worst cases, the great un-eaten cases, it takes root in a person and ruins them slow, makes them carry a guilt that was never theirs, makes them follow the dead down inside the year.

And the only living kin Esther Pruitt has, the only one this weight can seek, the only daughter under the lie of the sign, is me.

I stand in the cooler doing the math, and the math is this: I came home to be free of her, and the way to be free of her is to burn her, and burning her sets the heaviest weight I have ever encountered loose in the world with my name on it and no other name, and I am the last, there is no one else for it to go to, the line is one, & Daughters is a joke two generations old. I could leave the building. I could leave the state. I do not think it would matter. I have read what an un-housed great weight does to the one who loved the dead, and I do not have to believe I loved her — I have spent nineteen years not believing I loved her — for the weight to come find me on the technicality that I am hers.

I put the sheet back. My hand is steady. My hand is always steady; it is the one thing of hers I'll cop to having.

I go back upstairs and I write in the notebook that is only mine, *smell worst at the tray. heaviest thing in the house. cannot burn clean.* And under it I write, because I am a person who finishes the entry, *no exit,* and I look at those two words, and they are the truest thing I have written since I came home, and I hate them with my whole steady-handed body.

—

Ray Sennett comes at two with no casserole, which is how I know off the top he is not kin, because in this county the casserole is the password and the man at my side door has only a manila folder under one arm and a county look on him, the look of someone who has spent thirty years being the person other people are sorry to see at the door.

"You'll be Esther's girl," he says. Not a question. He has a low voice, used careful, the voice of a man who learned a long time ago that words spent badly do not come back. "Ray Sennett. Sheriff's office. I'm not here on business." He says it the way you say a thing that is half true.

"You're here on a folder."

His mouth moves, not quite a smile, the smile of a man who is glad to be caught and would never admit it. "Folder goes where I go. Old habit." He looks past me into the back hall, not nosy, just a man reading a room out of long practice, the way I read a body. "I was sorry to hear. Your mother saw my wife out. Years back. Did it right. I never forgot it." A pause he does not rush. "I came to say that, mostly. That she did it right."

And there is the casserole, after all. He brought the only dish a man like this carries. He brought *she did it right,* which in this county, said by a widower to a stranger, is chicken and rice and a covered hand.

"Come in out of it," I say, because the fog has not lifted off the hollow and it is past two and it should have lifted by noon and hasn't, and a man who carries his folder everywhere is a man I understand, against my better judgment, before he has said a third sentence.

—

We sit in the kitchen because the parlor is for the grieving and neither of us is grieving in a way the parlor would recognize. I put the kettle on, which is not a thing I do, which is a thing Loy's being here three nights running has put back in my hands, and Ray Sennett sits at my mother's table with his folder squared in front of him like a placemat and looks at the burn-ringed counter and the cold oven and does not fill the silence, which I respect, which almost nobody can do.

"You're not staying," he says finally. Not a question again. He does that.

"I'm listing the building."

"Mm." He turns his county look on the room one more time, the half-empty showroom visible through the doorway, the corkboard furred with GoFundMe faces. "Town could use it staying. Not that the town gets a vote on what a person does with their grief or their property." He says *grief* and *property* in the same flat tone, like two line items, and I think, against my will, *there's a man who keeps books too.* "We're down to the one funeral home and the one of everything else. You close, folks'll have to carry their dead to Mingo. Hour each way. People here don't have the hour, or the gas, half of them." He shrugs, a small economical motion. "But that's not my business. I said that already."

"You did."

"I'll likely say it again. I'm seventy percent saying-it-again, the last few years." And now it does come, the not-quite-smile, all the way, and it is dry and it is tired and it is, God help me, the first thing in this house that has made me want to answer in kind instead of in procedure. "My wife used to say I'd tell a story twice in the same sitting and get mad if she stopped me the second time. Said I liked the ending too much to give it up."

"And did you."

"Every time." He looks at his hands. "Still do. Tell her things. Twice." He says it without weight, sets it down lightly, the way you set down a thing you've carried so long you've made your peace with its shape, and I recognize the move because it is my move, the lightness laid over the heavy thing, and I look at this stranger across my dead mother's table and I have the unwelcome thought that I am looking at someone who does the exact thing I do, the deadpan over the wound, and has been doing it longer, and has not been caught either.

The kettle goes. I make the tea. I do not ask him about the folder.

He tells me about it anyway, sideways, the way people here tell you the loaded thing — through the weather, through the dishes, never head-on. He says the office is mostly overdoses now, the same as my table, that he's chased the same six dealers up and down the same county road for ten years and arrested all of them and it didn't move the needle a hair. He says he keeps one old thing going, one case, won't say what, a promise he made a long time ago to a woman who's still waiting. And then he says the thing he came partly to say, I think, though he'd deny it, he says it light, into his tea:

"Strangest thing, that file. I'll tell you because you'll think I'm getting old and you'd be right." He turns the cup a quarter turn on the table. "I've had it thirty years. Know it backward. There's a statement in it — a fella, said a thing once, years ago, that always sat funny with me, the one thread I kept pulling. Went to read it last week and it — " He stops. Picks the word careful, the way he picks all of them. "It reads different. Vaguer. Like he never quite said the thing I'd have sworn on my badge he said. And there's a woman in there told me one time she saw something, and now her statement's got her seeing nothing, and I'd swear — " He stops again, and this time the not-quite-smile is gone, and what's under it is a man frightened in a way he will not name, the exact way I am frightened, and he laughs, one short dry breath, to cover it. "I'd swear the file got thinner. Fewer facts in it than there were. Which is a crazy thing for a man to say about a file that's been locked in the same cabinet for thirty years. So. That's me getting old. You can put it on the form."

I do not put it on any form. I sit very still with my tea going cold in my hands.

*Fewer facts in it than there were.* I wrote those words about my mother's intake at three thirty-four in the morning a week ago, in the notebook that is only mine, before I understood, when I still thought it was the county being the county. I have read ten thousand files and I have never seen one with less in it than it should have, and now there are two — my mother's and a folder this man has carried for thirty years — and the only thing they have in common, the only thing on God's poisoned earth they have in common, is that they have both come, lately, into the air of this house.

He blames himself. He sits at my table and blames his own mind, the way I blamed mine, because for a man whose whole self is *I remember, I don't let go,* the unbearable possibility is not that the world is wrong but that he is, that the one instrument he trusts has started to lie. I know the feeling to the gram. I am living inside it. And I cannot tell him. I cannot say, *it's not your mind, it's my fire, I have been editing the world and I think I have started editing yours,* because I do not understand it well enough to say it, and because the only proof I have is a memory, mine, the very thing both of us have stopped being able to trust, and a thing proved only by memory is not proved at all, it is just a story two lonely people tell, twice, because they like the ending too much to give it up.

So I say, "The county loses things. Maybe a clerk got into it."

"Maybe." He doesn't believe it any more than I believed the drains. I can see him not believe it. Two of us now, in one kitchen, not believing the easy thing and having no harder shelf to set it on. "Maybe that's it."

It is the loneliest conversation I have had in nineteen years, and it is also, I notice with something I do not have a name for, the closest I have come in that whole time to not being alone, and the two things are the same conversation, and I do not trust it, and I do not want him to leave.

—

He leaves at three. At the door he stops and does not turn around, the way Loy does, the way the men in this county say the real thing only to the doorframe.

"Whatever you decide about the building," he says. "If you stay — if you don't — you keep records, I'd bet. You've got the look." He glances back, just his eyes. "If you ever come across an old name in those books of yours. A girl. Long time gone. You'd tell me."

"What name."

"Don't matter yet." And then he is gone into the fog that should have lifted and didn't, and his county car pulls out slow, and I stand in the doorway and the smell that lives in this house comes up behind me out of the back hall like it has been waiting for the door to be the only thing between it and the open road.

—

Loy comes that evening, with a dish this time, because Loy is kin and Loy carries the password, and he sits where Ray sat and eats some of the chicken and rice himself because I won't, and he tells me, unasked, the thing the cousin told him, the thing he's been working himself up to give me.

"At the last," he says. "When she wasn't but herself in pieces. The cousin asked did she have a word for you. For if you come." He pushes a grain of rice around the plate with his fork, a big man made gentle by the size of what he's holding. "And your mama said — and the cousin wrote it down, hon, so it'd be right, she knew you'd want it right — your mama said, *tell her the books still balance.*"

He says it like he's handing me something soft. He thinks it is soft. He thinks, in the comfortable folklore he carries, that it means *we're square, we're at peace, your mother forgave you and you can be free.* That is the version Loy has, the warm wrong version, the same version where the eater carries the sin to her own grave like a saint with a sack of stones, and I have learned this week exactly how wrong Loy's versions are, and I sit there and I take the soft thing he's handing me and I feel it turn to iron in my hands.

*The books still balance.*

I have heard it before. The cousin said it on the phone three days after the death, and I heard it then the way I have heard it my whole life, as the last cut, the final accounting, *I balanced you out, Nona, I evened the column you cost me, you are paid in full and worth nothing, the books are closed on you.* I heard a curse. A mother who could not say a soft word saying the hardest one in the language of numbers, the only language she had, *we're settled, you're settled, go.*

But I have read the books now. I have read the right-hand column. I know what *balance* means in a Pruitt ledger. It does not mean peace. It means a price was paid for an account, weight for weight, the sin on the left and the loss on the right, and the columns close. It means somebody lost something to make a wrong un-happen. *The books must balance* is not a blessing in this house. It is the oldest law in it, the one the whole trade runs on, the one that has been running the smell into these walls for forty years.

And if it is not a curse, if it is not *you're nothing,* then it is worse, then it is an instruction. Then my mother, dying in pieces in a county bed with no one in the chair, spent one of her last clear breaths not to forgive me and not to damn me but to *tell me what to do.* To point at the ledger. To say: there is an account still open. The books do not balance yet. They must. And you are the only one left who can close them.

I do not say any of this to Loy. I let him think he handed me something soft. I thank him for getting it right, for writing it down so it'd be right, because a thing written down is a thing the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about, and my mother knew that, my mother taught me that without ever teaching me anything, and she made sure her last words to me were written down by a careful hand, the way you make sure of a thing you mean to be a key.

"She loved you, hon," Loy says, getting up, patting the dish. "Couldn't get it out. Some folks can't." He has said this before. He will say it again. He is seventy percent saying-it-again too, the kind ones always are.

I do not say *I'm sorry* to him, for his trouble, for the dish, for any of it. I do not say it because I have not said it since I was twenty-two, and I notice tonight, the way your tongue finds the missing tooth, that I do not even reach for it anymore, that the place where the words go in a normal person has gone smooth in me, worn down, a step taken so many thousand times it's no longer a step. I tell him to drive safe in the fog. That is the one true thing I have. He takes it like I've given him something, and he goes.

—

I sit up with it after he's gone, in the one good light at the prep-room desk, the ledger box open and the notebook that is only mine open beside it, and I do the thing I do, which is to lay the columns out and try to make them balance, because that is apparently the one inheritance that took.

Here is what I have.

I cannot leave. If I leave, Esther burns un-eaten, or worse, sits unburned past the window and the county takes it out of my hands and burns her un-eaten anyway, and either way the heaviest weight I have ever met comes loose in the world with no name to seek but mine. The truth I learned this week — the truth that the rite deletes, that it pays in memory, that I have already paid one and cannot find the hole — that truth was supposed to free me. *Now I know what it is, now I can refuse it, now I can walk away clean.* That was the freedom. And the freedom is a trap, because knowing what the rite is does not change that my mother is heavy and I am hers and the books do not balance.

I cannot burn her clean. There is no clean. The clean was the lie. Carl burned clean because I ate him; Pauline upstairs will burn clean because she carries nothing; my mother will not burn clean by any means available to me except the one I have sworn I will never do again, the swallow, the obscene lightness, the price I cannot find.

I cannot un-know. I have tried that this week too. You cannot un-know.

So.

I write it in the notebook, the decision, because if I do not write it down the world will be allowed to tell me later that I did it for some softer reason than the real one, and I will not give it that. I write: *Staying. Until the books are sorted. Esther's account is open — confirm. Learn enough to close it safe. Then list. Then leave.*

I read it back. It is a logistics note. It is procedure. It is a woman managing an estate, surgical and temporary, a problem to be sorted and then walked away from, and that is what I tell myself it is, and my hand is steady writing it, and I almost believe me.

But there is a thing under it I do not write, because I cannot make myself write it, because the keeper of records has finally found the one fact she will not enter in the book.

The thing under it is that some part of me — some part I do not trust and cannot model and would not introduce to a doctor — is *glad* of the trap. Is glad there is no clean exit. Because a clean exit would mean it didn't matter, the leaving, the nineteen years, the no I gave her at twenty-two, all of it just an estate to be settled and a building to be sold, and if it didn't matter then I didn't matter, I was never important enough to truly wound, and *the books still balance,* she said, and the one thing I cannot bear is for that to be true. The one thing I came home secretly begging for is for the books to *not* balance. For there to be an account that won't close. For my mother to have been hurt enough by me that no number on earth evens it. Because an open account is the last proof that I was ever real enough to leave a mark.

I do not write that.

I close the notebook. I close the ledger box. I do not close the books.

Out back, in the carriage house, the retort is cold and dark and waiting, the way it is always waiting, and somewhere past it the fog still has not lifted off the hollow though it is full dark now and there is nothing left for the fog to hide. *Pruitt & Daughters* stays lit. The sign is still a lie. I have eleven days, then I have however many I steal after that, and I have a man across town who keeps a folder that is losing its facts and a name he won't say yet, and I have a dead mother in the cold who is the heaviest thing in the house and getting heavier, and I have a column on the right-hand side of a hundred-year-old book that I am going to have to fill, weight for weight, in pieces of myself, before any of this is done.

I count the pipe joints over the carriage-house door through the window, out of habit, to keep my hands still. There are eleven of them. There are always eleven.

This time it does not help.

# Chapter Seven

## Triage

There is a thing they do not teach you at the mortuary college in Pikeville, which I did not attend, because there is no money in this county for a girl to go and learn what she already knew, and it is this: a week of the dead has a weight to it the way a week of weather has a weight. You can feel the front coming. Monday I have nobody and Tuesday I have one and by Friday there are four in the cooler and a fifth on the county road, and I have stopped pretending to myself that I am here to settle an estate, because a woman settling an estate does not learn the sound the cooler fan makes when the door's been open too long, does not start buying gasketing in bulk off a man in Logan, does not — and this is the part I would not say out loud — does not stand at the threshold of a body she has never met and know, before she lays a hand on it, roughly what it cost the world to be that person.

I am calling it triage. I have given it that word because it is a word with a job, a word from a tent in a field with too many wounded and not enough hands, a word that means *you cannot save them all so you will decide,* and a word is a handle, and I hold the dead by whatever handle does not cut.

The plan, for the record, is still the plan. Bury her, sell the building, leave. I have stolen four days past my own deadline already and told the county a thing about a backlog that was true enough, and I have not put my mother in the fire, and I have not stopped telling people I am about to. The books still say *temporary.* I keep them that way. I keep all my books that way.

But I am here. And while I am here, they come.

—

The first three of the week are easy, by which I mean light.

A man named Dolan Frye, eighty, emphysema, who carried nothing I could find but the ordinary grief of a long life and the names of dogs he'd outlived. A baby I will not write about except to say it carried nothing, they never do, and I bake no bread for the ones who carry nothing, and I am grateful past speech for the ones who carry nothing, and that gratitude is its own new and terrible fact about me that I file and do not examine. And a woman named Pauline — not the Pauline from before, a different Pauline, this county runs out of names long before it runs out of dead — who had one small mean thing in her, a lie she told at a custody hearing thirty years gone, and I read it the way you read a price tag in a dim store, squinting, holding it to the light, and decided it was light enough to leave.

That is the new arithmetic. *Light enough to leave.* I lay a hand flat on the sternum, not the bread, just the hand, and I have learned this week that the weight comes up into the palm like heat off a stove you're checking with the back of your fingers, and I can grade it now. A little warmth, an old ache, the residue of being a person — that, I leave. It goes in the fire and it goes out of the world the honest way, the slow way, the way grief is supposed to go, carried by whoever's left and worn down by time and not by me. Pauline-the-second goes into the retort with her lie still in her, and she burns the long ordinary way, two hours and fifty minutes, the blower ticking its tick, the smell of a body that kept its own counsel, which is not the wrong smell, which is just the smell of the work, and I log her remains at five pounds one ounce and I do not feel light afterward and I am glad of it.

I am telling you I left three. I want that in the record. Before I tell you about Verlie, I want it known that I let three of them keep what they had, because the part that comes next is the part where I start to enjoy it, and I would like the jury to see that I knew the difference between enough and too much, once, at the start, before I stopped being able to tell.

—

Verlie Stiltner is the fourth, and she is kin to half of them, the way everyone here is kin to half of them — Stiltner is the name on Earl's container still waiting on his nephew, and I do not ask how they connect because the answer is always *cousins* and always twice over. She is eighty-six. She died in her own bed in Cinder Bottom with a church directory on the nightstand and a daughter who held her hand, which in this county is a death so clean and lucky they will talk about it admiringly at the IGA for a week, *Verlie went easy, Verlie went right, surrounded.* The daughter is named Sheila and she comes to make the arrangements in a sweatshirt with a wolf airbrushed on it and she cries the good clean cry of a woman who got to say goodbye, and she picks the second-cheapest urn, and she tells me her mother was a saint.

They are always saints. I write *saint* in the same hand I write *201 lb,* which is to say I write it down and I do not believe it and I do not have to, my believing is not part of the service.

But that night I lay my hand on Verlie Stiltner's sternum and the weight that comes up is not the ordinary weight. It is small — I want to be exact, it is small, smaller than Carl by a long way, Carl was a stove left on for forty years and this is a single coal somebody banked sixty years ago and never quite let die. But it is *specific.* That is the thing about a buried sin against the ordinary ache of a life: the ache is general, it has no edges, it could belong to anyone, but a kept secret has *edges,* it is shaped exactly like the act, and what comes up into my palm off this old dead saint is shaped like an envelope.

I will not pretend I read it like a story. It does not come like a story. It comes the way Carl came, colder and quieter than a voice, a knowing without a teller — but I have done this enough now, God help me, four times now and the week not over, that I can hold the knowing and turn it and see its shape, and the shape is this: there was a girl, when Verlie was twenty-some, and the girl had a young man, and the young man wrote the girl letters, and Verlie took them. Took them off the hall table at a boarding house where the three of them stayed, one and then another and then all of them, and burned some and hid some and let the girl believe the young man had gone cold, and let the young man believe the girl never answered, and stood in the gap between them she had made and waited there until the young man, lonely and answered by nobody but her, turned to her instead. And married her. And the girl — her name comes up too, a small dim name, *Orpha* — the girl left the county and married a man in Ohio she did not love and lived a whole gray life there and died, the knowing tells me, the knowing is very sure of this, *thinking she had been unlovable,* when in fact she had been loved and the letters had been stolen, and Verlie kept the secret for sixty-four years and put a church directory on her nightstand and went easy, surrounded.

So.

Here is the triage. Here is the godhood, since we are calling things by their names tonight.

I could leave it. It is small. Orpha is dead and the young man — old man — Verlie's husband, dead, and Verlie dead, and the whole triangle is three headstones now, and nobody living is standing on this secret the way Brandy was standing on her father's, nobody flinches because of Verlie Stiltner's stolen letters. I could lay her in the retort with the coal still banked in her and let her wake heavy and the heaviness would have almost nobody to seek — Sheila, maybe, a little, a vague souring of the good clean grief, a heaviness in the house in Cinder Bottom that would lift in a year. Light enough to leave. By my own new law, light enough to leave.

And I do not leave it.

I want to be honest about why, because the why is the disease, and the disease is the thing I am supposed to be watching for. I do not eat Verlie Stiltner's sin because the kin will suffer if I don't; they will barely suffer, I have just said so. I eat it because I can. Because it is small and clean and *fixable,* a single banked coal I can put my hand over and snuff, sixty-four years of one woman's quiet cruelty that I can simply — remove, like lifting a splinter, like correcting a sum, like reaching back across six decades into a boarding-house hallway and putting the letters back on the table where Verlie's hand found them. Because there is a girl named Orpha who died in Ohio thinking she was unlovable, and I cannot bring her back, the rite does not bring back, but I can reach into the only ledger that's left and change what's written, make it so the letters were never taken, so Orpha got them, so somewhere in a re-knit past a gray Ohio life becomes a different life, and a young man and a young woman who should have had each other —

I stop the thought there. I notice I have stopped baking the bread numb. I notice I am baking it with something almost like appetite.

That is the disease. I name it and I bake the bread anyway.

—

The recipe is in three hands and I do not need any of them anymore, which is its own small horror I have stopped flinching at. The Welsh hand, the rust ink, *take of flour two handfulls;* Mam Pruitt's rounder hand, *thin as you can, it must be plain;* my mother's, the last ink, *plain is the whole of it, they don't need it good, they need it true.* I work it quick and bake it quick and let it not rise, on the enameled range with the pilot I light off a kitchen match, the *whump* of it catching that I have stopped hearing as anything but the sound of starting. Tap water, not well. Nothing has to be anything.

I lay the morsel on Verlie's breastbone, in the hollow over the heart where it sits like it was cut to fit, and I let the cold of her draw the warmth up out of it, and I count — not steps, not tonight, tonight I have stopped counting steps and started counting the breaths I don't take, which I notice and do not examine — and when the bread goes heavy in the air above her the way my mother said it would, *you'll know it,* I say the words.

I will not write the words. I have promised myself that and it is the one promise to myself I have kept since I came home. They go low and they go old and they go past whatever God this county still half-believes in, straight to the dead, and they name the weight, and they take it, and they say *go on, now, go on,* and I mean it more than I used to, which is the disease, which is the thing.

And then I eat it.

—

The relief is small this time, and that is how I know the law is real, the gradient is real, weight for weight, sin for price — a small sin gives a small mercy, a single banked coal's worth of obscene lightness, a breath let out instead of the whole lungful. With Carl it was a bus ticket at twenty-two, the floor dropping away, a thing so good I was ashamed of it before I was afraid of it. With Verlie it is — less. It is the small clean click of a thing set right, a sum that finally adds, a splinter out. It is the size of the sin and not one gram larger, and I feel it go, the snuff of the coal, the letters back on the table, sixty-four years quietly un-happening in some hallway that no longer exists, and for one second I am not twenty-two on a bus, I am — somewhere smaller, somewhere I cannot quite —

And here is the price. I almost miss it. It is that small.

I am standing at the threshold of the retort with my mother's recipe still on my hands and Verlie cooling on the tray behind me, and I reach for the somewhere I almost went, the small good place the lightness took me for half a second, and it is a — there was a candy. There was a candy I had as a child, I can see the wrapper, I can see the precise red of the wrapper, twisted at both ends, and I had it from somebody's hand, and it was — 

I go to taste it. The way you do. You think of a thing you ate and your mouth remembers, that is how a mouth works, that is the oldest honest record there is, older than ink. I think of the red wrapper twisted at both ends and I go to taste the candy and there is nothing there. Not a wrong taste. Not a faded taste. *Nothing.* My mouth has the wrapper and not the candy. I have the red and not the sweet. I stand there working my jaw like a fool, like an old dog at an empty bowl, summoning a flavor up out of forty years and getting back a clean smooth blank where it lived, and the worst of it, the exact worst of it, is that I cannot even be sure it was ever there to lose. Maybe I never had a candy. Maybe I am making a hole and putting a wrapper in front of it. A deletion does not leave a gap you can read. It leaves a wrapper and no candy and a woman who cannot prove to herself she was ever a child who was handed something sweet.

*Huh,* I say, out loud, to the cold room, to the retort, to nobody.

That is all. *Huh.* The whole price, paid and noticed and dismissed in a single dumb syllable, because it is small, because it is a candy, because who grieves a candy, and I turn back to the work and I bring the temperature up and I lay Verlie Stiltner in the fire and she goes up light and odorless, like paper, like the others I have eaten, and the wrong smell that was never strong on her is gone clean as though it was never there, and I log her remains at four pounds fourteen ounces, and I do not write *huh* in any book.

I should have written *huh* in a book. I know that now. I am the keeper of records and I let a price walk out of the building unrecorded because it was the size of a candy, and the not-writing is the disease too, the disease wears a hundred faces and one of them is *it's too small to bother.* That is exactly how a person disappears. Not all at once. A candy at a time.

—

Wren finds me at three in the morning eating gas-station taquitos off a paper towel on the loading dock, which is where we live now, the two of us, in the small warm hours between bodies, and she has brought the taquitos because it was her turn, we have a system, and she sits down next to me with her boots hanging off the dock over the gravel and she says, "You look like somebody took your candy."

I do not laugh. I want it noted that I came within a hair of telling her she has no idea, and that I did not, because how do you say that, *somebody did, I think, I traded it for an old woman's mercy and I can't get it back and I can't even prove I had it.* You don't. You eat a taquito.

"Long week," I say.

"Four in the box," she says, around her own. "Five if Earl's nephew don't come get him, which he ain't, I called the number, it's a vape shop now." She says this the way she says everything, fast and flat and a little feral, a girl who learned a long time ago that the only thing worse than the joke is the silence the joke is covering, and I recognize the move because it is my move, and I have started to be afraid of how much I like sitting next to somebody who makes it. "I logged Verlie before you did her. Wrote her in real neat. You want to check it I won't be offended."

"I'll check it."

"You always check it." She is not offended. She is the opposite of offended; she has figured out that *I'll check it* is the only way I know to say *I trust you with this,* the way Ray's folder is the only dish he carries, and she takes it the way you take a thing you've decoded. "Verlie's the one stole that girl's fella, back when. The letters." She says it idly, licking grease off her thumb. "My granny told it. Everybody's granny told it. Verlie Stiltner stole Orpha Coleman's letters and married the boy and Orpha went off to Ohio and that was that. Granny said you could set your clock by Verlie at church, front pew, sixty years, like she was paying something off a little every Sunday." She shrugs. "Reckon she's square now."

And I sit very still on the dock with a taquito going cold in my hand, because Wren has just told me, idly, eating, that the secret I ate tonight was not a secret. That the whole county knew. That Verlie Stiltner spent sixty years in the front pew paying it off Sunday by Sunday in the only ledger she had, and that *everybody's granny told it,* and that I have just reached into a sixty-four-year-old hallway and put the letters back on the table and un-happened it — which means that, somewhere, by tomorrow, Wren's granny never told it, and the clock you could set by Verlie at church was set by something else, and Orpha Coleman, whoever she was, did not go off to Ohio thinking she was unlovable, she went off to Ohio for some other reason or did not go at all, and the whole town's small shared knowing of a small old cruelty is, as of tonight, a thing that was never known because it was never done.

I have not made Verlie square. I have made Verlie *innocent.* There is a difference and I am the only one left who will ever know there was.

"You all right?" Wren says. "You went somewhere."

"I'm here," I say.

"You do that. Go somewhere. So'd she." She says it without cruelty, the way she says all the unbearable things, and I do not have to ask who *she* is. "Your mama. She'd be right in front of you and then she'd be — gone off. Reading them books at the desk all hours. I asked her once what was in 'em and she looked at me like I'd asked her something dirty and said *accounts.*" Wren picks at the airbrushed wolf that is not on her own sweatshirt, that is on Sheila's, I am confusing them, I am tired, the dead and the living wearing each other's clothes. "What do you do," she says, and it is not idle now, she has gone careful, she has put the taquito down, "with the bread. And the — " she waves a hand, the universal sign for a thing you don't have a word for. "The mumbling. Over 'em. I seen you do it. I don't say nothing. But I seen you."

There it is. The door. I have known the door was there since the second week — the ledger is plain about the door, the line takes by the eating and not strictly by the blood, a non-Pruitt girl could be made the keeper, could be handed the bread and walked through her first swallow under my hand — and I have been holding the door shut with my whole body without ever once admitting there was a door, and here is Wren Tice, nineteen, half-feral, the only warm thing in this whole cold building, asking me to open it, and not even knowing that's what she's doing. She thinks she's asking about a superstition. She thinks the worst thing she could learn is that I'm a little crazy, that the Pruitt women had a weird private prayer, that grief made strange.

She does not know I could ruin her with an answer. She does not know the kindest thing I will ever do for her is the thing I do now, which is lie.

"It's an old family thing," I say. "Welsh. My grandmother's grandmother. You say words over them so they go easy. It doesn't do anything." Three sentences. Two of them true. The third the truest lie I have ever told. "It's for the saying, not the doing. Like a prayer for people who don't believe in one."

"Huh," Wren says — and I flinch, because it is my word, the word I said over Verlie's lost candy, and she does not know that either. "That's kind of nice, actually." She picks her taquito back up. "Dakota'd say it was witch shit."

"How is Dakota."

"In jail," she says brightly, "thank God, finally, it's so peaceful," and then she is off, the Cavalier and the mailbox and the deer that was a mailbox that was Tammy Sizemore's, and the aunt who won't speak to her over a Crock-Pot, and the cosmetology school in Beckley that takes payment plans, the whole bright catastrophe of being nineteen and alive in a dying town, and I let her talk, I let her fill the cold dock with the noise of a life that has nothing in it heavy enough for my fire, and I eat the cold taquito, and I think, the way you think a thought you immediately wall off, *she carries nothing yet. She is light as a girl. She would burn easy and need no bread.*

I think it the way I thought it about a corpse.

I slam the door so hard I hear it.

—

Ray comes Thursday, dry and circling, and he does not bring a casserole because he is not kin and he does not bring his folder *under his arm* this time, he brings it *out,* open on my kitchen table before the kettle's even on, which is a thing, which is him deciding to show me his cards, and a man like Ray does not show you his cards to play a hand, he shows them because he is lonely and you are the only one at the table who keeps her own cards the same way.

"You'll know everybody's people," he says. "That's the thing about your line of work. You're the one place in the county that's got 'em all, sooner or later. Living and dead." He turns a photograph around on the table with two fingers and slides it to me. "You know this girl?"

It is a girl. Seventeen, maybe, school-portrait blue behind her, the bad feathered hair of a long time ago, a chipped front tooth she's smiling around like she doesn't care, like she's got time. *Dana,* he doesn't say yet. He lets the photograph say it.

"Should I?"

"Don't know. Hoping." He does not take the picture back. He lets it sit between us, this dead girl, on my mother's table where Esther sat reading the books that everybody's granny knew the contents of. "Went missing in '94. I was new. Made her mother a promise I been keeping ever since, which is that I wouldn't close it, wouldn't let it go cold the way they let things go cold, file in a drawer. Her mama's still living. Out the Bottom. Waits on me to come by and tell her I'm still looking." He turns his cup a quarter turn. "Thirty years I been telling her I'm still looking. And the funny thing — " and there it is, the *funny thing,* the same two words he used the first time, and I have learned that *funny* is the word Ray uses for the thing that is the opposite of funny, the way *check it* is mine — "the funny thing is, the file gets thinner. I told you that. You thought I was getting old. I keep thinking I'll come in some morning and there'll be one sheet of paper in it says *girl, gone,* and that'll be the whole of it, and I'll have promised her mother thirty years over a folder that empties itself."

*That'll be the whole of it.* He does not know he is quoting my mother's note. He cannot know. But the phrase lands in me like a stone down a well, *that is the whole of it,* the thing the daughter does to the mother, the thing Verlie's lost letters are now, the whole of it, the smooth full wrong record left when a truth has been lifted clean.

"Maybe somebody got into it," I say. "Mislaid the pages."

"You said that about the county." He almost smiles. He remembers I said it. He remembers everything, that is the unbearable thing about Ray, that is what makes him my mirror and my noose in one body: he is a man who does not let go, sitting in a house where I make things let go for a living, and his file is getting thinner because I have been eating the men who put the girl in it, *I think,* I cannot prove it, the only proof is a memory and we have both stopped trusting those — but I look at the photograph of Dana with her chipped tooth and her stolen time and I think about the bodies I have eaten this month and the ones still coming, the dying man Ray hasn't named yet that I can feel coming the way you feel the front, and I understand, cold, that the day a certain heavy old man with black lung comes through my side door, this girl's whole truth is in my hands, and I will have a choice, and there is no version of the choice where Ray gets to keep his promise.

"You'd tell me," he says. Not a question. "If she turned up in those books of yours. The name. Dana Coyle. You'd tell me."

"What if there's nothing to tell."

He looks at me a long moment, and the not-quite-smile goes out of him, and what's left is just a tired man who has decided to trust the wrong person because she's the only one shaped right.

"Then tell me that," he says. "Tell me there's nothing. I'd rather a person told me there's nothing than have the folder do it." He puts the photograph back in the file, careful, face up, the way you'd lay a child down. "I don't trust paper to tell me anymore. Paper's started lying. But a person — " He stands, gathers the folder, taps it square against the table, an old reflex. "A person can keep faith. That's the one thing left I'd bet on."

—

He leaves at four. I do the cool-down on Verlie and the processing and the labeling, *STILTNER, VERLIE,* spelled right, the floor below which I will not go, and I take down the green ledger from the box under the sink to enter the account, because that is the one inheritance that took, the entering, the keeping.

LEFT column: *Name & what was carried.* RIGHT column: *Paid.*

I write, in the left, in my own steady hand, the hand that is hers: *Verlie Stiltner — the letters; Orpha; sixty-four years.*

And then I sit with the pen over the right column, the way I sat over Carl's right column, except that with Carl I left it blank because I could not name what I'd lost, and tonight I can almost name it, tonight I have a wrapper if not a candy, and I think about writing *a sweetness, a child's, no longer locatable,* and I think about how small it is, and how nobody will ever read this book but the next keeper, who I have just sworn over a paper towel will not be Wren, which means nobody will ever read this book at all, which means the books I am keeping so carefully are a record kept for no one, a wall built against an erasure that is erasing the wall —

and I notice that I want to leave the right column blank again.

Not because I can't name it. Because it's a candy. Because who writes a candy in a hundred-year-old book of the town's worst secrets. Because some part of me has decided, in the four short weeks I have been doing this, that the prices are too small to count, that a sweetness here and a kindness there are the reasonable overhead of a useful trade, that I am getting more than I'm giving — un-happening cruelties, snuffing coals, making Orpha Coleman never unloved — and the candy is just what it costs, and you do not itemize what it costs, you round it off, you absorb it, you tell the county a true-enough thing about a backlog and you keep the home lit and you do the work because the work *feels* —

I make myself write it. *A sweetness, a child's, gone.* Five words in the right column, weight for weight, because the one thing I will not let this trade take while I can still stop it is my willingness to write down what it takes. The books must balance. My mother's whole law, the law that has run the wrong smell into these walls for forty years, and I have hated it all week, and tonight I obey it for the first time on purpose, because I have understood, sitting here with the pen, that an honest ledger is the last thing standing between me and the smooth full wrong record Ray's folder is becoming, Verlie's secret is becoming, my own self is quietly becoming, a candy at a time.

I balance the line. I close the book.

And then I sit there in the one good light and I do the thing I have been not-doing all night, all week, since the first easy one I left and the first small one I took, which is to ask myself the question under the question, the one I will not write even in the book kept for no one.

The question is not *am I doing harm.* I know I am doing harm; I have known since Della came in her yellow dress. The question is worse than that. The question is why, when I lay my hand on a sternum and feel the weight come up and grade it and decide, when I bake the plain bread and say the old words and feel the small clean click of a thing set right — why it feels, more and more, every body, every week, like the only time all day I am not lying.

Out back the retort ticks as it cools, fingernail on a screen door, and Verlie Stiltner is four pounds fourteen ounces in a container with her name spelled right, innocent now, never a thief, and somewhere in Ohio a gray dead woman was never unloved, and somewhere on a loading dock a girl who carries nothing is sleeping in her car because Dakota's in jail and the aunt won't speak to her, and somewhere in a cooler my mother is the heaviest thing in the house and getting heavier, and somewhere across town a tired man is laying a folder down like a child and betting his last faith on a person to keep what the paper won't.

I count the holes in the ceiling tile over the desk. Forty to a tile, give or take. I get to two hundred and lose the count, which I have never once done in my life, and I do not start over.

I think: *I am good at this.*

I think it the way you'd notice a thing on your own body in a mirror, a mark you didn't put there, in a place you can't reach.

# Chapter Eight

## A Clean Thing

The trade has a thing it does to you that nobody warns you about, which is that it makes you good company for yourself. I have spent three weeks now learning the size of the dead by standing near them, and I have gotten good at it, and being good at a thing is the loneliest pleasure there is, because there is no one you can tell.

Dwight Probst comes in on a Tuesday, and I know what he is before the county boy has the cot all the way down the ramp.

I want to be precise about how I know, because precision is the only honest part of any of this. It is not a voice. It is not a chill. I have read the literature on what people claim to feel around the bad ones, and it is all weather and goosebumps and the hair on the neck, and it is all a lie people tell to make a wrongness sound like it came from outside them. What I get is quieter and worse. It is a pressure, low, behind my own breastbone, where the bread goes — a heaviness in the air over a body the way Loy said his aunt said, *you'll know it when the bread goes heavy in the air over him,* except I do not need the bread anymore to feel the air change. I have done this enough now that the air just tells me. And the air over Dwight Probst is the heaviest I have stood in since my mother, and my mother has been forty years filling a house with hers.

"He's a Probst," the county boy says, like that settles something, and reads me the transfer. Fifty years old. Found in the trailer off Cinder Branch by a meter reader, three days gone with the heat left on, which is its own kind of sentence and I do not say so. Next of kin: a sister, Reva, and a nephew nobody can reach.

"I got him," I say, and sign the line, 2:14, and I do not say *sorry for the wait* or *appreciate you running him up,* I just sign, because the boy is in a hurry to be out of the air too. He felt it. He couldn't name it. People feel it all the time in this house and blame the drains and the dead and the old wood. I am the only one in the county who knows the smell is a thing with a cause, and the cause is on my cot now, three days into October, and even the rot can't cover what's under it.

—

The families come the next day, and that is when I learn what the air already told me.

You learn a man's life at the arrangement table. People think the funeral is for the dead and it is not, it is the last interview the living get, and they say things to the funeral director they would not say to God because the funeral director is the one person who has to write it down without flinching. I have heard everything at this table. I have heard a woman price out a casket for a husband she is glad is dead and I have written the check amount in my steady hand and said *that's a fine choice* and meant nothing by it.

Reva Probst sits where Della Mott sat three weeks ago. She is a hard small woman, fifties, with her brother's same close-set eyes, and she does not cry, and she does not pretend to, and the first thing she says when she sits down is, "Cheapest you got. He don't need a box. Burn him and give me whatever's left and I'll figure out the rest."

"We have a basic—"

"The cheapest." She looks at the corkboard with the GoFundMe faces on it, the help-us-lay-Bobby-to-rest ones, and her mouth does something. "Ain't gonna be one of them for Dwight. I'll tell you that. Nobody's passing a hat."

And then she tells me, not because I asked, but because the table is the table and three days of October have softened something in her that wanted out. She tells me sideways, the way people here tell you the worst things — through what the dead is *not* getting, through who is *not* coming. She tells me which cousins won't be at the service and why. She tells me her own girl is grown and gone to Ohio and won't set foot back in this county and hasn't in eleven years and Reva doesn't blame her, *Lord, I don't blame her, I'd have took her myself if I'd have known sooner, if I'd have let myself know,* and her voice goes flat on that the way mine goes flat, the way you sand a board down so it won't give you a splinter when you grip it.

I do not write what she is telling me on the intake. There is no line for it. There is a line for marital status and a line for cause of death and there has never been a line on any form in this trade for *what he did to the children in his family for thirty years that everybody half-knew and nobody could prove, that everybody smelled and called the drains.* That line does not exist. The county does not keep that record. Nobody keeps it.

Except the air over him keeps it. And me.

I get one detail and I am going to give you exactly one, because the rule I have set myself, the floor below which I will not go, is that I will not make a meal of this man's victims for the sake of a paragraph. The detail is a photograph Reva slides across the table, not of Dwight — she has none of Dwight she'll touch — but of her own girl at maybe seven, a school picture, the gap-toothed kind, on a marbled blue background, and Reva looks at it and not at me and says, "She used to be sunshine. You understand me. Before." And puts it back in her purse like she's putting a hand over a wound.

*Before.* The whole indictment is in one word and the word is *before*, and there is an *after*, and the after is a grown woman in Ohio who won't come home, and the air over Dwight Probst is heavy with every year between.

"He'll go quiet," I tell Reva, which is the kind of true I deal in, and she nods like that's all she came for, and maybe it is.

—

Here is the part I am not proud of, so I am going to set it down plain, in the order it happened, because the ledger only works if you don't lie in it.

That night I stand over Dwight Probst in the prep room with the bread already baked and cooling on the steel beside him, and I do the math I have learned to do, the triage, and the triage says: I have a choice.

I do not have to eat him.

I could let this one burn heavy. I have read the ledger now; I know what *waking heavy* means; I have seen the columns and I am beginning to understand the price, even if I can't yet say it whole. And the math of refusal, the cold math, is almost clean here in a way it never is. His kin are few. The nephew nobody can reach won't be reached. And Reva — Reva is not innocent of him, Reva said it herself, *if I'd have let myself know,* Reva spent thirty years deciding not to know, and the part of me that keeps the books wants to make her hold the weight. Wants the heaviness to come find her, room to room, the bad dreams, the sourness, the thing the ledger warns of, and wants it to be *fair,* because she let her own girl be the after of a man she protected with her not-knowing.

I want him to suffer. That's the truth. I want his weight to stay in the world un-housed and find the people who let him, and I tell myself that is justice, and for about a minute I let myself stand there and want it.

And then I look at the school photo I am not holding, the gap-toothed girl, the marbled blue, sunshine *before,* and I think about who else lives downstream of a weight you leave un-housed. It does not seek only the guilty. It seeks the kin. It seeks whoever is closest. And there is a great-niece somewhere I have never met, and there are people in this town carrying enough already, and I am not God, I am a woman with an apron and a furnace, and if I let this one go heavy out of *spite,* the spite will not land where I aim it. Weight never does. It is a flood and it goes downhill into whatever room is lowest, and the lowest rooms in this county are always full of children.

So I will eat him.

But I will not pretend I'm doing it gently. I lay the bread on his sternum, over the breastbone where the weight sits, on a man who deserves no soft hand, and I do not feel the nausea I felt over Carl. I feel the other thing. I feel my jaw set. I feel something in me that has been cold and patient my whole life come forward and put its hands flat on the table.

The bread draws. On a cold body it takes a count I know by feel now, and I count it, and as it draws I read him — not a voice, not a vision, colder than that, a *knowing* coming up through the crumb the way damp comes up through a cellar wall — and I am not going to write what comes up. I am going to tell you only that it is exactly what the air promised, and that it is years of it, and that it has the particular texture of a thing done patiently, by a man who had time, who was never once in a hurry, who counted on the silence the way I count pipe joints. There are eleven pipe joints over the carriage-house door. I count them in my head while his life draws up into the bread, all eleven, and it does not help, which is the first time counting has not helped, and I file that, and I keep counting anyway because the alternative is to feel the whole shape of what is in the bread now.

I say the words. Low. The way Loy showed me, the way it goes back and back to a Pruitt woman in Wales who is a column of ink now. A naming of the burden.
