A taking of it. *What lies heavy on thee, I take to me.* And here, over this man, the old worn line that is supposed to be mercy comes out of my mouth as something else, comes out hard, comes out like a verdict — *go thou light,* I say, except I do not wish him light, I am sending him light, there's a difference, the way there's a difference between forgiving a debt and foreclosing on it — *the books are balanced. Go on, now. Go on.*

And then I eat it.

—

I told you about the first time, with Carl. The obscene lightness. The clean relief I was ashamed of before I was afraid of it.

This is worse, and the worse part is that this time there is no shame in front of it at all. This time the relief comes and I let it come and it feels *correct.* It feels like the click of a thing seated true. It feels — I have to say the real word, because the ledger doesn't work if I lie — it feels like *justice.* Like I have reached into the world and lifted out a tumor and the body of the town will be lighter for it and never know why, and I did that, with my two hands and a flat scone and a recipe a dead woman copied in three failing hands, and for one long second standing at my own steel table I am not a tired woman in an apron at all.

For one second I am something with a verdict in its mouth and the power to carry it out. For one second I understand exactly why my mother kept doing this for forty years. For one second the word that should be *sorry* — the word I haven't said since I was twenty-two, the word worn smooth in me — that empty place where the word goes fills up instead with this, with the clean iron taste of having *settled an account,* and I think, almost out loud, almost fond:

*Instead of sorry.*

That's what Loy calls it. The warm old comfort. *You don't say sorry over them, hon. You eat it and you say the words. That's the mercy. That's instead of sorry.* He meant it kind. He meant it as the gentlest thing a person could do for the dead.

It is not gentle in my mouth tonight. Tonight it is a fist. Tonight *instead of sorry* means *instead of letting you live in the world a second longer than you have to, instead of mercy, instead of forgiveness, here is what you get from me, here is the only absolution in my house and it is a deletion and I am glad to give it.* I swallow Dwight Probst's whole patient evil in three bites the way you take a pill, and the relief is so total and so righteous that I stand there in it like a hot shower, and I am not ashamed, and that — God help me, that lack of shame — is the most frightened I have been since I came home, and I won't know it for hours.

—

He burns like paper.

I take him out to the carriage house and charge him and the Power-Pak that has fought me for three weeks, that sulks below sixteen hundred and ticks in the blower like a fingernail on a screen door, lights him and *catches,* eager, the temperature barely dipping before it climbs. There is no smell. There is no fight. A heavy man, a hundred-and-some pounds of patient cruelty, and he goes up odorless and quick the way the truly empty go, and the house that has reeked for three days — the struck match held too long, the wet stove, the third thing I never found a shelf for — the house goes *clean.*

I notice it the way you notice a fan you didn't know was running, when it stops. The wrongness lifts out of the back hall and the prep room and the stairwell all at once, like a held breath let go, and I stand in the carriage house doorway and breathe the ordinary October in — coal-dust and creek-rot and cold — and it smells like *nothing,* like a normal building, and I should be glad.

I am glad. That is the trouble. I am standing in a house I have made lighter by burning a monster odorless, and I am glad, and somewhere in the burning I have started to like the gladness more than I should, and I write the time down because writing it down is what I do.

Burn started 11:40. I write it in the cooler notebook. *Burn anomalous — low resistance — temp held — no smell.* The same words I wrote over Carl. I am building a record, entry by entry, of a thing that is not supposed to leave a record, and I do not yet see that this is the only thing I am doing right.

—

The cost comes for me that night, the way I am told now they always do, at the swallow, but I do not feel it land. That is the design of it. You cannot feel a thing being taken if the thing it takes is the very part of you that would have noticed.

I feel it later. I feel it the way you feel a tooth gone — not at the pulling, you're numb at the pulling, but after, when your tongue goes back out of old habit to a place that is just smooth now.

It is near three in the morning and the building is clean and quiet and I am tired the particular bottomless way the trade makes you tired, and I want a thing I have wanted on bad nights my whole life, which is to be small again in a warm room while a woman who does not say soft things moves around a kitchen being unsoftly kind. That's the whole of the want. It is not complicated. I have carried it like a coal in a closed hand for forty years, even after I left, even after nineteen years of not speaking, even now with her on a steel tray forty feet from where I stand — the one warm thing I let myself keep of her, kept because it asked nothing of me and cost nothing and could not be taken back.

It was the kitchen. The smell of it. I would close my eyes on the worst nights of my life — the bus, twenty-two, the cold table in the lab, the years — and I would put myself back in my mother's kitchen and I would —

I go to think of it.

I go to put myself back in the smell of my mother's kitchen, the way I have a thousand times, the exact warm particular smell of it, bread and the gas pilot and the —

The —

I go to think of —

There is nothing there.

I do not mean I cannot recall it clearly. I mean I reach, the way you reach in the dark for a glass you set on the nightstand, sure of the place, and my hand closes on air, and there is no glass, and worse, there is no nightstand, there is no surface where the thing was, there is only the smooth dark and my own hand coming back empty and me not even certain, now, what shape I was reaching for. The smell of my mother's kitchen. I know the *words.* I can say them. *The smell of my mother's kitchen.* They are a label on an empty jar. I turn the jar over and nothing comes out and I cannot even remember what color the thing inside used to be.

I stand in the prep room at three in the morning with my hand still half-lifted toward a warm room that I no longer have, and I do not know that I have lost it. That is the cruelty I could not have understood until right now. A thing you misremember, you can feel the wrongness of, the edges that don't meet. A thing that is *deleted* leaves no edges. It is not a fog. It is a clean nothing, water closed over a stone, and the records-keeper, the one whose whole religion is that what was true stays true, stands there feeling only a faint, sourceless, unbearable *hunger* — a homesickness for a house I can no longer find the smell of — and tells herself it is grief. Tells herself it's only that her mother is dead and she's tired and the night has been long, and a person gets melancholy near the cooler at three in the morning, and there is nothing wrong, nothing has been taken, who would take it.

I do not write this one down. That is the worst part. There is a notebook that is just mine, for the things the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about, and the whole reason it exists is for exactly this, and I stand there with the pen ten inches from my own hand and I do not write it, because I do not know there is anything to write. The record fails at the one moment it was built for, because the rite did not just take the kitchen. It took the part of me that would have known to log its absence. It balanced the books. The sin on the left; the price on the right; and the price was a warm room, traded clean for a monster's evil, so that the world tonight is lighter by one cruelty and I am lighter by one mercy and the ledger calls that even.

It is not even. I can feel that it is not even, the way you can feel a scale is wrong with your eyes shut, and I cannot prove it, and I am so tired, and the house smells like nothing at all.

—

I go to bed at last in the narrow upstairs room that is exactly as it was, my room, with her door shut down the hall, and I lie in the dark and I do the thing I have always done to get to sleep, the oldest one, older than counting pipe joints, and I —

I go back to the kitchen.

And there is no kitchen to go back to.

There is a *fact* of a kitchen. I know there was one. I know there was an oven with a burn-ringed counter and a pilot you held with a match and a woman moving around it. I have the inventory. I have the deed and the dimensions. What I do not have, what I went looking for and could not find, is the *warmth* of it — the smell that meant safe, the thing under the words, the one good coal I carried in my closed hand out of that house and across nineteen years and back — and I lie there reaching and reaching into a smooth dark place where a warm thing used to be, and I think it is just grief, and it is not grief, grief has a shape and this has none, this is a hunger with the food deleted out from under it, and I am the only one who paid for tonight's clean house and I do not even know what I paid, only that I am hungry in a way that has no bottom, and that somewhere across town a grown woman who won't come home from Ohio is, tonight, for no reason she could ever name, a little lighter, a little less herself, missing something she'll never know she lost.

We match, that woman and I. We were both emptied out tonight to make the world kinder. Neither of us will ever know it. The books are balanced.

I lie in the dark and reach for my mother's kitchen, and my hand comes back empty, and comes back empty, and comes back empty, and I tell myself it is only that she is dead.

# Chapter Nine

## The One She Won't Eat

Tug Ammons comes in on a Tuesday with the kind of paperwork that tells you the whole story before you lift the sheet. Found by a wellness check. Found is the word the county uses when nobody was looking until the smell got into the hall and a neighbor called the landlord and the landlord called somebody with a badge, and *wellness* is the word they use for the visit that confirms there is none. He had been gone two days in a closed apartment with the heat on, which is its own arithmetic, and I do not write that part down, because there is no line for it on any form and because I am, despite everything, still trying to be gentle with the dead in the only way I have left, which is to not say out loud the things their bodies say to me.

Forty-five. The transport sheet says forty-five and the body says fifty-five and the truth is the difference, which is what the difference always is. I log him in the cooler notebook. *AMMONS, TUGWELL R.* I spell it right. There is a Tugwell two counties back, a family that gave the boys the grandmother's maiden name like a coat that didn't fit any of them, and I knew one once and he was kind, and this is not him, and I write the name the way his grandmother would have wanted it written, which is the floor below which I do not go.

He weighs a hundred and thirty-eight. He should weigh more than that. A man does not get to forty-five years on this earth weighing a hundred and thirty-eight unless the last few years were spent feeding something other than himself, and I know what he was feeding, the whole county knows, it is on the corkboard by the showroom in a printed face that says *help us bring Tug home* with a thermometer drawn beside it colored in to about a third, and I do not look at the face, because the face is already on my table and the GoFundMe will close the way they all close, at a third, with a thank-you and the rest made up by whoever is left holding the difference. Which, in Tug's case, is a sister.

—

Her name is Carla and she comes in at four with a folder.

That is the first thing about her. She comes in with a folder, a real one, a green accordion thing with the years written on the tabs in a careful hand, and she sets it on the arrangement desk and takes out the death certificate and the Social Security card and a printout of the policy that will not pay because it is two months lapsed and a sheet of paper with her own handwriting on it that turns out to be a list of what she can spend, which is four hundred dollars, which is the *Slate* urn and nothing else, and she has done the math before she got in the car, you can see she has, because she does not flinch at the number when I say it. She just nods, the way you nod at a thing you've already grieved.

I have met a thousand of her. I have not met one in a while that kept a folder.

"You'll want the basic cremation," she says, before I can walk her through anything. "No viewing. He wouldn't of wanted people looking at him." She squares the certificate against the edge of the desk. "Two years sober," she says, to the certificate, not to me. "I was. Am. Two years and four months." A small breath. "I keep it in the folder. The chip. So I know where it is."

"Congratulations," I say, which is the wrong word, and we both let it be wrong, because there isn't a right one.

"He's the reason," she says. And then, because that landed strange in the air between us, she fixes it, makes it exact, the way I would fix it: "Not — he didn't get me clean. Don't get me wrong. He was using the whole time I was. We used together. That's how it — " She stops. Squares the certificate again though it is already square. "When he stopped answering, the first week, I thought he was just gone on a run. And I got so scared. Not for me. *For him.* And being that scared for somebody else, every hour, for a week — " She looks up at me for the first time, and her eyes are dry and very steady and there is a folder between us and I understand her completely. "That's what did it. That's the thing that finally — I couldn't be high and that scared at the same time. There wasn't room. So I picked." She nods at the certificate. "I picked being scared for him. And then they found him, and now I get to keep the being-scared, except there's nobody on the other end of it. And that's the only thing holding me up. Does that make any kind of sense."

"Yes," I say.

"I figured it might," she says, "to you," and she does not explain what she means by that, and I do not ask.

I take her four hundred dollars. I give her a receipt and a little plastic chip of my own — a card with a date on it, the date of the burn — and she puts it in the folder, in a tab, and she goes, and the building is quiet again, and the smell that came in with Tug is still here, and now I know what I am going to have to decide.

—

Because Tug is heavy.

He was heavy the second they rolled him off the cot. Not the hundred and thirty-eight pounds — that is the wrong heavy, that is just a body, light as a kite, the dead always are. The other heavy. The pressure I have learned to read, the thumb laid flat against the breastbone from the inside, *here, this is where it sits,* and the smell that comes off a body like that, the struck-match-held-too-long, the wet-stove, the third thing I still have no shelf for after all these weeks of shelving it nightly. He is one of the heavy ones. Whatever he buried, he buried deep, and he carried it all the way down, and it is in him now like water in a drowned man, and the ledger is clear about what happens if I put a body like this in the fire un-eaten.

*Wake heavy.* The weight does not move on. It stays. It goes looking for the living kin.

The living kin is a woman with a folder and a chip and two years and four months and exactly one thing holding her up.

So I do what I have learned to do, which is the rite-without-the-rite, the reading. I do not bake the bread. I have not decided to do the bread. I go out to the cooler at two in the morning when the building has gone quiet enough to hear itself think, and I open his drawer, and I lay my two hands flat on his sternum the way you do, not the bread, just the hands, and I let it come up into me a little, the way you put your palm to a door to feel if there's fire on the other side.

And I read him.

I will not write down what Tug Ammons did. Not because the page can't hold it — the page has held worse and will hold worse — but because there is a thing I have started doing lately, a discipline, that I did not have when I started: I have started to ask, before I read a man all the way down, *who does the knowing serve.* And there is no one this serves. There is a child involved. The thing he buried has a child in it, and the child is alive, and the child is in this county, and that is all I am going to say about the shape of it, except that it is the genuine article, the black thing, the kind of sin the rite was built for, and the easiest swallow I will ever be offered, because no one alive would mourn its deleting and a part of me — the part I have learned to watch, the part Dwight Probst woke up in me and I have not gotten back to sleep — that part is already at the cooler before I am, already saying *take it, take it, this is a clean one, this is a righteous one, you can give Carla her brother back the way he should have been and the world will be one black thing lighter and who will it cost.*

And here is the thing I have learned, the thing that cost me a piece of my own warmth to learn, that I reached for in the dark two nights ago after Dwight and found a blank where it used to live, a kitchen with no smell in it, the door of some room in me standing open onto a clean swept floor where a thing of mine used to be:

It will cost Carla.

Not the way the part of me at the cooler means. Not a memory. Carla won't lose a memory; I'm the only one who pays in those. Carla will lose the thing the sin is holding up.

Because if I eat what Tug buried, the thing un-happens. And the thing un-happening means there was never a man worth being that scared for. There was never a week of not answering that finally, at the bottom of it, made a woman pick being terrified for somebody else over being high. The fear that is holding Carla up is built on the floor of who her brother actually was — the whole black weight of him, the using and the loving and the thing with the child and the not-answering and the dying — and you cannot pull one board out of that floor and leave the rest standing. Eat the sin and you eat the man, and eat the man and the fear has nothing under it, and the fear is the only thing under *her.*

I have done this enough times now to see the shape of it before I do it. That is the new and terrible competence. I can stand at the cooler with my hands on a man's chest and run the whole erasure forward in my head like a burn I've timed a thousand times, watch it cool and process and come out the far end as a clean light handful of nothing, and see exactly who is standing in the room when the smoke clears, and who isn't.

The room, after, has Carla in it. But she's lighter. She's lighter the way Della Mott is lighter, the way the absolved are always lighter, and she's got a different folder in her lap, one with no chip in it, because there was never anything to get clean from, because there was never a week scared enough to pick against. Two years and four months — gone, un-needed, un-earned, because nothing ever happened that needed surviving. She's at peace. She's emptied out. She's exactly the kind of happy that means a grave, and somewhere in the new past a woman who used to keep a folder doesn't anymore, and uses again, easy, light, with nothing holding her up, because I took the floor.

I take my hands off his chest.

"No," I say, out loud, to a dead man in a cold room. "No. Not you."

—

Loy is horrified, in his soft way, which is the only way Loy does anything.

I tell him at the kitchen table the next morning, because I have learned that Loy is owed the telling even when he's wrong, that being kin to this means he gets to be afraid with me, and he sits with his coffee going cold in his big hands and he gets a look I have not seen on him before. Loy at a funeral is a comfortable man; grief is his weather and he wears it well. Loy across a table from a thing he doesn't have a comfort for is something else. He looks, for a second, his actual age.

"You can't send one on heavy, hon."

"He's not going on light, Loy. There's a child in what he did. I'm not — "

"That ain't — " He sets the coffee down. "You're thinking it's about *him.* Whether he deserves it. It was never about whether they deserve it. None of 'em deserve it, that's the whole — that's why it's a *mercy,* Nona, you don't earn a mercy. You're thinking like the law thinks. The bread ain't the law." He works his thumb across the back of his hand, the old habit, faster now. "You send him heavy and it don't go *to* him. He's past it. It comes here, and it sits, and then it goes looking, and what it finds — " He stops. He has scared himself. "I seen it once. When I was a boy. A man my mama wouldn't eat for — she had her reasons, I never knew 'em, she didn't tell a child — and his widow." He shakes his head. "Inside the year. Hon. Inside the year she was confessing to things in the street that wasn't hers to confess. Things he done, coming out of her mouth like they was her own. She followed him down by Easter. That's what heavy *is.* It don't stay in the box."

"I know what the ledger says it is."

"It ain't the ledger says it. *I* say it. I was there." And then, gentler, because he is always gentler: "I know you've got a reason. You always had a reason; you was a child that always had a reason. But the reason's about the living and you're forgetting the weight don't know who's living. It just goes to whoever loved him most." He looks at me. "Who loved him most, Nona?"

A woman with a folder. A chip in a tab. Two years and four months.

"I'll handle it," I say, which is what I say instead of an answer, which Loy knows, and which he lets me have, because he is kin, and because he has run out of the words a comfortable man keeps on hand.

—

I burn him un-eaten on Thursday night.

I want to be honest about why, because I have built a whole self out of being honest about why, and the why is not as clean as the no I said in the cooler. The no in the cooler was clean. *Not you. Not the floor under that woman.* That was the records-keeper's no, the no of a woman who has decided that a true thing standing is worth more than a false thing at peace, that you do not get to delete a man's daughter's whole terror to spare his sister the truth of him, that the wronged child in Tug Ammons is a record and I will not be the one who closes it smooth as water over a stone.

That part I'll defend to anyone.

But there is a second why under it, the way there is always a second why under mine, and the second why is this: I am tired of getting lighter. Dwight took something out of me and I felt it go and I have spent two nights since with my hand on the place it used to be, and I do not want to pay again. I do not want to wake up Friday reaching for a warm thing and find a swept floor. A refusal costs nothing. That's the trade's one charity to its keeper: you only pay for the swallow. Lay the bread, eat it, lose a piece of yourself; don't lay the bread, keep the piece. So when I tell myself I am protecting Carla, I am also, in the same breath, the same heartbeat, protecting *me,* protecting the diminishing pile of myself, and I cannot tell anymore which why is the load-bearing one, and that — not the burn, not the smell — that is the thing that scares me, standing at the panel at eleven at night with a heavy man going into the fire.

The Power-Pak takes him at 11:14.

And he burns wrong.

I knew he would. I have heard a light burn now — Carl went up like paper, like a thing eager to be gone, the temperature barely dipping before it climbed — and I have heard the ordinary ones my whole life, the honest heavy ones, Earl Stiltner taking his slow two and a half hours, the fat feeding the fire. Tug is neither. Tug fights.

The temperature drops and will not come back. Eighteen hundred, then sixteen, then fifteen-forty and sitting there like a stone in a creek, and I feed the burner the way you feed a sick animal, in coaxings, and it takes the gas and does nothing with it, and there is a sound. There is a sound I do not have in my notebook, low and wet and continuous under the roar, not the blower-tick, not the gaskets, something from inside the chamber, something that sounds — and I write this down because writing it down is the only power I have over it — something that sounds like a man clearing his throat to speak and never getting to the words. Over and over. *Hk. Hk.* For an hour. I check the door seal. I check the secondary. I check everything I know to check and the numbers are all fine and the sound is not in any number.

And the smell.

The secondary chamber is supposed to eat the smell. That is its whole job, eighteen hundred degrees of after-burner so that what goes up the stack is nothing the neighbors would call about. It is doing its job; I watch the gauge; it is at temperature. And the smell is in the carriage house anyway. Not up the stack. *In here,* with me, at the panel, the struck match and the wet stove and the third thing, and it is not coming from the retort. I walk the room with it, the way you walk a room hunting a gas leak, and it does not get stronger at the chamber and it does not get weaker at the door, it is simply *present,* evenly, the way grief is present in a house, a thing with no source because the source is everywhere, because the weight has come out of him the way I knew it would and it is not going up the stack with the smoke. The smoke is clean. The weight stayed.

It is in the building by midnight. By the time he is down to bone and cooling I can smell it in the prep room and in the hall and faint at the foot of the back stairs, the same wrong, settling into the house the way damp settles, room to room, patient, and I open the windows even though it is October and cold and there is fog coming up off the creek, and the cold comes in and the fog comes in and the smell does not go out.

It will not air out. The ledger said it wouldn't and I believed the ledger the way I believe a thing I've read, which is a different belief than the one I have now, standing in a cold house at one in the morning with every window open, knowing it the way you know a fact about your own body.

I process him at three. I weigh him — six pounds even, which is heavy for the cremated remains, the bone didn't fully calcine, the burn ran cool the whole way — and I put Tugwell R. Ammons into the *Slate* urn with his name spelled right, and I set it on the shelf, and I write in the cooler notebook, *burn ran cool, low temp held throughout, anomalous — un-eaten, heavy,* and I do not write the rest, the *hk hk,* the smell that won't leave, because some things you write down to control them and some things you do not write down for the same reason, and I have learned, lately, the difference.

Then I go and sit in the kitchen, which is the only room with the window I haven't opened, and the smell is in here too.

—

Carla comes for the urn on Saturday.

She has the folder. Of course she has the folder. She opens it on the desk to put the date-card away with the others, the careful tabs, the years, and I watch her hands, and her hands are not careful anymore.

It's small. You'd miss it if you weren't a person who reads bodies for a living, if you didn't spend your nights with your palm flat against the place where the weight sits. Her hands shake. Not much. A tremor, a fine one, in the fingers that square the certificate, and she squares it twice, three times, against the edge, and it doesn't get square, and she keeps doing it, and there is something behind her eyes now that was not there Tuesday, a draftiness, a window left open somewhere in her that the cold is coming through.

"You sleeping?" I ask, because I am a coward and that is the closest I can get to the real question.

"Not — " She laughs, a little. "Funny you'd ask. Dreaming bad. Since Thursday." Thursday. The burn. "Same thing every night, can't even tell you what, just — heavy. Like there's a person sitting on the bed. I wake up and my mouth's already moving, like I been talking in my sleep, saying — " She stops. Squares the certificate. "I don't know what I'm saying. Carl says — " She has a Carl. Everyone has a Carl. "My fella says I keep apologizing. In my sleep. Sorry, sorry, sorry, all night, like I done something. And I can't think what I done." She looks at me, and the steadiness is gone, that dry steady thing that read me so clean on Tuesday, and what's under it is a woman with a draft coming through her. "Is that grief? Is that what grief is? 'Cause I been clean two years and four months and I have never wanted to use as bad as I have wanted to since Thursday, and I can't — I can't even tell you *why,* there's no reason, he's been gone two weeks, why is *Thursday* the — "

She doesn't finish.

I know why Thursday. I am the only person on this earth who knows why Thursday, the way I am the only one who knows why Brandy Mott flinches and why Della wears yellow, and that is the whole of my inheritance, the keeping of whys that no one can be told, and I stand on my side of the arrangement desk with the green folder open between us and her hands shaking on the certificate and the smell of her dead brother's un-housed weight faint in the room around us both, the weight I would not eat, the weight I sent looking, and it found her, exactly the way Loy said it would, exactly the way the ledger said, and it is sitting on her bed at night putting *sorry* in her mouth that was never hers to say, and her two years and four months are a chip in a tab in a folder her hands have stopped being careful with.

And I did this. Not by eating. By *not* eating. I kept the truth of her brother standing — the wronged child, the record I would not close — and the cost of keeping it true is being carried, right now, in front of me, by the one person it should have spared, in the one currency I told myself a refusal didn't spend.

There is no clean hand. I understand that now in a way the ledger could not teach me. Eat the sin and you murder the truth and the wronged are unmade and the absolved go light and false and someone's whole survived life is quietly deleted. Refuse the sin and you keep the truth and the weight comes off the dead and goes hunting and it finds the living and sits on their bed and unpicks them board by board. Either way a woman ends up lighter than she should be. The only difference is which one is the lie.

"I'm sorry for your loss," I tell her, the coin across the counter, the words that mean *I see you and I can't help you and this is the shape my hands make when they're empty.* And I do not say the other thing, the real one, the one I have not said since I was twenty-two, even though it is the only true sentence in the room, because I am not going to put into Carla's hands the one word that her brother's weight has already taken hostage, sitting on her bed every night saying it for her, sorry, sorry, sorry, until it wears the meaning out.

She takes the urn. She closes the folder. Her hands are not careful with the closing.

—

I lock up after her and I stand in the hall, and the smell is everywhere, the brother in every room, and I am tired in a way that the work does not usually make me, a draftiness of my own, and without deciding to I lift my own hand and lay the palm flat against my own sternum, over my own breastbone, where on every body I have ever read the weight sits.

It is a stupid thing to do. The keeper can't read herself. The rite doesn't work on the eater; that's the first rule, the locked one, the cruelty at the bottom of the whole arrangement — the one weight I could never lift is my own. I know that. I have known it for weeks. There is nothing to feel.

I feel it anyway.

Faint. Far down. Under the brother, which is the loud new thing, there is an older thing, quieter, the way a house has a smell under the smell you can name, and it is mine, it has always been mine, I have been carrying it so long I stopped knowing it had a weight, the way Della carried hers, the way they all do, and my hand is flat on my own chest in a cold hall at one in the morning and I am standing in the exact place I have stood over forty-one bodies and read what they buried, and what I read on myself is *here. This is where it sits.* An account. Open. Old. Mine. Heavy as anything that has ever been on my table.

I take my hand off my chest the way I took it off Tug's.

I do not have a shelf for this either. But I know, now, what the smell under the smell in this house has always been, the one I blamed on the drains my first night home — I know it is not only Esther's, the way I'd started to let myself believe, her forty-year account sealed in the cooler waiting for me.

Some of it is coming off me.

I go and check the windows. They are all still open. The fog has come all the way into the parlor now, lying along the floor between the stacked folding chairs, and the cold is total, and the house smells of un-housed weight, the brother's and the mother's and, I am only now letting myself count it, mine, three accounts open in one building, and not one of them will air out, and I leave the windows open anyway, all night, because I cannot stand to close them on a thing already inside.

# Chapter Ten

## Good People

The house has not aired out from Tug Ammons in nine days and I have stopped pretending it will.

I have run the secondary chamber on empty twice, which you are not supposed to do, which wears the refractory and which I do anyway because the alternative is to keep smelling him — the slow loud wrong of a body sent on heavy, struck match and wet stove and the third thing I have no shelf for, only now it is everywhere, in the carpet, in the showroom, in the cold breath of the cooler where it has no business being because the cooler is thirty-eight degrees and weight is not supposed to care about cold but this weight does. I have lit candles, which is a thing I have never once done in this building, the cinnamon kind from the Dollar General, three of them, and all they do is teach the smell a new note. Now it is struck match and wet stove and a faint false cinnamon, like a thing that died at a fall festival.

So when Haz Greer comes in, I am glad. I want to be honest about that, the way the first chapter of anything should be honest, before the lying starts. I look at the manifest the county faxes up and I read *GREER, HASSELL R.,* age fifty-eight, and the place of death is the regional and the cause is the long Latin of a heart that quit climbing, and something in me unclenches, because I know that name, everybody in three hollows knows that name, and it is a good name. It is the name you say when you want to prove the town is not all the way dead yet.

A clean one, I think. Finally. A man I can do right by.

I get the table ready like it is a thing I am owed.

—

He comes in at 8:40 and the smell does not get worse when they roll him through, which is the first thing I check now, always, the way you check a stove is off before you leave the house — I stand at the dock and I take the air around the new body the way Loy says his aunt used to, and Haz Greer is light. Not light in the pounds. I log him at a hundred and ninety-four and that is a real number, a man who ate at every covered-dish in the county and was welcome at all of them. Light the other way. Light in the air over him.

For a second I am relieved all the way down, because I think: nothing buried. A man who lived clean. Pauline was like this, weeks back, light as a girl, will burn easy and need no bread, and I had thought there was a category of person who simply did not accumulate, who got to the end with the books already even, nothing on the left, nothing owed. I had wanted Haz to be one of them. I had wanted there to be more than one.

Then I put my hand flat on his sternum to draw the sheet straight and I feel it.

It is not the black I felt in Dwight Probst, the cold thing that made me want to scrub to the elbow after. It is small. It is so small that if I had not learned, these last weeks, to read a body the way you read a face you have known a long time — the tell at the corner of the mouth, the thing under the thing — I would have called it nothing and burned him clean and never known. But I have learned. God help me, I am good at this now. And under the lightness of Hassell Greer there is one heavy thing, the size of a stone you'd carry in a coat pocket, worn smooth from being carried, and it is old, and it has a particular shape I am only beginning to be able to name, which is the shape of a thing a man did once and spent the rest of his life answering for.

I take my hand off him.

The families start at nine.

—

There are four arrangements in the book before noon and three of them are for Haz, which is its own kind of grotesque arithmetic — three different sets of people who each believe they are the ones who get to bury him. The sister, who is blood and has the death certificate and the right. The man from the volunteer fire who wants a flag, which Haz did not rate, and I do not say so, I say *I'll see what we can do* and write *no flag* in the margin where the family won't read it. And a woman named Coreen Bostic, who is not kin to him in any way the county would recognize, who comes in with her hair done and her good shoes on and sits down across from me and cannot, for a while, talk.

"He paid my boy's tuition," she says finally. She says it like it is the cause of death. "Eight years ago. The whole first year at Bluefield, out of his own pocket, and made me swear I'd never tell, and I told everybody, because what was he going to do, sue me." She laughs and it breaks in the middle. "He'd have been so put out, knowing I'm in here saying it. He hated being thanked. He'd go red as a beet and find somewhere to be."

I write it down. *Coreen Bostic — son, Bluefield, tuition yr 1, ~8 yrs ago.* I do not know why I write it down. It is not in any field the form has. There is no line on a cremation arrangement for *the dead man was kind to me once and it changed my life.* But I write it in the margin in my own hand, small, the way I write everything, because a thing written down is a thing the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about, and I have never been able to bear it otherwise, and I have a notebook for the retort and a notebook for the cooler and a notebook that is just mine and I cannot, if you held a match to me, tell you the day I started or the reason. It is simply the floor I stand on. You write it down. You keep the record true. There is a why under it the way there is a floor under a rug, and you do not think about the floor, you just do not fall through it.

I write Coreen's boy down, and I think nothing of the writing. That is worth saying now, before. I think nothing of it at all. It is the last thing I will ever do without thinking.

—

Loy comes by at two with no casserole, which I have learned to read as bad, and sits at the kitchen table while I bake.

I am baking the bread for Haz. I have decided — somewhere between Coreen's good shoes and the manila folder — that I am going to eat this one, and that it is going to be the clean thing I have been wanting since I sent Tug on heavy and watched a family rot from the inside for nine days because I thought refusing was the high ground. I learned. Refusing has a body count too. I have his sister's bad dreams and his niece gone quiet to prove it, the smell that won't leave to prove it, and I am not doing that to Haz Greer's people. Whatever the small stone is, I will take it, and he will go light, and the town will get to keep its proof that goodness was real here, and I will pay whatever I pay, and at least this time the man on the table will have deserved the mercy.

That is the speech I give myself with my hands in the flour.

My hands do the rest without me. Take of flour two handfuls, of the water a little, salt and no more, work it quick, work it quick, do not let it rise. Heel of the palm, fold, turn, the thumb dragged through to score it. Thin as you can. It must be plain. I have stopped being frightened that my hands know this. No — that is a lie, I have stopped letting the fright reach my face, which is a different thing, and Loy is watching me do it with his wet old eyes and he says, "You've got her hands," and I say, "So I'm told," and the pilot catches with a *whump* and the kitchen fills with the smell of nothing, plain hot bread that is somehow the smell of nothing, and underneath it, very far down, the other thing, patient, the smell that lives in the bread the way the weight lives in the man.

"He was a deacon," Loy says, of Haz, settling in. "Out at the Free Will, before it closed. Wasn't ordained nor nothing, they just called him that, deacon, on account of he done everything. Set up the chairs. Drove the old ones. Paid the light bill some months when the plate come up short and never said." He shakes his head, fond, grieving. "Good as they come, Hassell. You don't get many."

"I know it."

"You'll do him gentle."

"I'll do him right, Loy."

He hears the difference. He always hears the difference; he is seventy and mostly repeating himself but the man can hear a fault line under a flat word. He looks at me a while. Then he says, careful, "There ain't nothing on him, is there. A man like that." And it is not really a question, it is a hope wearing a question's coat, and I do the thing I do, which is not answer, which is take the bread out pale and flat and scorched at the one edge and set it on the rack to cool, and Loy watches me not answer and his face does something old and tired, and he does not ask again.

You can be the best man in three counties, is the thing I do not say to him. You can drive every old woman to every appointment for thirty years. And the reason you did it — the engine of it, the thing that got you up at five to set the chairs — can be a stone in your coat pocket you have been trying to outwalk since you were a young man, and the two facts do not cancel. They are the same fact. I am only starting to understand that they might always be the same fact, that the good might grow up out of the bad the way the green comes up thickest out of the ground over the old mine, where the ground is poisoned worst. I do not have the words for it yet. I will, after Pearl. Right now I only have a stone the size of a coat pocket and a man who deserved better than what I am about to learn about him.

—

I read it at the table, the way I read all of them now, with the bread on his breastbone settled into the hollow like it was cut to fit, the count going by feel — wait for the weight to come up into the crumb, wait for the bread to go heavy in the air over him the way Esther said, you'll know it — and the knowing comes, cold and quiet, not a vision, not a voice, colder than that, the way you know a thing about your own life at four in the morning.

He was nineteen. There was a man he worked under, an older man, a foreman on a job whose name I get and will not keep, who trusted Haz with the week's cash money to carry to the bank on a Friday because Haz had a good face even then, a face you'd hand money to. And Haz did not take it to the bank. He took it to a bus and a girl and a bad idea three states off, and it went the way those go, and by the time he came back with his tail down the older man had been blamed for the missing money — had been called a thief by men he'd worked beside twenty years, had lost the job, the standing, the one thing a man in a place like this has, which is that his word is good — and the older man did not get it back, ever, and drank himself small over a handful of years and died still wearing it, the name *thief,* that belonged to the boy with the good face who let him wear it.

That is the stone.

Haz never told. He came back and he kept his mouth shut and he watched a man go down for him and he said nothing, because to say it was to lose his own good face, and a young man will trade an old man's whole name for his own face every day of the week and twice on Sunday. That was the cowardice. Not the theft — boys steal — the *silence after.* Thirty-nine years of silence, and under the silence, the rest of him: every chair he ever set up, every old woman he ever drove, every light bill, every tuition, Coreen's boy at Bluefield, all of it, the whole good towering decent life of Hassell Greer built course by course on top of a thing he did at nineteen and could never put down and could never, ever say, so he paid it the only way the proud know how, which is to spend your life buying back a name you can't return to a man who's been dead since the eighties.

The bread goes heavy in the air over him.

I should stop here. I want the record to show I knew, at this moment, enough to stop. I had Della. I had the columns. I had nine days of a man I refused stinking up my house to teach me that the other door is no cleaner. But I did not yet have Pearl, and the stone was so small, and the man was so good, and I was so tired of there being no clean thing anywhere in this trade, that I told myself the lie I had to tell, which is: *it is little. It will cost little. Let the old man's name stay lost — he's been dead forty years, what's a name to the dead — and let Haz go light, and let the town keep him good.*

I say the words. Low. The naming of it — *what lies heavy on thee* — and the taking — *I take to me* — and the release — *go thou light, the books are balanced, go on now, go on.* And I eat the bread.

—

It is small.

That is the obscene part, that I can feel the *size* of it now, that I have become an instrument calibrated finely enough to weigh a sin on the way down. Dwight was a wet coat coming off. This is a stone going out of a coat pocket — a little lighter, a little easier to stand, the smallest clean unweighting, and I am ashamed of how I have started to *measure* the relief, to grade it, like a woman who has learned to tell a good year of a bad thing by the finish.

And then, the way it always goes, the world is just quietly different and I am the only one who finds it wrong.

It does not announce itself. There is no shimmer. Haz burns light and odorless an hour later — goes up like paper, the temp barely dipping before it climbs eager, the blower tick the only hard sound in the room — and I weigh him at four pounds nine ounces and spell his name right on the container and that is that. The change is somewhere else. It is small and it is scattered and it takes me until the next afternoon to find even the edges of it, because the cascade of a small sin reaches only as far as the small sin reached, and the silence of a nineteen-year-old reached exactly as far as one ruined name and the slow narrowing of one ruined life — and now that the silence never happened, the name was never ruined, the foreman kept his job and his word, and he did not drink himself small, he lived an ordinary plain length and died of something ordinary in a bed somewhere, unremarkable, un-haunting, and *good* — and so the man Hassell Greer became, who was built entirely out of carrying that ruin, has fewer courses in him now, because some of them have nothing left to stand on.

Coreen Bostic comes back the next day for the death certificate copies and she is the same and she is not. She is lighter in the way they all are. And when I say, because I cannot help it, because I am a keeper of records and I have to check the record against the world, "Your boy. Bluefield. How's he doing," she looks at me kind and a little blank and says, "What boy, hon? I never had no children. The Lord didn't see fit," and she says it without grief, easy, a settled old fact, and there is no son, there was never a son at Bluefield, because the tuition was a thing a guilty man did to buy back his soul and the guilt is gone now and so is the gift and so is the boy it made, somewhere, a man in his late twenties simply thinned out of the world along with one paid year of school he no longer needed because the kindness that funded it un-happened along with the stone it was paying down.

I had written him in the margin. *Coreen Bostic — son, Bluefield.* In my own hand.

I go and find the folder that night. The margin is smooth. Not scratched out. Not erased. Smooth and full and a little meaner than it was, my own writing run on past where his name was to fill the space his name left, the way water closes over a stone, and I sit there at the prep-room desk under the one good light at the hour I am always at it, and I look at the place where I wrote a true thing about a living boy, and the true thing is gone, and the boy is gone, and the only record left that he was ever real is in me, in the corrupted instrument, in the one account I keep that the rite has not yet learned to reach.

And then I reach for *why.*

—

This is the part I have gone back to, since. This is the part I keep, because I have to keep something and the rite keeps taking the things I'd choose.

I am sitting at the desk and I am frightened — properly, the cold kind — and I do the thing I do when I am frightened, which is reach for the floor under me, the thing that does not move. I keep the record. That is who I am. It is the one true thing I have always been able to say about myself, that whatever else, *she keeps the record true,* and there is a reason for it, there is a why, there has always been a why, deep down where you don't look because you don't have to, the floor under the rug — and I reach for it, the way you reach in the dark for the wall you know is there, to steady myself, to remember *why I am the kind of woman who would notice a boy is missing,* and—

My mother's kitchen smelled like—

No. Not that. That one is already gone, I know that one is gone, I learned that hole weeks back and I have stopped putting my tongue in it. A different floor. The reason. The reason I keep the books by hand, the reason I write everything down, the first one, the *first* time, the thing that taught a child to make a record of the world so the world could not—

I reach.

There is nothing there.

I do not mean I can't recall it just now, tired as I am. I mean I reach for the place the reason lived, the bedrock thing, the *because,* and my hand closes on the shape of where it was — there is a where, there is a hollow, a smooth-walled socket the exact size and shape of a reason — and the reason itself is gone, subtracted, taken clean, so that what is left is the habit with no root, the ritual with no god, a woman who keeps records compulsively, helplessly, every day of her life, and cannot any longer tell you *why,* only that she must, only that the must is still there, humming, rootless, like a hand that goes on doing the motion of a trade after the thing it was making has been taken off the bench.

I keep the books by hand because—

The sentence has nowhere to go.

I sit very still. I try to count something. There are eleven pipe joints over the carriage-house door and I count them in my head, one through eleven, and it does not help, it is the first time in nineteen years it does not help, because counting is only a smaller version of the same broken religion, *write it down, keep it true, the number is the same every time and the sameness is the comfort* — and now I do not know why the sameness ever comforted me, the why is gone, and the count is just a count, eleven of a thing, meaning nothing, a tic with the soul scooped out of it.

I have traded the foreman his name. I gave a dead man back his good word and a guilty man a clean death and a town its proof that one of them was decent all the way through. And the rite took, in exchange, weight for weight, like for like — a man kept his good name by losing the engine that drove him to be good, and I kept my whole forensic life of *keeping things true* by losing the reason I ever started — and now I am exactly what Haz was. A practice with no root. A goodness, if you want to call it that, that I can no longer trace to its cause. Ritual without god. I am still doing the motions. I will do them tomorrow. My hand will reach for the notebook at 3 a.m. and I will write down a weight, and I will not be able to tell you, anymore, what I am afraid will happen if I don't.

I write, in the notebook that is just mine, at 4:11 — because my hand goes there even now, even for this, even to record the loss of the reason for the recording —

*reason for the logs is gone. cannot recover it. keep doing it anyway. afraid of why I'm still afraid.*

And then, because the keeper in me does not have the decency to die when its reason does, under that, smaller:

*Coreen had a son. Bluefield. one year paid. he was real. I am the only one left who has it.*

—

Wren finds me on the loading dock at three in the morning with my back against the roll door and the cinnamon-and-corpse smell of the whole building behind us and the cold mountain dark in front, and she does not ask what's wrong, which is the thing I love about her and have not told her and will not, because to say it is to make it a weight, and I am trying not to hand her anything heavy.

She has taquitos. She always has taquitos. The Marathon does two for a dollar after midnight and Wren Tice has never once in her life let a deal like that go unhonored.

"Dakota's getting a tattoo," she says, handing me one in its waxed sleeve, sitting down beside me with her knees up, nineteen and half-feral and warm as a stove. "On his neck. His *neck,* Nona. Of a *wolf.* I told him, I said, baby, you work at the Sheetz, ain't no wolf about it, and he goes" — she drops her voice into a wounded boy register that is so exact I laugh before I mean to — "'it's about how I *feel inside,*' and I go, what, like you feel inside the Sheetz? 'Cause that's the only place you been all week."

"He's a poet," I say.

"He's a *idiot,*" she says, with the helpless fondness of a girl who is going to take him back twice more before she's done, and bites her taquito, and chews, and looks out at the dark, and for a minute neither of us says anything and the not-saying is the whole point of it, the not-saying is the kindest room I get to stand in all week. She is going to leave this place. She's going to go to the cosmetology school in Princeton if her aunt ever quits using the tuition jar for scratch-offs, and she's going to do somebody's hair for a wedding someday and make them feel beautiful on the best day of their life, and she does not know yet that this is a holy thing to be able to do for people, to make them feel good and lose nothing in the trade, to give without it coming out of you somewhere they can't see.

"You ever think about how weird it is," she says, around the food, easy, not knowing she is walking up to the edge of the worst thought I have, "that you could just — teach me the rest of it. The bread thing. The mumbling. Whatever the deal is with you and the dead ones at night." She shrugs, picking at the wax sleeve. "I'm here anyway. I ain't scared. I could be, like. Useful." And she grins at me sideways, generous, offering herself up the way the young do when they don't know yet what a thing costs, *here, take me, I'm not doing anything with it* — and for one second, one obscene second, the door in me swings open and I see it whole: that I could. That the line takes by the eating, not the blood. That I could put the bread in this girl's hand and say the words over her once and never be alone with it again, never reach into the dark for a reason and find a smooth-walled hole, because there would be two of us, there would be a daughter, *& Daughters* would be true at last—

I shut it so hard my teeth click.

"No," I say.

It comes out wrong, too fast, too hard, and her grin falls and she says, "Okay, dang, I was just—" and I make myself soften, make myself reach for the dry thing, the wall, the joke that lives one beat before the feeling.

"You can't even spell *cremains,*" I say. "I'm not handing you the family business."

"I can *too* spell cremains. C-R-E—" and she stops, and squints, and goes, "...it's spelled stupid," and I laugh, real, and she laughs, and the door is shut and she does not know it was ever open, does not know that for one second I weighed her — weighed her the way I have learned to weigh the dead, by the warmth in the air over her, by how much of her there is to lose — and found her so heavy with her own bright unfinished life that the wanting to use her was the closest thing to evil I have done in this house, closer than Dwight, closer than the silence I just ate, because Dwight was already dead and the foreman was already dead and Wren Tice is nineteen and alive and eating a gas-station taquito beside me in the cold and telling me her idiot boyfriend feels things inside the Sheetz.

I would burn this whole town to the ground before I'd put the bread in her hand. I know that, sitting on the dock. I hold onto it. I write it down later, in the notebook that is just mine, because writing it down is the only religion I have left and I no longer remember why, only that I must:

*Not her. Whatever it costs. Not her. — and I can't tell you why I keep these books anymore, but I'm going to keep this one. This one I keep.*

Which is a strange thing to swear, in a hand whose reason has been eaten out from under it. To go on guarding the door of a house when you've forgotten what's inside. To keep the record true for a self you can no longer fully account for.

I look at the line a long time. Then I put the notebook in my apron, against the place where the bread goes, and I go back inside to a building that smells of struck match and wet stove and a dead man's stolen cinnamon kindness, and somewhere in three hollows a woman named Coreen sleeps easy with no son, and I am the last place left in the world that her boy was ever real, and I cannot, anymore, tell you why a thing like that should matter to a person.

Only that it does.

Only that I will be at the desk again tomorrow, writing it down.

# Chapter Eleven

## The Thinning

The burn-window the county gave me on my mother was eleven days, and I have stolen nineteen.

I did it the way you steal anything in a dead county, which is by paperwork and patience. A refrigeration affidavit. A note from a doctor who owes the home a favor going back to before I was born. A line about pending out-of-state kin that is not a lie because I am the out-of-state kin and I am pending, I am very pending, I have been pending in this house for going on a month now and Esther is still on her tray at thirty-eight degrees and the smell is still worst at her door and I have stopped pretending I do not know why.

Nineteen days. The number is not lost on me. It is the kind of number I would once have written down without deciding to.

That is the trouble. The *would have.*

—

I keep a notebook for the retort and a notebook for the cooler and a notebook that is just mine, and tonight I open a fourth.

It is a green county-supply ledger, the same cloth-bound kind the home buys by the case, the same kind the newest book in the fireproof box is written in, which I notice and do not let myself sit in. I take it up to my room, the narrow one at the top of the back stairs that is exactly as I left it at twenty-two, which is its own obscenity, and I sit on the floor with my back against the bed because the desk is too small for a grown woman and always was, and I rule a line down the middle of the first page.

On the left I write: *what I think I had.*

On the right I write: *what is there now.*

I sit and look at the two columns for a while. I have made a ledger of myself. The keeper of records has finally found the one set of books nobody kept, and it is her own, and she is going to keep it the only way she knows, against a thief she cannot catch and cannot name and cannot, the worst of it, even prove has been here.

I start with the candy.

I know there was a candy. I have a memory of the *memory* — a shelf of it, a wrapper, a child's certainty that this was the good one — but where the taste should be there is nothing, a smooth place, a step that isn't there in the dark. I write on the left, *a candy, the good one, root-cellar cold, a wrapper that —* and the sentence stops because the wrapper has no color and the candy has no taste and the whole thing is a doorway into a room that has been bricked up so cleanly you would never know there was a room unless you had been the one to brick it.

On the right I write: *gone. Can name it. Can't taste it.*

I do not know when. That is the part I keep having to learn over. It is not that I forgot it the ordinary way, the way you forget a phone number, a slow fade you can feel happening and grieve a little as it goes. It is that one day the taste was a thing I owned and the next day there was a smooth place where it had been, and no edge to the smoothness, no scar, nothing to tell me the day, because a deletion does not leave a gap you can read. It just leaves you lighter by exactly one true thing and lets you go on thinking you were always this weight.

I do this for an hour. I do it like an autopsy. I open myself and I go organ by organ and I write down what is there and try to feel for what isn't, and the trouble with feeling for what isn't is that you are using the same hand that's been robbed to check your own pockets.

There is a kitchen with no smell in it.

I find that one and I stop.

I know my mother had a kitchen. I am sitting twenty feet above it. I could go down the back stairs right now and stand in it, the enameled range, the burn-ringed counter, the oven that is the oldest thing in the house, and I have stood in it, I bake the bread in it, my hands know it. But when I reach for the *smell* of it — not the room, the smell, the particular warm freight of a kitchen that a mother is in — there is a kitchen with the air taken out. A photograph of a smell. I know it was there the way I know there was a sun over a house I cannot picture. I write on the left, *her kitchen, the smell of it, the one warm —* and the pen sits there and does not finish, because I do not have the word that came next, I had it an hour ago, I think I had it, and now the line is just over, a sentence walking off the edge of a step that isn't there.

On the right I write nothing. There is nothing true to put in it. I leave it open, the way I left Carl's open, and I am building a book now where the right-hand column is mostly white, and a records-keeper knows what an account looks like when only the left side is filled.

It looks like a debt nobody is ever going to collect, because the creditor is dead, because the creditor is me.

—

Ray finds me Tuesday at the diner out on the county road, which is the kind of finding that is not an accident and that we both let stand as one.

It is the only diner left, a tin-sided thing with a sign that says EAT and a second sign below it that says OPEN that has been lying for two years, and it is open at the wrong hours for the wrong people, which is to say my hours, which is to say I am there at two in the afternoon eating eggs because two in the afternoon is my breakfast, and Ray comes in with his folder under his arm the way some men carry a Bible, and he does not ask to sit, he just sits, which I have come to allow.

"You look like hell," he says, friendly, the way you'd remark on weather.

"I keep the same hours as the dead. Improves the complexion." I push the toast across because I never eat the toast and he always does, and this is a thing we have now, a small economy, and I notice myself liking that we have it and I file the liking the way I file everything, in the column marked *things that can be taken.*

He sets the folder on the table between us. He does it gently. He has done it before and I have learned that the gentleness is the tell, that Ray Sennett is at his most dangerous when he is at his kindest, the way I am.

"I want to show you something and I want you to tell me I'm not losing my mind," he says.

"I can't promise the second part."

"Nobody can." He opens it. He is older than me by fifteen years and he carries every one of them in his hands, big careful hands that he uses to square the papers, and he turns the folder so it faces me, and there it is, the thing he has been circling since the night he stood in my parlor and said *an old name, a girl, long time gone.*

Her name is Dana Coyle. She is fourteen in the photograph, '94 hair, a yearbook smile aimed at a camera and a future she did not get, and she went out one night and did not come back and was never found, not a body, not a bone, not a shoe by the creek, nothing, for thirty years, which is longer than she was alive twice over.

"I promised her mama I'd never close it," Ray says. "Verna. She's eighty-one now. I go up there Sundays. Thirty years I been going up there Sundays to tell her I got nothing, and she makes me coffee, and she says *you'll find her,* and I say *yes ma'am,* and that's the whole visit." He taps the photo, once, and stops himself, the way you stop yourself touching a sore tooth. "There was a man. Everybody knew. You know how it is here, everybody knows and nobody can say. Cole Ferry. He's up at the VA now dying of his lungs, black as a stovepipe inside, the doctors give him weeks." He says the next part flat, and the flatness is how I know it costs him. "And I had him. Thirty years ago I had a thing on him. A fella named Pruett — no kin to you, different spelling — gave a statement put Ferry on that road that night with that girl. And I read that statement last week to keep my hand in, the way I do, and it don't say that anymore."

I go very still.

"It says he *might've* seen a truck. It says it was dark and he *couldn't be sure.*" Ray turns a page and there it is, typed, faded, a thing that used to be a knife and is now a damp rag of *I couldn't rightly say.* "And there was a woman seen them together at the Marathon and her statement's in here too and now she's not sure it was that night, not sure it was Ferry, not sure of much." He closes the folder. He looks at me with eyes that have spent thirty years reading people across tables exactly like this one, and he says, "Nona. I have never let go of a single thing in my life. It's the one thing I'm good at. And I am sitting here watching a case I have carried for thirty years get *lighter,* page by page, and there's no white-out and no missing pages, the ink's just *different,* and the only explanation I have is that I'm an old man and my mind is going, and I would honest to God rather that be true than the other thing."

"What's the other thing," I say. My voice comes out level. I have a level voice. It is the one inheritance I'll cop to.

"That the file's right," he says, "and I'm the one that's wrong. That I been carrying a girl in my head for thirty years that the world already let go of, and I'm the last one still holding her, and I can't even tell anymore if I'm holding her or just the shape where she was."

He has described me. He does not know he has described me. He has described the inside of my own skull so exactly that I have to look down at my eggs and count the tines of the fork, four, and the slits of light in the blinds, eleven, and I get to eleven and it does not help, which is new, which is a thing I write down later, *the count did not hold,* because the count has never once not held, the count is the floor under the floor, and tonight at the diner the floor under the floor moved.

I know what is happening to Ray's file. I have known since the night he first said it reads funny. I am what is happening to Ray's file. Not Ferry — I have not had Ferry, Ferry is still up at the VA breathing his last black breaths — but the others, the men I have eaten this month, the monster and the small mean thief of letters and the good deacon whose goodness I unstitched, and somewhere in the cascade of all that softening reality, all those re-knit histories, a thread ran out through this folder and pulled, and a girl who was *taken* is becoming, line by line, a girl who *might have just left,* because that is cheaper for the world to carry, and I did it, I am doing it, I am sitting across a chipped table from the kindest man in the county eating his toast and unmaking the one thing he has left.

"You're not losing your mind," I tell him.

It is the truest cruel thing I have ever said, because it lets him off the hook he should stay on and hangs the whole weight on a hook he can't see, and he hears only the kindness, and his shoulders come down an inch, and he says, "Thank you," soft, and I have to look at the blinds again.

—

I go up to Della's that evening because I tell myself it is a wellness check and it is not. It is me, checking my own arithmetic against the world the way I check the green ledger against my skull, going to the one address in this county where I can see the thing I did standing up and breathing and happy.

Della Mott is happy.

She is in the yard when I pull up, deadheading a hydrangea gone rust-brown for the season, and she is in a cardigan the color of butter and she waves at me like I am a neighbor and not the woman who burned her husband, and she comes to the truck and tells me, unprompted, that she scattered Carl up at their spot, off the old fire road, on a clear day, and that she sat up there a while after, and that it was peaceful, and that she misses him, she says, the way you'd miss a good thing, the way you'd miss morning coffee, *forty-one years that man brought me my coffee in bed.* She has a wrist she turns and turns as she talks, working the stiffness of the cold out of it, and it is smooth, and there is no thickening in the bone, no small wrongness where a wrongness used to be, because in the world the bread made she was never held too hard, never once, and her body has agreed to it down to the marrow.

And Brandy is on the porch.

Brandy is forty and built long like her father and she has her sleeves pulled down over her hands in the warm evening and she holds her keys in her fist with one key out between two fingers, points-out, the way they teach you, the way a woman walks to her car in a parking lot at night, except she is on her own mother's porch in the daylight, and when Della laughs too loud at her own joke about the hydrangea, just laughs, nothing, a happy sound, Brandy's whole body does the thing.

It is a flinch with the thinking burned out of it. It is a body remembering a thing the woman who taught it the lesson no longer carries the memory of having taught. Della does not see it. Della has never once seen it; Della cannot see it, because to see it she would have to know what it was for, and the knowing is the exact thing I ate. So there is a daughter on a porch afraid of a ghost, and the ghost is her own happy mother, and the only two people in Caudle's Mercy who know why are a forty-year-old woman who can't say it and a sin-eater who won't, and Brandy and I look at each other across the yard for a second, and she does not know me and I do not know her, and we are the only two living records of a man the whole world has agreed was gentle.

I tell Della she takes care of that wrist in this weather. I get back in the truck. I do not say I'm sorry. I have not said it to Brandy and I will not, and the reason I give myself has gotten worse since the spring: it is not only that *sorry* would mean there was something to be sorry for and the town has agreed there wasn't. It is that I am the one who agreed it for them. I served the motion and I ate the vote.

—

Ray and I end up on the tailgate of his truck that night because he follows me back and neither of us says why, and we sit in the carriage-house light with the retort cooling behind us and we do not talk about the file.

We talk about his wife. Carol. Esther burned her, years back, did it right, he says, and I let him tell me a story I can tell he has told before, about Carol and a dog they had that ran off every spring and came back every fall fat as a tick, and he tells it twice, the back half of it, the way he does, *and she'd say, that dog's got a better life than us, and I'd say —* and he catches himself, *did I already* — and I say no, and let him finish, because that is a kindness that costs me nothing and gives him the thing he is starving for, which is to be a man whose mind has not yet started letting go.

And it is good. That is the part I will keep, if I am allowed to keep anything. It is two people on a tailgate who have stopped expecting to be understood and are, a little, and the night is cold and clear and the fog is already coming up out of the hollow the way it does, white and slow, swallowing the road by inches, and Ray laughs at his own dog and I laugh at it too, and for the length of the laugh there is no folder and no ledger and no green book of holes upstairs, and I am just a woman on a tailgate in the cold who likes the man beside her.

Then he says, easy, looking out at the fog, "You ever come across her? In them old books of yours. Dana. Coyle." He does not look at me. "I keep thinking. Your people kept everything. Maybe somewhere —"

"I'll look," I say.

I will not look. There is nothing in the books to find, and if there were, finding it would be the start of the worst thing I could do to him, which is give it to him, which is take it away.

He nods. He believes me. He goes home into the fog, and his taillights are two red coals that the white takes one at a time, and then the road is just road again, and I am alone with the cooling retort and my mother forty feet away and a question I have been not-asking all month, the way you don't ask a thing because the asking is the answer.

—

I go back up to my room and the green ledger and I add the night's holes. *The count did not hold at the diner.* On the left. On the right, nothing, because I do not know yet what loose thread in me came undone to make eleven stop helping; I only know that it did.

And then, because I am tired, and because the wall is thinner some nights than others, I let myself form the thought I have been keeping behind the work, the way you keep a flood behind a door by leaning on the door.

I have eaten — I count them, this much I can still count — a handful of accounts since the spring. And each one took a true thing out of me, weight for weight, the bad man's evil paid for in my good, and I cannot get any of it back, and the column of holes is getting longer, and one night, if I keep on, the column will reach the thing I am leaning on the door to protect.

It is not the candy I am afraid of losing.

I have a reason I came home and did not leave again. I have a reason I will not eat the woman in the cooler, will not lay the bread on my mother's breastbone and take her cleanly off the world and walk away light. The reason has a shape and a weight and nineteen years in it, and it is the heaviest true thing I own, and it is mine, and it is the proof of a great many things I am not ready to lay out in two columns.

And the thought I form, on the floor, against the bed, in the room I left at twenty-two, is the one the green ledger cannot help me with, because it is not a memory of a thing that happened, it is a memory of a *reason,* and reasons are made of the same stuff the bread takes.

*If I keep doing this — will I still know why I won't forgive her.*

I do not write that one down.

I cannot make the right-hand column for it. There is no *what is there now* for a thing you are still holding, that you can feel going thin under your hand even as you hold it, light, lighter, the way a body on the table gives up its inches to gravity, the way my mother weighed a hundred and thirty when she put me on a bus and a hundred and twelve when the county brought her to my door, the difference spent on something in the years between, a difference I am beginning, finally, in the cold, to understand the spending of.

I close the green book. The retort ticks once behind the wall, fingernail on a screen door, the house settling its old bones.

I tell myself I will know why tomorrow.

I have started telling myself things, lately, the way Ray tells himself it's just his mind going. We are a pair, the two of us. The last two people in this county still holding a girl by the shape where she was.

# Chapter Twelve

## Cole Ferry Is Dying

The grapevine in this county runs faster than the creek and cleaner, and by the time Loy says it out loud in my kitchen I have already heard it twice — once off the methadone window, where a girl named Sheena who comes for her dose at seven told me through the glass that her uncle's neighbor was "circling the drain, bless him," and once off the corkboard, where a new GoFundMe went up overnight, *help us keep Cole comfortable at home,* a printed face I did not look at long because I did not yet know I'd be looking at it on my table inside the month.

Loy says it like weather. "You heard about Cole Ferry."

"I heard he's poorly."

"Poorly." Loy turns his coffee cup a quarter-turn on the burn-ringed counter, which is a thing he does when he's deciding how much of a sentence to let out. "He's down to weeks, hon. Black lung, the worst kind, the kind that drowns you in the open air. He won't go to the county. Wants to die in the house he was born in." He says it the way you say a thing you half-admire and half-can't-stand. "He's a Caudle's Mercy man, Cole is. Born up the Ferry branch and never left it but for the war and the mines."

"Which means," I say.

"Which means he comes here." Loy says it gently, like it's a kindness, like he's giving me a customer. "When it's time. Ferrys always come here. His daddy did. I helped your mama with his daddy."

I have known for about four seconds and I already know the rest of it, the way you know the floor is there before your foot finds it, and I set my own cup down so it won't shake. Because I have lived in this county again for some weeks now, and I read the obituaries the way other people read horoscopes, and I have heard the name *Cole Ferry* exactly one place before Loy's mouth: in Ray Sennett's, said sideways, said careful, said the way a man says a name he has been carrying so long it has worn a groove in him.

The man Ray has been chasing for thirty years is going to die into my fire.

I count the pipe joints over the door out of habit before I remember I'm not in the carriage house, I'm in the kitchen, and there is nothing here to count but the dead pilot light on the range and the chairs, four of them, and Loy's two big hands, and none of it helps.

—

I do not decide to drive up the Ferry branch. I tell myself I am taking the GoFundMe a covered dish, which is what you do, which is the courtesy, and it is even a little true — I make the chicken and rice from Loy's recipe because it is the only thing I know how to cook for the grieving and the soon-to-be, and I drive it up the branch road with the foil sweating on the passenger seat, and that is the story I am telling, and the story is a coat over the thing.

The thing is that I want to read him.
