The branch road climbs and narrows and the green closes over it, that wet close mountain green that doesn't care that the mine killed the creek it drinks from, and the fog is burning off the hollow late this morning, sitting in the low places like something that doesn't want to leave, and I find the house by the trucks. Three of them in the yard, one up on blocks, a tarp over a woodpile, a satellite dish bolted to a porch post like a thing nailed up against the dark. A girl maybe twenty on the steps vaping, who is the GoFundMe's photographer, I'd guess, the granddaughter, and a smell coming off the whole place before I'm even out of the car.

I want to be exact about the smell, because exact is the only handle that doesn't cut, and because I am the only one in the county who can smell it, and a thing only I can perceive is a thing I had better get on paper right.

I have smelled it on the dead. The struck match held too long after it's out. The inside of a wet stove, the rust and the cold and the old burned. I have smelled it worst around my mother in the cooler, where it should not be at thirty-eight degrees and is, the heaviest thing in the house and old. But I have never, until I stand in Cole Ferry's yard with a dish of chicken and rice going cold in my hands, smelled it coming up off a man who is still breathing.

It is on the porch. It is on the granddaughter, faint, the way a smell gets on you from a house you live in. It thickens at the screen door. And when his daughter — a hard tired woman named, she tells me, Reba — lets me into the front room where they have set his hospital bed up under the window so he can see the ridge, it is so strong I have to make my face do the thing my face does at the dock, the nothing-face, the face that has met a body as freight, because the wrongness coming off Cole Ferry alive is worse than anything I have ever smelled off the dead, and the reason it is worse is that on the dead it is leaving and on him it is still *in there,* thirty years of it, marinated, rotted down, gone septic in a living man.

"He's resting," Reba says, which is the word, though what he is doing is laboring, each breath a thing he has to climb up onto and then fall off of. "You're Esther's girl. From the home."

"Nona."

"He thought a lot of your mama." A flat thing, said over a dying man, the way mountain women say the formal courtesy that is also a fence. "She did his daddy right."

I set the dish where she points. I am not going to be in this room long; the smell alone is a kind of pressure, a thumb laid flat on my sternum, *here, this is where it sits,* and it is sitting on me harder than it has any business to, like it knows what I am, like it is reaching toward the one person in the room who could take it off him. And I look at Cole Ferry — a yellow old man caved in around his own ruined chest, an oxygen line in his nose, hands on the blanket the size of shovels gone to bone — and without laying a finger on him, without bread, without a word, with nothing but the nearness and the trade in my blood, I read him.

Not a vision. Not a voice. I have tried to explain this to myself in the notebook and the best I can do is *knowing,* a cold flat knowing that arrives whole, the way you know a word is spelled wrong before you can say which letter. With Carl it came up through the bread, slow, a poultice drawing a fever. With Cole Ferry it does not need the bread. It is so heavy it leaks.

A girl. 1994. Younger than the granddaughter on the steps. A truck and a back road and a thing that he meant to be one thing and became, in his hands, another, faster than he could call it back, and the great animal panic after, and a strip-mine cut up past the old Number Nine where the overburden goes down forever and the company itself helped him bury her without knowing it, dragline and dozer over thirty years, and a mother — he carried this too, this is the part that rotted him — a mother he would see at the Dollar General and the church and the gas station for thirty years, a mother who hugged his neck once at a funeral and asked him to pray for her lost girl, and he prayed, out loud, holding her hands.

The name comes up last, the way the worst thing always comes up last. *Dana Coyle.*

I take the foil off the dish I have already set down, because my hands need a job and there is no other one available, and I fold the foil into a small square and put it in my coat pocket, and I say to Reba the only true thing I have, which is, "He went to a lot of trouble to die at home. That's worth something."

And Cole Ferry opens his eyes.

They are wet and pale and there is nothing monstrous in them, and that is the part I will write down tonight and underline. Loy would want him to be a monster. The whole county has wanted him to be a monster for thirty years because a monster is a thing you can hold at a distance. But what is looking at me out of that yellow ruined face is just a frightened old man who did one unspeakable thing in one unspeakable minute when he was thirty and has been dying of it ever since, slowly, and is now dying of it fast, and he looks at me — at me, the funeral woman, the smell-of-it woman, Esther's girl — and his mouth works, and what he says, in a voice like wind through a busted screen, is: "You do the burning."

"When it's time."

"You do it good." Not a question. A man making an arrangement. "Your mama. She had a way." His chest climbs and falls. "A man could go easy out of her place. Folks said. A man could lay something down."

I do not answer that. There is no answer to that that I can give in this room.

I drive back down the branch with the window open because the smell has gotten into the car, into my coat, and the fog is gone off the hollow now and the day is doing the thing the days do here, beautiful and poisoned in the same frame, the green and the slag, and I pull over by the dead creek and I take out the notebook and I write the date and the time and I write *Cole Ferry — Dana Coyle — strip cut past Number Nine — he prayed with the mother* and then I sit there a while because I have just understood, fully, the shape of the thing I am holding, and the shape of it is this:

I can make it never have happened.

—

Here is the arithmetic, and I am a keeper of records, so I will do the arithmetic, because the arithmetic is the horror and there is no looking at it sideways.

If I eat Cole Ferry's sin off his sternum before the fire, he dies clean and light and the smell goes out of the world. That is the mercy as I have learned it. But the sin does not go into me to be carried; I know that now, I paid for that knowledge with a piece of myself I can't even find the hole of; the sin goes *out of the world.* And a sin that never happened takes its truth with it.

Which means Dana Coyle was never taken.

Which means there is no body in the strip cut past Number Nine, because no one put one there.

Which means Dana Coyle — fourteen, 1994 — simply *left.* Ran off, the way girls run off from these hollows, the way the county has half-believed for thirty years anyway because believing it is easier than the alternative, and she got on a bus or in a stranger's car and she never called and she never came back, and somewhere in a softened, kinder, emptier past she is a woman of forty-five who didn't love her mother enough to phone.

And her mother — and here is where the arithmetic stops being arithmetic and starts being a knife — her mother, who has spent thirty years holding onto the one terrible thing she has left, which is that *her daughter did not choose to leave her,* that her daughter was taken, that somebody did this, that Dana would never, never have walked away from her mama on purpose — her mother would lose that. Would wake up, in the new gentle past, a woman whose daughter ran off and broke her heart and never looked back. The murder converted to abandonment. The worst grief in the world traded for a slightly lighter, slightly emptier grief that is also a lie.

And Ray's case — thirty years, a promise to a dead girl's mother, the file he won't close, the thing his whole self is built on, *I remember, I don't let go* — Ray's case would dissolve in my hands like sugar in rain, because there would be no case, because there would be no crime, because I would have un-committed the murder he has spent his life trying to prove, and he would feel it as his own mind going, as I have watched him feel it already, a man losing his grip finger by finger on the one thing he never dropped, and not knowing it is my doing, my fire, my mouth.

That is if I eat him.

If I refuse him, he wakes heavy. Loy's words, the folklore's dread, made concrete now after Tug — the weight does not move on, it stays in the world unhoused and it seeks the living kin, and the Ferrys are a houseful of living kin, the hard tired daughter and the vaping granddaughter and whoever else is up that branch, and the smell will flood my house and not air out, and over the weeks the grief in that family will go septic the way the man himself went septic, bad dreams and a heaviness room to room and somebody up there starting to confess to things they didn't do, because a great un-eaten sin takes root in whoever loved the dead most, and rides them down.

But Dana stays taken. The truth stays. The strip cut keeps its girl. And Ray — Ray keeps his case, keeps his grip, gets, maybe, if there is any justice left in a county that ran out of it about the time the mine did, his confession at last off a dying man's mouth before that mouth shuts. And a mother keeps the one thing holding her upright.

I sit by the dead creek and I do the arithmetic and there is no answer in it. There is no row I can run that balances. To give the mother peace I have to murder her daughter a second time. To keep the daughter true I have to let a family rot and deny a mother the only relief left on this earth. There is no clean hand. There has not been a clean hand since Carl, and I keep reaching for one anyway, the way your tongue keeps going to the place the tooth was.

I write, in the notebook, under his name, two words. *No exit.* And then I look at them, because I have written them before, about my mother, and they are the same two words, and that is the first time it occurs to me that the trade only ever asks the one question, over and over, in different bodies, and the question is *whose truth do you kill,* and the only mercy it offers is the choice of victims.

—

Ma Coyle comes to the home on a Tuesday, and she does not come for Cole Ferry, because he isn't dead yet, and the cruelty of it is that I have to stand in my own parlor and make small grief-talk with the woman whose daughter I am deciding whether or not to un-murder.

She comes for somebody else's wake — old Pauline's people are doing a covered-dish thing in the back parlor, the no-weight woman, the light one, the one who needed no bread, and the town turns out for it the way the town turns out, and Ma Coyle is just one of the church women carrying a green-bean casserole, small and stooped and steel-haired with a cardigan buttoned to the throat in October like she's always a little cold now, and Loy, who does not know what he is doing to me, brings her over by the elbow and says, "Nona, this here's Verna Coyle, she knew your mama," and leaves us.

She has the most tired face I have ever read off a living person, and I have read a lot of them now, and it is a different tired than Reba Ferry's. Reba is tired from a deathwatch. Verna Coyle is tired from thirty years of a thing that does not end.

We talk about Esther. She liked my mother; everybody seems to have a piece of my mother I never got. "She done right by folks," Ma Coyle says, which is what they all say, *done right,* and I am beginning to understand it is the highest thing they can say and that it means something specific and terrible that none of them know they're saying. We talk about the weather and the casseroles and the methadone window that used to be a clinic. And then, because grief finds grief the way water finds the low place, because she has clocked that I am a woman who handles the dead and therefore a woman she is allowed to say the unsayable to, she says it.

"You'd of been young," she says, "when my Dana went."

"I was gone by then. I left at twenty-two."

"Mm." She nods like that's an answer to a question I didn't hear her ask. Her hands are around a styrofoam cup of the bad coffee and they are not steady, an old woman's tremor, and over it, faint, so faint I almost miss it under the green beans and the coffee and the funeral-flowers — the smell. On *her.* Not the rotted septic reek of Cole Ferry with the thing still in him. A different register of it. The smell of a weight that is not hers, that was put on her, that has sat on her thirty years unhoused because it never had anywhere to go. The un-housed truth of a murdered girl, and her mother carrying the shape of it without ever being allowed to know what it was.

The body remembers what the world won't say. Brandy flinches at a ghost her mother forgot. And Verna Coyle smells, to the one person who can smell it, of a daughter nobody ever found.

"Folks always thought she run off," Ma Coyle says, to her coffee, not to me, the way you say the thing you have said ten thousand times to yourself in the dark. "That's the easy thing to think. Girl gets to be fourteen up a hollow like this, no daddy, mama working two jobs, folks think, well, she lit out, they all light out." Her thumb works the lip of the cup. "But she didn't. I want you to know she didn't. She wouldn't of. She'd of called me. Whatever trouble she got in, she'd of called her mama, she was that kind, she called me from the school once just to tell me she got a star on a paper." The tremor gets into her voice and she lets it, because she has had thirty years to stop being ashamed of it. "Somebody took my girl. I don't know who and the law don't know who, that Sennett's tried, Lord he's tried, but somebody *took* her, she didn't leave me. That's the one thing. That's the one thing I got left of her, that she didn't leave me on purpose."

And then she looks up at me, this stooped steel-haired woman who carried a casserole into a stranger's wake, and she says the thing, the thing the whole trade is about, the thing my mother heard a thousand times and the thing I will hear for the rest of whatever I have left, and she says it to me as if I might, being a woman of the dead, have some authority over the matter, some pull with whatever office issues these rulings:

"I just want it to be *over,*" Ma Coyle says. "Thirty years. I just want some peace before I go. I want to know what happened to her and I want to lay her down and I want it to *stop hurting.* That's all. Is that so much. To want it to stop."

I look at a mother begging me, without knowing she is begging me, for the one mercy I can actually give — and the mercy she is asking for would take from her, in the same motion, the only thing she just told me she has left. *Somebody took my girl. She didn't leave me on purpose.* If I give her peace I take that. The peace and the truth are the same lever and they pull against each other and there is no setting where she gets both. I could make it stop hurting. I could make it stop hurting tonight, with bread and a word, and the cost would be that her daughter becomes a runaway who broke her heart, and she would never know I'd done it, she'd just be a little lighter and a lot emptier and grieving the wrong grief for the rest of her life, content, the way Della is content, *done right by.*

This is the trade. This, exactly. Not Carl, not Dwight, not even my mother yet. This is the rite stripped down to the bone of what it is: a begging woman, a clean relief in my pocket, and the knowledge that the relief is a second murder dressed as a kindness, and that she would thank me for it.

I do not say *I'm sorry.* I have not said it since I was twenty-two and I am not going to start in a parlor over a styrofoam cup, and besides, *sorry* is a coin I could hand her, *I'm sorry for your loss,* the trade-phrase, the thing you say, and I find I cannot even spend that one, because she has not lost Dana yet in the way that *sorry* covers; she has lost her and kept her, kept her *taken,* and the only sorry that would mean anything is the one that says *I'm sorry I can give you peace and I won't, because your peace is a lie I refuse to tell your dead girl,* and that one I cannot say, because it would break her, and because I have not decided it yet, and because there are no words in the trade for *I am choosing to let you hurt.*

So I say, "I hope you get your answer." Which is a thing I should not say, because I am the one place in the county the answer lives, and saying it is close to a lie, and I deal in the kind of true that doesn't cut and that one cuts.

She pats my hand with her cold tremoring one. "You're good to listen to an old woman," she says, and goes back to her green beans, and I stand in my own parlor smelling a thirty-year-old murder on a living mother and I think: *Loy calls this instead of sorry. The mercy you give instead of the apology nobody can make. She wants instead of sorry so bad she'd take it off a stranger's hands and call it grace.* And she has no idea — none of them have any idea, my mother made sure of that, the whole town's mercy was always this and they never knew — that *instead of sorry* doesn't mean the comfort I was promised. It means *instead of it ever having happened.* Instead of Dana having been a girl who got a star on a paper and called her mama to say so. Instead of her having mattered enough to be missed as *taken.* You buy the peace with the mattering. You always have. The books balance and the balancing is the murder.

—

Ray finds me on the loading dock at near midnight, which is its own kind of admission, that he knows where I am at midnight, that he has learned my hours the way I have learned his case.

He doesn't have the folder. That is the first thing I notice, because he always has the folder, the manila one, soft at the corners, that he carries the way Loy carries grief, like a coat he forgot he had on. Tonight his hands are empty and he stands at the edge of the dock light with the dark of the dead creek behind him and he looks like a man who set something down and can't remember where.

"You hear about Ferry," he says.

"I heard he's poorly."

"Poorly." He almost laughs. It is the same word Loy used and it does the same work, holds the thing at arm's length. He comes up the dock steps and sits on the cold edge of it without being asked, the way you sit somewhere you've decided you're allowed, and I sit too, because I have learned that with Ray you talk by sitting next to a thing and looking at it together rather than across a table at each other, two keepers of records comparing the one book nobody else can read.

"I'm gonna tell you something," he says, "and you're gonna think I'm losing it, and maybe I am." He looks at his empty hands. "The Coyle file. Thirty years I've had that file like a — like a tooth I can't stop touching. I know it cold. I could recite you the witness statements in my sleep, I have recited them, my wife used to say I talked the Coyle case in my sleep more'n I ever talked to her awake." A flat dry grief, set down and looked at. "There was a fella. Statement he gave in '94. Put Cole Ferry's truck on the Number Nine road the evening Dana went. Wasn't enough by itself, never was, but it was the spine of the whole thing, it was the one hard fact I had." He stops. The creek goes on behind us, the dead creek, saying nothing. "I pulled the file last week. That statement reads different now. Reads like the fella wasn't sure. Reads like maybe it was a different evening, maybe a different truck. And I *know* — Nona, I know the way I know my own name — that statement used to be certain. It used to say *that* truck, *that* evening. And now it doesn't. And there's no — there's no edit on it, no white-out, no retyped page. It's the same paper. It's just less than it was."

The same paper. It's just less than it was.

I have read those exact words in my own hand, in my own notebook, about my mother's intake folder, the night the county brought her. *File thin. Fewer facts in it than there should be.* And about Carl's folder, the line I wrote that wasn't there in the morning, the world editing my own record while I slept. The same paper, just less. It is the seam. It is always the seam. And I am sitting next to a man whose life's-work file is going thin the exact way my dead mother's did, and I know why, and I cannot tell him why, because the only proof I have is my own memory, and memory is the very thing both of us have started to distrust, and if I say to Ray Sennett *the world is being edited and I am the one editing it,* he will hear a woman who handles too many bodies finally come unstrung, and I will lose the one person in this county who keeps a record the way I keep a record, the one mirror I have, and I am not — I find, sitting on the cold dock — I am not willing to lose him to the truth.

So I say, "Maybe you're misremembering." And it is the cruelest thing I have said to anyone in nineteen years, because I know it isn't true, because I am telling a man to doubt the one faculty he has left, to protect a secret that is eating him alive, and I say it in my flattest dock-voice, the freight-voice, and it lands on him like I knew it would.

"That's the thing," he says quietly. "That's the thing keeps me up. I've never misremembered a case in thirty years. I don't let go. It's the only — it's about the only thing I was ever any good at, holding on." He scrubs a hand down his face. "And if Ferry dies before I can put it together — and he's gonna, he's dying right now up that branch — then that little girl never even gets to have been *taken.* You understand? Right now, today, she's a murdered child and I'm the man trying to prove it. But if I can't prove it, and the one man who could tell us dies, then in twenty years she's just — a runaway. A girl that left. A cold name in a drawer that nobody pulls. Her mama dies thinking maybe she just lit out after all." His voice does a thing it has not done in front of me before, goes thin at the edge, a man's grief finding the one crack in the procedure. "I made her mama a promise. I told Verna Coyle I'd never close that file. And I'm sitting here watching it close itself."

He does not know he is describing the rite. He thinks he is describing failure, age, a mind going. He is describing *me,* my mouth, my fire, the choice I have not made yet, and he is describing it more exactly than I could, and he is grieving it as a man grieves his own decline, and I sit next to him in the dock light and I hold the whole of it — that I am the office Ma Coyle was begging, that I am the thing dissolving Ray's file, that one body up the Ferry branch is going to make me decide whether Dana Coyle was murdered or merely left, and that either way I decide, I am taking something from someone who is sitting close enough to touch — and I keep my freight-face on, and I count the boards of the dock between my feet, eleven of them, and it does not help, it has stopped helping, the counting, somewhere back there it stopped, and I did not write down when.

"He asked me to do it good," I say, before I can stop myself. "Ferry. When I went up with a dish. He said a man could lay something down at my place. That my mother had a way."

Ray turns his head and looks at me in the dock light, and for a second — for one long bad second — there is something almost like understanding in his face, like he has caught the edge of a thing too big to see whole, the way you feel a draft and know a door is open somewhere in a house you thought was shut.

"Lay something down," he says slowly. "What's a man like Cole Ferry got to lay down, you reckon."

And I look at the dead creek, and I do not answer him, and the not-answering is the loudest thing on the dock, and somewhere up the Ferry branch a yellow old man climbs onto another breath and falls off of it, and the books, the books that must balance, wait open on a page I am going to have to fill in one direction or the other, and there is no row that closes, and there never was, and I am the only one who knows it, and the smell of a murdered girl is still in my coat.

# Chapter Thirteen

## What the Mother Has Left

Cole Ferry dies on a Thursday at 4:40 in the afternoon, which I know to the minute because Reba calls the home herself instead of going through the county, and I am the one who answers, and I write the time on the intake sheet before she has finished saying the words. *He's gone.* She says it flat, the way you say a thing you have been climbing toward for weeks and have finally fallen off the far side of, and then she says, "He wanted your place. He was real set on it," and I say I'll send the van, and I do.

He comes in at 6:15 with the light already failing, and I meet him at the dock the way I meet all of them, with the clipboard and the nothing-face, and I sign on the line that says receiving, and I lift the sheet at the corner to confirm against the toe tag, which is procedure, and what is under the sheet is a yellow caved-in old man who weighs, the cot scale says, a hundred and twenty-nine pounds, which is a big-framed man rendered down to the staves of himself by a thing that eats you from the inside out. I log him. *FERRY, COLE D.* I spell it right because I always spell it right, that is the floor below which I will not go, and below the name I write the weight and the time, and I do not write the other thing I know about him, because there is no line on a county form for the other thing I know about him.

The smell comes off him through the sheet.

It is not the same as it was up the branch. Up the branch, alive, it was the worst I have ever met, the septic in-there reek of a thing rotting in a living body, and I had braced to meet it again at the dock and instead it is — different. Lighter is the wrong word. *Leaving* is the word. On the living it was a thing held in; on the dead it has started, the way a held breath starts to go out of a body the second the body quits holding it. It is the struck match and the wet stove and the third thing I have no shelf for, and it is dreadful, but it is dreadful the way a thing is dreadful when it is finally on its way somewhere, and I understand, standing on my own dock in the last of the Thursday light, that I am smelling thirty years begin to look for the door.

It can go two ways out of him. That is the whole of what I am, now: the woman who decides which door.

I wheel him to the cooler, and I do not put him next to my mother, though I could not have told you, if you asked me, what rule I am keeping when I roll his cot to the far wall instead.

—

I bake the bread anyway.

I tell myself I am only keeping my hand in. I tell myself a thing I have told myself before, which is that I have not decided, and that a baked loaf is not a decision, that the bread on the counter is not the bread on the breastbone, that there is a whole night and a whole choice between the oven and his sternum. But my hands do not believe me. My hands hold the recipe my mind swears it never learned — the fold, the heel of the palm, the turn, *thin as you can, it must be plain* — and they do the bread the way they have done it nine times now, with a thing in them that is almost calm, that I have stopped letting reach my face, and the dough comes together quick and ugly under the pilot light and I score it with my thumb and I put it in the hot oven on the old enameled range, and I stand at the counter in my mother's kitchen and I wait for it the way you wait for a thing you are afraid you are going to use.

It comes out pale and flat and scorched at the one edge it always scorches, because the range cooks hot on the left, because it has cooked hot on the left for forty years, because some things in this house do not change and some things in this house change while you sleep and you can no longer tell, standing in it, which kind of house it is.

I set the loaf on the steel to cool. I break a morsel off — not to eat, just to have it ready, the way you load a thing you have not decided to fire — and I carry it back to the cooler in the flat of my palm, and the smell of Cole Ferry meets me at the door, *leaving, leaving,* and I stand there with the bread in my hand and the cold breathing out of the dark and I do the thing I came back here to do, which is the only thing I am good at anymore. I read him.

I do not need to lay the bread on him. With Carl it had to draw, slow, the warmth cooling up into the crumb; I needed the bread to get at Carl. I do not need it for Ferry. It came up off him alive, in his own front room, with nothing but the nearness; it comes up off him now through the cooler dark like cold coming off a slab, and it is the same as it was, exact, and I will not write the worst of it down even here, even for myself, because there is a child in it, fourteen, and she has had enough people make a record of the worst minute of her life, and I am not going to be one more.

A girl. A truck. A back road. A minute that went one way when he meant it to go another and then would not go back, no matter how he reached after it, and the great animal scrabble of a man trying to un-do a thing with his hands while it is still warm. And then the long part. The part that rotted him. The strip cut past the old Number Nine, the overburden going down and down where the dragline had been, the company's own machines covering her over for thirty years without ever knowing they were sextons. And the mother. Always, under everything, the mother — a small steel-haired woman at the Dollar General, at the church, at the gas station, thirty years of her, and the one time at a funeral she took his neck in her arms and asked him would he pray for her lost girl, and he prayed, out loud, holding her hands, and meant it, and that is the part I cannot get out of the cold air, that he meant the prayer.

The name comes up last, the way the worst thing always comes up last.

*Dana Coyle.*

I stand in the cooler with the bread in my hand and the dead all around me and I think a thing I have been not-thinking since Loy said *Ferrys always come here.* I think: *I could take this off the world tonight, and no one would ever know it had been here, except me.*

—

Ma Coyle comes to the home on Friday morning, and she does not come for Cole Ferry, because nobody but me and Reba knows yet that Cole Ferry is on my far cooler wall, and the cruelty of it is so exact I half believe the trade arranges these things on purpose, that the universe that runs on balanced books has a sense of humor and the humor is this: she comes to pick up Pauline.

Old Pauline's cremated remains are ready — the no-weight woman, the light one, the one who needed no bread — and Pauline's people have asked could a church woman fetch the urn since the family's all working, and the church woman they sent is Verna Coyle, because of course it is, because grief in this county runs errands for grief, and she comes up my porch steps small and stooped with her cardigan buttoned to the throat in the warm of a Friday morning, like she is always a little cold now, and I have a dead man's daughter's murderer in my cooler and a begging mother on my porch and a loaf of bread on the steel that I have not decided whether to use.

I give her Pauline. I have her sign for the urn, and she signs slow, the way old hands sign, and she pats the box like it is a person, and then she does not leave, because she has clocked me again the way she clocked me at the wake, clocked that I am a woman who handles the dead and is therefore a woman she is permitted to say the unsayable to, and she stands in my front hall with another woman's ashes in the crook of her arm and she says, to me, but really to the room, the way you say a thing you have worn smooth:

"They's a Ferry passed, I heard. Up the branch."

The hall goes very clean and very far away, the way it did at the dock the night they brought my mother, and I make my face do the dock-thing, the freight-thing, and I say, "I heard he was poorly."

"Mm." She nods. "Cole Ferry. He prayed with me, you know. Many a time." She says it like a kindness she is reporting, a thing about a good neighbor, and I have to hold the edge of the hall table because the floor of me drops out clean through the foundation. "After my Dana went. Cole was good to me, after. Lots of folks didn't know what to say, you can't blame 'em, what do you say. But Cole, he'd take my hands and he'd pray for her to be found. Right out loud." Her steel head bows a little. "Last I seen him at the church he could hardly get a breath and he still prayed. *Lord bring her home.*" She is quiet a moment. "He'll be with her now, I reckon. Wherever she's at. He'll know before I do."

I am going to be sick, is the honest report. I stand in my own front hall holding the table and I look at a woman who has spent thirty years being prayed at by the man who killed her daughter, who has wept onto his hands, who is right now telling me he is a *good neighbor,* and who believes — because he made her believe it, because they all believed it, because believing it was the only way through — that wherever Cole Ferry is now, he is closer to her lost girl than she is.

And the worst of it, the part the trade saves for last the way the knowing saves the name for last, is this: she is right. He is closer. He is forty feet from me on a steel tray and he knows exactly where Dana Coyle is, down to the foot, down to the seam of overburden the dragline dropped over her in the fall of 1994, and the only other creature on this earth who knows it is me, and I got it for free, out of the cold, off a dead man, and her — her it has cost everything, and bought her nothing, for thirty years.

"You'd of been young," she says again, the same as the wake, the worn groove, "when my Dana went."

"I was gone by then."

"Mm." She shifts Pauline's box in her arm. And then she looks up at me, and her eyes are wet the easy way the very old go wet, no shame in it anymore, thirty years past shame, and she says the thing. Not the begging she did at the wake, *I just want it to stop hurting* — something quieter than that, something worse, the thing under the begging, the floor of it.

"I think about it," she says, "what I'd give. To know. Folks say let it go, Verna, it's been thirty year, you'll make yourself sick. And I can't. I won't. Because the day I let go is the day I let 'em make her into a girl that run off. And she didn't run off." Her chin comes up, that small steel defiance, the one true thing, the thing she said at the wake and will say at her own grave. "Somebody took my girl. She didn't leave her mama. I'd give the rest of what I got to prove it. But I won't ever give *that.* They can take everything else. They can't have that."

And she pats my hand with her cold tremoring one, and tells me I am good to listen to an old woman, and goes down my porch steps with Pauline in her arms and the un-housed truth of a murdered girl coming off her so faint and so certain that I stand in the doorway long after her car is gone, smelling a thing that only I can smell, *a daughter nobody ever found,* and I understand that she has just told me, without knowing she told me, exactly what the rite would take from her if I gave her the only peace I have.

*They can take everything else. They can't have that.*

I am the everything else. I am also the that.

—

Here is the thing I cannot make anyone understand, and there is no one I would even try, except this notebook, which is mine, which the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about: the relief is *right there.*

It is in my pocket, almost. It is a morsel of bread on a steel table and a low handful of old words and one swallow, and it would be the easiest of all of them. Ferry wants it. He arranged his whole death to get to my table; *a man could lay something down,* he said, and he was not wrong, he could, I could take it off him, I have taken worse off worse and felt the obscene clean lightness of it and been ashamed of how good it was before I was even afraid. And it would not only ease a dying man. That is the part that makes it a temptation and not just a chore. It would *end* something. The smell goes out of the world. The strip cut keeps no secret because there is no secret. And Ma Coyle —

Ma Coyle stops hurting.

I want to be exact about this, because exact is the only handle that doesn't cut. If I eat Cole Ferry's sin off his sternum before I burn him, then Dana Coyle was never taken, and a girl that was never taken simply *left,* and Ma Coyle wakes up tomorrow a different woman with a different grief, a lighter one, an emptier one — a woman whose girl ran off at fourteen and broke her heart and never called, which is a grief thousands of women in these hollows carry and survive, which is *survivable,* which would let an eighty-one-year-old woman finally, after thirty years, set the thing down and go on to whatever short time she has left without the daily knife of it. She would not know I had done it. She would just be — a little lighter. The way Della is lighter. *Done right by.* She would stop saying *somebody took my girl* because no one took her girl, and she would stop coming undone at the gas station, and she would sleep.

I could give her that tonight. With bread and a word. And she would thank me, if she knew, which she never would.

And it is a murder.

It is the second murder of Dana Coyle, and it is dressed as a mercy, and the one who would be doing it is me, and the worst of it — I write this down, I make myself, because a thing not written down is a thing the world is allowed to lie about later, even to me — the worst of it is that the murder would erase *the very thing Ma Coyle just stood in my hall and told me she would never give.* She said it to my face. *They can take everything else. They can't have that.* And the only peace I can sell her is the one that takes exactly *that,* the one true thing, the load-bearing thing, the thing she has built thirty years of a ruined life on top of, *my girl did not leave me on purpose,* and trades it for a quiet sleep she did not ask for and would not have chosen and will never know she was robbed of.

She thinks she is keeping the truth by refusing to let go. She does not know that the truth is forty feet away in my cooler and that I am the one who decides whether she gets to keep it. She thinks the choice is hers. It is mine. It was always going to be mine. That is the obscenity at the bottom of the whole trade, the thing my mother never told a living soul: *the begging is a formality.* The mother does not get a vote. The office she is begging is a woman in an apron with a loaf of bread, and the woman in the apron decides, and the mother is never told there was a decision, and either way she will say *done right by.*

I sit at the prep-room desk under the one good light at one in the morning with the morsel of bread on the steel beside me, and I do the arithmetic again, every row of it, looking for the row that balances, the way your tongue keeps going back to the hole where the tooth was. And there is no row. To stop her hurting I have to unmake her daughter. To keep her daughter I have to let her hurt until she dies. There is no setting on the machine where Verna Coyle gets both peace and her girl, because in this trade peace and the girl are the same lever and they pull against each other, and I am the hand on the lever, and I have to choose, tonight, before the fire, because fire is the seal and after the fire the choice is gone forever and you cannot eat ashes.

I pick up the pen. I am a keeper of records. When I cannot do anything else I can at least write down what is true.

And I find that I already know. I have known since the hall, maybe since the cooler, maybe since I was twenty-two and decided that a thing not written down is a thing they can take from you, and that I would rather keep the wound true than have it taken. I write it small, under his name, the way I write the things I am sure of:

*No.*

And then, because *no* is not enough, because *no* has to be a record and not just a flinch, I write the rest of it. *Will not eat C.F. Will not buy V.C. peace with the one true thing she has. She keeps her girl taken. He goes heavy. His kin will pay — God forgive me, his kin will pay — and Ray gets no help and maybe no proof and the books do not balance and they will not balance and I am leaving this account open on purpose.*

I look at the word *open* for a while. My mother left an account open. I have spent two weeks reading her open account as the most dangerous thing in this house, the smell that worst won't air out, the weight that cannot burn clean. And here I am opening one with my own pen, choosing it, choosing the heavy un-balanced thing over the clean balanced lie, and I do not know yet that I am rehearsing. I do not know it is her I am practicing on. I only know that I would rather leave the books wrong than make them right by deleting a girl who got a star on a paper and called her mama to say so.

—

I burn him at three in the morning, un-eaten, and the retort tells on me, the way it always tells.

I charge him and I bring the Power-Pak up — eighteen hundred, where she runs right, where Carl went up like paper and Verlie went up like paper and Dwight went up like paper, the clean nothing of erasure, the burn that lies. And Cole Ferry does not go up like paper. The temperature, which should hold, does not hold. It drops — not the way a fat body drops it on the first surge, a dip and a climb; it drops and it *stays* down, sixteen hundred, fifteen-forty, and it will not come back, and I stand at the panel feeding her like a sick animal and she will not take the feed, she sulks under the weight of a thing the fire cannot get at, because the fire only takes what is housed, and what is in Cole Ferry was never housed and is not going up the flue with the smoke.

The smoke is clean. I check the stack with a flashlight from the yard, out in the three-in-the-morning cold with the fog already coming down into the hollow, and the smoke is clean, it is white and thin and ordinary, the secondary chamber catching everything the neighbors might call about — the smoke is fine, it is the *weight* that stayed, it did not go up, it came down, it is in the building with me.

And there is a sound.

It is not the dry irregular tick of the blower I do not like; I know that one, it is mechanical, it is gaskets. This is under that. This is a low sound that should not be there, a long held pressing sound like a chord with one note flat in it, like a man leaning his whole weight on a thing that will not open, and it goes on under the roar the way grief goes on under a conversation, not loud, just *there,* not stopping. I have heard a wrong burn before. I heard Tug go down wrong, the wet catching sound of him, a man clearing his throat to speak and never getting the words. Ferry is not that. Ferry is the after of that. Ferry is the sound of a thing that has already given up on speaking and has settled in to *press,* to lean, to wait, and I write it in the cooler notebook because I write everything down — *burn ran cool, temp would not hold, low pressing sound under the blower, anomalous, un-eaten, heavy* — and I do not have a better word than *pressing* and I sit with it.

He takes four hours. The eaten ones go fast; the fire is *eager* for them, the log keeps saying, *eager,* like the fire knows there is nothing in them to fight. There is everything in Cole Ferry to fight. He comes out at last over six pounds, the bone not fully gone to meal because the fire never got hot enough to finish him, a cool burn, a wrong burn, and I run the remains through the processor and label the container and spell his name right, and the smell —

The smell is everywhere now. It is in the carriage house and the back hall and the prep room and up the stairs. It is the struck match held too long, the wet stove, the third thing, and it is not leaving, it is not *leaving* now the way it was on the dock, that was the smell on its way to a door and I shut the door, I shut it on purpose, and now the weight has nowhere to go but the house, and the house has the people in it, and the people are his kin up the branch and a granddaughter who vapes on the steps and a hard tired daughter who called me herself, and somewhere in the next weeks one of them — whoever loved him most, the rule says, and somebody up that branch loved him, even him, even after, because that is the unbearable thing about people — somewhere up that branch a person is going to start carrying a guilt that was never theirs, and start confessing to things they did not do, and the smell will get into their house too, and I did that, I chose that, with a pen, at one in the morning, to keep a dead girl true.

I open every window in the house. The fog comes in off the dead creek and lies in the rooms and the smell sits inside it, patient, and does not lift, and I knew it would not, I have done this once before, with Tug, I know now that opening the windows is not a thing you do to make it leave. It is a thing you do so you can tell yourself you tried.

—

Ray calls at dawn. He has heard — the grapevine, the GoFundMe taken down overnight — that Cole Ferry is dead, and his voice on the phone is a man standing at the edge of a hole he has been digging toward for thirty years and finding it already filled in.

"He's gone, then," Ray says.

"He came in Thursday. Burned him last night."

A silence. I can hear him not asking the thing he wants to ask, which is *did he say anything,* because Ray is a man who knows that the dead do not give statements to funeral women, that confession is not a thing that gets laid down on a steel table in the dark, that I am not the office he needs and there is no office he needs anymore because the only mouth that could have told him is ash in a labeled container on my shelf.

"That's it, then," he says finally, and it is not a question, it is a man closing a drawer he swore to a mother he would never close. "Thirty years. And he dies in his bed up the branch like a Christian and takes it with him." A dry sound that is not a laugh. "I pulled the file again last night. Couldn't sleep. Knew he was dying. The Pruett statement's gone to nothing now, reads like the fella never saw a truck at all. The whole thing's — there's hardly a case left to lose, Nona. I been losing it for weeks and I told myself I was just getting old, and now the one man who could've made it whole is gone, and you know what the worst part is."

"Tell me."

"The worst part is I'm relieved. For about one second this morning when I heard, before I caught myself, I was *relieved,* because it means I can stop. Means there's nothing left to hold. Thirty years holding that file and I felt it go slack in my hands and part of me just — let it." His voice does the thin thing at the edge. "I made Verna Coyle a promise. And the way I'm gonna keep it is by not being able to remember why I made it."

He does not know he is describing the rite. He thinks he is describing a mind going. He is describing a thing I could have stopped and chose not to and a thing I could not have stopped at all, both at once, and I cannot tell which part is which anymore, and I cannot tell him any of it, because the only proof I have is a memory, and a memory is the one thing neither of us trusts now, and he is the one mirror I have left in this whole drowned county and I am not willing to lose him to the truth.

So I say the thing that is true, the kind of true that doesn't cut, the only kind I deal in. "You kept it longer than anybody would have. Thirty years. Nobody held on that long but you."

"Lot of good it did her." And then, quiet, the question under all the questions, the one he cannot know he is asking the one person who can answer it: "You reckon she'll ever get to know? Verna. Before she goes. You reckon there's any way that girl ever gets to be a girl that was *taken,* and not just one that left?"

I stand in my kitchen with the windows open and the fog in the rooms and the smell of an un-housed murder in the walls and a labeled container of a man on my shelf who knew, who *knew,* down to the foot, and I look at the loaf of bread on the steel, gone cold now, the morsel broken off it, the thing I did not eat, and I think: *she already knows. She has always known. She is the only one in this county who never stopped knowing, and the price of her knowing is that she will hurt every day until she dies, and I had the power last night to make it stop hurting, and I would have had to lie to her dead girl to do it, and I would not, and that is either the only honest thing I have done since I came home or it is the cruelest, and I cannot tell which, and I am terrified that I am starting not to be able to tell which.*

"I don't know," I say. Which is a lie. Which is the second cruelest thing I have said to this man, and I have said both of them this month. "I hope so, Ray."

I hang up. The fog does not lift. The smell does not lift. The books do not balance, and they are not going to, and I am the one who left the account open, on purpose, with a pen, and I stand in my mother's kitchen looking at a loaf I baked and did not use, and for the first time it occurs to me, cold, all the way through — the way the floor is there before your foot finds it — that I am getting *good* at the not-eating. That the refusal is starting to feel like the only true thing in the house. And that a woman who has learned that the only mercy left is to withhold mercy is a woman who is being taught something, body by body, for a reason, by a trade that does not do anything without a reason, and that the lesson is aimed at a tray on the far cold wall, where my mother is waiting, heavy, un-eaten, with her own account open, for the daughter the note says will close it.

I count the chairs in the kitchen. There are four. It does not help. It has not helped in a long time, and I still do not know the day it stopped.

# Chapter Fourteen

## Sister Pearl

Cole Ferry has been ash for four days and the house still smells of him.

I have run the carriage-house doors up on their tracks every night since, propped them with the cinder blocks I keep for the purpose, and let the cold October come through the place like a tide, and it does not matter. The smell of a man who burned heavy does not air out. It is not in the air. The air is fine; I have stood in the draft with my face up like a fool and breathed it and the air is clean mountain nothing, woodsmoke and the dead creek and the diesel ghost of the transport van. The smell is in the *house.* It is in the seam of things. Struck match, wet stove, the third thing with no shelf, and now a fourth note under it that is particular to Ferry, a sweetish rot like fruit gone over in a bowl you can't find, and I know that fourth note, I have placed it, it is thirty years old and it is the underside of a strip cut past the old Number Nine, and I let a murderer carry it into my fire on purpose, and so now I live in it.

I am keeping a record of the smell. Of course I am. In the just-mine notebook, dated, a line a day. *Day 3: worst in the back hall, faint in the parlor.* *Day 4: prep room, evenly, all night.* As if measuring a thing makes it a thing you are doing something about. As if the line on the page is a hand on the wound. I have lost the reason I do this — I know I have, I wrote that down too, in another book, the green one, *reason for the logs is gone, keep doing it anyway* — and I do it anyway, in the dark, the hand moving on a faith it can no longer name, and that is its own smell, if a thing like that had a smell, and maybe it does, maybe that is part of what is in the house, the keeper going thin while she measures the going.

So when Loy calls at six in the morning with the news, I am awake, because I am always awake now, and I am at the prep-room desk writing down the smell.

"Pearl Asher passed," Loy says, and there is a thing in his voice I have not heard from him over a body before, not even my mother's, and it takes me a second to name it because I have not had much call to name it lately. It is *grief.* The clean kind. The kind that is only sad, that has nothing rotted in it, that is grieving a thing it loved and not a thing it has to forgive.

"Who's Pearl Asher," I say, because I have been gone nineteen years and come back to a county of strangers wearing the faces of people I half-knew.

"Lord, hon." A long breath down the line. "Sister Pearl. You don't — no, you wouldn't. She come up after you left." He says it like an apology, like the town went and acquired a saint while I wasn't looking and he's sorry I missed it. "She run the Ark. Up the Caudle's Run road, that old Sizemore farmhouse. Took in the ones nobody'd take. The ones come off the dope, the ones the families give up on. She'd of had thirty, forty of 'em through there over the years, and half of 'em's alive that wouldn't be." His voice does the thing again, goes soft and full. "She got my sister's boy clean. Eight years he's clean. Eight years, on account of Pearl Asher took him in when I'd have told you he was a goner." A silence. "She was a good woman, Nona. There's not a soul in this hollow'll tell you different. You won't have to eat a thing off of her. She's clean as a child."

And something in me — God forgive me, I can read it now and I could not read it then — something in me *unclenches* at that. *You won't have to eat a thing off of her.* After Carl and Verlie and Dwight, after Tug burning wrong with that throat-clearing sound for an hour, after Haz and the boy I un-funded out of the world, after Ferry, after standing in my own parlor and denying a begging mother the only peace on earth to keep a dead girl true — after all of it, here is a body I will not have to weigh in the palm and grade and decide about. Here is a clean one. Here is, maybe, the thing I did not know I was starving for: a death I can just *tend.* Bread or no bread, light or heavy, mercy or murder — none of it. Just a good woman gone, and a town that loved her, and me doing the one part of this trade that never cost anybody anything, which is spelling the name right on the way out.

I should have heard it. *Clean as a child.* I have learned by now that the cleanest-seeming are the ones to fear, that the dangerous weight is the quiet kind, the kind that has had a whole life to learn how not to click. Wren told me that her own self, on the loading dock, about the living. I wrote it down. I knew it.

But I wanted the clean one so bad I let myself not know it for two more days.

—

The Ark comes to the home that afternoon, and I have never had mourners like them.

They come up the porch steps in twos and threes all afternoon, young mostly, younger than me, some of them so young it stops my breath, and they are wrecked the way you are wrecked over a mother, and they are *together* the way I have not seen people be together in this county that is mostly people sitting alone in trailers waiting for the methadone window to open. They hold each other in my parlor. They cry and then they laugh, the awful necessary laugh, telling Pearl stories over the folding chairs, and the stories are all the same story, which is *she would not let go of me.*

There is a young man named Dustin, maybe twenty-five, with a neck tattoo half-finished the way they all are around here, and he sits with his big hands hanging between his knees and he tells me, because I am the funeral woman and you tell the funeral woman things, that he was three days into a relapse in a Bottom motel and did not call his mother and did not call his sponsor and called *Pearl,* at four in the morning, and she drove the Caudle's Run road in the dark in her housecoat and sat on the edge of that motel bed and did not say one word about Jesus or about choices, she just held his hand and said *I'm not leaving, so you might as well not either,* and stayed till the sun came up. "Two years Tuesday," Dustin says, and his voice cracks on it, two years Tuesday, and he looks at his hands like he can't believe they're his clean hands, and I think *I could give this man a clean death for her. I could send Pearl up light as the smoke off a candle and let him keep that.* I think it the way you think a kind thought. I do not yet think the other half.

There is a woman named Renae, thirty maybe, with a toddler on her hip and another one's drawing clutched in her free hand, and she tells me Pearl is the reason she has the babies at all, that the state took them and Pearl drove her to every class and every test and every court date for fourteen months and sat in the gallery in her good dress, and the day the judge gave them back Pearl was the first one Renae called, before her own mother, and Pearl just said *I never doubted it, now go get your young'uns.* The toddler on her hip is named Pearl. Of course she is. Half the babies in this hollow under three are named Pearl, I will learn, the way the town used to name them after the mine super and then after the union man and then after nobody, and now after a woman who would not let them drown.

And there is the girl.

She comes late, near dark, when the others have thinned out, and she comes alone, and she is maybe twenty, in a hoodie with the sleeves pulled down over her hands the way Brandy pulls hers, and she stands in the doorway of the parlor like she is not sure she is allowed, and Wren — Wren is here, Wren has been here all afternoon carrying coffee and being good with the wrecked ones in a way that costs her nothing, the way warmth costs her nothing — Wren goes to her and says, "Hey. You can come in. There's chairs," and the girl comes in.

Her name is Kayla.

I do not know yet what Kayla is. I do not know, watching her fold herself into a folding chair at the back with her hands inside her sleeves, that I am looking at the load-bearing point of the whole structure, the keystone of a thing I am about to pull down. I only know she is the quietest one, and the youngest-seeming, and that when Renae goes over and sits by her and takes her hand, Kayla starts to cry the way you cry when somebody has finally come and sat by you, and Renae says, "I know. I know, baby. She loved you the most, you know that? She always said you were her miracle."

"She talked me down," Kayla says, to the floor, to her sleeves. "Off the bridge. You all know that. Front Street bridge. I was up on the rail and she — she come out of nowhere, she was just *there,* and she got up on the rail with me, she's sixty years old and she climbs up on the rail next to me, and she says—" Kayla laughs, a wet ruined laugh. "She says *now we're both being foolish.* That's all she said at first. *Now we're both being foolish.* And then she just talked. About nothing. About her tomatoes. Till I come down." She wipes her face on her sleeve. "That was four years ago. I got a baby now too. I got — " She can't finish it. She doesn't have to. The room is full of people who got a *now* because Pearl Asher would not let them have an ending.

And I stand in the doorway of my own parlor with a coffeepot going cold in my hand, and I look at a room full of people who are alive, vividly, warmly, hilariously alive, alive in a way that fills a dying town's funeral home with so much life that for a minute the smell of Ferry is gone under it, drowned in coffee and crying and the awful holy laughter of the saved — and I think, *this is what she leaves. This is the weight she leaves. Not a sin. A roomful of breathing.* And I want, with everything in me, with a wanting that scares me even as I feel it, to do right by this. To send her up clean. To give all these living people the gift of a saint who went easy. To be, for once, for one body, the thing Loy thinks I am. The thing my mother was, that they all keep telling me, *she done right by folks.* A giver of mercy. Not a murderer. Just once.

Wren catches my eye across the room and tips her head at the kitchen, *come on,* and I go, because I cannot stand in that doorway any longer wanting what I want.

—

"That's the saddest happy room I ever been in," Wren says.

We are on the loading dock with the doors shut behind us against the cold, and she has produced from somewhere two of the gas-station taquitos she lives on, the ones that have been turning on the Marathon roller since the Clinton administration, and she hands me one without asking, the way she does, and I take it without wanting it, the way I do, and we eat them in the cold looking out at the dark where the dead creek is.

"You did good with them," I say. "The mourners. You're easy with people."

"I'm easy with sad people. Happy people I can't do." She chews. "Sad people I get." A beat. "My mom went to the Ark. You know that? Couple years before I come work for you." She says it flat, the way I say things flat, and I understand suddenly where the girl learned it, the flat voice over the unbearable thing, she learned it the same place I did, from carrying a thing too heavy to say soft. "Didn't take, with her. She come home and she didn't take and she's gone now, not — not *gone* gone, she's down in Bluefield, but gone. But Pearl never said a word against her. Most people, your mom can't get clean, they look at you like it's in the blood, like you're next." She wipes her mouth with the back of her hand. "Pearl seen me at the IGA after and she didn't look at me like that. She looked at me like I was just a kid getting groceries." Wren's voice does the thing, the crack she covers fast. "That's the whole of it, ain't it. Getting looked at like you're just a person. She was the only one in this whole county done it for free."

*That's the whole of it.* She says it the way they all say it, the way my mother wrote it on the recipe, *plain is the whole of it,* the way the note says it, *that is the whole of it,* the phrase worn smooth in this county's mouth till it means *this is the bottom, this is the floor, this is the true thing under all the other things.* And the true thing under Pearl Asher, as Wren says it, is that she looked at the unlovable like they were lovable and asked nothing for it.

I want to give that woman a clean death so bad my hands ache for the bread.

"She suffer?" Wren asks. "At the end?"

"Heart, Loy said. In her sleep." Which is the kind thing, and which is even true. "She went easy."

"Good." Wren nods hard, like she's settling an account. "Good. She earned it." She balls up the taquito wrapper and pegs it into the dark. "Somebody oughta go easy around here."

*Somebody oughta go easy around here.* And there it is, the thing she says that finishes arming the gun, except I do not hear it as a gun, I hear it as a benediction, I hear it as permission, I hear my own want said back to me in the mouth of the one person whose warmth I trust. Somebody ought to go easy. After Ferry and Tug and the throat-clearing burn and a begging mother in my parlor — somebody, *one body,* one good body, ought to be allowed to go up light and clean and forgiven, and the whole town ought to get to keep her good.

I do not know that Kayla is sitting in my parlor with a baby at home because Pearl Asher climbed up on a bridge rail. I have not connected it. I will not let myself connect it. The connection is right there — the bridge, *Front Street bridge,* Kayla said it twice — and I am a keeper of records and I connect things for a living and I do not connect this one, because to connect it I would have to read the rest of Pearl, all the way down, the way I have learned to, and I do not want to read the rest of Pearl. I want her clean. I have wanted one of them clean since the night the county brought my mother. So I keep my hands off the knowing the way you keep your tongue off a tooth you suspect is bad, and I tell myself it is respect, letting a good woman keep her privacy, and it is not respect, it is need, and there is no smell for the difference but there ought to be.

—

I read her at midnight, and I read her wrong, because I make myself read her wrong.

She is small on the steel tray, smaller than the size of her in that parlor would suggest, a thick-waisted old woman with a hard worker's hands and a face that even dead has kindness pressed into it, the grooves of a person who smiled at the unsmiled-upon for thirty years. I lay my hands flat on her sternum the way I do now, the way I have learned, to take the weight of her before I decide about the bread.

And there is weight.

I want to be honest about this, because I have built my whole self on being honest about the record, and the record has to show that *I felt it.* There is weight on Pearl Asher. It is not the rot of Ferry, not septic, not leaking — it is deep, and it is still, and it is *old,* a heavy thing sunk so far down and held so long that it has gone quiet the way the worst weights go quiet, the way a stone at the bottom of a creek stops being a thing that fell and becomes part of the bed. My hands find it and my hands know it and the trade in my blood says *here, this one is heavy, this one is carrying.*

And I tell myself it is grief.

I tell myself: she carried thirty, forty broken people. She drove the Caudle's Run road at four in the morning a hundred times. She climbed up on a bridge rail next to a child. A woman who loves the unlovable for a living *accumulates,* she takes on weight that isn't sin, she carries other people's drowning until it furrows her, and what I am feeling under my hands is the residue of all that carrying, the honest exhaustion of a saint, and *that* is a weight I do not have to eat, that is a weight the fire takes clean, that is grief and not guilt and there is no bread for grief.

That is what I tell myself, with my hands flat on her breastbone in the cold prep room at midnight, and the smell in the house is Ferry's and I let myself believe the heaviness under my palms is the same ambient wrongness that has soaked the seams since I burned him, leaking in from the walls, not coming up out of *her.* The diagnostic instrument, the one true thing I have, the smell and the weight that have never once lied to me — I override them. I read the gauge and I do not like the number so I decide the gauge is wet. *Grief,* I write, in the just-mine notebook, at 12:40. *Pearl Asher — heavy, but grief. A carrier's weight. No buried thing. Clean.* And under it, because some part of me is screaming and I have trained that part to scream only on paper: *(confirm.)*

I do not confirm.

I take my hands off her and I do not draw the weight up and read its shape the way I know how, because if I read its shape it will have a shape, and a shape is a *thing,* a day, a face, a sin, and I cannot bear for this one to have a sin. I have decided she is clean. I close her in the cooler at 38 degrees and I go and bake the bread, and even that I tell myself is only courtesy — you lay the bread on every one of them, clean or not, it's the form of the thing, it's respect, it doesn't have to *do* anything — and I am lying, I am baking it to eat, I have already decided to eat it, the deciding happened back in the parlor when Kayla said *off the bridge* and I made myself not hear the architecture of it.

—

I bake it in my mother's kitchen at two in the morning, on the old enameled range, and for the first time since I came home I bake it *gladly.*

This is the thing I will not be able to forgive — one of the things; there is a list now and it is getting long. Every other loaf I have made in this house I made with dread or numbness or appetite-that-shamed-me. The Carl loaf I made not knowing. The Dwight loaf I made like a verdict. The Haz loaf I made with my hands moving without me, baking toward an obscenity. But the Pearl loaf I make *believing in it.* I hold the pilot down with a kitchen match and the burner catches with the *whump* it always gives, and I work the flour and the water quick the way my hands know, *thin as you can, it must be plain, they don't need it good they need it true,* and I think, *this one is true.* This one is the rite as it was meant to be, the rite Loy described to me with his wet kind eyes, the mercy, the taking of a good soul's weight so she can go light. I think the bread is finally going to be what they always told me it was.

I am happy in my mother's kitchen, baking bread to commit a massacre, and I do not know it, and the not-knowing is the worst innocence I will ever have, because it is the last one.

The loaf comes out plain and pale and scorched at the one edge the way it always does, and it smells of nothing, the way it always does, flour and gas and far down the other thing — and tonight even the other thing I read as grief, as the honest weariness of the saint cooling out of her and up into the crumb, a thing I am glad to take, a thing I will be glad to swallow, the first weight in this house I have wanted in my mouth.

I set it on the rack to cool. Across the dark kitchen the cooler hums, where Pearl Asher lies with a drowning sunk in her chest like a stone in a creek bed, going quiet, waiting; and up the Caudle's Run road the Ark stands full of beds with people sleeping in them who are clean because she could not live with what she did; and in a trailer somewhere a girl named Kayla sleeps next to a baby that exists because a sixty-year-old woman climbed onto a bridge rail and talked about her tomatoes; and in the parlor the folding chairs are stacked against the wall, and I do not count them, I am too happy to count, and that should have told me, the not-counting, that should have told me something was wrong, because I count when I am afraid and tonight I am not afraid, tonight I have talked myself all the way out of afraid, and a woman who is not afraid in this house has stopped reading the instruments.

Tomorrow night I will lay this bread on Pearl Asher's breastbone, over the drowning I have decided is grief, and I will say the old words low, and I will eat it gladly, the good one, the clean one, the mercy at last, and I will feel the obscene lightness leave me lighter than it ever has, and I will not understand, until the cascade is already running and the town has finished re-threading around the hole and a girl who was crying in my parlor tonight has been thirty years dead since before she could have been crying — I will not understand, until I am on the floor, that the weight I called grief was the only thing holding a roomful of the living up out of the water.

The bread cools. It is the most beautiful thing I have made since I came home. I sit at the table where my mother sat and I look at it and I am glad, God help me, I am glad, and the smell of Cole Ferry moves through the house behind me like a tide going out, and out there in the dark the saved are sleeping, every one of them, breathing, for one more night.

# Chapter Fifteen

## The Bridge

I lay the bread on Pearl Asher's breastbone at twenty minutes to one, over the place where the weight sits, and for the first time since I came home I do it the way Loy must have always pictured me doing it — gently, the way you'd lay a cloth on a sleeping child's chest, with both hands, with something I have not let into this room before, which is hope.

The bread is the good one. I made it the good one. It sits pale and plain and scorched at the one edge over her sternum, and her sternum is cold now, thirty-eight degrees of cold, and I have learned the count by feel, the count Esther meant when she wrote *you'll know it when the bread goes heavy in the air over him*, and so I do not need the Lord's Prayer slow three times, I have my own clock, I have my hands. I rest the bread and I rest my hands beside it on the cold breast of a saint and I wait for it to draw.

It draws.

I have got good at not reading them all the way down. I learned it off Tug, the discipline, *who does the knowing serve*, and I have used it since like a man keeping his eyes on the road and not the wreck. But you cannot lay your hands on the breast and have the weight come up into the crumb and feel nothing of its shape. Some shape always comes. With Pearl I had decided the shape would be grief. I had written it down. *A carrier's weight. No buried thing. Clean.* I had told myself a woman who loves the drowning for a living takes on the water of it, and what would come up under my hands would be the honest exhaustion of thirty years of pulling people out.

What comes up under my hands is the water.

But it is not her carrying it. It is her in it. It is dark and it is moving and it is closing over a roof, over a windshield, over a small pale shape pressed up against the inside of the glass where the air went last, and there is a bridge above it, the low concrete one on Front Street that I have crossed ten thousand times, and there is a girl up on that bridge thirty years younger than the girl I will become who is Pearl and she is so high she is laughing and she has run a car off the road into the creek that the mine poisoned and she is standing on the bank watching the water take a family the way you'd watch a thing on a screen, far away, not real, and she does not go in after them. She does not go in. She stands on the bank with the dope singing in her and she watches the air leave the glass, and then she walks home, and she never tells, and she carries it down so far for so long that it goes quiet the way a stone at the bottom of a creek bed goes quiet, until thirty years later she is climbing up onto that same rail next to a child and saying *now we're both being foolish* because the only thing she has ever found that makes the watching bearable is to not let it happen to one more.

That is the shape. That is the weight I called grief.

And I am holding it in my two hands over her cold chest, and the part of me that is a keeper of records, the part that has never once in nineteen years let me lie to my own ledger, stands up in me at last and says, very quietly, the thing I would not let it say in the parlor or the kitchen or last night with the loaf cooling on the rack: *Nona. This is the engine. The drowning is the engine of the Ark. Take it out and the Ark stops.*

I hear it. I want to be honest. The record has to show I heard it.

And I eat the bread anyway.

—

I will try to tell it the way it happened, in the order it happened, because the order is the only thing I have left that is mine to keep, and because if I let it go out of order it will be a thing I am protecting myself from, and I have spent my whole life protecting myself, and I am done.

I say the words first. Low, over her, the old worn ones, the naming and the taking and the letting-go, *what lies heavy on thee I take to me, go thou light, the books are balanced, go on now, go on* — and I mean them, God help me, I mean them the way Loy means them, I say them like a blessing over a good woman and not like a verdict and not like a foreclosure, and that is the difference, that is the thing I will not be able to take back: every other one I ate, I ate knowing it was a deletion and steeling myself or, worse, wanting it. This one I eat as a *mercy*. I eat it loving her. The town's saint, the woman who would not let go of anybody, and I lay her down light with love in my mouth.

Then the swallow.

And it is the cleanest it has ever been. I have to say that too. I have braced you, all this long way, for the obscene lightness, the relief so clean it shames me, the one second where I am twenty-two and stepping onto a bus — and this is more than that, this is the most of it I have ever felt, because Pearl is the heaviest good thing I have ever held and the unweighting of her is correspondingly vast, a whole reservoir of held water going out of me at once, and for one breath, one single breath, I feel the way she must have wanted to feel for forty years and never could. I feel *forgiven*. Not the forgiver. The forgiven. The thing lifts off and I am light as the smoke that hasn't started yet, light as a child, light as a girl who never stood on a bank, and it is so good, it is so good, it is so much better than it has ever been that I make a sound, alone in the prep room at one in the morning over a dead woman, I make a sound like relief, like a person setting down the last thing.

And then it starts.

—

My phone is on the steel counter where I always set it, face up, and it lights.

It is nothing. It is a notification, the dim middle-of-the-night kind, the town's grief always running on these little screens now, GoFundMes and casserole sign-ups and the funeral-home Facebook page that Wren talked me into and runs because I won't. It lights and I glance at it the way you glance, and the post that was there yesterday — the one I let Wren make, *In loving memory of Sister Pearl Asher, who never let go*, with the photo of Pearl on the Ark's porch in her housecoat squinting into the sun and seventy comments under it, seventy, a whole hollow's worth of *she saved my brother* and *two years Tuesday* and *the Ark saved my life* — the post is gone.

Not deleted. Gone the way the smell went the night I ate Carl. Gone smooth, as if it was never made. In its place, at the top of the page, is an older post, three days older, plain: *Asher, Pearl L., 63, of Caudle's Mercy, passed at home. No services per family wishes.* Eleven words. No photo. No comments. A quiet sad woman nobody much knew, gone in her sleep, the kind of death this county turns out a dozen of a week and nobody marks.

I stand there and I read it eleven times. I count them. Eleven. The count does not help. It has not helped since Haz and it does not help now and I keep doing it anyway, the way I keep doing all of it, the hand moving on a faith with no root, and I read the eleven words eleven times and the words do not change because the words are right now, the words are true now, this is the only past there is.

I open the phone the rest of the way. I do not want to. I do it the way you put your tongue to the tooth.

The Ark has no page. Search it — *the Ark Caudle's Mercy* — and the county gives me a museum in Pennsylvania and a church van service two states off and nothing, nothing on the Caudle's Run road, no farmhouse full of beds, no thirty saved, no forty, because there was no woman whose guilt could not let her rest, because I just ate the guilt, and a recovery house does not get founded by a quiet sad woman who passed easy in her sleep with no services per family wishes. The Ark needed the drowning. I took the drowning. The Ark unhappened around the hole the same way Della's bruises did, the same way the church clipping rewrote itself in dead ink on the same fold — quietly, completely, in real time, while the bread was still going down.

—

I am going to make myself write the rest of it the way I found it out, one at a time, because they were people, one at a time, and the least I can do, the one thing left that the trade has not taken from me yet, is to not let them be a number.

Dustin.
