The young man with the half-finished neck tattoo who called Pearl at four in the morning from the Bottom motel three days into a relapse and did not call his mother and did not call his sponsor, called Pearl, and she drove the Caudle's Run road in her housecoat and sat on the edge of that bed and said *I'm not leaving so you might as well not either* and stayed till the sun came up. Two years Tuesday. I scroll for him the way you'd scroll for anyone, because the screens hold the dead now, and I find him, I find Dustin, and the page is a memorial. It is eighteen months old. *Rest easy brother. Gone too soon. Found him in the Bottom, that same motel I bet.* He went under in the spring of the year before last because there was no woman to drive the road, no edge of a bed, no hand, and his mother set up a GoFundMe to bring him home and it made four hundred dollars and is still pinned, three towns over, to a corkboard I have a furred-up twin of by my own showroom door, and I will meet his mother on my own table by spring, I think, and I will spell his name right, and I will not be able to tell her that her boy had two years, that there was a Tuesday he was proud of, that he looked at his clean hands like he couldn't believe they were his, because in this world he never had it, in this world he just went under in a motel like they all do and nobody marked it and the marking is gone, I ate the marking, the marking lived in a woman in a housecoat and I put her in my mouth.

Renae. The babies. *The state took them and Pearl drove me to every class and every court date and sat in the gallery in her good dress and the day the judge gave them back Pearl was the first one I called.* The toddler on her hip named Pearl, half the babies in the hollow under three named Pearl. I make myself look. Renae is in Bluefield, the same county records that lose my mother's decade keep this one fine, and there is a line on a page that says the children were placed in 2019, permanent, and there is no first phone call to a woman in a good dress, there is no Pearl on her hip because you do not name a baby after a woman who was never in the gallery, you name her after nobody, the way this town has always named them after nobody, and Renae has the babies still, somewhere, but she does not have them *back*, she has them *taken*, and there is a difference, I have learned this week there is a difference, *somebody took my girl, she didn't leave me on purpose* — there is a whole world of difference between a child given back to you and a child you watched a stranger carry out the door, and I made one into the other with a piece of bread.

And the girl.

—

Kayla.

She came late, near dark, alone, sleeves pulled down over her hands the way Brandy pulls hers, and she stood in the doorway of my parlor like she was not sure she was allowed, and Wren went and got her, *hey, you can come in, there's chairs*. She is maybe twenty. She has a baby. She said *off the bridge*, she said it twice, *Front Street bridge*, *I was up on the rail and she come out of nowhere and she got up on the rail with me, she's sixty years old and she climbs up on the rail next to me* — and I stood in my own parlor and heard her say it and would not connect it, kept my tongue off the tooth, because I wanted Pearl clean and you cannot want a thing clean and also let yourself know that the woman climbed onto the rail she once stood on the bank below, watching, while a family went under.

I connect it now. There is nothing left to want. I connect it now and I look for Kayla on the little screen the way I looked for the others, and I want you to understand that my hands are steady doing it, I have a steady hand, it is the one thing of my mother's I will cop to, my hand is perfectly steady on the glass.

There is a Kayla. The age is wrong. The age is so wrong that for a second I think it is a different girl, a cousin, an aunt, the county runs out of names and hands them down. But it is her face, younger, a school photo, the marbled blue behind her, and the date under it is not last week. The date under it is 1996. She went off the Front Street bridge in the fall of 1996 because there was no woman to come out of nowhere, no sixty-year-old to climb the rail, no *now we're both being foolish*, no tomatoes, nobody, because the woman who would have done that did not become the woman who would have done that, because the woman who would have done that was built out of a drowning and I ate the drowning two hours ago, and so the girl who was alive in my parlor last night, who took my hand, who had a baby at home, who said *I got a now*, the girl Renae sat down beside and held and said *she loved you the most, you were her miracle* —

She has been thirty years dead since before she could have been born into the now she had.

There is no baby. There was never a baby. There is a school photo and a date in 1996 and a creek that the mine already poisoned taking one more thing it was owed, and there is me, in a cold prep room, the only person in the turning world who remembers that last night she was warm and crying because somebody finally came and sat by her.

—

This is where I lose it.

I have been telling you in order, in sentences, the way I keep the books, and I cannot anymore, the books will not hold this, there is no column for it, sin on the left and price on the right and what do you put, what is the left-hand entry, *Pearl Asher, the drowning, a family in the dark water* — and what is the right, what is the price, weight for weight, the right column is *a town*, the right column is *Dustin Kayla Renae's babies and the thirty more and the forty more whose names I never got because they came and went over years she gave them, all of them, every one, alive yesterday and un-alive now and not even un-alive, never-alive, never-saved, never-pulled-back, gone so smooth that grieving them is a thing only I can do and a thing I cannot even do right because you cannot stand at a grave that was never dug* —

I try to think of how many. That is the reflex, that is the wall, I have stood over the worst things in this trade and counted them and the counting held the floor. Folding chairs, pipe joints, the steps to the cooler, grams, minutes, teeth in a jaw. I try to count the dead. I try to count how many Pearl saved so I can know how many I un-saved, so I can write it down, so it will be a number and a number is a thing the world is not allowed to quietly change its mind about —

and I cannot.

I cannot count them because the count is gone with them. There is no roll. There is no page. The forty are not forty, they are not anything, they are a feeling I have with no event under it, the worst kind, the kind my mother's whole ledger is full of, *forgot her brother's face*, *could not after recall her own wedding*, a hole shaped like the people who fell into it, and I am on my knees, I notice I am on my knees, I have come down off the stool and I am on the floor of the prep room with my back against the cabinet under the sink where the fireproof box is bolted, where the ledger is, a century of dead women's holes, and I have my hands over my face and I take them down and look at them, my steady hands, the one thing of hers, and they are not steady. They are not steady at all.

I am not going to dress this up. I have dressed up every hard thing I have ever told you. I told you my mother's body weighed nothing in a way that was a joke about gravity so it would not be a thing about my mother. I told you the fat ones go faster. I have made the worst things in this trade a little funny because the alternative is this, is the floor of the prep room, is the sound I am making, and there is no joke in me now, I reach for one out of nineteen years of habit and there is nothing there, the way you reach for a step that isn't there in the dark and your whole body drops, I reach for the dry thing and the floor is gone and I am just a woman on the floor of her dead mother's house making a sound she does not recognize because she has not made it since she was twenty-two, and what I am saying, what I hear myself saying, into my own ruined hands, over and over, to a dead saint and a drowned town and a girl who is not dead anymore, is not *sorry*, I cannot say sorry, I have not said sorry since I was twenty-two and even this, even on the floor, even now, I cannot — what I am saying is *no*, just *no*, *no, no, no*, like Kayla on the rail, like a child, like a woman who watched the air go out of the glass and could not go in.

—

The retort takes her at a quarter to three.

I do not remember most of the time between. I have a hole there too now, but it is an ordinary one, the kind grief leaves, the kind everyone is allowed. I am at the panel and the Power-Pak is up and I am putting Pearl Asher into the fire and I do not remember walking out to the carriage house or sliding her in, and I stand at the controls with the eleven pipe joints over the door and I do not count them, I am past counting, I just watch the temperature.

And she goes up like paper.

I knew she would. I have learned the sound of it, the eaten ones, the way they burn light and eager and odorless, the temperature barely dipping before it climbs, the blower-tick the only hard sound in the whole easy ascent of a body with nothing left to resist the fire. Carl went up like that. The monster Dwight went up like that, three days of October rot in a trailer going clean as a held breath let go. Light. Odorless. Paper.

And here is the thing I did not know a body could do to me, the thing that breaks the last of it: Pearl goes up the lightest of all of them. The cleanest. The most odorless burn I have ever run. A saint goes into my fire and there is no smell, none, not the struck match, not the wet stove, not the third thing with no shelf, not even the ordinary smell of an ordinary burn — there is the clean nothing of a woman with nothing in her, because I took the only heavy thing she had, and she rises out of the chamber of that old machine into the October dark over Caudle's Mercy like the most forgiven thing that ever lived, like smoke off a candle, like a child's breath on a cold morning, and somewhere under her, down the Caudle's Run road, there is no house full of beds, and three towns over a boy went under in a motel, and in a county record a girl's date is 1996, and the lightness of this burn, the holy odorless ease of it, is the lightest thing in the world sitting on top of the heaviest, and that is the obscenity, that is the whole obscenity of the trade in one breath of clean smoke: *the cleaner the burn, the more it has erased.* The peace on a body is the measure of the murder. Pearl Asher is the most peaceful thing I have ever burned and she is rising off the bodies of everyone she ever saved, light, light, unbearably light, and no one in the sleeping hollow will ever smell the wrongness of it but me, because I am the only one left who knows there is anything to smell.

—

I am at the bridge by the time it goes gray.

I did not decide to come here. The processing is done, Pearl is in a temporary container with her name spelled right because I always spell it right, four pounds and a few ounces of a saint, lighter than Carl even, and the home is behind me with its forty-year smell and its open accounts, and I am standing on the Front Street bridge in the cold with the fog still in the hollow the way it sits till noon, and below me the creek the mine poisoned is moving, low and brown and patient, the way it has always moved, the way it moved the night a family went under it and a girl stood on the bank, the way it moved the night Kayla came up onto this rail with nobody to climb up beside her.

I put my hands on the rail. The same rail. Cold under my palms, wet with the fog.

I have read every body that has come through my fire and I have read the dead on the living and I read this rail now without meaning to, the way you can't help it once your hands have learned, and there is weight here, there is so much weight here, this little concrete bridge over a dead creek on a dying street, and none of it is housed, none of it was ever eaten, because the people it belongs to are not even dead, they are *never*, and the never has nowhere to go, and so it sits here in the rail under my hands, the un-housed weight of a town I un-saved, and it does not smell, it does not have a body, it is just cold and patient and mine to carry because I am the only one who knows it is here.

The books must balance. My mother said it. The books are balanced, I say it, over every one of them, *go thou light, the books are balanced, go on now, go on.* I have been balancing the books all this time. Pearl on the left, a town on the right. Carl on the left, a woman's whole survived life on the right. The monster on the left, his children's hard-won truth that they lived through him on the right. And every line of it closes, every account, the columns meet at the bottom, the books balance perfectly, they have never once not balanced —

and I understand, finally, on a wet rail at dawn over the water that took the family that built the saint that I just unmade, that *balance was never justice.* Balance was the murder. An account closes when the wrong is gone and the wronged are gone with it. My mother's whole century of balanced books is a century of holes that the world re-knit so smooth that only the women who dug them ever knew, and the women who dug them paid in their own remembering, page by page, brother's face by wedding day by the color of the river, until they did not even know what they had buried, until they were as empty as the people they'd erased, until they were my mother, going thin in a cold house, *checking that it balanced*, and the only honest ledger, I see it now, the only honest ledger is one that does *not* balance, one with the wrong on the left and nothing on the right, an open account, a debt that will never be collected, the truth that the thing happened and the people lived and were wronged and were real, kept on the page against the world's endless quiet mercy of forgetting —

and that, *that*, an open account that ruins the keeper and saves nothing and balances never, is the only thing left in the world that is not a lie. My mother left one open. The most dangerous thing in the book. I called it her last cruelty. I am standing on a bridge at dawn over my own massacre and I am beginning, God help me, to understand that an open account might be the only mercy she ever showed anybody, including me, and I do not want to understand it, because the next thing I will have to understand is what she left open, and why, and whose name is at the top of the only account in a hundred years that nobody balanced.

—

I should be cold. I am standing on a bridge in October at dawn with the fog wet on me and I have been here long enough that I should be cold, and I am, my hands are cold on the rail, but there is a different cold I am reaching for and not finding, and it takes me a while to notice the reaching, because I have got so used to the reaching coming up empty.

There was a night. I am sure there was a night. I am trying to find it because I am standing on a bridge over water with my hands on a rail and something in me went looking for it the way a tongue goes to a tooth, *you have done this before, you have stood at water and not let a thing go under* — and I reach for it and —

I reach for it and there is a smooth-walled place where it should be, the exact size and shape of a night.

I know its shape. That is the cruelty, that is always the cruelty, I can feel the *socket* of it. There was a girl, or a woman, younger than me or my age, and there was water, dark water, moving, and there was a rail or a bank or a ledge, and I was there, and she was going to go in, or she was in, and I — I did the thing. I did the thing Pearl did. I was the one who came out of nowhere. I climbed up beside her, or I went in after, or I just talked, about nothing, about tomatoes, about anything, until she came back over the rail, and she lived, somebody lived because I did not stand on the bank and watch, once, one time, when I was young, before I was this, I was a person who did not let someone go under —

and I cannot find it.

I can find the shape of it and not the night. I have the rail and not the girl. I have the water and not her name. I have the fact that I once, before all of this, did the one good thing that is the opposite of everything this trade has made me, the single piece of evidence in my whole life that I am not only a woman who balances books over the drowned — and it is gone, it is the price, weight for weight, like for like, the rite is cruel and it balances cruelly, it took a drowning-and-a-saving out of the world and it took my own saving out of me to pay for it, the one night I was Pearl, the one night I climbed the rail instead of watching from the bank, and now I am the woman on the bridge with no proof she ever did anything but watch.

I stand on the Front Street bridge with my steady hands on the cold rail over the patient water, and I cannot remember the only time I ever saved anyone, and below me the creek moves the way it always moves, and the fog will not lift till noon, and there is no smell, anywhere, of anything, the whole hollow light and clean and emptied and falsely fine, and I am the only one awake in it who knows that yesterday it was heavier and truer and full of the saved, and I do not have a single word in me — not the dry one, not the count, not the joke, and not the other one, never the other one — I do not have a single word in me for what I have done.

# Chapter Sixteen

## Unmaking

The town is fine.

That is the part I cannot get past, the three days after. I walk Front Street to the Dollar General for coffee filters because there is no coffee at a funeral home that is also a house, there never is, and the street is fine. Sheena is at the methadone window with her hood up, and the Marathon has a hand-lettered sign for two-for-five taquitos, and somebody's grandkids are riding the curb on bikes too big for them in the cold, and the fog is doing what it does, sitting in the hollow like it has nowhere it would rather be, and every single person I pass is lighter than they were on Tuesday by a weight none of them can feel.

Lighter. That is the only word and it is wrong, because lighter sounds like a mercy. The street is *thinner*. There are people not standing in it. There is a woman who should be coming out of the IGA that closed who never got the years to come out of it because she went under in a motel a year ago instead of two years from now, and there is a girl who should be pushing a stroller up the broken sidewalk who has been gone since 1996, and the street has re-knit around the holes the way skin closes over a splinter, smooth, no scar, and the only place in the world where any of them still leave a mark is behind my own eyes, which is to say nowhere, which is to say a place that will be empty too the day I forget, and I am forgetting, I am the leakiest vessel in the county.

I buy the filters. The girl at the register is new, or she is not new and I have lost her, I can no longer tell the difference between a thing I never knew and a thing the bread took, and that not-being-able-to-tell is the whole disease in one feeling. She says have a good one. I do not have a word for her. I have run out of the words you hand across a counter, the way you run out of anything you spend and don't replace.

—

I want to say it plain, because I have spent fifty pages saying everything sideways and the sideways is over.

I am not carrying anything.

I never was. There is no sack on my back, no stone in my chest, no weight I am hauling to my own grave the way Loy said, the way his aunt said, the way some Pruitt woman in Wales who is a column of ink told her daughter, who told her daughter, back and back until it reached me in a warm kitchen as the kindest sentence I had ever heard. *You take it into yourself, hon. You carry it. That's the mercy.* It is not the mercy. It is not even true. I have not taken a single thing into myself in this entire trade. I have *taken things out of the world,* and I have paid for the taking out of myself, and those are the only two motions there have ever been, subtraction on the left and subtraction on the right, and somebody, generations ago, looked at what this actually was and could not bear it and built a lie soft enough to live inside, and the lie was *carrying,* and the lie is the only reason any of us could keep doing it.

My mother knew.

I have to sit with that one. I sit with it on the back steps in the cold with the filters in the bag beside me. My mother stood at the box at night reading the books, and she told Loy she was *checking that it balanced,* and Loy heard a tidy old woman doing her sums, and I heard, when he told me, a curse. But she was not checking her sums. She was reading a hundred years of women who emptied themselves to empty the world, brother's face by wedding day by the color of the river, and she knew what she was looking at, she knew it was a list of murders dressed as a list of mercies, and she did it anyway for forty years, and then she had a daughter and she let that daughter believe the soft lie too, let me think I was being asked to be a sponge and not a knife, because the truth — *you will delete people, and you will delete yourself, and you will be the only one left who knows they were ever here* — is not a thing you can hand a child you want to stay.

She did not hand it to me. She handed me *carrying.* And I have spent three days deciding whether that was the last cruelty or the first kindness, and I cannot decide, and I am beginning to understand that *not being able to decide* is going to be the shape of the rest of this.

—

I do the thing I know how to do. It is the only thing I know how to do. I count the damage.

I bring the fourth book down from my room, the green one, the one I started when I understood I was leaking — *what I think I had* on the left, *what is there now* on the right, a ledger of myself, an autopsy I am performing on a body I am still inside. And I bring up the home's records, the intake folders, the cooler notebook, my own hand going back, and I sit at the prep-room desk under the one good light at three in the morning where I sat with my mother's thin file on the first night, and I try to make a true accounting of what I have done.

I cannot.

That is the whole horror of it and I keep walking into it like a glass door. You cannot count what isn't there. I write *Pearl Asher* on the left of a fresh page and I go to write the right, the price, weight for weight, and the right is *a town,* and I do not have the town, I have a feeling shaped like a town with no names in it, because the names went down with the people and the people went down with the drowning and the drowning went down my own throat with a piece of bread I baked tender, with love in my mouth. Dustin. I have his name only because I wrote it on the back of my own hand in the prep room that night like a woman tying a string on her finger, and it has half washed off, and when it is gone I will have nothing, there will be no Dustin, there will only be a memorial three towns over that has always been there. Renae's babies, who were given back and are now only taken. Kayla. *Kayla,* who I have written eleven times in this green book in eleven different places like a woman who has lost a child in a crowd and keeps calling the same name into the same indifferent noise, and the noise does not answer, because the crowd insists there was never a child.

I go back further, because I am a keeper of records and the records are all I have, and I make myself read my own ledger of myself, the column of holes I have already dug in me. A candy I can name and not taste. The smell of my mother's kitchen — and I sit there a long minute over that one, *the smell of my mother's kitchen,* and I reach for it the way you reach for a step in the dark, and there is the smooth-walled place where it should be, and I have to write, on the right, the same thing I wrote before, which is nothing, which is *blank,* because you cannot record the smell of a thing the record can no longer smell. The reason I keep these books by hand — gone, the root pulled, the hand still moving, the hand moving right now across this page on a faith with no god under it. And I understand, finally, fully, at the desk, with the one good light on the holes, what the right-hand column of my mother's whole life was. It was not prices. It was *her.* Page after page of her, spent. She did not balance the books. She *was* the balance. She paid down the town's account in installments of herself until there was not enough of her left to be a mother with, and her coldness, the cold I have hated for nineteen years, the cold I crossed the mountains to get away from — that cold was not a thing she was. It was a thing that had been *taken out of her,* one warm memory at a time, to make the town lighter, and I called it rejection, and it was amputation, and she could not even tell me, because the telling was one of the things she'd spent.

I put my head down on the open ledger. I do not cry. I am past the crying, I think, I did the crying on the floor under the sink, and now there is just the cold clear understanding, which is worse, which is the thing the crying was protecting me from.

—

Wren comes at four.

She has a key. She has had a key since before I came home; she was the one keeping the place from rotting while Esther emptied out, and I have never asked for it back and I am not going to. I hear the side door, and her boots, and she does not call out — she has learned the house has a body in it and you don't shout in it — and she comes into the prep room and she stops in the doorway and she looks at me, the green book and the spread-out folders and my face, which I have not seen but which must be a thing, because Wren is nineteen and feral and unafraid of the dead and she has never once looked at me the way she looks at me right now.

She does not ask what's wrong. That is the first mercy. She does not say are you okay, because she can see I am not, and she does not say what happened, because she has learned in this house that some of what happens does not have words you can hand across a counter either.

What she does is hold up a gas station bag.

"Got the last two taquitos," she says. "Guy was about to throw 'em in the trash. They been on the roller since like noon, so they're basically jerky." She comes in and sets the bag on the steel counter, on the cold steel where the bread cools, where the bodies lie, and she hops up beside it the way she does, boots swinging, and she pulls one out and looks at it. "This one's got a corner that's just, like, fully carbon. You want the carbon one or the merely-burnt one."

"The carbon one," I say. My voice comes out wrecked and small and she does not flinch at it, she just nods like that's the right call, and she hands me the worse taquito, and she takes the merely-burnt one, and we sit in the prep room at four in the morning and eat gas station food that is mostly char, next to the cooler, and she does not make me talk.

"Dakota texted," she says, around a mouthful, because she knows, she knows that the only thing that will reach me right now is something with no weight in it at all, something stupid and alive and ongoing, the regular dumb churning of a person who still gets to have a future. "Three a.m. He wrote — hold on." She pulls out her phone. "He wrote, *u up.* Just that. *U up.* I'm like, it's three in the morning at a funeral home, where do you think I'm at, the club?" She shakes her head. "I'm not even gonna answer it. I'm leaving him on delivered. Watch, that'll be the thing that kills him, not the Cavalier, not the neck tattoo, gettin' left on delivered."

I make a sound. It is not a laugh. It is the ghost of where a laugh used to live, the reach for the dry thing, and there is more there than there was on the bridge, not much, but more, because she is here, because she brought me jerky and Dakota and the regular ruin of a regular girl, and for the length of a bad taquito I am a person sitting in a room with another person and not the last witness of a massacre in a sleeping town.

"You don't gotta tell me," she says, after a while, quieter, looking at the carbon corner of nothing in her hand and not at me. "Whatever it is. I'm not — I'm not good at the words, like the saying-things. But I can just be here. I'm real good at just being here. I been just being here my whole life." She shrugs, like it's nothing, like it isn't the only thing keeping me on this side of the floor. "So. I'm here."

I am here, she said. I am good at just being here. Nineteen years old and she has named, without knowing it, the exact thing the trade is the opposite of — the trade does not stay, the trade *leaves,* the trade is a machine for making people not be here, smoothly, so no one has to grieve — and here is a girl with a dead phone and a worthless boyfriend and a tuition jar her aunt is feeding to scratch-offs, and she is the most present thing in the county, she is *here,* and I want to put my arms around her and I do not, because I do not do that, but I let her stay, I let her stay in the dark with me till the window goes gray, and that is the most I have ever let anyone do.

There is a door in this house. I have felt it before. It swings on the thought *the line takes by the eating and not the blood,* it swings on *& Daughters could be true at last,* and it swung open hard once, in this very room, and I shut it so fast my teeth clicked. It does not swing tonight. I want to be honest. It does not even creak. Whatever else is gone out of me, the part that knows *not her, whatever it costs, not her* is still bolted shut and I do not have to slam it, because I am sitting next to the reason it can never open, and she is eating jerky, and she is leaving Dakota on delivered, and she is here.

—

Ray comes the next afternoon, and Ray is not fine.

I see it before he is up the porch steps. I have got to where I can read the dead on the living, the un-housed weight coming off a person who is carrying a thing the world won't take, and Ray is not carrying a buried sin, Ray's weight is a different kind, it is the weight of a man whose ground is going out from under him and who cannot find the edge of the hole. He has the manila folder. He always has the manila folder. But he is holding it the way you hold a thing you no longer believe is yours.

He sits at the kitchen table without being asked, which is his right now, we have got there, and he sets the folder down and he does not open it, and he looks at his own hands on top of it for a while.

"It's gone," he says.

I don't say anything. I have learned, from him, that you let a man get to the thing.

"Dana Coyle." He says the name like it costs him. "Thirty years. I could've told you that file blind, every page, who said what and when and which of 'em was lying and which was just scared. The Pruett statement. The boy at the Marathon. I had a *shape,* Nona. I knew what happened to that girl even if I couldn't prove it, I knew it in my hands, and now—" He opens the folder. I make myself look. There are pages in it and the pages are thinner than they were, I can see it, the language gone soft, *might have seen a truck, couldn't be sure, didn't get a look,* a case dissolving into fog in a man's own handwriting. "Now I read it and there's nothing there. There's no shape. I sat in my truck this morning and I could not remember the *road.* The road I drove a hundred times, the road I walked with the dogs that first week, and I'm looking at the map, and it's just lines, it's just a place I've never been."

I am the road. I want to say it so bad it is a physical thing in my throat. *Ray. You're not losing it. The road is gone because the girl is going, because the man who put her in the ground is dying and the thing he did is leaking toward my fire, and I have been eating men like him for weeks and every one I eat takes a piece of your case down with it, and the only reason your hands feel empty is that the truth has been edited out from under you and you are the second-most-alive record of it and I am the first.* I have it all, right there, the whole confession, the only confession that would make a man losing his mind not be losing his mind.

"I think I'm losing my mind," he says.

And he says it so quietly, this big patient man who has spent thirty years on interviews where words mattered, who chooses every word because he learned the hard way what a word can cost, he says it like a man confessing a sin of his own, and I look at him and I am looking in a mirror, I am looking at the only other person in this whole turning world who knows what it is to be a keeper of records watching the record go, to distrust the one instrument you built your whole self out of, to wake up not sure if a thing you remember is a thing that was, and the loneliness of it is the exact loneliness I have been carrying alone since the first night, since the thin file, and here it is sitting across my mother's kitchen table holding a folder full of fog.

"You're not," I say.

It is true and it is the cruelest thing I have, because the next sentence, the one that would let him keep his mind, is the one I cannot give him, because to give it to him is to make him carry it, to put the un-housed truth of an erased girl into a man who already cannot hold the truth he's got, and because some animal part of me, the part that ate the vote, is afraid of what he would do with it, and because — and this is the worst part, the part I will have to live with — because there is a way out of this that I can feel in my hands, the way I felt the bread before I ever knew I knew it. I could *take it off him.* Not the case. Him. His knowing, his grief, his thirty years on a road he can't find — I could lay the bread and say the words over the right body someday and make Ray Sennett a man who never had a cold case, never made a promise to a mother, never spent his life refusing to let go, and he would be lighter, and he would be emptier, and he would be falsely, gently fine, and he would never look at me across a table again the way he is looking at me now, which is the way you look at the one other person who is awake.

I do not say it. Either thing. I sit with him in the kitchen and I let him not lose his mind out loud, and I almost tell him, I get so close that my mouth opens, and what comes out is, "You want coffee," and he says, "I'd take a coffee," and I make the coffee, and we are two people who remember at a table in a house that smells of nothing, and it is the closest I have come to being known since I was twenty-two, and I would burn the building down before I would erase it.

—

After he goes I stand in the kitchen a long time.

I can never do this again. I know that now, the way Ray knew the shape of his case, in my hands. I have eaten a saint and unmade a town and I felt myself get lighter doing it, I felt the relief and made a sound, and there is no version of me that can stand at the bread again knowing what the swallow is and call it mercy. Never again.

And I cannot stop.

Because there is a woman in the cooler, thirty-eight degrees and the heaviest thing in this house, and her account is open, the only open account in a hundred years, and the county's clock is running, and if I burn her un-eaten she will wake heavy and the weight will go looking for the only living kin the way Tug's went for Carla, and the only living kin is me, and the only person who could eat her is me, and the line is one, and *& Daughters* is a joke two generations old, and there is no exit, my mother wrote *no exit* into the architecture of this when she had me, *the daughter eats the mother, that is the whole of it,* and I called it a curse and I am no longer sure.

Never again and I cannot stop. Both. At once. That is where I live now.

And there is one more thing, and it has been standing at the edge of me for three days the way the smell stood in the back hall on the first night, patient, waiting for the house to go quiet enough, and now the house is quiet enough. I have been eating myself this whole time. I know that. Candy and kitchen and the root of my own hands. But it is older than that. There was a night I cannot find, the night I climbed the rail beside someone, the one good thing, gone now to pay for Pearl. And before that, further back, all the way back, there is the oldest hole of all, the one I was born next to and stopped seeing the way you stop seeing the air — there is no father in this house. There is no photograph, no story, no grief, no torn-out blank where one was. Just a smooth-walled place the exact size and shape of a man, and a daughter who grew up beside it and built her whole self into a wall against forgetting without ever once asking *why does a child need a wall that high, and what is on the other side of it, and who built it, and what did it cost them.*

I have spent my life keeping records so the world could not change its mind about a thing.

I am only now beginning to wonder what was already changed before I learned to write, and whose steady hand did it, and whether the reason I cannot forgive her — the reason I crossed the mountains, the reason I will not eat her, the one thing I came home certain of — is itself a thing made out of the same stuff the bread takes, thinning under me even as I lean my whole weight on it.

I do not write that down. For once in my life I do not write it down. I am too afraid of what the page would do with it, and I am too afraid it is already half gone, and I stand in my mother's kitchen that smells of nothing at all and I cannot remember why I hated her, only that I did, only that the hating was the last solid floor I had, and I put my hand flat on the cold counter where the bread cools and I hold on to a feeling I can no longer find the bottom of, like a woman in the dark putting her hand to a tooth that is no longer there.

# Chapter Seventeen

## No Sin at All

Since Pearl I read them at the door now. I cannot help it. The cot comes off the van and I am already reaching with the part of me that is not hands, the part I would give anything to give back, laying it on the sheet the way you lay a palm on a stove to learn if it is hot, and I read the weight before I ever read the name, because I have to know what I am being asked to murder before I have to look at its face.

Most of them are light. People forget that. Most of the dead in this county carry nothing you'd cross a room to take — a mean word said to a sister in 1971, a dog put down too soon, the ordinary freight of having been a person, which the fire takes clean and asks no bread for. Those are the easy nights now. Those are the only easy nights I have left, the ones where I get to be nothing worse than a woman who burns the dead for forty dollars over cost and spells their names right.

Harlan Voss is not light.

I know it before the cot is all the way down. I know it the way you know a storm is in the next county, a pressure in the teeth, a wrongness the body clocks before the mind gets the bulletin, and the smell comes with it — the struck match a half-beat after it dies, the wet-stove iron, the third thing I have never had a shelf for — and it comes up off this little dead man so thick that the county driver, who feels nothing, who will never feel anything, wrinkles his nose and says, "He's been a minute," meaning dead a while, meaning the apartment was hot, and I let him think that, because *he's been a minute* is a thing you can hand across a counter, and the truth is not.

The truth is I am standing at my own side door at one in the morning bracing for another atrocity.

That is what weight has meant since Pearl. Weight means a thing buried, a thing done in a minute thirty years ago that a soul has been dying of ever since, a thing that, if I eat it, will reach back down the years and unmake whatever good or grief or *person* was standing on it. I have got to where I flinch from the heavy ones. I, who used to grade them in the palm like fruit and pick the ones light enough to leave — I now meet a heavy body with my whole back gone cold, because I know what heavy costs the world, and I know what it costs me, and I am almost out of both.

So I take Harlan in, and I do the paper, *VOSS, HARLAN T.*, time 1:14, weight a hundred and forty-nine pounds, a man gone small the way they go, and I wheel him to the cooler and I shut the door on the smell and it does not help, the smell is the kind that is in the room because it is in the *world* now, leaking out of a thing that has not been housed, and I go and I bake the bread, because that is what I do, my hands do it without me, and I tell myself I am only getting ready, I have not decided, I read first, I always read first now.

I lay the bread on his breastbone at three. Where the weight sits.

And I put my hands flat on either side of it, and I let it draw, and I read Harlan Voss all the way down to the floor of him, braced for the black thing —

and there is no black thing.

—

There is a kitchen.

That is the first of it, the first thing the bread brings up through the crumb and into the cold knowing of me, and it is so unlike Dwight, so unlike Ferry, so unlike the drowned stairwell of Pearl, that for a second I think I have read it wrong, I think I have lost the knack the way I am losing everything else. A kitchen, a long time ago, two young men, one of them Harlan with his hair still on and the other one laughing at something on the radio, and their hands not touching, their hands very deliberately not touching, two inches of formica between two hands that wanted across it more than I have ever wanted anything in my life, and the wanting is the weight. The not-touching is the weight. Sixty years of two inches.

His name comes up the way the worst names come up, last, after everything, like a thing kept at the bottom of a drawer under the church bulletins. *Emmett.*

I take my hands off the bread.

I have read a lot of buried things on this table. I have read a man who hurt children patiently for thirty years and I have read a woman who let a family drown and built a kingdom on the bottom of the river, and I know the texture of a sin the way I know the sound of my own furnace, the cold oil-slick of it, the way it tries to slide out from under your hands. This is not that. This has no oil in it. This is the cleanest thing I have ever read off a corpse and it is sitting in him at the weight of a killing, it is bowing the breastbone like a stone, because Harlan Voss carried his one good thing for seventy-four years as if it were the worst thing a man could do, because somebody told him so, because everybody told him so, because the God they handed him in the little block church up the creek had a special shelf for exactly this and Harlan put himself on it at nineteen and never once got down.

There is no victim here. I keep waiting for the victim. That is how the rite works, you read down through the weight and you find the wronged, the wife with the smooth wrist, the foreman who died wearing another man's name, the girl in the strip cut, and I read down through Harlan and there is *nobody at the bottom but Harlan,* nobody he hurt, nobody he took, nothing he stole but his own sixty years, given freely to a shame that was never his to owe.

The smell is still coming. I had thought, all this time, that the smell was sin — the reek of a thing that should have been confessed and burned and was not. I stand in the prep room at three in the morning over a little dead man and I understand, finally, that the smell is not sin. The smell is *weight.* The rite cannot smell the difference between a thing that should never have happened and a thing that should never have been called wrong. It smells the not-housed. It smells the carried. And love carried where it can't be set down smells exactly, to the one person in the county who can smell it, like a struck match held too long — like grief with a body — like the inside of a wet stove.

I have a piece of bread on my counter that will take this off him.

I look at it. I am ashamed of how long I look at it.

—

Because here is the new shape of it, the shape I have not had before, and it is worse than Pearl in a way I can't yet hold all the way around.

If I eat it, the shame goes. Of course the shame goes; that is what the bread does, it takes the weight, and the weight here is the shame and the love together, fused, sixty years in one stone, and the rite cannot pick them apart any more than you can pick the heat out of a coal. So the shame goes — and the love goes with it. Harlan Voss burns light and clean and odorless and goes on, and somewhere in the new past a man named Emmett was just a fellow Harlan knew, a neighbor, a name on a Christmas card, and the kitchen with the two inches of formica never happened, the wanting never happened, the whole long ache of a life *never happened,* and Harlan dies having loved no one, and the town is right after all, the town was always right, there was nothing here, *see, hon, nothing here, he kept to himself.*

I could give a dying man a death with no shame in it. I could also give him a life with no love in it. They are, it turns out, the same gift. They have always been the same gift; I am only now standing close enough to read the card.

That is what the bread does to a sin, and it would do it to a sacrament, and it cannot tell which one is on the table. It only knows the breastbone is heavy. It does not ask why.

—

Emmett comes the next afternoon, and the town does to him exactly what the town does.

They are kind. I want that on the record, because it is true and because the truth is the only thing I have left to keep. They are kind the way mountain people are kind, which is with food and with showing up, and the back parlor has covered dishes lining the sideboard, a ham, two slaws, somebody's chess pie under foil, and the folding chairs are mostly full because Harlan was the man who plowed the church lot for free and drove the old ones to the clinic and you come to that man's funeral, you come whether you understood him or not. Niece up front. A nephew. The preacher from the new church, the one that didn't close, saying *a private man, a faithful man, a man who kept his own counsel,* which is the kindest set of words this town has for it, which is the words they use so they won't have to use the others.

And Emmett sits in the third row from the back, on the aisle, where a man sits who is not family.

I know him the second he comes in because he is the only person in the room carrying it. Everybody else is light, the ordinary light of mourners who liked a man and will miss him a normal amount, and Emmett comes through the door bowed under the same stone Harlan died of, the love and the not-saying, except his is worse, because Harlan's at least is *over* and Emmett's has sixty more years of road under it and a fresh hole at the end where the other half used to be, and the smell comes off him so plainly that I have to set my hand on the doorframe. A man at his own beloved's funeral, who may not say the word *beloved,* who has come to bury the person he loved most on this earth and must do it from the third row from the back, on the aisle, like a man who came to pay respects to an acquaintance.

He has brought a dish. Of course he has. He has brought a dish so that he will have a reason to be here, a thing to set down, a way to be in the room that the room will accept, and it is banana pudding in a Pyrex with a strip of masking tape on the bottom that says VOSS in ballpoint, his own hand writing the dead man's name on the bottom of a dish so he gets it back, and I look at that piece of tape and I have to go stand in the hall a minute.

He does not cry where they can see. He has had a lifetime of not crying where they can see; it is the most practiced thing about him. He shakes the niece's hand. He says, "He was a good neighbor," and the niece says, "He thought the world of you, Mr. Pruett — Emmett," catching herself, because everybody in this county is half a wrong name, and Emmett says, "Well," which is the whole of his eulogy, the only one he is allowed, *well,* a syllable you could fit a sea inside, and he goes and sits back down on the aisle in the third row from the back and folds his hands and grieves the regulation amount a man grieves a neighbor, with sixty years stuffed down under it like a body under a dragline, and nobody in the room knows what is in that chair except me.

I know what is in that chair. I am the only one. I am always the only one now; it is the trade's one consistent wage.

—

I do not eat Harlan Voss.

I want to be plain about it because I have spent this whole book learning what plain costs. I stand at the bread on his breastbone in the small hours with the parlor dark and the covered dishes gone home and Emmett's banana pudding washed and on the rack waiting for a hand that will come back for it, and I take the bread *off,* and I crumble it into the sink and run the water, and I do not say the words, and I let Harlan Voss stay heavy.

Not because I am good. I have given up the idea that I am good; Pearl took that the way she took everything. I refuse because of the one thing in me the bread has not reached yet, the thing under the records and under the counting and under the wall, the thing that made a child write the world down so it could not change its mind — and that thing looks at sixty years of a man loving another man in a kitchen, two inches and a whole life of two inches, and it will not, it *will not,* let me make it never have happened. It is the truest thing I have read off a body since I took up this trade. It is the only thing I have read that was not a wound. And I have unmade enough true things. I have unmade a town. I will not stand here and unmake the one clean love that ever crossed my table just to spare a dead man a shame that a living God-talk built and a living town maintained and that was never, not for one of his seventy-four years, his to carry.

There is a cost. There is always a cost; the trade's whole grammar is that there is no sentence without one. Harlan will wake heavy. I burn him Thursday and the Power-Pak fights me the way it fights for the heavy ones, temp dropping and sulking and refusing to climb, the low wet sound under the roar, and he goes to six pounds and some bone that won't calcine, and the smell floods the house and the windows do nothing, and it will not air out, because the love has nowhere to go now, I would not house it, and an unhoused thing seeks the living kin, and Harlan's only real kin, the only person he was ever truly *of,* is a man in the third row from the back with a Pyrex to retrieve.

It will find Emmett. I know it will. The most tender haunting this house has ever made — not a sin sitting down on a guilty man, but a *love,* aching, unspent, sixty years of it, coming home to the one who would have given anything to house it and was never once let. Emmett will start to feel a weight he can't name, a presence on the bed, a heaviness room to room, and he will think it is grief, and it will be grief, and it will be more than grief, it will be the actual unhoused substance of being loved that long and that hidden, and he will carry it now for both of them, because I would not eat it, because I decided his dead man's love was worth more kept than gone.

I think I am right. I cannot tell you I am sure. That is the other thing Pearl took.

—

I do the dishes at four because I can't sleep and because Emmett's banana pudding is still on the rack and I cannot stand the masking tape, the VOSS in ballpoint, a man labeling his grief so it would come back to him, and I wash the Pyrex twice though it is clean and I set it where he'll find it, and Wren finds me there at the sink at four with my hands in the water and nothing to wash.

She doesn't ask. She has stopped asking; she learned in this house that some things don't have words you hand across a counter. She just leans in the doorway in her coat and looks at the clean dish in my clean hands and says, "Whose is that."

"Emmett's. He'll come back for it."

She knows who Emmett is. Everybody in this county knows and nobody knows; that is the whole architecture of the thing. She is quiet a second, nineteen and feral and unafraid of a single dead body in this building but careful now, careful the way you get careful around a person who's worn thin, and she says, "My granny used to say Harlan and that Emmett were *real* close. She'd say it like that. Real close." She does the air with two fingers, not mocking it, just showing me the shape of the thing the town made, the little fence of quotation a whole community built around a love so it could stand to have it on the same street. "Like there was a word for it she had and just — wasn't gonna spend."

"There was a word for it," I say.

"Yeah." Wren looks at the Pyrex. "Banana pudding's the good kind, too. The from-scratch. You don't make the from-scratch for a neighbor."

And that — *you don't make the from-scratch for a neighbor* — is the truest eulogy Harlan Voss is going to get in this county, said at four in the morning by a girl with chipped polish over a kitchen sink, and it is more than the preacher gave him and more than the program allowed and I have to put the dish down and grip the edge of the counter, and Wren does not say anything, she just comes and stands next to me, close, her shoulder almost to mine, two inches, and does not close them, and I think she has no idea, she has no idea she is doing the exact holy arithmetic of the kitchen I read, the staying-near, the not-asking, the being-here that asks nothing — and it is the warmest thing in the building and the building still smells of a love I would not take, and both of those are true in the same room, which is the only way anything has been true since I came home.

—

Here is what I take from Harlan, since I would not take anything of his.

I take it lying awake after, with the smell of him in the house that will not leave for days. The rite cannot tell the difference. That is the thing. I have known the rite was a deletion since Della's yellow dress; I have known it was a murder since the bridge; but I did not know until tonight, until I read a clean love sitting in a man at the dead weight of a crime, that the rite *does not know what it is eating.* It only knows the breastbone is heavy. It would take a killer's thirty years and a saint's drowned river and an old man's hidden tenderness with the exact same indifferent appetite, because to the bread they are one substance, *weight,* and weight is weight, and there is no second column where it writes down whether the thing it ate should have been grieved or never should have been a sorrow at all.

No sorry was owed here. That is the part that will not let me sleep. There was no sin, there was nothing to be sorry for, the whole apparatus of *instead of sorry* — eat it, say the words, send him light, that's the mercy — was built for the guilty, and tonight the guiltiest-smelling thing in my house was a man who never did a wrong thing in his life except love quietly in a place that called it filth. And the town wanted him eased anyway. The preacher wanted him *private* and *faithful* and *kept his own counsel,* the program wanted him unburdened, the whole street would have been glad, *glad,* to have Harlan Voss go to his rest with that off of him, not knowing, never knowing, that the thing they wanted lifted was the best thing he ever made.

And then I am awake the rest of the night with the part I cannot stop, the part that is always now where this trade is concerned, which is that there is a woman in my cooler at thirty-eight degrees, the heaviest thing in this house, an account open a hundred years, and I have spent seventeen chapters of my life certain that what sits in Esther is a sin.

But I read a love off a man tonight that weighed the same as a killing. I felt it bow the bone. And the rite would have eaten it without blinking and called it mercy.

So what is in my mother.

I had thought I knew. I had thought it was simple, the cold of her, the leaving, the nineteen years, a debt with a clean shape that I could refuse to pay or be forced to pay but at least could *name.* And I lie here with Harlan's love souring the air of the whole house because I would not unmake it, and I think: I don't know what is in her. I have never read her. I won't, I can't, the keeper can't read herself and a daughter is too close to read, and so the one body in this building I most need to know the weight of is the one body whose weight I will never get to learn until the bread is on her breastbone and the words are half said and it is far, far too late to take them back.

And if love can weigh as much as a killing — if the rite can't tell the wound from the holy thing tangled up inside it — then whatever is in Esther, whatever I have hated for nineteen years and crossed the mountains to keep hating, there might be something in there with it. Under it. Fused to it the way the love was fused to the shame in Harlan, one stone, no seam, nothing you could pick apart even if your hands were steady, even if you had a hundred years.

I could eat my mother to be free of the worst thing she did to me.

I had not, until tonight, let myself wonder what I would be eating along with it.

I get up before light and I go down and I stand at the cooler door and I do not open it. The smell of Harlan's kept love is everywhere in the house, in the hall where the smell stood the first night, patient, and under it, very far, older, the other one, the one that has been here forty years, hers, and for the first time I cannot tell you for certain that it is the smell of a sin.

I put my hand flat on the cold steel door. I do not say anything. There is nothing I would let myself say. And I stand there in the dark with my palm on the door between me and my mother, the two of us two inches apart and a whole life of two inches, and I do the thing I cannot stop doing, the only prayer I have, which is no prayer at all: I try to read her through the door, through the steel, through the cold, knowing I can't, knowing it doesn't work that way, knowing I will get nothing —

and I get nothing.

Just the smell. Just the weight of her on the other side of my hand, the heaviest thing in the house, and no way on God's poisoned earth to know, until it is too late to un-know it, whether the thing I have been refusing to forgive is a wound, or a wrong, or the last love anybody ever buried in this town and called a sin.

# Chapter Eighteen

## Half a Sin

Junie Ratliff comes in at eleven on a Tuesday with a face I half know and a sin I can feel from the cooler door, and I tell myself this is a small one, a manageable one, a body I can do clean and fast and right, because I have learned by now to lie to myself in the language of procedure.

She is fifty. She died in the regional with a tube in her and a daughter in the hall, which the county sheet says and Loy confirms, and she weighs a hundred and sixty-three pounds, which I log, *RATLIFF, JUNIE F., 163 lb,* 11:08, my hand steady, because the hand is the one inheritance I will still cop to. The daughter wants the cheap urn and a graveside and no viewing. The daughter, on the phone, sounds tired in the specific way of a woman who has been doing the dying for someone else for a long time and is not sorry it's over and is ashamed of not being sorry. I know that tiredness. I write down what she wants. The pen works fine.

The sin is a betrayal. I knew that before I touched her, the way you know weather is coming off the smell of the creek — a particular wrongness, lower and meaner than Carl's, not as black as the ones I won't say. I read it without meaning to, just from being in the room: a man, years back, a job at the tipple, and Junie told the company something about him that was true enough to ruin him and false enough to be a lie, and she did it for money, because the company paid in those days for the right kind of telling, and the man lost the work and then the house and then the rest of it, the slow way, the way men in this county lose the rest of it. She kept the money. She kept her mouth shut. She sat three pews back from his widow at two funerals and let the widow think the world had simply been hard.

I have eaten worse. That is the sentence I am living inside now, the one that should frighten me and mostly doesn't anymore, *I have eaten worse,* a sentence with a column under it. Pearl was eleven days ago. After Pearl I know exactly what the bread does and exactly what I am, and the knowing did not stop me, which is the part I do not write down even in the book I keep for writing down what I cannot say.

So I tell myself: this is mid-weight. A medium wrong. The kind that wakes heavy enough to sour a tired daughter's grief if I leave it, and small enough that the world barely flinches when it goes. I can do this one carefully. I am very good now at doing them carefully.

I bake the bread at half-past midnight. My hands do it. I have stopped pretending I'm surprised that they know.

—

The retort is warm from the afternoon's last burn and the carriage house is the only warm room in the building, so I bring Junie out on the cot and do it there, on the steel, at the threshold, the way it's meant — body present, before the fire, the fire the seal. The smell is up around her, struck match and wet stove and the third thing I still have no shelf for, but lower than it was at Esther's tray, lower than Ferry, a smell you could walk past if you were anyone but me.

I count the pipe joints over the door out of an old habit that has stopped working. There are eleven. I do not say it helps, because it does not, has not since Haz, since whatever it was the rite took the night I ate the deacon and the count came up hollow in my mouth like a word in a language I used to speak. I count them anyway. You keep doing the thing after the why is gone. That, I have learned, is most of what people are.

I lay the bread on her sternum, over the breastbone, where the weight sits. It settles into the small hollow there the way it always does, like it was cut to fit, because it was, because a hundred years of Pruitt women cut it to fit and I am only the last hand on the knife. I let it rest. It draws. I feel the betrayal start to come up into the crumb the way damp comes up through a cellar wall, slow, and with it the man — I get the shape of him now, the way I always do at the drawing, a cold knowing, not a voice, not a picture, colder than either. A thin man. A man who whittled. A man who, when the company came to his door with what Junie had told them, said *that's not so,* once, quietly, and then never said it again because saying it changed nothing and a man learns fast in this county which words change nothing.

His name comes up last, the way the names do.

And I begin the words. Low, the way Loy showed me, the way his aunt showed him, back and back to a Pruitt woman in Wales who is a column of ink now. The naming first — *what lies heavy on thee.* The taking — *I take to me.* I am most of the way to *go thou light,* my mouth shaping the old worn shape of it, the bread halfway to gone, the swallow already starting down, that first obscene loosening at the back of the throat where the lightness lives —

The carriage-house door bangs open and Wren says, "Okay so you're gonna be so mad—"

—and the floor of me drops out, and the swallow goes wrong.

—

I want to be exact about this because exact is the only thing I have ever owned and it is going fast and I will need, later, to have written it true.

The swallow does not finish. That is the thing. The bread is in my mouth, half-down, the draw still coming, and at the bang of the door my whole body does what a body does, flinches, jerks, the throat closing on a thing it was in the middle of taking — and the rite is in me at the exact wrong instant, half-pulled, the man half-drawn up into the crumb and the crumb half-swallowed, and instead of the clean total loosening, the unweighting, the bus-ticket of it, there is a wrenching. A tearing. Like a splinter pulled most of the way and snapped off under the skin. Like a thread yanked and broken with the needle still in the cloth.

It hurts. I want to be exact: it has never hurt before. Every other time the swallow was relief, was the obscene clean nothing, was the worst-good feeling I have. This is the inside of that turned out and ragged. Something goes out of me and something stays half-in and the seam between them is raw.

I am on one knee on the carriage-house floor. I did not decide to be. The bread is gone from the steel and gone from my hand and most of the way down my throat and I do not remember the rest of the swallow, only that it happened wrong, only that I am down here on the cold floor with my hand splayed flat on the boards and the retort ticking and Wren in the doorway with a paper sack of taquitos going dark with grease and her mouth still open on the rest of what she was going to say.

"—oh my god," she says. "Nona. Nona, are you—"

"I'm fine." It comes out wrong. It comes out from a place that is missing a piece of its floor. "I'm fine. I had a—" The procedure. Reach for the procedure. It is right there where it always is, the flat true mortuary thing to say. *I had a dizzy spell, the fumes, the warm room, go on back inside.* I reach for it. My hand closes on nothing. For one whole second the room of me where the deflection lives is just walls. "—a spell," I get out, finally, from somewhere, and it is the wrong word, it is Loy's word, an old woman's word, and Wren hears that it's wrong, I watch her hear it.

She comes and gets down on the floor with me, which is the worst thing, which is the kind thing, nineteen years old and unafraid and crouching on a crematory floor with her hand on my back like I am the one who needs not to be scared of the dead. "You were saying something," she says, low, careful. "When I came in. You had your—" She looks at the steel. At Junie. At the place on the sternum where the bread was and is not, the small clean rectangle in the grease-film of it where a thing sat. "—you had the bread on her."

"It's an old Welsh thing." The lie is there now, late, it crawls back up to me from wherever it went. "It doesn't do anything."

"Okay," Wren says. She does not believe me. She is kind enough not to make me know she doesn't. "Okay. You want to sit up?"

—

I burn Junie an hour later because there is nothing else to do with her. The rite is past. You cannot finish a swallow you broke; the moment is the moment, and it's gone, the bread is down, the draw is severed, and the body cannot wait on me to figure out how to un-ring a bell. The county wants her disposed. The fire is the seal and the seal comes down whether or not the thing under it is whole.

She does not burn clean and she does not burn heavy. She burns *wrong* in a third way I have no log line for. Eaten bodies go up like paper, light, odorless, eager — the clean nothing. Un-eaten heavy ones drag and roar and make the *Hk. Hk.* sound, the throat clearing that never gets to the word, the smell flooding the house. Junie does neither. The temp holds and then stutters and then holds, like a thing that can't decide. There is a smell but it is intermittent, it comes and goes, it is here and then it is not and then it is back in a different part of the room, the way the swallow was here-and-gone, the way *she* is, now, I am starting to understand, here and gone. I log the burn at 2:51 to 4-something. I do not finish writing the time. The pen stops on the page and I look at it and I cannot, for a moment that frightens me, remember what number I was making.

I weigh the remains and they come up four pounds eleven ounces, which is an ordinary number, a whole number, a body's worth, and I stand there holding the scale-reading and the wrongness is not in the ash. The ash is fine. The ash is just a person. The wrongness went the other way, out into the world, into the thin man, the whittling man, half of him gone and half of him left and the cut not closing.

I learn that part the next morning.

—

His name was Press Hatcher. I have it written, *Junie Ratliff — the telling; Press Hatcher; the tipple; the money,* in the LEFT column, in my own hand, made last night with a hand that was already wrong. I have it because I write everything down, because a thing not written down is a thing the world is allowed to change its mind about, and the world is changing its mind about Press Hatcher right now, in front of me, and the only place he is staying still is in my book.

I drive out to where he lived because I have to know, the way I had to know about Della, the way the keeper of records cannot leave an account she can't read. It is a holler off the Coon Branch road, a trailer and a leaning shed and a yard gone to chest-high broom sedge, and I stand at the fence in the wet October morning and the smell is here. The struck-match, the wet-stove, the third thing. It is on the trailer and on the shed and in the broom sedge and it does not air out, the fog is sitting in the holler the way it does and the smell sits with it and neither one moves, and I understand that this is what a half-eaten sin does: it stays, unhoused, the way a skipped sin stays, except it is only half a weight, and there is no body for it to seek because the man it belongs to is half gone.

A woman comes out on the trailer's little porch. Older than me, in a man's flannel, a cigarette going. She looks at me the way you look at a stranger at your fence.

"Help you?"

"I'm sorry to bother you." The trade-words come easy; they always come easy; they are a coin I hand across a counter, nothing like the real one I keep in the jaw I won't open. "I'm with Pruitt and Daughters. I'm—I think I might have known somebody lived here. Press Hatcher."

And here is the thing. Here is the thing I will write down exact and read again later when I am even less than I am now.

She frowns. She takes the cigarette out of her mouth. "Who?"

"Press Hatcher." I watch her face. "Whittled, I think. Worked the tipple, way back."

"...Hatcher." She says it slow, like a word in the back of a drawer. "There was a—" She stops. Something moves behind her eyes and then doesn't. "I want to say there was a Hatcher. Older fella. Kept to himself." She shakes her head, not at me, at the inside of her own head, at the place where a thing should be and is going. "I don't know. My mamaw might've. He passed, I think? Long time? Or moved on. I couldn't—" She laughs, embarrassed, the laugh you laugh when your own memory shorts out and you're ashamed of it the way Ray is ashamed, the way I taught myself never to be because I never thought it could happen to *me.* "Lord. I don't know why I can't—it's like it's right there. You said the name and it was right there and now it's—" She makes a small empty gesture with the cigarette hand, at the air, at nothing.

At nothing. He is becoming nothing in her mouth while I stand at her fence. Yesterday he was a ruined man with a widow and a name and a slow bad ending; this morning he is a frown and a *who* and a thing right there that won't come; by spring he will be a smell in a holler that nobody can account for and broom sedge growing over a shed nobody remembers belonged to anyone. He is not dead. Dead I could log. Dead has a weight and a date and a line in a book. He is *un-becoming,* fraying out of the world a thread at a time, half here, half un-happened, and I did it, I did it with my throat closing on a door-bang, and there is no column for it because there is no balancing it.

"I expect I've got the wrong place," I tell her. "I'm sorry to have troubled you."

"It's all right, hon." She is relieved I'm leaving. She is relieved to stop reaching for a thing that isn't there. "You take care now. Foggy. Drive slow off that grade."

I drive slow off the grade. The smell follows me to the cattle guard and then it doesn't, the way it does, here and gone, here and gone.

—

The price comes that night, and I do not understand at first that it has come, because it came in two pieces and they don't line up, and a thing that doesn't line up is the one kind of thing my whole self was built to refuse.

I am at the prep-room desk going through the home's deep files for a reason I'll keep to myself, and in a banker's box that has not been opened since before I left there is a bundle of letters, tied with a brown shoelace, and I undo them because I undo everything, because there might be a fact in them I need to keep.

They are to me. They are in a man's hand, slanted, fast, the *g*'s looped hard. They start *Dear Nona* and they are about a winter, an apartment over a laundromat, a job he was going to get, a thing we were going to do in the spring. They are from someone who loved me. I read three of them at the desk under the one good light and I feel nothing, less than nothing, I feel the specific blank of reading a stranger's mail — a dead stranger, a fond dead stranger, and I am ashamed for him, for whoever he was, pouring all this *Dear Nona* and *when you get here* and *I keep your bus ticket in the drawer* into a woman who is sitting at a desk twenty years on reading it like a transcript.

When I was twenty-five. The dates are there. I was twenty-five and there was a man and an apartment over a laundromat and a spring we were going to do a thing in, and it is gone, all of it, cleanly gone, a smooth-walled socket the exact size of a love, and I do not know his name from the letters because he signs them with an initial I do not recognize as belonging to anyone in my life because no one is in that part of my life, that part of my life is a holler with the broom sedge grown over it, that part of my life is becoming a *who.*

I think: the rite did this. I think it the cold flat way I think the true things. *Junie was a betrayal, and the rite balances like with like, and it ate a betrayal off her, and it took being-loved off me, weight for weight, the way it took my mother's kitchen for a monster and the reason I keep these books for a good man's silence.* I have the pattern now. I have had it since Della. I can do this arithmetic in my sleep, which is funny, which is a joke I would make to Wren on the dock if Wren did not look at me now the way she has looked at me since the carriage-house floor.

But it is not clean. That is the new horror, and it takes me half the night to find the edge of it.

Because I gather the letters back up to tie them with the shoelace, and my hand passes over the front of the top one, over the slanted fast hand, the looped *g*'s, *Dear Nona,* and my whole body *flinches.* The way Brandy flinches. The way a hand jerks off a stove it didn't know was hot. A flinch with nothing under it, no event, no memory, no man — I have no man, the man is gone, the socket is smooth — and yet the *body* of me knows that handwriting, recoils from it or toward it, I cannot tell which, the two are the same motion when the thing they're about has been cut out and only the nerve left live.

I sit there with my own hand a stranger to me, flinching at a stranger's letters, and I understand what a half-take costs the eater.

It does not take the love clean. It takes the *knowing* of the love and leaves the *mark* of it. It leaves me the residue — the flinch, the recoil, the body remembering what the world and my own mind no longer hold — except residue is supposed to be the thing that survives in the *wronged,* in Brandy, in the daughter who still ducks. Now it is in *me.* I am Della and Brandy at once: the woman who can't remember, and the body that can't forget, in one skin, at one desk, holding one bundle of letters from a man who is half a holler off the Coon Branch road in my own life, fraying.

I take out my own notebook, the green one, the self-ledger, *what I think I had* on the left and *what is there now* on the right.

On the left I start to write *a man, at twenty-five, an apartment over a—* and the sentence walks off the edge of what I have, because I do not have the apartment, I only have the letter that says there was one, and you cannot put a thing in the left column that you are reading off a document instead of remembering, that is not *what I think I had,* that is *what the paper says I had,* and those are different columns, those are a different book, those are the difference between a memory and an autopsy.

On the right I should write *gone.* But it is not gone. Half of it is gone and half of it is in my hand, flinching, and there is no word for that, there is no entry for a thing that is subtracted and present at once, an account half-collected, a debt that is paid and owed in the same line. The columns will not hold it. For the first time in nineteen years of keeping books I have a thing that cannot be entered in any book, because it is torn, because the page itself is torn, and you cannot balance a torn page, you can only look at the tear.

I write, on the right, in a hand I make myself keep steady:

*Can't enter this one. Half. There was a man. The paper has him. I don't. My hand does.*

And under it, because it is true and because writing the true thing is the last religion I have left whose god I can still half-feel:

*This is what mine will look like, if hers goes wrong.*

—

Because that is the thing the night has actually been about, under the man, under Press Hatcher fraying in his fog. That is the screw the door-bang turned.

I have a mother in the cooler with the heaviest open account in a century of these books, and a burn-window closing on her, and a note in her hand that I read every way but the true way, and I have been telling myself, in the place where I make my plans, that when the day comes I will do it *carefully.* That I will hedge. That I will find some half-measure, some way to do a little of it, to take a little of the weight off so she doesn't wake heavy and seek me, without doing the whole obscene unmaking, without dissolving her all the way down into the lighter emptier nothing the rite makes. I have been holding a half-rite in reserve like a mercy I could give us both.

There is no half-rite. I know that now in my throat. There is the whole swallow, clean, which I will not survive as myself, and there is the broken swallow, which makes a tear that will not close in her and in me both — a mother half-erased, fraying out of the world a thread at a time, a *who* on a stranger's porch, and me on a crematory floor with a piece of her half-in and half-out and the seam raw forever. Esther's account is the largest, most load-bearing thing I will ever lay bread on. If a tired woman's tipple-betrayal, interrupted, tears a holler's worth of reality and half a love out of me at once — what does my mother, interrupted, tear?

Everything. The answer is everything. The father-shaped hole and whatever sits in it and nineteen years and the whole engine the note points at — half-pulled, snapped off under the skin, in me, forever, and no fire able to seal it and no column able to balance it.
