So it is the whole thing or it is nothing. Perfect or catastrophe. No hedge. No little mercy held back. When I stand over my mother I will have to choose all the way, both feet, no door left open behind me — and I will have to do it with a mind that is going, that flinches now at handwriting it can't place, that lost the count and the kitchen and the *why,* that just tonight failed for one whole second to find the lie it has hidden behind since I was twenty-two.

I sit at the desk a long time. The retort has gone cold. Somewhere off the Coon Branch road a man I loved is being forgotten by a stranger's mother, a thread at a time, in the fog, and somewhere closer my own hand is lying on a bundle of his letters, very still now, the way you hold still near a thing you are afraid to startle, the way you hold still when you do not know whether the next thing your own body does will be to flinch away or to reach.

I do not pick them up again. I leave them tied. I turn off the one good light.

In the dark the place where his name should be is smooth. The place where the flinch lives is not.

I have made a tear in the world that no one but me can see, and it will not close, and it has taught me the one thing I most needed not to know: that there is no safe way to do this to my mother. There is only the way that ends me clean, and the way that breaks us both and leaves the seam open in the fog forever.

The books, my mother said, must balance.

I am beginning to understand that some of them can't.

# Chapter Nineteen

## The Man Who Remembers

There is a betrayal-shaped smell in the prep room that will not leave, and I have stopped opening the windows on it, because the cold comes in and the fog comes in and the smell stays, the way it stayed for Tug, except this one is worse than Tug's because Tug at least went on whole. Junie Ratliff went on as a tear. I made her into a tear four nights ago, with Wren's hand on the door and the bread already going down and the words cut off in the middle, and now there is a man up the Coon Branch road named — named, I think, I have it written, I wrote it on my hand and then onto a card because the hand washes — a man named Press Hatcher who is becoming forgotten while he is still alive, and the smell of him won't leave his trailer, and the smell of what I did to him won't leave my prep room, and these are the two facts I have learned to stand inside the way you learn to stand inside any weather.

So I am not in good shape when Ray Sennett comes up the porch on a Tuesday with no folder.

I see the no-folder before I see his face. He has carried that manila folder into this house every time, the way a man carries a thing he is using to hold his own hand, and today his hands are empty and swinging and he comes up the steps like a man walking to a thing he has decided to walk to, and I put down the intake I am not really reading and I go to the side door and I let him in, and we stand in the kitchen, and he does not sit, which is also new, because we had got to where he sits without asking.

"I want to say a thing," he says, "and I want you to let me get all the way to the end of it before you tell me I'm crazy."

"All right."

"Because I been a police a long time. And the first thing they teach you, the only thing that's any good, is when your evidence and your gut go to war, you go with the evidence. Every time. The gut lies. The gut wants it to be the husband. So you go with the paper." He stops. He looks at his empty hands like he's just noticed the folder isn't in them. "I have spent thirty years going with the paper. And the paper's gone, Nona. You know that. I told you that, in this kitchen, the file's gone soft, the road I can't find, Dana getting vaguer in her own murder book every week I'm not looking. And a man with sense, when the paper goes, he lets it go. He retires. He says I'm old, my mind's slipping, it was a long time ago."

"Ray."

"Let me get to the end."

I let him get to the end.

—

He paces the kitchen once, the small kitchen, four steps and turn, and he is not a pacer, he is a sitter, a man who learned stillness on a thousand interviews, and the pacing is its own confession, the pacing is a man whose stillness has run out.

"I started going the other way," he says. "I stopped trusting the paper. Because the paper's the thing that's lying now, the paper's the thing that keeps changing on me, and a man can't lose his own handwriting, Nona, I have read my own reports change under my own eyes, and that is not a mind slipping, a mind slipping forgets, a mind slipping leaves the same wrong thing the same wrong way, it doesn't go back and *neaten* it." He says *neaten* like it disgusts him. "So I quit looking at what the paper says and I started looking at *when.* When did it start going. And where I was standing when it did."

I am very still. I am the stillest thing in the county. I have had practice.

"And it's this house," he says.

He says it gentle. That is the part I was not ready for, that he says the worst sentence anyone has ever said to me in this kitchen as gently as a man telling a widow her husband went easy. He is not accusing me. I can hear the difference. I have heard accusation, I have a whole jaw of it I carry, and this is not that, this is a man laying down a thing he is sick to be right about.

"Every case I ever lost the grip on," he says, "every one, I can put a date on the week it started slipping, and every one of those weeks, somebody died in this county and got brought here. To your fire. I went back. I sat with my own calendar like it was a suspect. Verlie Stiltner. Hassell Greer. There was a fella, Probst, that nobody'll miss and his whole — his whole *file,* the things the county half-had on him, the complaints that never went anywhere, they read like nothing now, they read like a man nobody ever even *worried* about, and I *worried* about that man for years, Nona, I had a folder." He stops. He puts both hands flat on my mother's table, the way I put my own palm flat on the counter at night to hold a feeling that isn't there anymore. "It runs through here. Through your fire. Through" — and his voice does a thing, it goes careful, it steps onto the ice — "through your mother. Going back. Years before you came home. I can't prove a word of it and I'm not trying to prove it, I'm past proving, I just need one human being on this earth to tell me I'm not out of my mind for seeing it."

And there it is. The whole confession, his and mine, sitting on my mother's kitchen table between his two flat hands.

I have it all. I have had it all since the first night. I could give it to him right now, the road and the girl and the men I have eaten and the way each one took a piece of his thirty years down its throat with it, I could make this big patient ruined man not be losing his mind, I could hand him the one true thing that would let him keep his own hands, and it is the most expensive thing in the world and it is sitting right behind my teeth.

"You're not out of your mind," I say.

It is true. I will not lie that far. I have lied to him before — *maybe you're misremembering,* the worst thing I have ever said to anyone, I gave him his own madness wrapped as kindness — and I find I cannot do it again, not with his hands flat on her table, not with the no-folder, so I give him the one true sentence I can afford, *you're not out of your mind,* and I stop there, on the cliff of it, with the rest of the truth roaring under me, and I do not go over.

He looks at me a long moment. He is too good at this. Thirty years of watching faces tell their small lies, and he watches mine, and he sees the cliff, he sees that I stopped on the edge of a thing on purpose, and he does not push me over it, because — and this is the worst tenderness — because he has decided he would rather have me half-honest and intact than push me into the creek to get the rest. He chooses the live witness over the closed case. He is the only person I have ever met who would.

"Okay," he says. Quiet. "Okay. That's — okay. That's enough for now."

It is not enough and we both know it and he says *okay* anyway, and that is the most adult thing anyone has done in this house in nineteen years, and I have to turn around and put the kettle on so I am not looking at him when I feel the thing I am not going to name.

—

I make the coffee. We are two people who remember at a table in a house that smells, today, of a betrayal four nights old, and he can smell it too — not the way I smell it, he smells *something,* a wrongness, a wet-stove note he keeps not quite turning his head toward — and we drink the coffee and neither of us says the true thing and it is the closest I have come to being known since I was twenty-two, except for the night on the floor under the sink, and I am not going to think about that night.

"How do you do it," I ask him. I don't mean to. It comes out the way the worst things come out, sideways, in the gap. "Thirty years. One girl. Her mama up the hollow waiting on you every Sunday. How do you keep holding a thing you can't put down and can't finish."

He turns the cup in his hands. He has a way of turning a cup that is the same as my counting, I have watched it, it is the thing his hands do instead of the thing his chest is doing.

"You don't hold it," he says. "That's the trick I never tell the families. You don't hold it the whole time, you couldn't, nobody could, your arms'd give out. You set it down. You set it down all the time, you go to the Ruby Tuesday, you watch the ballgame, you fix the gutter. You set it down." He looks up at me. "But you don't *lose* it. There's a difference, and it took me twenty years to learn it. Holding's for your arms and they give out. Keeping's for — I don't know. Somewhere else. You keep it whether you're holding it or not. Verna Coyle, she set her girl down years ago, she has to, you can't carry a child in your arms for thirty years. But she *kept* her. Every day. That's not holding. That's just — refusing to let the world have the last say on whether the girl was real."

*Refusing to let the world have the last say on whether the girl was real.*

I have spent my life keeping records so the world could not change its mind about a thing. He has spent his keeping a girl so the world could not decide she was never taken. And I have spent the last two months *being* the thing that changes the world's mind, the hand that lets the world have the last say, the deletion with a face, and I sit at my mother's table across from the one man who has built his whole life on the exact opposite of what I do, and the loneliness of it is so total that I almost tell him, I get so far that my mouth opens, and what comes out is, "There's more coffee," and he says, "I'm all right," and that is where I leave it. On the edge. Where I live now.

—

He goes at dusk.

And when his truck is gone off the ridge I do the thing I have been not-doing for two months, the way on the first night I did the thing I had been not-doing all evening, her intake, building my mother out of paper. Tonight I build her out of the other book.

I go to the prep room and I crouch at the fireproof box under the sink and I take out the ledger, the whole century stack of dead Pruitt women, and I do not open it to the bread-recipe or the old worn words or the oldest Welsh pages I have read a dozen times now for the cold of it. I open it to *her.* To Esther. To the green county-supply book that is the newest, the one in my mother's hand, the one that ends with Carl Mott half-written, the pen lifted mid-name because she died mid-account, *Carl Mott —* and nothing after the dash, the last thing she ever wrote in here a man she did not live to eat, who waited in my cooler for me to come home and eat him instead.

I have read this book. I have read all of it. But I have read it the way you read a thing for what it teaches you to do — the bread, the count, the words, the prices in the right-hand column that taught me what the right-hand column was. I have never once read it the way a keeper of records is supposed to read a ledger, which is to *check that it balances.* Which is the thing my mother told Loy she stood here doing at night. *I'm checking that it balances.* And Loy heard a tidy old woman doing her sums, and I heard, when he told me, a curse, and I have been so busy learning the trade and losing myself and watching a town go thin that I have never once done the one thing she said she was doing, the one thing the books are *for,* the one thing I, of all the women who ever held this trade, am built down to the bone to do.

I check that it balances.

I go account by account, back through her forty years, left column and right, the sin and the price, *Ottie Lyle — the church fire — could not, after, find the way home from the IGA she had walked to her whole life.* *Burl Sizemore — the Justice girl — lost the name of his own first dog and wept at a dog on the television and did not know why.* Left, right. Left, right. Closed, closed, closed. Page after page of murders dressed as mercies and every one of them, every single one, paid for and recorded and closed, the books balanced in her cramped getting-smaller hand, a woman keeping faith with the only god she had, which was the column on the right, which was the truth that the price had been paid.

And then I find it.

It is not late in the book. That is the first wrong thing. It is *early,* it is decades back, it is in a hand that is still strong, still Esther-at-thirty before the years took the strength out of the letters — and the left column has an entry in it and the right column does not. The right column is *blank.* Not blank the way Carl is blank, the dash and the death stopping her. Blank the way a thing is left blank on purpose by a woman who balanced every other account in a hundred-year book, who stood here at night her whole life *checking,* who would no more leave an account open than she would leave a body half-burned.

One open account. In a century of closed ones. Left side filled. Right side white.

And the left side does not have a name in it.

—

Every other entry in this book has a name. *Burl Sizemore. Ottie Lyle. Verlie Stiltner.* The whole point of the left column is the name, *Name & what was carried,* the name is the keeping, the name is the refusal to let the world decide they were real. A hundred years of Pruitt women wrote the name first, every time, because to eat a person's sin and not even write down whose it was would be the one unforgivable thing, the deletion completing itself in the very book that was supposed to stand against it.

My mother wrote no name.

She wrote — and I have to hold the book under the one good light, because the ink is old and her hand was shaking in a different way here, not age, something else, something that makes the letters lean — she wrote, in the left column, in the place where a name goes, only this:

*what was done, and the one it was done to, and that I took it so the child would not have to carry him.*

And then the right column, white. The price unrecorded. The account open. For forty years.

I sit on the prep-room floor with my back against the cabinet under the sink, the cold of the steel coming through my shirt, and I read it again, and again, *so the child would not have to carry him,* and the prep room is very quiet, the retort is cold tonight, there is no body going, there is only the smell of a betrayal four nights old and now this, this older smell underneath it that I have been calling the drains since the first night, the smell that is worst at her tray, the smell that is worst at her tray *even at thirty-eight degrees because the cold cannot kill it because it is not the body, it is the account, it is the open account, it has been waking heavy in this house for forty years and I have walked through it my whole life and called it the air.*

*The child.*

There is one child. There has only ever been one child in this house. *& Daughters* is a joke two generations old; Mam had my mother and my mother had me and that is the whole tree. The child is me.

—

I do not let myself run at it. I make myself do it the way I do everything, slow, in order, a keeper of records cross-referencing a thing against the world, because if I run at it I will get there on a feeling and a feeling is a thing the bread can take and a thing on the page is not, or is harder to, or I have to believe it is harder to, it is the last religion I have.

I go up the back stairs. I have not been up them much since the first night, since I stood in the narrow hall with the framed things and noticed the thing I had stopped seeing as a child the way you stop seeing the air. I go up and I turn on the hall light and I stand in front of the wall.

The home in 1911, men in hats. Mam Pruitt, severe as a hinge. My mother at thirty in front of the sign when the sign still had its paint, not smiling, holding a baby that is me.

There is no photograph of a father anywhere. There never was. Not a man, not a wedding, not a Sunday, not even a torn-out blank where one used to be — and I stood in this hall on the first night and felt that absence as a pressure, a low wrong, a thing the air does where a thing should be, and I told you then it was like my tongue finding a missing tooth in the dark, and I told you I had lived so long beside the blank I forgot it was unusual.

I am not forgetting it tonight. I am standing in front of it under the hall light at forty-one and I am looking at it straight on, the way I have not let myself look at it in my whole life, and I am asking it the question I have never once been allowed to ask, because the question never had a *shape* before, the question was just the shape of the air, and now the question has a shape and the shape is a blank left column with no name and a white right column and the words *so the child would not have to carry him.*

Him.

Not a wedding she never had. Not a man who never was. A man who *was,* a man who *did a thing,* a man it was *done to someone else by,* a man whose whole self has been lifted out of this house and out of this hall and out of my own head so cleanly that I have spent forty-one years not as a girl whose father left but as a girl who *never had one to leave,* no grief, no anger, no story, no torn place — because you do not get the torn place, that is the whole horror of it, that is what I have watched happen to a town for two months, the deletion does not leave a tear you can read, *a deletion does not leave a gap you can read,* it leaves a smooth-walled socket the exact size and shape of a man, and a child grows up beside the socket and breathes it like air and never knows to grieve a thing the record can no longer hold.

I put my hand flat on the wall beside the photograph of my mother holding me, the way I put my hand flat on the counter, the way Ray puts his flat on the table, the way we all do the thing with the flat hand when the thing we are reaching for is not there.

*Did you eat him.*

I say it out loud. In the hall. To a photograph. *Did you eat him. Did you take a person out of my life and call it a mercy. Is that what's in the open account, is that the thing you couldn't write a name for because the name was* mine, *the name was* his-and-mine, *the name was the one account in a hundred years you couldn't close because to close it you'd have to pay a price out of yourself you'd already spent, or because to write the right-hand column you'd have to look at what you'd done in the left, or because — because there was no one to eat* yours, *because the line is one and the keeper can't eat her own sin and you'd have to wait for the daughter, you'd have to wait for the child you spared, you'd have to wait for me.*

*The daughter eats the mother. That is the whole of it.*

I have carried that note for two months as my mother's last cruelty, *make my disgusted daughter swallow me,* and I am standing in the hall and I feel it begin to turn over the way a body turns over on the table when the gas goes out of it, slow, and wrong, and toward something I cannot look at yet, and I do not look at it yet, I am not ready, I make myself not be ready, because there is a difference between knowing the question and knowing the answer and I am a keeper of records and I will not write the answer until I have the page that proves it, I will not let it be a feeling, a feeling is a thing the bread takes.

But I know the question now. I have named it. It is on the wall in front of me in the shape of a man-sized nothing and it is in the ledger downstairs in a left column with no name and it has been the smell in this house since before I could write.

—

I go back down. I do not sleep. There is no body going so there is nothing to do but the bookkeeping, and I do the bookkeeping, I sit at the prep-room desk under the one good light where I sat on the first night with her thin file, and I take out the fourth book, the green one, my own, *what I think I had* on the left and *what is there now* on the right, the ledger of myself, and I add to it.

I do not add a loss tonight. Tonight I add a reason.

Because Ray taught me, this very evening, at this very table, *you don't hold it the whole time, you couldn't, your arms'd give out, you set it down all the time, but you don't lose it,* and I understand now what I am running out of and it is not my arms. My arms are fine. I can lift a body, I can lay the bread, I can turn the page. What I am running out of is the *keeping.* The thing somewhere else that holds it whether the arms are holding it or not. Every rite takes a piece of that, the place where Ray keeps Dana and Verna keeps her girl and I am supposed to keep — what. I do not even know what I am supposed to keep anymore, that is how far it has gone, I have eaten so much of the somewhere-else that I am no longer sure what I came home to refuse.

So I write it down. While I still can. I write, in the green book, in my own hand, on a faith with no god under it:

*I will not eat her. Reasons, for the day I cannot feel them:*

And then I list them, the way you'd list anything, left margin, one per line, a keeper of records making the only kind of will she knows how to make — not who gets the building, not who gets the urns, but who I was, and why, set down in ink against the night the bread comes for the argument before it comes for me.

*1. Because the rite deletes, it does not carry, and I have seen what the deleting does, I have seen Della happy and Brandy afraid of nothing and a town gone thin and a man named Press becoming a tear, and I will not do it to her even if it is a mercy, even if she asked for it, because a mercy that erases the one it saved is the murder Loy never understood he was teaching me.*

*2. Because if I eat her I forgive her, and if I forgive her there was never anything to forgive, and if there was never anything to forgive then nineteen years was nothing, and if those years were nothing then I was nothing, never important enough to wound, and I would rather keep the wound than lose the proof that I was ever real enough to be left.*

*3. Because —*

And I stop. Because the third reason is the one I cannot write, the one I felt turn over in the hall in front of the man-shaped nothing, the one that is the answer and not the question, and I will not write the answer until I have the page, *I will not let it be a feeling,* and so I leave the third reason the way she left her one account, open, the left side reaching and the right side white, and I sit there with the pen on the page in a house that has smelled for forty years of a sin with no name in it, and I understand, finally, fully, why my mother could keep a hundred years of books in perfect balance and leave exactly one account open her whole life.

Some things you cannot close because closing them is the crime.

I cap the pen. The retort is cold. The smell of Press Hatcher's tear is in the prep room and the smell of my mother's open account is under it and somewhere up the Coon Branch road a man is forgetting himself while he's still alive and somewhere up another hollow a girl in a strip cut has been waiting thirty years to have been taken, and the county's clock is running on the woman in my cooler, the heaviest thing in this house, the only open account in a century, and her note is in my apron pocket against the place where the bread goes, and I have just written down, in a book only I will ever read, the reasons I must not do the one thing I cannot get out of doing, and I have left the truest reason blank.

The daughter eats the mother.

I am beginning to be afraid it was the kindest thing she ever did, and that I will reach the table not remembering why I came home to refuse it.

# Chapter Twenty

## The Open Account

The county calls at nine in the morning, which is a thing the county does not do unless it wants a body gone, and the woman on the phone is kind about it the way people are kind when they are reading from a screen. She says *decedent* and she says *disposition* and she says, gentle, like it is a favor, that I am coming up on the outside of the window, that after the twentieth day the law would *rather* see the deceased disposed of, that nobody's going to come out here over a day or two but she has to put a note in the file. She has to put a note in the file. I tell her I understand. I write it down myself, on the intake, *day 19, county courtesy call,* and then I write the date the window shuts under it and I draw a box around the date the way I draw a box around a thing I am afraid of, and I count the days left on my fingers under the desk where the phone can't hear me, and there are not many, and I will not say the number, because saying the number makes it a clock and it is already a clock, it is the only clock in the house, it is ticking in the cooler at thirty-eight degrees and it is my mother.

So I have a deadline now. Good. I work better against a wall. I have spent my whole life working against a wall; it is the one inheritance besides the steady hand I will cop to.

I hang up and I do the thing I have been not-doing for two months, the same way the first night I did the thing I had been not-doing all evening. Only this time the thing is upstairs.

I go up the back stairs to my mother's room and I open the door I have not opened since I came home, and I go through her like a detective and a thief and a daughter, all three, none of them clean.

—

The room is exactly as she left it, which is its own obscenity. I have prepped a hundred rooms of the recently dead, I have stood in them while the family went through the drawers, I have watched a grown man hold up his father's reading glasses and not be able to put them down, and I know what a left-behind room is, I know the way it holds the shape of a person the way a chair holds heat, and I have always been on the working side of it, the apron side, the side that does not feel. I am not on that side this morning. This morning the room is my mother's and she is a hundred yards away in a steel drawer and her bed is made the way she made it, hard, the corners squared, a woman who would no more leave a bed loose than she would leave an account open, and I have to stand in the doorway a minute and let the room be a room before I can let it be evidence.

Then I let it be evidence.

I am methodical because methodical is the only way I do not come apart. I take the drawers in order, top to bottom, left to right, the way I'd take a body. There is not much. She lived the way she died, with nothing extra. Two cardigans, folded. A box of papers — the deed, the licenses, the insurance, all of it filed and labeled in her cramped hand, *particular as you,* the cousin said, and he was right, she kept the home's paper better than the county keeps its own, which is the joke under everything, that my mother was a keeper of records too, that I did not invent the thing I am, I inherited it the way you inherit a nose, and I have spent nineteen years thinking it was the one thing I made myself out of nothing and it turns out it is hers, it is the most hers thing about me, and I did not know.

I find her reading glasses in the drawer of the nightstand and I make myself not be the man holding the glasses. I set them on the made bed. I keep going.

There is no diary. I did not think there would be. My mother did not keep a diary any more than she'd keep a soft word; the diary of Esther Pruitt is downstairs in a fireproof box, written in two columns, and I have already found the page that is going to take the floor out of me, and I came up here because a keeper of records does not build a case on one page, she cross-references, she corroborates, she finds the second source, and I need the second source the way I need air, because if I have only the one page then it is only a feeling, and a feeling is a thing the bread can take, and I have learned not to trust the things that can be taken.

What I find instead of a diary is the bottom drawer of the chest, which does not open, because it is not a drawer, it is a face screwed onto a box, and the box has a key, and the key is not in the room.

I know where the key is. I knew before I finished the thought. The key is on the ring under the prep-room sink with the key to the fireproof box and the key to the retort-room and the key to the chemical cabinet nobody uses anymore, the ring my mother carried and the cousin handed me in a sandwich bag the second day with the death certificate, *she'd want you to have these,* and I have carried them for two months and used three of them and never once asked what the fourth was for.

I go down and I get the ring and I come back up and I kneel at the bottom of my mother's chest of drawers and I try the small key in the small lock and it turns, and I understand, kneeling there, that I have just done a thing my mother set up for me to do — left the key on the ring, left the ring for the cousin to hand me, left the lock for the day I'd come home and be the kind of person who'd think to try it — and the understanding goes down my back like cold water, because it means she planned, it means somewhere in the emptied-out years she sat in this room and thought *when she comes, she'll want the proof, she's mine, she'll need it on the page,* and she made sure the page would be here, and that is not the act of a woman who didn't love me, that is the act of a woman who knew me down to the bone and left me a trail, and I have to put my forehead on the cold edge of the open drawer for a second, just a second, before I can look at what is in it.

—

What is in it is a second ledger.

Not the trade-book. The trade-book is downstairs; this is something else, smaller, a school composition book gone soft and brown at the edges, the marbled cover worn pale where a hand held it, and I open it on the floor of my mother's room with the light coming gray through the curtain she made, and it is not accounts.

It is her.

It is the thing I said did not exist. It is the diary, except it is not a diary the way other people keep one, it is a diary the way *my mother* would keep one, which is to say it is an inventory. There are no feelings in it. There is no *dear diary,* there is no weather, there is no day I went to town. There are columns. Of course there are columns. The woman could not think a thought that did not have a column. But these columns are not the trade's columns, not the sin and the price; these columns are headed, in her oldest strong hand, the hand from before the years bent it, two words I have to read three times because they take the air out of me:

*Had. Lost.*

And under *Had,* down the left side, in a list that goes on for pages, the smallest things. The plainest things. A child's whole life rendered in the only language she had, which was bookkeeping. *Had: the sound she made laughing, the one with the hiccup in it.* And across from it, under *Lost,* a date, and nothing else. *Had: that she liked the blue cup and would cry for the green.* A date. *Lost.* *Had: the smell of her head after a bath. Had: that she said* lellow *for yellow until she was four and I never corrected it because I wanted to keep it. Had: the weight of her asleep on my chest, exact, eleven pounds, I weighed her on the home's scale to know it for certain.* And a date. And *Lost.*

I am the *she.* I am the *her.* I have a list in my hands, in my mother's hand, of the things she had of me and the dates she lost them, and the dates are not random, I know they are not random before I am even done thinking it, because I am a keeper of records too and I can already feel the shape, and I get up off the floor and I take the soft brown book downstairs to the prep room where the other book is, the trade-book, the one with the prices in the right-hand column, and I lay them side by side on the steel table under the one good light, my mother's two ledgers, the one for what she did to the town and the one for what it cost her of me, and I do the thing I was built down to the bone to do.

I cross-reference the dates.

—

They match.

Every date in the *Lost* column of the soft brown book is a date in the right-hand column of the trade-book. Every single one. The day she ate Burl Sizemore's cruelty against the Justice girl is the day she lost *the sound she made laughing, the one with the hiccup in it.* The day she ate Ottie Lyle's fire is the day she lost *that she said lellow for yellow.* The right-hand column of the trade-book says *forgot her brother's face* and *lost the name of his own first dog,* the prices the dead Pruitt women paid in their own hands going back a century, and I always read that column as the cost of the rite, the toll, the thing the bread takes — and it is, it is exactly that, but my mother kept a second book that the others did not, my mother could not bear to only record the *fact* of the loss, my mother sat down on the day she paid each price and she wrote, in a book she locked in a drawer, *what the price was made of,* because the trade-book only says *the price was paid* and my mother needed somewhere to say *and this is the thing it was paid with, this is the piece of my own child I handed over to take a stranger's sin out of the world, and I am writing it down so that even though it is gone from me, even though I will not remember the hiccup in her laugh, there will be a place in the world that remembers I once had it.*

She made a record against her own erasure.

She made the exact thing I made. The fourth book. The green one upstairs, *what I think I had* and *what is there now,* the ledger of myself I started keeping the night I understood I was disappearing, a keeper of records using the robbed hand to check her own pockets, and I thought it was mine, I thought it was the one true original thing my grief invented, and it is forty years old and it is in a drawer in my mother's room and it is the most loving thing I have ever held and I did not know she loved me until I read the proof that she spent that love a date at a time to keep a town's dead from waking heavy on their children.

I sit down on the prep-room floor with my back against the cabinet, the same place I sat last night, the cold of the steel coming through my shirt, and I do not count, because counting stopped helping weeks ago, and I do not make a joke, because there is no joke, there has been no joke in me since the bridge, and I hold my mother's two books, one in each hand, and I weigh them.

That is the thing my hands do. They weigh. I have weighed every body that has come through this house and a great many that came before me; I know weight in the hands the way Ray knows a face; and I sit on the floor and I weigh the trade-book in my right hand, the book of what she gave the town, and the soft brown book in my left, the book of what it took of me from her, and they are the same book, they are the two columns of the same account, the sin on the left and the price on the right, except the price was always *me,* the price was the laugh and the *lellow* and the eleven pounds asleep on her chest, she paid the town's debts out of the bank of her own daughter and she kept the receipts in a locked drawer so that someone, someday, when the daughter came home grown and cold and certain she had never been loved, would find them.

I came home certain she had never loved me. I have built nineteen years on it. It was the one wall that held.

—

I want to tell you that I understood it all at once, on the floor, and that the wall came down clean. It does not come down clean. Walls don't. I have watched a building come down and it does not fall like in the pictures, it leans, and it groans, and it lets go a piece at a time, and a thing you were sure was load-bearing turns out to be holding nothing and a thing you never noticed turns out to be holding everything, and that is how it goes on the prep-room floor with my mother's two books.

Because here is the part the soft brown book makes me look at, and I do not want to look at it, and I am a keeper of records and I make myself.

I thought she was cold. I have a thousand memories of her cold. I have the morning I made the honor roll and she read the slip and set it down and said *good,* one word, *good,* and went back to the certificate she was filling out, and I took *good* up to my room and I cried into a pillow because *good* was a coin and I had wanted a hand. I have the night I came down sick at eleven and stood in the kitchen doorway wanting to be held and she felt my forehead with the back of her hand the way she'd test a body's temperature, clinical, exact, and said *you'll keep,* and made me toast, and I ate the toast and hated her. I have a hundred of these. I built the leaving out of a hundred of these. *She cannot love. She does not have it in her. I will not become a woman who cannot love. I will go.*

And I sit on the floor with the *Had. Lost.* book open on my knee and I find the date of the honor roll, near as I can place it, and I run it against the trade-book, and the week I made the honor roll my mother ate a man named Combs his theft and the price was — the soft brown column says it plain, in the failing-by-then hand — *Lost: the way joy felt. Not that things are good. That feels nothing. The lift under it. Gone. I read N's grade and knew it was good and could not find the place inside that should have risen.*

She read my grade and knew it was good and could not find the place inside that should have risen.

*Good,* she said. One word. *Good.* And I took it up to my room and cried because she would not give me the hand, and she could not give me the hand, the hand had been spent the week before on a thief named Combs so a thief named Combs could go light into the ground and not wake heavy on whatever children he had, and what I read as *she will not* was *she cannot,* what I read as a wall was a wound, what I built nineteen years and a whole self against was not a mother who would not love me but a mother who had been eaten down to the place where the love would have come out, who stood in that kitchen with the slip in her hand wanting, I have to believe now, I have the page that makes me believe it, wanting with everything that was left to feel the thing a mother feels and reaching inside for it and finding a smooth-walled socket the exact size and shape of joy, and saying *good,* because *good* was the true report, *good* was what was left when the feeling had been spent, *good* was a coin because she had handed away the hand.

It was not rejection. It was amputation. I have the inventory of the amputation in my lap, dated, cross-referenced, in her own hand, the most particular woman in the county keeping faith with the only god she had — the truth that the price had been paid — even when the price was the part of her that could have told her own child she loved her.

She did not stop loving me. She lost the parts you love *with,* one stranger's sin at a time, and she sat in a locked room and wrote down what each part was as it went, so that the love would have a record even after she'd spent the means to show it.

—

I do not cry. I want to be honest about that, because I have lied to you about easier things. I sit on the floor and the thing that is happening to me is worse than crying, the thing that is happening to me is that the wall I built my life on is leaning, is letting go a piece at a time, and I am realizing — slow, the way the building goes — that I came home to bury a cold woman and I am going to have to bury a woman who loved me so much she unmade herself rather than let me carry what she carried, and those are not the same funeral, and I only built apparatus for the first one.

I get up off the floor. I am still a person with a job. There is a body in the cooler and a window closing on it and two books on the table and a smell in the house I finally have the name for. It is not the drains. It was never the drains. It is the open account. It is the one page in the trade-book where the right-hand column is white, the one debt in a century my mother could not close, and I know now why she could not close it, I knew last night and I would not write it down, and I still will not write it down, because I do not have the whole of it yet, I have the *shape* and not the *name,* I have *that she ate a thing inside her own family and broke the trade's one law and could not bear to record whose it was,* and I have *that the thing sits exactly where my life has a hole,* and I have the father-shaped nothing in the upstairs hall, and a keeper of records does not write *father* in the open column until she has the page that proves it was a father, because there is a difference between the question and the answer and I will not let the answer be a feeling, *I will not let it be a feeling,* it is the last religion I have and I am holding it with both hands while the rest of me leans.

But I let myself look at it. That is the new thing. Last night I named the question; today I stand in front of it and I do not look away. There is a man-shaped hole in my memory and a sin-shaped hole in my mother's ledger and they are the same size, and they are forty years old, and the smell has been in this house since before I could write, and my mother kept a book of everything she ever lost of me except this — this one she could not even write the *Had* for, because to write *Had: a father* would be to write *Lost,* and a date, and the date would be the day she did it, and there are some things, I understand now, fully, on my feet in the prep room with my hands flat on the steel, that you cannot record, not because you do not keep faith with the truth but because the truth, written down, would be the confession of the crime, and she chose — my mother chose, the keeper of records chose — to leave one account open her whole life rather than close it with a lie or close it with the truth, both of which would have been worse, and she carried the smell of it for forty years instead, and called it nothing, the way I have called my own father-hole the air.

Some things you cannot close because closing them is the crime. I wrote that last night about her and I did not yet know I was writing it about the both of us.

—

Ray comes at the end of the day. Of course he does. He has been coming at the ends of days, the way the worst news in this county arrives, on a casserole or a cruiser, and tonight it is the cruiser, and he does not come in, he stands on the porch in the cold with his hands in his jacket and no folder, again, still no folder, a man who has set the file down and not lost it, and he says he was driving the ridge and saw my light.

He was not driving the ridge. The ridge does not go anywhere he had to be. He came to check on the live witness, and we both know it, and he stands on my porch in the dark and looks at me through the screen and says, "You don't look so good, Nona."

"I've been up in her room," I say. Which is true. Which is the kind of true I deal in.

And then I almost do it. I stand there with the screen between us and the two books still on the table behind me and forty years of my mother's spent love in a locked drawer and the smell of the open account in the whole house, and the loneliness of being the only one who knows is so total, so complete, such a weight in the hands, that I feel my mouth get ready to set it down. *Ray. Sit down. I have to tell you what I am and what she was and what this house is. I have to tell you the road, and the girl, and the men I have eaten, and why your file goes thin. I have to tell you my mother loved me so much she cut the loving out of herself a stranger at a time, and I have her receipts, and I am the only one who will ever read them, and I cannot carry that and be the only one, I cannot, your arms give out, you told me yourself, your arms give out.*

He sees it. He is too good at faces. He sees my mouth get ready and he goes still, that interview stillness, the stillness of a man who has learned that the truest things come when you do not reach for them, and he waits, he holds the door of it open, he does not push, and I stand on the cliff of it with the whole truth roaring under me the way it roared last night, and I think, *he would believe me. He is the one person on this earth who has done the work to believe me. He keeps a dead girl real for thirty years against a town that calls her a runaway; he would not flinch at a mother who eats sins; he is built for exactly this; tell him.*

And I do not. I do not, and I want to be honest about why, because it is not the reason I'd have given two months ago. Two months ago I'd have said I don't tell because it isn't his business, because I don't tell anyone anything, because the place where the words come out has gone smooth in me. That is not why tonight. Tonight I do not tell him because I have just spent a whole day reading what it cost my mother to take a weight off another person, and I will not hand Ray Sennett this one. He has the Coyle girl. He has thirty years of her. His arms are full. If I set this in them — what I am, what I did to his case, that the thinning of his life's work runs through my fire and my mother's — I do not lighten my load, I only double his, I make him carry the girl *and* the knowing of how she was taken from him a second time, by me, by us, by mercy. I would be doing to Ray the thing my mother spent her life doing, except I would be doing the honest version of it, the carrying version, I would be making him keep it with me — and some nights that is the kindest thing there is, two people keeping a true thing the world wants gone, and some nights it is just one more body laid across a man whose arms are already shaking, and I cannot tell tonight which it would be, and so I do the thing my mother could never do, the thing that is the whole of what she lost the means to do: I choose his weather over my relief.

"I'll keep," I tell him. Her words. I hear them come out of me and I do not even decide it. *You'll keep,* she said, with the back of her hand on my forehead, and I hated her for it, and I am standing on her porch handing the same two words to the one person I might love in this ruined town, and I understand, hearing myself, that *you'll keep* was never coldness either, that *you'll keep* meant *you are made of something that lasts, you will outlast this fever and this night and me,* that it was the most hopeful thing she had left to say with the warmth gone, and I have been carrying it nineteen years as a wound and it was a blessing the whole time, said in the only voice she had.

Ray looks at me a while. Then he nods, slow, and he says, "Okay," the way he said it last night, the way a good man says *okay* when *okay* is a lie they're both agreeing to tell, and he goes down the steps into the dark and his cruiser starts and pulls off the ridge, and I stand at the screen until the sound is gone.

—

Then I go back in to my mother's two books.

I should sleep. The window is closing and I will need to be a person with steady hands at the end of it, and I have not slept, and the smart thing, the survival thing, is to lie down. I do not lie down. I sit at the prep-room desk under the one good light, where I sat the first night building her out of a thin file and where I sat last night leaving a reason blank, and I put the trade-book on my right and the soft brown *Had. Lost.* book on my left, the way she must have sat with them, the two columns of the one account, and I make myself look at the open page one more time before I close the books for the night.

The white right-hand column. The price she never recorded.

And I think — and this is the thought that keeps me at the desk, this is the thought I am going to carry up the stairs and lie awake under — I think: she recorded *everything else.* A century of women paid the price and wrote *the price was paid* and that was their whole faith, the fact of it, the closed account. My mother could not bear that. My mother kept a second book so that the *contents* of every loss would survive her, the laugh and the *lellow* and the way joy felt, the actual texture of every piece of herself she spent, written down against the day she'd spent it, so that it would not just be *paid* but *remembered as a thing that had been hers.* She did the impossible bookkeeping. She kept faith down to the gram.

Except here. Except this one. The one account where the *Had* would have been a father and the *Lost* would have been the day she ate him, and she could not write the *Had,* she could not even start the entry, because to write *Had: him* in the book of the things she loved and lost would have been to admit she did it not for the town but for me, broke the one law for me, took a person out of her own daughter's life and called it a kindness — and that is the one loss she could not keep faith with by recording, because recording it would have made it true in the one place she most needed it not to be: her own hand.

So she left it open. Forty years. Smelling up the house. The heaviest thing in the building. And she waited.

She waited for me.

I close the books. I put my hand flat on the soft brown cover, my mother's inventory of everything I was before I can remember being it, every piece of me she kept a record of because she could no longer keep me, and the smell of the open account is in the room with me, under the betrayal-smell of Press Hatcher's tear, the way it has been in this house under everything my whole life, and I understand now that it is not a stranger's weight at all. It is hers. It has always been hers. It is the one thing she could not put down and could not write down and could not eat — because the keeper cannot eat her own sin, that is the law, that is the one mercy she was never allowed — and so she carried it, whole, for forty years, the way the old folklore said you were supposed to, the way Loy still thinks you do, *you carry it to your own grave, hon, that's the mercy* — my mother is the only person in the whole rotten history of this trade who actually did it, who carried instead of deleting, and it nearly killed her by the inch, and the reason she carried it, the reason she did not eat her own father-sin and go light, is sitting at this desk with her two books, because to eat it she would have had to teach the daughter the rite, and to teach the daughter the rite was to doom the daughter, and she would rather carry a sin for forty years until it hollowed her out and made her child believe she was unloved than hand the child the thing that is hollowing me out now.

I came home to refuse to forgive a cold woman.

I am going to have to find a way to refuse to forgive a woman who loved me down to the bone and proved it in two columns and left the proof in a locked drawer for me to find on the worst week of my life, and I am going to have to do it while the bread is already reaching for the reasons, and I do not know, sitting here with her books under my hand and her body in the cooler and her smell in the air, I do not know anymore which of us the open account was waiting to be closed by, and I am beginning to be afraid the answer is both.

# Chapter Twenty-One

## The Father-Shaped Hole

A keeper of records does not run at the answer. She cross-references it. She lays the thing she is afraid is true against the world like a toe tag against a wrist, and checks, and checks, and only when the paper and the body agree does she let herself write it down. I have said this to myself so many times in the last two months that it has stopped being a discipline and become a kind of prayer — the only one I have left, said to a god whose name I cannot find, the reason for it eaten out of me some night I cannot place, leaving the habit standing there with nothing under it, the way a porch will stand a while after the house behind it burns.

So I have not written it down. Not yet. I have known the *question* since I stood in the hall last night and said *did you eat him* to a photograph — and a question is not an answer, and I have learned the hard way which one the bread can take.

But this morning I am going to find the page that proves it. That is the difference between last night and this morning: last night I stood at the bottom of the thing and would not look up; this morning I have the ladder out, and I am going up, because a thing I will not look at is still a thing that is happening, and I am done letting things happen to me in the dark.

It is just after dawn. The fog is in the hollow the way it always is, to the second-floor windows, and the retort is cold because there is no body this morning, which is its own small dread now — a quiet morning in this house used to mean rest and now it means the smell has the whole place to itself. I make the coffee. I do not drink it. I go up the back stairs to my mother's room, which I have not opened since the first night, and I put my hand on the cold knob, and I open my mother's door.

—

I have been through ten thousand of the dead's rooms. It is half the trade, after the burning — you go where they lived and you find the policy and the deed and the name of the second cousin in Akron, you open the drawers a stranger's hands have no right to open, and you do it gently and you do it fast and you do not, ever, read the letters you are not looking for. There is a way to do it that keeps it work. I have been doing it twenty years.

I cannot do it here.

My mother's room is small and plain and cold and it is so exactly *her* that walking into it is like walking into the inside of her chest. The bed made with hospital corners she would have made the morning she went to the county to die. A chair by the window facing the ridge. A dresser with a comb and a jar of cold cream gone hard and a photograph turned face-down, which I do not turn up, because I am not ready to know who she kept face-down by her bed. The closet with six dresses, all of them some color that is barely a color, gray-blue, brown-gray, the colors of a woman who decided early that to be looked at was a kind of debt she would not run up. And the smell. The smell is here too, of course it is, the smell is everywhere she was, but it is thinner here than at her tray, which I do not understand yet and will, by tonight, understand entirely.

I go through it the way I cannot help going through it, which is not like a funeral director and not like a daughter but like the thing I actually am, which is a detective of the erased. I am not looking for a deed. I am looking for a *man.* I am looking for the one thing this house has never once held, in any drawer, in any box, in forty-one years — a sign that a father was ever here — and I am looking for it knowing I will not find it, because I already know I will not find it, because the not-finding is the proof.

And I do not find it.

No letters in a man's hand. No photograph with a man cut out, no photograph with a man left in. No ring in the jar with the cold cream. No second toothbrush gone yellow in a forgotten cup. No work boots too big for her in the back of the closet, kept the way widows keep boots. Nothing. A whole life and not one molecule of him, and I stand in the middle of my dead mother's room at dawn and I understand that I am looking at the cleanest crime scene I have ever worked, because the only place you get a room this empty of a man is a room a man was *lifted out of,* root and thread, so completely that even the absence has been tidied, even the hole has been swept.

I have seen this before. I have made this before. Twice in two months I have made a thing this clean — a margin note run smooth past a boy's name, a clipping that rewrote its own ink — and I know the texture of it now the way you know your own stitching. This room is stitched in my mother's hand. She did to this room what I did to Coreen Bostic's son. She closed it up smooth as water over a stone.

So I stop looking for the man. The man is not findable; that is the whole point of what was done to the man. I go looking, instead, for the place where she kept the thing she could not say.

—

It is in the ledger box, and it is not.

I have read the ledger box. I have read every account book in it, a century of dead Pruitt women, and last night I finally read it the way it was built to be read, account by account, checking that it balanced, and found the one open account in a hundred years — early, her hand still strong, the left column filled and the right column white, and in the place where a name goes, no name, only *what was done, and the one it was done to, and that I took it so the child would not have to carry him.* I have that. That is the ledger's confession, and it is enough to know the shape and not enough to know the man, because the whole horror of the entry is that it names no name, gives no face, leaves the wronged a *one* and the man a *him,* the grammar of a woman who has eaten the people out of the sentence and kept only the act.

The ledger is where she recorded the trade. But there is a thing under the ledger.

I find it because I am not, this time, looking for paper. I am crouched at the open box with the books out on the prep-room floor around me, and I have my hand down flat in the bottom of the steel box on reflex, the flat hand, the thing we all do — and the bottom of the box is not the bottom of the box. There is a false floor, a sheet of the same fireproof board cut to fit, sitting a quarter-inch proud where a hundred years of being lifted in and out have worn it loose at one corner. I get a nail under it. I lift it.

Underneath, in the half-inch she stole from the bottom of a box meant to outlast fire, there is one thing.

It is a cassette tape. A little clear-shell cassette, the kind that came free with a thing you bought in 1985, the label gone but for a ghost of adhesive, and someone has written on the shell itself in ballpoint, scratched in tiny over the molded plastic, two words, and the two words are in my mother's hand and the two words are *for Nona.*

—

I have to find something to play it on.

This is the part nobody tells you about a revelation — that it arrives and then makes you go find a tape deck. The entire weight of *for Nona* is held up for forty minutes by the stupid mechanical fact that I own nothing that plays a cassette, and neither, anymore, does most of the world.

It is in the carriage house. Of course it is. A boom box out by the retort, paint-spattered, one speaker blown, that has lived on the workbench since before I left — my mother ran it for the radio while she worked the dead, because even she could not do this in total silence — and I had walked past it a hundred times and filed it under *clutter,* never once as the one machine in the county that could give me back her voice.

I carry it to the prep-room desk under the one good light, the desk where I built her out of her thin file the first night and out of the open ledger last night. I do not sit in the chair. I sit on the floor with the cabinet behind me and the box in my lap, the cold coming up out of the steel against my back, a forty-one-year-old woman holding a child's tape player.

I put the tape in. The little door clacks shut, plastic, cheap, loud in the quiet. I press play with my thumb and the mechanism catches and pulls and there is the long soft roar of blank tape, the hiss of 1985 still spooling in there after all these years, and then there is a click, and a breath, and a chair, and my mother.

—

I have not heard her voice in nineteen years.

I had forgotten — no. I want to be careful, here, with the word *forgotten,* because I have learned in this house exactly how much that word can be made to carry. I had not *forgotten* her voice. I had let it go quiet the way you let a thing go quiet that hurts to keep loud. But I had it. It was in there. And the proof that I had it is that the second it comes out of the one good speaker, scratchy and small and forty years young, my whole body knows it before my mind catches up, the way your body knows a stair in the dark — there is a drop in me, the floor going out, the room going clean and far away, and then her voice fills the place the floor was.

She does not say *hello.* She would never. She does not say *if you are hearing this.* She is not a woman who narrates herself. There is the chair, and the breath, and then she is already in the middle of it, the way she was in the middle of everything, no door, no porch, straight into the room:

*"You'll have found the open one by now. I know you. You'll have done the books before you did anything else, because you're mine whether either of us liked it, and you'll have found the one I left open and you'll be standing there hating me for it, because an open account to you is a thing left undone and you have never in your life been able to bear a thing left undone. So. I'll tell you why it's open. And then it's yours."*

I sit very still on the floor with the cold coming through my shirt. I am the stillest thing in the county. I have had practice. I have had a lifetime of practice at being still while a thing I love is taken out of a room, and I did not know until this exact second that that was the practice I was getting.

*"There was a man,"* she says.

And the tape hisses, and the chair creaks, and my mother, dead in the cooler twenty feet from where I am sitting, tells me, in her own flat unhurried accounting voice, the one open account in a hundred years.

—

She does not tell it like a confession. She tells it like an intake. That is the thing that breaks something in me, sitting there — not grief in her voice, not pleading, none of the soft ones she could never get out, but the trade voice, the column-on-the-left voice, *name and what was carried,* reciting the facts of the worst thing she ever did as if she were logging a stranger's weight, because that is the only register she has for the unbearable, the same register I have, the one I got from her along with the steady hands and the missing god under the habit of writing things down.

*"There was a man and he was your father and I'm not going to tell you his name,"* she says, *"because I spent it, and I won't give you back a thing I can't give you whole. You'd only go looking. There's nothing to find."*

*"He did a thing. I'll not tell you the whole of it either, and that's not mercy, that's the same reason — I can't give it to you whole, I took it apart years ago to carry it, and the pieces I have left would do you more harm than the blank. Know this much. It was the kind of thing a man does once and is a different man after, and the person it was done to was not you, you were too small, you were in the next room, you slept through it the way a baby sleeps through the end of the world. And he left. The morning after, he left, and I stood in that kitchen with you on my hip and I watched the truck go down the ridge and I understood two things at the same time, the way you understand two things when the floor's gone, and I have never told a living soul either one."*

The chair. The hiss. I do not breathe. I count nothing. There is nothing to count and for the first time in days I do not even reach for it.

*"The first thing I understood was that he was never coming back, and that I was glad, which is a thing a woman is not allowed to be and I was it anyway. The second thing I understood was that you would grow up his daughter. That you'd carry him. That every year there'd be the question on a form, the empty line at the school, the thing the other children have and you don't, the asking and asking that never gets an answer, and worse than the asking — the *knowing,* someday, when you were grown, the day somebody finally told you what he did, the day you had to be the daughter of it. I'd watched it. I'd watched a hundred families carry a thing a dead man did and I'd stood between them and the worst of it with the bread, that's the whole of the trade, you stand between the child and the thing the parent did. And here was a thing my own child was going to have to carry her whole life, and I was the one woman in the world who could take it off her."*

—

I knew. I want that on the record. I have known since last night, I have known in the shape of the question since the first night, the man-sized nothing in the hall, the smooth-walled socket; the reader of my own life has known where this account was going since the hall light. So it is not surprise that comes up in me on the prep-room floor when my mother says the next thing. It is something worse than surprise. It is *confirmation,* which is the thing a keeper of records lives for and the thing that, this once, I would have given my steady hands never to receive.

*"So I ate him,"* my mother says.

Flat. The intake voice. *I ate him,* the way I would say *fifty-one kilograms,* the way I would write *burn anomalous, low resistance, temp held.*

*"I laid the bread on the table where he'd sat that morning, because the rite wants a body and I didn't have his body, I had the warm place he'd left, and I have never told you and I'll tell you now that it can be done that way, just barely, just the once, if the leaving is fresh enough and the weight is heavy enough and you're willing to pay what it costs to do a thing the rite was never meant to do — and I said the words over the chair where my husband had sat, and I named the weight, his leaving and what he did and the one he did it to, and I took it to me, and I ate it. And the bread went down. And he was gone. Not dead. Gone. Un-happened. There had never been a man. You looked up at me from the floor with the morning on you and there was no shadow on you at all, none, you were the lightest child in the county, and I had done it, I had spared you the whole weight of him before you were old enough to know his face, and I want you to hear me say that I would do it again. I'd do it again right now. That's the part you'll hate worst and it's the truest thing on this tape. I would do it again."*

—

I am crying. I notice it the way you notice rain has started, from the evidence, from the wet, not from any decision of my own — I have not cried in this house, I did not cry the night they wheeled her in, I did not cry on the floor at Pearl, I said *no, no, no* on a floor at Pearl and the *no* came out where the crying would not, and now I am on another floor and the *no* is nowhere and the water is just coming, silent, off my jaw, onto the cheap plastic of a child's tape player in my lap, and I let it. I do not wipe it. I let her keep talking through it because she is almost done and I am not going to be the one who stops her this time. I stopped her once. I stopped her at twenty-two with a no in a doorway and a lie about the smell, and she died nineteen years later believing the lie, and I am not going to stop her again, not even now, not even when stopping her would only be the *play* button under my own thumb.

*"You'll want to know what it cost me,"* she says, and her voice does a thing here, the only place on the whole tape it does it, it goes — not soft, she can't do soft, but *thin,* the way a thing goes thin that has been pressed too long. *"You're mine. You'll want the right-hand column. I never wrote it. I want you to understand that I didn't write it because I couldn't, not because I wouldn't — by the time I had the pen the price was already paid and I couldn't *find* it to write it down, you know how that is now, don't you, I can hear it, you know exactly how that is. A deletion doesn't leave you a thing to write. But I'll tell you what I think it was, because I've had forty years to feel the shape of the hole, and a woman gets to where she can read her own holes the way a blind woman reads a face."*

*"I think it took the part of me that was warm to you."*

The tape hisses. The cold comes up through the floor. Twenty feet away, in a cooler at thirty-eight degrees, the body this voice came out of is waiting for me to come home and eat it, which I have, I came home, I am the home it came to.

*"I think,"* my mother says, *"that the rite looked at what I was taking — a father, the warmth of a man in a child's life — and it balanced it the way it always balances, like with like, and it reached into me and took the warmth I'd have given you in his place. The mothering warmth. The arms-around warmth. The soft words. It took the soft words, Nona. That's why they never came. Nineteen years you thought I was a cold woman who couldn't love her child, and I let you think it, because the truth was worse and there was no way to say the truth that didn't put the weight back on you — I wasn't cold. I was *spent.* I spent the warm part of myself to buy you a childhood with no father-shaped wound in it, and the joke of it, the joke I've laughed at alone in this kitchen more nights than you'd believe, is that I bought you a childhood with no warmth in it instead, and you grew up wounded anyway, just by a different hand. By mine. I traded you a wound you'd have known the name of for one you never would. And I've never been sure, not one day in forty years, whether that was a mercy or the cruelest thing a woman ever did to her own child."*

—

I knew the warmth was amputation. I figured it after Pearl, I figured it in the prep room with Ray's hands flat on the table — *her coldness was not rejection, it was not-having* — and I had it cool and clean as a thing I'd decided, off the evidence, off the columns. A keeper of records knows the difference between a theory she built and a fact she received, and the difference is the whole of why I am on the floor. A theory you reasoned your way to does not get up off the page and put its arms where its arms were supposed to be your whole childhood and weren't. Her voice in the one good speaker, thin, saying *it took the soft words* — that does. I thought I had already grieved this. I had only diagrammed it.

I think of the photograph upstairs, the one of her at thirty in front of the sign, holding a baby that is me, not smiling. I have looked at that photograph my whole life as the proof of what she was — a woman who would not even smile holding her own child. And she was not not-smiling because she didn't love me. She was not-smiling because, by the time that shutter clicked, she had already spent the smile. It was already gone. She had eaten it for me, a year or two before, in a kitchen, off a chair, and what is left in that photograph is a woman doing the only thing she had left to do for me, which was *stay,* which was hold the baby and face the camera and be the one who didn't get on the bus. She is the daughter that stayed. I am the one who got on the bus. We have been arguing for nineteen years about which of us abandoned which, and we were both wrong, and only one of us is alive to find that out, and it is the one who left.

—

There is more tape. The hiss runs long. I think for a second it is over, that she has said *it took the soft words* and let the machine run out, which would be like her, to end on the worst true thing and not soften it. But she comes back. One more time. And this is the part the whole book has been built to mean the opposite of, and I do not know that yet, sitting on the floor, I only know that my mother is about to tell me what the note means, the note I have carried in my apron pocket for two months against the place where the bread goes, the note I have read every night as her last cruelty.

*"You'll have found my note,"* she says. *"In the green book. You'll have read it the way you read everything I ever did — as the worst thing it could be. I know you. You'll have read it and thought, there she is, cold to the end, the old woman's last cut: make the daughter who couldn't stand the smell of me come home and swallow me whole. I want to tell you what it says. Not what you think it says. What it says."*

*"The daughter eats the mother. That is the whole of it."*

She says it exactly. Word for word, the way it is written, the way it has been written in my pocket for two months, the way it was written in the gutter of the green ledger in her failing hand — and out of her mouth, in her voice, the same words turn over in the air of the prep room like a body turning over on the table when the gas goes out of it, slow, and wrong, and toward something I could not look at last night and cannot look away from now.

*"It's not a thing I'm making you do,"* she says. *"It's the only door I've got left to open for you. Listen. I left one account open in a hundred years of keeping them closed, and you'll have wondered why a woman who checked the books every night her whole life could stand to leave one open. Because I couldn't close it. The keeper can't eat her own sin — you know that, it's the oldest law there is — and the sin in that open account is mine, it's the worst thing I ever did and it's the only thing I ever did out of love, and they're the same thing, and I could not reach it, and I could not write the price because the price was already paid, and so it has sat open in me for forty years, waking heavy, souring this house, the smell you'll have been calling the drains. An open account is a wound that won't close. And there's only one way to close it. The way the trade always closes a mother's account. The daughter."*

—

I understand it all at once, the way she said you understand two things at once when the floor's gone, except it is not two things, it is the whole architecture of my life arriving in one piece, and I have to put both hands flat on the cold floor on either side of me to keep from going over sideways, the flat hands, the thing we all do when the thing we're reaching for isn't there, except this time the thing I'm reaching for is the floor and the floor is there, the floor is the only thing that is.

She did not write *the daughter eats the mother* to make me swallow her out of spite. She wrote it to tell me the only way out. The account is open. An open account wakes heavy and ruins the living kin, and I am the only living kin, the line is one, *& Daughters* is a joke two generations old, and so when she dies that open account comes for me — it is already coming for me, I read it on my own sternum the night I would not eat Tug, *an account, open, old, mine,* I thought it was my own and it is hers, it has been crossing the half-inch of air between her tray and my chest for two months. And there is exactly one way to close a mother's open account and free the child it would otherwise crush, and it is the rite, it is the daughter, it is me laying the bread on her breastbone and saying the words and *eating* her — eating the open account, the father-erasure, the worst thing she ever did and the only thing she ever did out of love.

And if I eat it, I forgive her. That is what eating is, the thing Loy never understood he was teaching me — it is not carrying, it is forgiving, it is *un-happening.* If I eat her open account I forgive the thing she did, which means the thing she did never happened, which means — and here is the architecture, here is the whole house coming down on me at once — which means *he comes back.*

Not him. Not the man. The man is gone past any eating; you cannot eat your way to restoring a thing, the ledger only runs one direction, I cannot un-eat what she ate. But the *erasure* — her erasure, the rite she did on her own family — if I eat *that,* if I delete the sin of her deleting him, then the deleting un-happens, and what un-happens when a deleting un-happens is the *deletion itself,* which means the father-shaped hole, the smooth-walled socket, the man-sized nothing in the hall — that fills. Not with him. With the *wound* he would have left. With the grief and the anger and the empty line on the form and the torn place I never got. I would get the torn place back. I would, at forty-one, finally become the girl whose father left — and lose, in the same swallow, the woman I built on not being her.

*To forgive her is to be unmade back into the child she tried to make.*

—

This is the thing I have to sit with on the floor for a long time, longer than the tape, which has run out — there was nothing after *the daughter,* she ended it there, on the door, she let the machine spool down into hiss and then into the soft mechanical knock of the end of the reel, and I have not turned it off, I am letting it knock, *tk, tk, tk,* because turning it off would mean it is over and I am not ready for the last thing my mother will ever say to me to be over.

Here is the thing I have to sit with.

I have wanted, my whole life, exactly two things, and I have spent my whole life believing they were enemies. I have wanted to be *free* of her — of the trade, the town, the ledger, the wound, the nineteen-year argument I lost the day she died unable to be argued with. *Bury her and sell the building and never see this town again.* That is the want I drove eleven hours on. And under it, the want I would not say even inside my own skull, the real one: I have wanted to be told, by something I cannot argue with, that what I did to her *mattered* — that I was real enough, that day at twenty-two, to truly wound her — because if I wasn't, then her nineteen years of grief were nothing, and if they were nothing then I was nothing, never important enough to leave a mark. I have wanted to be released and I have wanted to be guilty, and I have built my whole self on the war between them.

And my mother, dead, on a cassette tape, from inside the trade, has just shown me that they are not at war. They are the same act. The rite she is asking me to do — eat her, forgive her, close the account, go free — would release me from all of it in one swallow, the cleanest, most total release there is. And it would, in the same motion, take the wound, take the mattering, un-happen the thing I did and the thing she did and the nineteen years between, fill the father-hole with a grief that would overwrite the woman who never grieved, and leave standing in my place a lighter, emptier Nona with no two months of this and no Pearl and no Ray and no idea, none, that she was ever the keeper of anything. The thing I want most and the thing I fear most are the same swallow. And the only proof I was ever loved enough to be wounded by *is the wound* — the warmth she spent, the father she ate, the whole subtracted shape of a childhood I have read my whole life as her not loving me, and that turns out to be the largest act of love anybody has ever done for me.
