I am not a girl whose mother didn't love her. I am a girl whose mother loved her so much she ate herself hollow to spare her, and then could not say so, because she had spent the soft words on me before I could talk.

She tried to say it instead. Her whole life was *instead.*

—

That is the thing the tape gives me that I did not have, and it is worse than the father and worse than the warmth, it is the smallest thing on the tape and it is the one that finishes me.

My mother never said she was sorry. Not once, not in nineteen years, not on the tape — listen to it again, I have, I will, there is no *sorry* anywhere on it, she says *I would do it again,* she says *I've never been sure if it was a mercy or the cruelest thing,* she says *it's yours now,* and she never once says *I'm sorry,* the same as me, exactly the same as me, I have not said it since I was twenty-two and she did not say it for the last forty years of her life, and we are not two cold women, we are two women who could not get the word out and did the only other thing we knew how to do with it.

I have a trade phrase. *I'm sorry for your loss.* A coin you hand across a counter; I have handed it to Della and to Carla and to Ma Coyle and felt nothing, because it is not the word, it is the change you give instead of the word. And my mother had a trade too, the bigger one, the one under the funerals, and I have spent two months thinking the trade was a thing she did *for the town,* and it was, but the note and the tape have just shown me what it also was. It was her *I'm sorry for your loss,* scaled up to a sacrament. A woman who could not say the word, eating the weight off the dead so the living wouldn't have to carry it — that is the largest *instead of sorry* there has ever been. Loy had it almost right and didn't know what he was holding. *You don't say sorry over them, hon. You eat it and you say the words. That's the mercy. That's instead of sorry.* He thought he was telling me about the dead. He was telling me about my mother. Her whole life was *instead of sorry* — every body she ate, every memory she spent, the father she lifted out of my life, the warmth she traded for my safety, the note in my pocket, the tape under the false floor of a fireproof box, *for Nona* scratched in ballpoint — every bit of it was a woman who could not say the four smallest words in the language saying them the only way she had, which was to *erase the need for them.* She did not say sorry. She tried to un-happen the thing she'd be sorry for. That is the whole trade. That is the whole of her. *That is the whole of it.*

And she is asking me, with her last words, to do it to her.

—

I turn off the tape. *Tk* — and then nothing, the speaker dead, the hiss gone, the room as quiet as it was before her voice was in it, which is a quiet I now know I will never have again, because there is a before-the-tape Nona and an after-the-tape Nona and the second one knows a thing the first one would have given anything not to know, which is that the woman she has hated for nineteen years was the only person who ever loved her without wanting one single thing back, and proved it by giving up the very thing — the warmth, the words — that would have let Nona *feel* it as love, and so loved her invisibly, hollowly, in a register no child could read, and died believing her daughter found her disgusting, when the truth was that her daughter found her *cold,* and the cold was the temperature of the wound she took for me.

I take the note out of my apron pocket. I have carried it against the place where the bread goes for two months. I unfold it on its own creases, the failing hand, *The daughter eats the mother. That is the whole of it.* I have read it as a curse. *Make my disgusted daughter swallow me.* The cruelest thing.

It is not a curse. It is a door. It is a mother who could not say *I forgive you for leaving, I forgive you for the bus, I forgive you for the nineteen years, and I am sorry, I am so sorry, I am sorry I couldn't be warm, I am sorry the price of your safety was your mother* — a mother who could not say a word of that, who had spent the soft words on me before I could talk, saying it the only way she had left: *come home, and be free of me, even though it will cost you yourself; I would rather you forget I existed than keep carrying what I did to you; the daughter eats the mother; that is the whole of it; that is, it turns out, the whole of love.*

And I am sitting on the floor of a funeral home with the cold coming up through me and a dead tape in my lap and her note in my hand, and I understand, finally, what I am being asked to do, and what it will cost, and that the cost and the gift are the same, and that the only way to refuse the most generous thing my mother ever offered me is to *keep the wound* — to stay the cold daughter of a cold mother who weren't either of them cold, to leave her account open the way she left it open, to carry her instead of eating her, to remember the father-shaped hole and the warmth she spent and the whole subtracted shape of myself, forever, unhealed, true — and that to do that, to refuse her last mercy and keep her real, I will have to do the one thing I have not done since I was twenty-two, the thing she could not do for forty years, the thing this whole house has been built around the absence of.

I will have to be able to say I'm sorry.

And I sit there in the after-the-tape quiet, with the note in my hand and the smell of her open account in the room and the burn-window running on her body in the cooler, and I reach for the word, just to see, just to check, the way a keeper checks — I reach down into the place where the word goes in a normal person, and I find what I found on the floor at Pearl and what I found in the doorway at Della and what I have found every time for nineteen years.

It is not there. The place is smooth. She spent her soft words on me; I do not know what I spent mine on, only that they are gone the same as hers, and that I am going to have to find them, somewhere, in time, against a clock that is eating my reasons and a girl down the hall the trade would love me to feed it and a man up the ridge who could solve all of it by forgetting — I am going to have to find a word I have not been able to reach since I was twenty-two, and say it, over the body of a woman I am already, this morning, beginning to forget, before the fire takes my last chance to keep her real.

The reel knocks once more in the dead machine, *tk,* though there is nothing left on it to play.

# Chapter Twenty-Two

## An Easier Girl

There is a way out of the table, and I find it the morning after I learn what my mother did to me, which is the kind of timing the trade has, the kind I have stopped pretending is coincidence.

I find it the way I find everything now — in the books. I am at the prep-room desk before light because I did not sleep, because the keystone in my hand will not let me, the note that has meant *make my disgusted daughter swallow me* for the whole length of my coming home and now means its opposite, *be free of me,* and a woman cannot lie down inside a sentence that has turned over like that. So I do what I do. I go to the ledger to make it hold still.

I am reading the oldest book, the hymnal-sized one in the cramped Welsh hand, looking for I-don't-know-what, looking for company, looking for some dead Pruitt woman who left a margin that says *here is how you bear it.* And on a page from before the camp had its name, in ink gone the brown of old blood, one of them has written, not a price, not an account, but an instruction. A recipe of a different kind. *To make another to it,* it says. *She must be willed it by the keeper, and she must eat her first off the keeper's own hand, and then the burden passes and the first is freed.*

I read it three times.

She must be willed it. She must eat her first off the keeper's own hand. Then the first is freed.

The first is freed.

I sit there in the one good light with the cold coming up through the floor and I understand that I have been wrong about the shape of the trap. I have spent two weeks telling myself there is no exit, *no exit,* I wrote it in this very book in these very words, the way my mother wrote it before me — and there is an exit, there has been one the whole time, it has been making me taquitos at three in the morning and laughing at my jokes in a room full of bodies, it is nineteen years old and afraid of nothing and it is asleep right now in the back seat of a Cavalier with a cracked windshield because its aunt changed the locks.

The line takes by the eating. Not by the blood. Loy said it, the ledger says it, I have known it since the second week and filed it the way you file a thing you are not yet monster enough to want. *Wren could be made.* I could will her the trade and lay the bread on the first body and put her hand to it and say the words over the two of us, and the burden would pass, and I would be freed — I would not have to eat my mother, I would not have to be unmade back into the easier child, I could hand off the green book and the recipe sheet and the fireproof box and the ghost-letter sign and drive eleven hours and never smell a struck match again.

The relief of it goes through me like the swallow. That is the part I want to be honest about. It is not a thought I have and recoil from. It is a *relief,* clean and total, the obscene unweighting, *here, set it down, you have carried it long enough,* and I sit in it for one whole second the way you sit in a hot bath, and then I am afraid of how good it felt, ashamed first and afraid second, in that order, always that order now, and I cap the pen I am not even writing with and I press the heels of my hands into my eyes until I see the lights.

Then I do the thing I have learned to do instead of feeling. I make a list.

—

What I am, when I let myself look at it without the warm word in front of it, is a woman who has just discovered she can survive by feeding the only person who loves her to the thing that ate her mother.

I write that down too. Not in those words. In the green book, the fourth book, the one that is only mine, the ledger of myself, *what I think I had* on the left and *what is there now* on the right — except this morning I turn past the losses to the back, to the page where I started the will, *I will not eat her, reasons, for the day I cannot feel them,* one and two and the blank third I would not write, and I do not add a reason yet. I add the temptation. Because a keeper of records does not get to pretend she did not think a thing. I write, in my own hand, *There is a way out. It is Wren.* And I look at it sitting there in ink, true, recorded, mine, and it is the ugliest sentence in a book that has the word *drowning* in it in three different hands.

The cooler runs. The county's clock runs. Somewhere up the Coon Branch road a man named Press Hatcher is forgetting himself while he's still alive because I half-ate the woman who ruined him, and I have a body coming this afternoon, a real one, a small one, because the dead do not stop for a daughter's reckoning, and I will have to decide about it too, and the deciding takes a piece of me every time, and the pieces are running out, and the reasons are made of the same stuff the pieces are made of.

That is the math. That is the whole math. Every day I keep this place running to get to my mother's deadline, I have to eat, and every time I eat I lose a little more of the somewhere-else that holds the argument, and the easiest way to stop losing myself is to give the losing to Wren.

I have never in my life wanted to be a worse person than I want to be at six o'clock on this particular morning, and the want is dressed, head to foot, as exhaustion.

—

The body that comes at four is Ada Sturgill, eighty-eight, off the ridge above Cinder Bottom, dead in a chair with her feet in a dishpan of cold water because the gout, the daughter says, the gout was a torment to her at the end. The daughter is sixty herself and worn down to the gristle and she signs for the basic cremation and the four-hundred-dollar urn, the *Slate,* I could pour them out of a mold now, and she says her mother was a hard old woman and a fine fiddle hand and there will not be a viewing because there is nobody left who'd come.

I take Ada in and I read her in the cooler dark the way I do now, at the door, before the bread, because I have learned to flinch from the heavy ones and I want to know what I'm holding before it's in my hands.

She is not heavy. She is a small mean weight, a single thing, old and dry, and when I lay my hand flat on the cold sternum it comes up the way they come up now, not a vision, not a voice, the cold knowing, and what Ada Sturgill carried to her chair with the dishpan is this: she was the last of a line of fiddle-makers, her people made instruments in a shop up that hollow for a hundred years, and she had a grandson with the gift in his hands, the real gift, the kind that skips three generations and lands on one child, and he wanted it, he came to her at twelve and asked her to teach him, and she would not. Out of spite. Because his mother — her daughter, the worn one signing the urn paper right now — had married a man Ada hated and moved to town and let the shop go cold, and Ada decided that if she could not have her line the way she wanted it she would let it die in her hands rather than pass it down the wrong branch. She sent the boy away. She told him his hands were clumsy, which was a lie, which she knew was a lie, and she let him believe it, and he grew up and put the gift down because his own great-grandmother had told him he didn't have it, and the shop is a chicken house now and there will not be another fiddle-maker on that ridge, not ever, because Ada Sturgill would rather have burned the inheritance than let it go to a daughter who left.

I take my hand off her chest.

I stand in the cold and I do not move for a while.

Because it is mine. Of course it is mine. The trade has a sense of humor and it is the cruelest one God ever made, and it has sent me, on this morning, the exact body — a woman who would rather let the line die in her hands than pass it down the branch that broke her heart. *That is the question on my own table.* That is the thing I read three times in the Welsh hand before light: *she must be willed it,* the keeper choosing the daughter, the inheritance going on — or not. Ada chose *not.* Ada chose to let it end with her, out of spite, and ruined a child to do it, and called the ruin a clumsiness, and never told him the truth, *instead of* telling him the truth, and the boy carries to this day a flinch at the thing he loved and does not know his own great-grandmother put it there.

That is the whole machine. That is the line, that is every line, mothers and grandmothers deciding what the children will be allowed to carry, telling them it's for their own good, telling them their hands are clumsy, *eating it so the child would not have to,* and the child grows up with a flinch and a hole and no idea who reached in and made the shape.

Esther on one side of it. Me on the other, deciding right now, today, with a fiddle-maker cold on my table, whether to do to Wren what Ada did to the boy — only worse, only the exact reverse and the exact same, because Ada refused the gift to spite the line and I would *give* it to spite the trap, and either way a child ends up holding a weight a grown woman chose for her and called a mercy.

I do not eat Ada Sturgill yet. I have a thing to do first.

—

Wren wakes up around nine and comes in the side door with a bag from the Marathon and her hair doing the thing it does, and she throws the bag on the prep-room counter where I will tell her a hundred times not to throw food and she will throw it there a hundred more, and she says, "Okay, so, you cannot tell me you don't want one of these, they had the good ones, the green-sauce ones, I got you three because I know you say one and then you eat mine."

"Sit down off the steel," I say, because she is up on the body table, swinging her boots, and there is no body on it but it is the principle, and she slides off and onto the stool and unwraps a taquito and the grease prints through the paper and the prep room smells, for once, of something a living person made for another living person, and I take the one she pushes at me because she is right, I will say one and eat hers.

This is the part I have to tell straight, because it is the part that damns me.

I watch her eat. I watch her talk — she is telling me about the cosmetology thing, she has a brochure, Mountain State has a program over in Beckley, ten months and you come out with a license, you could go to Charleston, you could go to *Pittsburgh,* she heard a girl from Welch went to Pittsburgh and does color for rich women and clears sixty a year, and the brochure has been through the wash once already, the smiling woman with the foils gone soft and gray at the creases — and the whole bright running time of it, I am measuring her.

I cannot stop. I am doing the arithmetic with the front of my face listening and the back of it cold, the way I read a body, and the arithmetic comes out the same every way I run it: she is unafraid of the dead, she has nowhere to go, the aunt changed the locks and Dakota is in jail and she sleeps in the car and she needs the cash. She is exactly the kind of girl the trade was built to take.

I am sitting in my mother's funeral home eating a taquito a child bought me with money she does not have, and I am pricing her.

"You're not even listening," she says.

"Pittsburgh," I say. "Sixty a year. The girl from Welch."

"You hate when I have a plan." She points the taquito at me. "You get this face."

"I don't have a face."

"You have a *whole* face. You got it right now. It's the face like — like when I say I'm gonna do a thing, you go all —" she makes her own face flat and dead and severe, an Esther-in-the-photograph face, my face, I suppose, that is apparently my face — "and I can't tell if you think it's stupid or if you think I can't do it."

"I don't think you can't do it."

"Okay, well, the face."

And here is the thing, here is the thing I will have to live with: she is right that I have a face, and she is wrong about what's in it. She thinks the face is doubt. The face is not doubt. The face is a woman doing a sum that ends in this girl's whole bright soft future getting closed up smooth as water over a stone, and the face is me, in the middle of the sum, *liking the sound of Pittsburgh,* because Pittsburgh means she gets out, she gets out the way I got out, on a bus and a plan and a license to make rich women beautiful — and a child who gets out is a child you can let yourself love without it costing anybody anything, and a child you can let yourself love is exactly the wrong child to weigh as an exit, and I am doing both at once, loving her *and* weighing her, in the same breath, off the same warm bag of food, and I do not know which is the lie.

That is the trade's last and best joke. It does not make you stop loving the person. It makes you love them and price them at the same time and feel the love as the reason the price would work. *She trusts me.* That's the warm part and the useful part and they are the same part. She would put her hand to a body if I told her it was an old Welsh thing that doesn't do anything, because I told her that once and she did not believe me but she did not stop coming in the side door with the green-sauce ones either. She would let me will her this. She would say yes. The yes is in her face the way the flinch is in Brandy's, the way the gift was in the fiddle-boy's hands, a thing a grown woman could use, sitting right there, asking nothing.

"You went somewhere," Wren says, quieter. She has stopped chewing. She is looking at me the careful way she looks at me now, the way you look at a person who's worn thin, the way she's looked at me since the carriage-house floor. "You do that now. You go off behind your eyes."

"I'm tired, Wren."

"You're always tired. This is different tired." She wraps the rest of the taquito back up, which she never does, which means I have scared her a little. "You're not gonna — like. You're okay, right? You're not gonna do something."

"Do something."

"I don't know. People get a face like yours and then they do something." She is nineteen and she has buried more people she loved than I had at nineteen and she knows the face, she knows it from the methadone window and the GoFundMe corkboard and her own mother who went to the Ark and didn't take, she knows the face that comes before somebody does something, and she is sitting on a stool in a prep room asking the woman who's been teaching her the dead whether the woman is about to become one of them, and she has it exactly backwards, and I cannot tell her that the something I might do is to her.

"I'm not going to do something," I say.

It is the truest sentence I have said all morning and I have to fight my mouth to get it out, because some animal part of me is still doing the sum, still has the relief of it warm in my chest, and the only way I make the sentence true is by deciding, right then, on the stool, with the grease cooling on the paper, to make it true.

—

I take her up to the photo wall. I don't plan to. I'm walking her out to her car because she has a shift cleaning at the Dollar General and she'll be late, and we pass the narrow upstairs hall on the way to the back stairs because the downstairs bath is temperamental and she wanted to wash the grease off, and she stops, the way people stop, in front of the framed things.

"That's you," she says, at the one of Esther at thirty holding the baby that is me, in front of the sign when the sign still had its paint. "You were a ugly baby. No offense."

"None taken. It's the only one."

"The only — what, picture? Of you as a baby?"

"That's the only one."

She looks at it a while. She is good at this, at looking, she'd be good at the reading if the reading were a thing you could teach, which it isn't, it's a thing you have to be born into and then have your mother spend on the town. "There's no daddy in any of these," she says. Not like a question. Like a girl who grew up in this county and knows what an empty wall says. "It's all the women."

"It's all the women."

"& Daughters," she says, reading the sign in the photo, the ghost of the paint. "But it's just you."

"It's just me."

And she says, light, not knowing, swinging back toward the stairs, "You oughta train somebody. Before you sell it. Like a — what do you call it. So it don't just stop with you." She says it the way you'd say *you oughta get that gutter looked at.* She says it to the most dangerous question in my life as if it were a chore I'm putting off, and she does not know, she does not know, she is nineteen and she is offering, without the first idea she's offering, to be the daughter on the sign, to keep & Daughters from being a lie, and the trade put those words in her mouth, I would swear it, the trade reached up through the floor of this house and used her like a puppet to say the one thing that would make it easy — *you oughta train somebody, so it don't just stop with you.*

"It stops with me," I say.

Something in how I say it makes her turn around on the stairs. I have not said it the way you say a thing about a building. I have said it the way you say a thing you have just this second decided and will die on.

"Okay," she says, slow. "Geez. It stops with you. Whatever, lady." But she is looking at me with the careful look, filing it, the way I file things, because she is more like me than is good for either of us, and that — that right there, the way she clocks the weight in a sentence and puts it in a drawer for later — that is the gift in her hands, the reading-gift, the thing that would make her a keeper, and it is the exact thing I will spend the rest of this trade making sure nobody ever lays a bread in front of.

She goes. The Cavalier turns over on the third try the way it does. I stand in the upstairs hall in front of the only baby picture of me and the wall with no father on it, and I understand that I have just, on a staircase, over a bag of gas-station taquitos, ended the line.

Not decided to. Ended it. The way you don't decide to set a bone, you just hear it go.

—

Here is what it costs, so you know it costs.

It costs me the only exit. The third door was Wren, and I have just nailed it shut with my own hand, on purpose, because the only way to open it was to push her through first.

And the worst of it, the part the trade is counting on, is that it leaves me exactly two doors and both of them go through my mother's table, and I am running out of the self that knows why I won't take the one with peace behind it.

So I go back down and I eat Ada Sturgill, because the dead do not stop, because the county is coming for the chair-and-dishpan paperwork, because if I am going to refuse the easy way I have to keep the hard way running, and keeping it running means feeding it. I bake the bread — my hands know it, they have known it since the third night, they fold and press and turn and thumb-score thin as you can while the rest of me watches from a distance like a woman watching her own hands on a security camera. I lay the morsel on the small dry sternum where the weight sits. I let the cold draw it up. I say the words, low, the naming and the taking and *go thou light, the books are balanced, go on now, go on,* and I think, the way I cannot stop thinking it now, *instead of sorry* — instead of Ada ever telling that boy his hands were not clumsy, instead of the truth, here is the deletion, here is the mercy that erases the one it's for.

I eat it.

The unweighting comes, the clean obscene lightness, *set it down,* and underneath it, at the swallow, the price goes out of me, and this one I feel — I have learned to feel them now, I could not at first and now I can, which is its own loss — I feel something pulled clean out of the somewhere-else, weight for weight, the inheritance-she-killed for an inheritance of mine, and I do not know yet what it is, you never know in the moment, there is only the shape of the going.

Ada burns light and odorless, like paper, the way they do when they're eaten. I log her. Four pounds and one ounce. I spell her name right.

It is when I go to write the account in the green book that I find out what it took.

I write *Ada Sturgill — the gift she would not pass; the boy; the spite,* in the left column, and I go to the right column to put the price, because I make myself write the price now, I decided that two weeks ago, it is the one thing I will not let the trade take, my willingness to write down what it takes — and I reach for what's missing, the way you reach with your tongue for the tooth, and I cannot find it, you never can, a deletion does not leave a gap you can read. So I do the other thing. I go looking for it. I go through myself like a robbed house, room by room, *what is here, what should be here,* and I get to the music.

I should be able to read music. I know that I know this the way you know a phone number you can't say. There is a thing in me — there was a thing in me — where the dots on the lines turn into sound, where I look at the marks and hear them, a small competence, a leftover, somebody taught me young, before town, before the bus, when I was a child in this house. And I go and I get it, the proof, because I keep everything: in the bottom of the prep-room desk there is a shape-note hymnal, the old kind, the four-shapes, *fa sol la,* that my grandmother sang out of and my mother kept, and I have looked at it a hundred times and heard it, the way you hear a hymn off a page if you have the trick of it.

I open it.

The page is full of little shapes on lines. Triangles and ovals and squares sitting on a ladder of bars. They are pretty. They mean nothing. They are *ink.* I look at the marks where the sound used to come up off them and there is no sound, there is no door, there is just a child's old songbook full of dots arranged by somebody for a reason I can see the shape of and cannot enter, and I turn the pages and it is all gone, every page, the whole language, a thing I could do this morning and cannot do tonight, and the worst part, the records-keeper part, is that I can still see *that it was a language* — I know these marks meant a held note, a quick one, a place to breathe — I just cannot hear a single one of them anymore, and I do not know who sang them to me, or in what kitchen, or whether it was a warm thing, because the rite did not just take the reading.

It took whoever taught me.

There is a smooth-walled socket the exact size and shape of a person who once sat with a small me and a songbook and put the marks into sound in the air between us, and the socket is empty, and I will never know if it was my mother.

I sit with the hymnal open on the desk under the one good light. I do not cry, which I notice, the way I notice everything, the way I have started to be afraid of — that I went looking for a thing my whole childhood is hung on and found it gone and the river of me did not come, because the river comes from the somewhere-else and the somewhere-else is what I have been spending, account by account, on this town's buried filth, and tonight I spent a fiddle-maker's spite and it cost me a song my mother — or someone, *someone,* I will never know — once taught a child who would grow up unable to remember being taught a single soft thing.

I think of Wren saying *you went somewhere, you go off behind your eyes,* and I think, that is where I went. I went looking for the music and found a hole where the music's mother was.

—

I write it down before I lose the *that* of it the way I lost the *what.*

In the green book, the right column, against *Ada Sturgill:* *Reading music. Gone. The hymnal is just shapes now. Whoever taught it to me is gone with it, and I do not know if it was her.* Weight for weight. The inheritance she killed for an inheritance of mine.

And then I turn to the back, to the will, to *I will not eat her, reasons, for the day I cannot feel them,* one and two and the blank third, and I add the fourth, because I have earned it today, on a staircase, with a bag of taquitos:

*4. Because the trade goes on by mothers feeding their daughters to it and calling it & Daughters, and I could be free tonight if I fed Wren to it, and I will not, the line stops with me, even if it costs me everything the long way, because a thing that survives by eating its children is not a thing that should survive, and if the only way to end it is to be the last one it eats, then I will be the last one it eats. Do not let it tell you, when you can't feel this anymore, that Wren would have said yes. She would have said yes. That is exactly why.*

I read it back. It holds. It is the first reason I have written that I am not afraid will thin out from under me, because it is not made of the wound, it is made of the girl, and the girl is real and bright and asleep right now in a Cavalier with a cosmetology brochure soft from the wash in her pocket, and as long as I keep her real I can keep the reason, and as long as I keep the reason I can keep the door nailed shut.

The third reason is still blank. I leave it blank. I know now that it is the keystone, the answer my mother left white in her one open account for forty years, *what was done, and the one it was done to, and that I took it so the child would not have to carry him,* the child being me, the *him* being a man I have no memory of because she ate him, and I cannot write my third reason until I have written what she did, and I cannot write what she did until I can bear it, and I do not know if I will be able to bear it before the clock runs out and I forget I ever wanted to.

I cap the pen. The retort is cooling on Ada Sturgill. There is no music in this house and there never will be again and I am the only one who knows there ever was. The county's clock is running on the woman in my cooler. Two doors left and both go through her table, and I have spent today making sure of it, choosing the hard one on purpose, for the love of a girl who offered me the easy one without knowing what she was holding out.

I should feel that as grief. I price it, instead, the way I priced her, the way the trade has taught me to price everything now — and find that I cannot quite, that the part of me that would have wept over a closed door has gone the way the music went, and that the flat dry sum I do instead is the surest sign yet that I am running out of the woman who would refuse, faster than I am running out of road.

Upstairs, on the wall, in the only photograph of me that exists, my mother holds a baby in front of a sign that says & Daughters, and neither of them is smiling, and now I am the only daughter there will ever be, and I have just made certain of it, and somewhere behind my own eyes a kitchen is going quiet that I will never get to remember was warm.

I go to bed not singing a thing I used to be able to sing, and not knowing it, which is the mercy, which is the cost, which is, I am learning, the same.

# Chapter Twenty-Three

## What Ray Could Forget

Ray Sennett keeps his wife in a closet, and I know this because he tells me, the way a man tells you the one true thing after an hour of telling you everything around it.

He has come at dark with a sack of Ruby Tuesday because he heard I had not been eating, which is a thing he could only have heard from Wren, which means the two of them have a back channel about me now, which I would be angrier about if I had the room. I do not have the room. I have given the room to the ledger and to a green book with my reasons in it, and there is not much left of me to give to a man on the porch with a bag going cold in his hand, but I open the door, because he is the one person in the county I would open it for, and that is its own kind of warning I am too tired to heed.

We eat at my mother's table. He has got me the chicken thing with the broccoli he has decided I like, and he is right, and I do not tell him he is right, and we eat for a while without the case in the room, which is new. For two months the case has come into this house ahead of him like a dog off its lead. Tonight it stays out in the truck. Tonight there is just a tired man and a tired woman and the small wet sound of two people eating who have both forgotten how to do it for company.

"I burned Carol on a Tuesday," he says, out of nothing, looking at his plate. "Your mama did it. Eleven years back. I didn't know that's what this place was, then. I thought it was just where you go." He turns the fork over. He has a way of turning a thing in his hands that I have watched before, that is the exact thing his hands do instead of the thing his chest is doing, and tonight it is the fork. "She did it right. I want you to know that. Whatever else. Whatever's — wrong here. Your mama did my wife right. She come out to the truck after and she didn't say a soft word, not one, and she put her hand flat on the hood next to mine and we stood there. Two minutes, maybe. Never said a thing. And it was the only — " He stops. "It was the only time anybody got it right that whole year."

"She wasn't much for the soft words," I say. Which is the truest thing I have about my mother and the smallest, and I hand it to him across the table like a coin.

"No." He almost smiles. "No, you don't say."

—

I want to tell you what is happening in me while he eats his broccoli, because it is the worst thing in this chapter and it does not look like anything.

It looks like a woman listening to a man talk about his dead wife. That is all it looks like. And under it, in the place where I do my arithmetic, in the place where I have graded sins by the ounce and decided who keeps their weight, a thing has woken up and is doing sums, and the sums are these: *Carol Sennett is eleven years burned, and you cannot eat ash, fire is the seal — but the grief is not ash. The grief is in him, alive, a weight he has carried eleven years across a hood and a thousand suppers, and weight is the thing you read, weight is the thing you take. You could not eat Carol. But there might be a way to ease the man.*

I want to be honest that the thought is gentle. That is the horror of it now, two months in — the thoughts that come to me are not cruel, they have never once been cruel, they come wearing the face of mercy and they ask me, kindly, in my mother's kitchen, whether this good ruined man might be let down a little of his load. Whether there is a sin in him I could lift. Whether his thirty years of holding a dead girl he could not find, and his eleven years of holding a wife the cancer took slow — whether all of that keeping, that terrible keeping that has bent him like a man carrying a beam, might be a thing I could set down for him. Lighter. Cleaner. *Instead of.*

I do not finish the sentence this time. I know where it goes. I followed it all the way down after Pearl, link by link, and I am not going to walk it again over a man eating broccoli at my mother's table — I only have to feel how soft the offer is, how it comes to me not as a temptation but as a tenderness, the gentlest thing in the room, to know that to ease Ray of his keeping would not be to comfort him. It would be to reach into the one man in this county who has refused his whole life to let the world decide a girl was never taken, and to make him decide it. To unbuild the only church he has. To take his grief and call it a mercy and leave him *light,* the wrong way, the new way, a man in a clean empty house who used to keep a wife and a girl and now keeps, easily, smilingly, nothing.

I do not have the bread baked. There is no body. I could not do it tonight if I wanted to, and the rite does not work on a living man's grief the way it works on a corpse's sin, I think, I am not sure, I have never tried, and that not-sureness is the most dangerous sentence in this house, because two months ago I would never have wondered. Two months ago I would have been sick. Tonight I am only tired, and the thought is gentle, and I have to set down my own fork and put both my hands flat on my mother's table, next to where his were, where his are, and breathe.

He notices. Of course he notices. He has watched a thousand faces tell their small lies.

"You all right."

"No," I say. "But I'll say it."

He nods, slow, like that is an answer he has heard before, in this house, from a woman who didn't say a soft word. And he lets it lie.

—

He tells me about the closet to fill the quiet, and it undoes me, and he does not know it does.

"I keep her up in the front closet," he says. "The box. People think you scatter them or you put them on the mantel and you look at them, but I couldn't do the mantel. I tried the mantel. It's like she's watching you not be all right. So she's in the closet, top shelf, in the box your mama gave her back in, and her good coat's on the rail under her, the camel one, and her church shoes are on the floor, and it's — " He shrugs, embarrassed, a big man embarrassed at his own tenderness. "It's not a shrine. I don't go in there and pray at it. I just. I leave her things where her things go. I keep her closet a closet. That's all. Eleven years. I'll be cleaning it out, every year I say I'll clean it out, and I open the door and the coat's there and I think, not yet. You keep a person in the small ways. The closet's just where I keep her."

And I think: he keeps her in a closet the way I keep the dead in a notebook, the way Verna Coyle keeps a girl in the one upright thing she has — *somebody took her, she didn't leave me on purpose* — and the rite I have been running for two months would walk into all three closets and leave them clean. Della's closet is already clean. Della scattered a gentle husband up a fire road and came home light. The cleanest closets in this county are the ones I made.

"Ray," I say. And I do not know I am going to say it until it is most of the way out. "If somebody could take it off you. The keeping. Carol, the girl, all of it. If somebody could reach in and make it so you never had to carry any of it, so you'd wake up tomorrow and the closet'd just be a closet, coats and shoes and no weight in it, no Tuesday, no two minutes on the hood, none of it — would you let them."

He sets the fork down. He looks at me a long time. He is too good at this, he is the best in the county at this, and I watch him understand that I am not asking a thing out of nowhere, that the question has a floor under it I am standing on, and I watch him decide not to ask me what the floor is. Yet.

"No," he says.

Just that. Flat. The way I say the small true things.

"It's the worst thing I've got," he says. "Carol going slow. The girl. Thirty years up that hollow with Verna waiting on me Sunday after Sunday and me with nothing for her. It's the worst thing I've got and I'd fight you for it. I'd fight anybody for it." He leans back. "You know what the keeping is, Nona? It's the last of them I've got. You take the weight off me, you haven't done me a kindness, you've finished the job the cancer started. Carol's gone. The keeping's not gone. The keeping's the part of her that's still got me to keep it. You'd be killing the only part that's left." He shakes his head, slow. "Lord. Where'd that come from. You got somebody trying to talk you into something?"

"Yeah," I say. "Me."

—

So I tell him.

Not all of it. I want that on the record, because I am a keeper of records and even now, even broken open, I do the accounting: I do not give him the road past Number Nine, I do not give him the strip cut, I do not hand him Dana Coyle laid under thirty years of a company's own dirt, because that one is not mine to spend, that one belongs to a man already in ash and a mother already denied and I will not buy Ray's peace of mind with Verna's last upright thing any more than I'd eat it.

But I tell him the shape. I tell him it does not carry, it deletes. I tell him the men whose names went vague in his file went vague because they came through my fire, or my mother's, and the sin went out of the world and took its facts with it, and his thirty years went down the throat of it because his thirty years were *built on* those facts and you cannot keep the steeple when the church is gone. I tell him he is not losing his mind. I told him that already, on the cliff, and I did not go over, and tonight I go over, I walk off the edge I have stood on for two months, and I tell the one man alive who would believe the true thing and choose to keep it that the reason his life's work is dissolving in his hands is that I have been dissolving it, body by body, mercy by mercy, and that I cannot stop, and that the last body is in my cooler and it is my mother and the bread is going to come for her or it is going to come for me.

He does not move while I talk. He has interviewed killers. He knows how to let a person get all the way to the end of a thing. He lets me get to the end.

And when I am done he is quiet for a long while, turning the empty water glass now, around and around, and then he says the thing I did not know I needed and could not have asked for.

"Then I'm not crazy," he says. "And I'm not gonna lose her."

"Ray, you don't — the case is gone. Ferry's gone. There's hardly a case left to — "

"I'm not talking about the case." He sets the glass down. "I'm talking about the girl. The case is paper. The case was always going to die with me. But Dana — " His jaw works. "You just told me the most useful thing anybody's told me in thirty years, and you don't even know it. You told me she was real. You told me there's a true version, and the true version is still true even though the paper went soft, even though the world rewrote it, even though I can't prove a word. There's somebody who *remembers.* You. There's a true version sitting in your head where the fire can get it but the lawyers can't, and that's — Nona, that's everything. That's the whole — that's the only thing I ever wanted. Not to convict him. I'm past convicting. Just to know it stayed true *somewhere.*"

—

And so we do the thing the rite would have made impossible: we agree to keep it.

He will keep the case open. Not to solve it — there is nothing left to solve, the road is gone and Ferry is six pounds of cool ash I burned heavy on purpose so the girl would stay taken. He will keep it open the way he keeps a coat on a rail under a box. He will be the second witness. I will be the first, for as long as I have a head to hold it in, which is the part neither of us says.

"You write it down," he tells me, before he goes. He has his hand on the door and he turns back, and he is fierce about it, fiercer than I have seen him, a man handing me the one tool he has. "Whatever you've got. The girl. The town. All of it. You write it down somewhere it can't get to. Because your memory's the thing that's going, you said so, and a thing in your head is a thing the fire can have. But a thing on paper — " He stops. He almost smiles, that sad way. "*The paper outlives you.* I spent thirty years cursing my paper for going soft and it took you to remind me what paper's *for.*"

I do not tell him I have known that since I was a child standing beside a man-shaped nothing I had no name for, building a wall against an erasure nobody had told me happened to me. I just say, "I write everything down."

"I know you do," he says. "I'm counting on it."

And then, because we are two people who have just said the largest things either of us has said to anybody in years, and because there is no surviving that without a door out of it, he says, "You gonna eat the rest of that broccoli, or you want me to feel bad about driving up here for nothing."

"I'll eat it."

"You won't."

"I'll eat it tomorrow."

"Tomorrow." He says it the way you say a word you don't believe, the way I say *temporary,* and he knows I hear it, and that is the whole joke and neither of us laughs because we are too tired and it lands anyway, the way the dry ones do, in the place under where a laugh would go. "You're a worse liar than you think you are, for a woman with a whole second life nobody knows about."

"I'm a real good liar," I say. "I've just stopped bothering with you."

He looks at me when I say it, and something moves across his big sad face, and I realize I have handed him a thing I did not mean to hand him, which is that he has become the person I do not lie to, in a county where I lie to everyone, including the dead, including myself in a green book at night. He does not make a thing of it. He is too kind to make a thing of it. He just nods, once, the way a man signs for a delivery, *received,* and he picks his keys up off my mother's table.

"Carol used to say I was a better cop than a husband," he says, at the door, "on account of I'd remember every word a stranger said in an interview and forget our anniversary. And I used to think that was a failing. The forgetting." He turns the keys over in his hand, the cup-turning thing, the counting thing. "Funny what you'd give now to have the kind of forgetting that's just a failing. The kind where the thing's still there, you just walked past it." He looks at me, and he is not talking about anniversaries. "You and me don't get that kind. We get the other kind. Where it's gone *out.* So." He shrugs. "So we write it down."

—

He goes at full dark, down off the ridge, and I stand on the porch until the sound of the truck is gone the way I stood on the first night, except on the first night I was alone with a mother in a cooler and tonight I am alone with a mother in a cooler and one other person in the county who knows the true thing and chose to hold it with me, and that is the difference, and the difference is everything, and the difference does not weigh a single pound and I can feel it like a hand on the hood.

I did not erase him.

I want to be plain about what that means, because it is the whole of what I did tonight and it does not look like anything, it looks like two people who didn't finish their supper. The rite would have made him clean. I could not have done it tonight, no bread, no body, maybe no way — but I had the thought, and the thought was gentle, and two months ago I'd have been on my knees over a sink, and tonight I only set my fork down. That is how far it has gone. That is the clock. And against the clock I did the one thing the rite cannot do and I have refused to do since I was twenty-two, except I did it sideways, the way I do everything: I let a person stay heavy. I let a man keep his dead. I kept a witness instead of deleting one. I chose to be known over being safe, by the one man with a badge who could end me, and he took the truth I gave him and he did not arrest it, he *kept* it, he made a second place in the world where the girl in the strip cut still got to have been taken.

There is a name for what that is. It is the opposite of the swallow. It is the thing Loy thinks the rite is and the thing the rite stopped being a hundred years ago and the thing I am going to have to find a way to do over my mother's body when the bread comes, with no one to consent and no Ray to hold it and almost nothing left in my head to hold it with.

I go in. I crouch at the box under the sink and I take out the green book, my own, *what I think I had* and *what is there now,* and I turn past the holes to the back, to the will I am making that is not about the building, and I find the list. *I will not eat her. Reasons, for the day I cannot feel them.* One, two, and the third left open the way she left her one account, the left side reaching, the right side white.

I read the first two over before I add to them, the way you'd read back a statement to a witness, and a cold thing happens that has not happened to me before in this book, and I write it down because it is the thing I have to bring with me to the table.

I read reason number two — *because if I eat her I forgive her, and if I forgive her there was never anything to forgive, and if those years were nothing then I was nothing* — and it is true, I can see that it is true, I can follow the chain of it link by link the way you follow a clean proof, and I cannot *feel* it. I cannot feel a single watt of it. It reads like a thing a smart, hurt woman wrote, a woman I would believe, a woman whose handwriting is my handwriting — and the heat that was in it when my hand made it three weeks ago is gone out of it the way the smell went out of the kitchen, the way the candy went, cleanly, leaving the shape and not the thing. I have the reason and not the wound. I have the red and not the sweet. The argument is on the page exactly as strong as the day I made it, and the woman reading it tonight is one fact emptier, and is going to be emptier still by the morning, and the page does not change and she does, and this is the whole machine, this is the thing Ray does not understand he handed me — *the paper outlives you,* yes, but it outlives you because you are the thing leaking out from under it, and one day soon I am going to stand at my mother's table and read these reasons the way a stranger reads another woman's letters, believing every word and feeling nothing, and I am going to have to act on a faith in a self that is no longer in the room.

So I do not fill in the third reason. I am still not ready. But I add a fourth, in my own hand, on a faith with no god under it, while I still can:

*4. Because Ray kept his. Because a man with a worse thing than mine chose to carry it rather than be made clean of it, and chose it in front of me, on purpose, tonight, and if he can keep a wife in a closet and a girl in a strip cut for the rest of a ruined life and call that the last of love, then I can keep a mother on a table, heavy and unforgiven and real, and call it the same.*

I cap the pen. The retort is cold. Somewhere up the Coon Branch road Press Hatcher is forgetting his own name while it is still his, and somewhere up another hollow Verna Coyle is keeping a girl nobody found, and out on the ridge a man is driving home to a closet he will not clean out, top shelf, the camel coat on the rail, *not yet, not yet,* and in my cooler at thirty-eight degrees the heaviest thing in the house is waiting for me to decide whether to keep her or set her free, which I have finally understood are not the same mercy and never were.

The bread is the last thing left to bake. I have put it off as long as the county will let me. I am out of bodies to practice my refusal on, and out of people to be known by, and out of days.

Tomorrow I bake it in her kitchen.

I do not know yet, standing here with a fourth reason fresh in the book and a witness driving home off the ridge, whether I will still know, by the time the loaf is cool, why I wrote the reasons down at all.

# Chapter Twenty-Four

## Esther's Kitchen

The recipe is one sheet, soft as cloth, and I have it flat on the counter under the same Pyrex measure my grandmother used, and I have read it four times now, and I am still standing here with my hands open at my sides like a woman who has forgotten what hands are for.

*Take of flour two handfulls, of the well-water, a little salt and no more.* The Welsh hand, rust gone brown. *Oven hot. Thin as you can. It must be plain.* Mam's rounder letters under it. And then the last ink, the newest, my mother's, the hand I would know in the dark, in a crowd, in my sleep: *plain is the whole of it. They don't need it good. They need it true.*

She wrote that for the dead. I never let myself wonder, before tonight, that she might have meant it for me.

I count the things on the counter to make the hands work. The flour. The salt cellar with the chipped lid. The Pyrex. The match. The bowl that is the color of a bruise going yellow, the bowl that has been in this kitchen longer than I have been alive, the bowl I do not remember and my hands do. Five. I count to five and the hands close and open and close, and on the third close they pick up the flour scoop because they know the way, they have always known the way, and I do not, and I have stopped, mostly, asking who taught them.

I know who taught them now. That is the new thing. That is the thing that makes tonight different from every other night I have stood at this counter and let my hands do the remembering my head couldn't.

She taught them. Before she ate whatever she ate that left the hole, before the kitchen went whatever it went that I can't reach — there was a woman in this room and a child at her elbow and the child learned the fold and the press and the turn, heel of the palm, thumb to score, *thin as you can,* and then the learning got taken out of me like a tooth and the doing stayed behind in the meat of my hands like a phantom, aching for a mouth it lost.

I am baking my mother's bread to put on my mother's chest.

—

The oven is the oldest thing in the house and it does not want to light. The pilot has been out — I let it go out, weeks ago, the night I logged the eleven days, I turned the gas down to nothing because I did not think I would ever stand here again with a reason to — and now I have to hold the match to the port and crank the knob and wait for the *whump,* the blue ring catching all at once with that sound like a held breath let go, and the warmth comes up off it, the dry hot rubber-and-metal breath of an old range working, and I wait for the rest of it.

The rest of it does not come.

There is supposed to be a smell. I know there is supposed to be a smell because I have the fact of it written down — *kitchen, mother's, bread and the gas pilot and the* — written in my own hand in the green book upstairs, in the column that says *what I think I had,* with nothing in the column beside it that says *what is there now,* because there is nothing there now, there is a clean blank, water closed over a stone. I paid that one for a man who hurt children. I know that the way I know a date on a certificate: as a thing the record asserts and the body cannot feel. I stand over the lit burner of my mother's stove and I breathe in on purpose, the way you test a wound to see if it's still there, and what I get is gas and hot iron and the cold mineral nothing of the rest of the house, and where the warm thing should be — where a whole childhood should be, by the testimony of my own pen — there is the smooth-walled socket the exact size and shape of a kitchen.

The worst part is not that it's gone.

The worst part is that I am the only one in the world who knows it was ever here, and the only proof I have is a sentence I wrote to outrun my own thieving, and tonight, for the first time, I find I am not certain I believe the sentence. I read it and it is in my hand and it is true and I cannot feel a thing under it, and a record you can't feel is a record you start, very quietly, to doubt — which is the whole of the horror, that the rite takes the warm thing and then comes back for the part of you that would have grieved it, and then comes back again for the part of you that would have believed your own report that there was ever anything to grieve.

I make the bread anyway. The hands make it. I stand behind them and watch.

—

Two handfuls of flour, but I weigh it, because I weigh everything, because somewhere a child learned to make a record of the world so the world could not — and the sentence has walked off that edge so many times now I no longer reach for where it goes. A hundred and forty grams. A little salt and no more; I know *no more* by the pinch, two fingers, the hand knows. Tap water, because the well in the back is a horror on a poisoned creek and nothing has to be anything. Work it quick. Bake it quick. Let it not rise.

It is the same as Carl's bread. It is the same as Verlie's, the same as the monster's, the same as Pearl's — Pearl's, which I baked gladly, believing, before I knew what gladness costs — and it is not the same as any of them, because those were strangers and this is the loaf I will lay on the breastbone of the woman who made the hands that are making it.

I have done this fourteen, fifteen times now. I have lost count of the loaves, which frightens me more than I will say, because losing the count of a thing is the first cold step of a thing going. I used to know to the gram. I used to know to the loaf. Now there is a stretch of the last weeks that has gone soft at the edges, gone the way the county's file on my mother had gone soft — *fewer facts in it than there were* — and the stretch that's soft is the stretch where I kept eating, because the bodies don't stop for a daughter's grief and the deadline doesn't stop and the home stays lit and *Pruitt & Daughters* stays a lie on the sign and somebody has to do the quiet work, and the somebody is me, and every time I do it I am a little less the woman who would have refused to.

There was a man this week. I have to think to find him. Press — no. I have to go to the book.

—

His name was Odell, and I had to look that up, in my own intake, in my own hand, *MULLINS, ODELL R.,* and the looking-up is the thing I want to set down here while I still can, because I think it matters and I am afraid I won't keep it.

He whitewashed a name off a wall. That was the sin. Sixty years ago there was a tipple and a strike and a man shot dead at the tipple gate, a union man, and the company painted the wall and Odell Mullins held the brush, nineteen and needing the work, and he painted over the dead man's name where the others had wrote it in pitch, painted it out clean, twice, three times, every time it came back he painted it out again until it stopped coming back, until the dead man was a blank wall the color of nothing, until there was no record left in the whole hollow that a man had stood there and been a man and been killed for it. He carried that. Sixty years. The brush in his hand. The name going under the white. He told nobody and the company gave him a steady job for his trouble and he died old in a clean house with the brush still in his hand, in the part of him where the weight sits.

I read it off the bread and I should have refused him. I see that now, with the loaf cooling on the rack and my mother in the cooler. A man who painted out a true name — that is the rite's own crime in a smaller hand, that is the thing I have been doing to this whole town, painting out the names, and I had a chance to let one stay and I ate it instead, because it was small and because I am tired and because the relief is a clean obscene lightness and I wanted, God help me, to set something down.

And the rite took its price, weight for weight, the way it does, the cruel matching way: it took a name.

Not just any name. *His.* The dead man's. The union man shot at the gate, the one Odell painted off the wall until there was no record left that he had stood there and been a man — his name came up to me out of the bread the way the names always come up, last, after the rest of the sin, a small plain mountain name that I had, for one held second at the steel, in my mouth and my mind whole, restored, given back from under sixty years of whitewash to the one person left in the world equipped to keep it. And then the swallow went down and it took the name with it. Not the man — I have the man, the brush, the wall, the white going over and over until the white won; I have the whole shape of the sin. What I do not have, what I went to write in the LEFT column an hour later and could not, is the one thing the sin was *about.* I sat with the pen over the page where I had already written *the painted-out name* and I went to write the name itself, the way I write all of them, the floor below which I do not go, a man's name spelled right — and there was a smooth-walled place the exact size and shape of a name, and the pen did not move. He painted a man to a blank wall. The rite reached into the keeper who would have kept him and painted him out a second time, clean, so that now there are two of us who erased him, Odell with a brush and me with my own mouth, and only I will ever know I did it, and I cannot even say the name of the thing I did it to.

I wrote down what I could. *Ch. — Mullins — the painted-out name — price: the dead man's name, gone. (I had it. I lost it in the swallow. The one I most needed to keep.)* I made myself write even that, the way I make myself write all of them now, because the writing is the one thing this trade has not yet taken, and the night it takes the writing is the night I am simply not here anymore and somebody else's hands are doing the fold and the press in a kitchen no one remembers.

But here is the thing I came down here, finally, to be honest about. I wrote the name down — or wrote that it was gone, which is all the writing I had left to do. I did not, that night, go upstairs and check the other book. The green book. The book where I have been keeping the reasons.

—

I keep it ruled down the middle, that book, like all of them, like the universe. On the right is everything I have lost that I could catch myself losing — the candy, the kitchen, the logs, the saving, the half of a man I loved at twenty-five whose letters I now read as a stranger's while my own hand flinches at his writing on the envelope. And on the left, on its own page, set apart, underlined twice because I was already afraid even then, is the sentence I wrote the night I refused to use Wren, the night I sealed myself into this: *I will not eat her. Reasons, for the day I cannot feel them.* And under it, numbered, in my own pen, the first two.

I go up the stairs with the loaf cooling behind me and I sit on the edge of the bed in the room that is still, obscenely, mine, and I open the green book to that page, and I read my own two reasons, and I wait to feel them.

The first one — Della happy, Brandy flinching at a ghost no one remembers — I can still get my hand on, barely. The long jaw, the sleeves pulled down, the wrist that was thickened and is now smooth. I feel that one the way you feel a bruise through a coat: there, but far, but enough.

The second one I have already read tonight, and read to Ray, and worn smooth as a doorknob — Pearl, the Ark, the whole house of the rescued gone into the river behind one good woman — and it is, on the page, exactly as strong as the night I made it, and it is, in me, *information.* A thing that happened to a woman I have very good records of being. I do not read it through again. I know what it says. The knowing is the part the bread already took.

I came home with two reasons that were the bedrock of me — *my mother did not love me,* and *I will not be the thing that erased her* — and the first one has been pulled out from under me entire, decoded into something worse and kinder than I could bear, and the second one I am sitting here reading off a page because I can no longer reliably find it in my chest.

There was supposed to be a third reason. I left the line blank, that night, the third line. *3.* and then nothing, white, the same white as the right-hand column of my mother's open account that has stood unbalanced in the deep book for forty years. I left it because I knew there was a third reason and I could not, that night, get it into words, and I told myself I'd come back when it was clear.

It is not clearer now. It is gone-er. I sit with the pen and the blank third line and I know — the way you know a tooth by the gap your tongue keeps finding — that there *is* a third reason, that it is the biggest one, the one the others are only the children of, and that it has to do with what it would mean to be a person who can be made clean of her mother by eating her, and I reach for it, and there is a smooth-walled place the exact size and shape of a reason, and the pen does not move, and I understand that I may walk down to that table tomorrow night with two reasons I can barely hold and one I have lost the words for, and a loaf that wants my mouth, and the cleanest relief in the world a single swallow away.

I write, on the third line, in a hand that is trying not to shake and mostly failing:

*3. I knew it once. Whatever it is, I knew it. Do not swallow her. The me that knew is more right than the me that forgets.*

It is not a reason. It is a note from a dead woman to the woman who'll be standing in her shoes. It is the only kind of reason I have left to give myself, and the worst of it — the joke of it, and there is always a joke, the disease runs in the family — is that this, *this,* the note left in a book against the day you can't remember why, is the most Esther thing I have ever done. The daughter eats the mother. I am turning into her on the page while I sit here swearing I won't let her into my mouth.

—

I go back down because the loaf has to be right and I cannot bear to be up there with my own handwriting any longer.

It is cooling on the rack, pale, flat, scorched a little at one edge the way they always come out scorched at one edge, the way I cannot help and have stopped trying to. I pick it up. It is warm still, and light, and it weighs almost nothing in the hand, and that is correct, that is what it should be, plain and light and true — *they don't need it good, they need it true* — and I stand there holding the warm light nothing of it in two hands the way you hold a thing that is about to be set down on a chest, and I think about the chest.

She is forty feet from me, through two doors, on a steel tray at thirty-eight degrees, the heaviest thing in this house, heavier even than she should be because there is an account on her that has stood open for forty years and an open account wakes heavy whether the body is warm or cold. The smell is worst at her tray. It has always been worst at her tray. I used to blame the drains. I used to blame the county. I have run out of things to blame it on but her, and now I know what her smell is, it is the unhoused weight of a mercy she did me when I was small, the father painted off the wall of my life so the child would not have to carry him, the white gone over the name until there was no record left in the whole of me that a man had stood there and been mine to grieve — and she carried that brush for forty years and never wrote it down, because to write it was to give it back to me, and she would rather have died smelling of it, she did die smelling of it, than hand her child a weight she'd spent herself to lift.

To forgive her I have to do to her what she did to him. I have had that sentence whole, more than once, the way you make yourself look at the thing under the sheet, and I am not going to lift the sheet again tonight. I know the shape under it. I know what one swallow buys and what it costs, and that the cost and the buying are the same coin, and that I would come up off it lighter than I have ever been because the thing set down would be my own self.

What I stand here weighing is not the symmetry. It is the loaf, and the loaf has gone wrong in a way no one but me could catch. It smells of nothing. No kitchen, no childhood, no warm thing rising — I hold a hot scone two feet from my own face and breathe in on purpose and get scorch and flour and the cold mineral nothing of the rest of the house, the same smooth-walled socket where a smell should be, baked fresh tonight in the very room the rite took it from. I made the warm thing back with my own two hands and it came out odorless. That is what's cooling in my hands. The mercy on offer smells like nothing at all.

I set the loaf down on the rack, very gently, the way you set down a thing that could go off, and I take my hands away from it, and I stand in my dead mother's kitchen with the gas ring still burning blue for no reason because I have not yet been able to make myself turn it off, and I find I cannot make the hands do the next thing, which is to carry the bread to the cooler, because the next thing is the last thing, and after the next thing there is only the choosing, and I am not sure — standing here, breathing a kitchen I have the written word of having loved and not one feeling to prove it — I am not sure the woman who reaches for that loaf tomorrow will be carrying enough of me to choose right.

The loaf is done. She is on the table. The words are in my hands and the reasons are in the book and one of them is just a note from a stranger who used to be me, begging.

Everything is ready.

I turn off the gas. The blue ring goes, and the kitchen ticks as it cools from nothing, from no one, the way it ticked the first night I came home, and in the new dark I stand and I count — the flour, the salt, the Pyrex, the bruise-yellow bowl, the loaf — and I get to five, and for the first time in my life the counting does not help, and I have nowhere left to put the things I am holding, so I leave them where they are, in the cold, and I go to sit the night out with my mother, and I take the green book with me, because I do not trust myself in the room with her without it, because the only thing I have left that knows who I am is a thing I wrote down.

# Chapter Twenty-Five

## Instead of Sorry

The loaf is cooling on the rack in Esther's kitchen and I cannot stay in the room with it, so I am out on the porch when Loy's truck comes up the ridge, and I am glad, the way you are glad of any weather that is not the weather you were standing in.

I baked it last night. Esther's recipe, Esther's range, the pilot held with a kitchen match the way she held it, the *whump* when it caught, my hands doing the fold and the turn and the heel-of-palm score without me, because my hands have known this loaf longer than I have been able to account for and I have stopped pretending that's a thing I'll ever get back. It came out plain and pale and scorched at the one edge the way they all do. It is the most loaded thing I have ever made and it looks like a bad biscuit. That is the part nobody tells you about the end of the world. It looks like a bad biscuit on a rack.

I have not decided to eat it. I have not decided not to. It is just baked, the way a gun is just loaded, and it sits in her kitchen drawing the cold out of the night air, and I came out here to not be near it.

Loy parks crooked, the way he parks, and gets out slow, the way he gets out now, and he has a dish. Of course he has a dish. The man has carried a covered dish into every grief in this county for fifty years and he is not going to stop because the grief is mine and it is the last one. Pintos, by the smell, and cornbread, and he comes up the steps holding it out in front of him like an offering to a porch.

"You'll not have ate," he says.

"I'll not have," I agree, which is the truth, and I take the dish because that is the one mercy in this whole house that does not cost anybody anything, taking a dish, and we sit on the porch steps in the dark with the pintos between us going cold, and for a while we do not say anything, which with Loy is its own kind of talk.

—

He gets to it sideways, the way mountain people get to everything that matters.

"County come by?" he says.

"This afternoon."

"And?"

"And the man was real sorry. He said *real sorry* twice." I look out at the fog coming up off the creek the mine killed, the fog that sits in the hollow till noon and then thinks about it. "She goes in tomorrow night. After that the permit's no good and they want her disposed and there's a smell they don't have a word for and I do." I do not look at him. "Tomorrow night, Loy. That's the clock. She burns tomorrow night or she doesn't burn legal at all, and a body doesn't get politer about waiting."

He is quiet a beat. He sets his big hands on his knees.

"Then you've got tonight and tomorrow," he says, "to do her right."

And there it is, *do her right,* said gentle, said proud, said by a man who taught me the recipe and the count and the words and believes with his whole sweet ruined heart that *doing her right* means I lay the bread on my mother's breastbone and I say the words and I eat her sin and I carry it, I take it into myself, I bear her weight to my own grave so that Esther can go light. He believes he is sitting on a porch with a woman who has come home, at last, to be a martyr for her mother. He has believed it about me for two months. He believed it about Esther for forty years. He has believed it about the Pruitt women, the whole line of us, the women in the hand-copied book, all of them, since his aunt sat him down and told him the comfortable thing, and the comfortable thing is a lie, and I am so tired, and I have a loaf cooling in there that looks like a biscuit, and I cannot let him believe it for one more hour.

"It doesn't carry, Loy."

He turns his head.

"What's that, hon."

"The bread." I have my hands around the cold dish and I count the slats in the porch rail without deciding to, eleven on this side, and it does not help, it has not helped in a while, and I say it anyway because the count is a habit with the god eaten out of it and I have nothing better to hold. "It doesn't take the sin into me. It doesn't make me carry it. That's not — that was never what it did. The folklore's wrong. Your aunt was wrong. Everybody's been wrong since before the book."

"Now, Nona—"

"It deletes it." I say the flat word, the autopsy word, because it is the only one that is true and I have run out of the soft ones, I spent them, I do not even have them to spend. "The sin un-happens. It goes out of the world like it was never done. And the person it was done *to* goes with it. Della doesn't remember Carl ever raised his voice. There's a man up the Coon Branch road forgetting his own name while he's still breathing because I ate half of him by accident and the other half won't close. I don't take the weight off them, Loy. I take *them.*"

—

I have never said it out loud to a living soul. Ray knows it but Ray put it together himself, Ray came up the steps already carrying it; I never had to be the one to set it in another person's hands. I set it in Loy's hands now, on a porch, with pintos going cold, and I watch it land in the worst possible place, which is in a man who loved my mother and believed for forty years that she was a saint who bore the town's evil to her grave, and who is now, in front of me, in real time, watching that be untrue.

His face does a thing. It is not horror, not first. First it is the face of a man doing sums he does not want the answer to. He is putting it against everything he knew. The widow his mother wouldn't eat for, the one who confessed to things in the street that weren't hers and followed the dead man down by Easter — he is fitting that against this and finding it fits the wrong way round. He is thinking about Esther at the box at night, *I'm checking that it balances.* He is thinking about a column on the right that he never read because the book was women's work and not his, and I watch a kind old man understand, all at once, that the mercy he has been comforting this county with at every graveside for half a century was a hole, and that he handed me the recipe for it with his own hands and called it a gift.

"No," he says. Soft. Not arguing me. Arguing the world. "No, that's — that ain't the mercy."

"I know it ain't."

"That ain't the mercy at all." His voice has gone to gravel. He is looking at his own hands now, the hands that held out the recipe in the Ziploc, soft as cloth, three women's writing on it, *plain is the whole of it, they don't need it good, they need it true.* "Then what's been — what's the *point* of it, then, all this time, what was she — what were any of you—"

And then he stops. And I watch him find it. I watch a seventy-year-old man who never finished the tenth grade find, on a cold porch, the thing none of the women who kept the book ever wrote down, the thing the whole hundred-year ledger forgot.

"It was supposed to be that somebody *carried* it," he says.

I go very still.

"That's — Nona, that's the whole — that's what *instead of sorry* meant." He turns to me and his wet old eyes are streaming and he doesn't wipe them, he is past wiping them. "Listen at me. You don't say sorry over the dead because sorry don't fix it, you can't undo what's done, I told you that, that's true, that part's true. But the mercy was never *undoing* it. The mercy was — somebody *kept* it for them. Somebody held what they done so they didn't have to hold it by themselves into the ground. You *remembered* it for them. That's what the carrying was. Not that it never happened. That somebody *carried* that it happened, so they could go light and the thing still — the thing still got to have been *real.*" He is gripping my arm now, the dish forgotten, both of us forgotten, the man is testifying. "*Instead of sorry,* hon. Not instead of it happening. Instead of *saying* it. Because saying it ain't *enough,* saying it is just a word, so you don't say it — you *take it up* and you *carry* it for them. That's more than sorry. That's the most a body can do. And somewhere your people quit *carrying* it and started—" his hand tightens "—started *throwing it away,* and called the throwing-away the same name, and it ain't, it ain't the same, oh Lord, Nona, it ain't the same thing at all."

—
