The fog is at the bottom of the steps now. The pintos are stone cold. I am sitting next to a weeping old man who has just handed me, by accident, on the night before I have to choose, the one thing my own dissolving mind could not have given me: a reason that is not only mine.

Because here is what the rite has been doing to me, under the bodies, the whole time. It has been eating my reasons. Every swallow takes a piece of me weight for weight, and the pieces it takes are not just the candy and the kitchen-smell and the man I loved at twenty-five, half of him gone and the other half flinching at his own handwriting in my hands — it takes the *why* under things. It took the *why* I keep the logs. And it is coming, I can feel it coming the way you feel weather, for the *why* I won't forgive her — which is why I keep a book upstairs with the reasons in my own hand, written against this exact night, *for the day I cannot feel them,* and why the most frightened I have been since the bridge is the plain fact that my own records have started to outlast me, that I am becoming a woman who has to be told by a book why she should not eat her mother.

And now Loy. Now a reason from outside the wreck of me. *The rite was supposed to carry.* The mercy was never the deletion; the deletion is the corruption; somewhere down the century my people took *remember it for them so they can go light* and rotted it into *erase it so nobody has to remember,* and they kept the holy name on the unholy thing, and the name was *instead of sorry,* and I have been living inside the rot and calling it the air, same as the smell, same as the father-shaped hole, same as everything in this house that was a wrong I grew up breathing.

I do not have to feel my reasons. I can read Loy's face instead. That is a thing the bread cannot eat. It is in him, not in me; it is in the old book wrong and in the old man right; and between the two of them they are holding the line my own memory can't.

—

"Esther never said it to me," I say. I don't mean to. It comes out the way the true things come, sideways, in the gap. "Sorry. In nineteen years. Not once. She asked me to come home and learn the trade and I said no and I let her think it was the *smell,* I let her die thinking I found her disgusting, and she never once said sorry and I never once said sorry and we just — we let it sit. Two women who couldn't say the word, sitting on it for nineteen years, and then she died."

"She couldn't get the soft ones out," Loy says. "I told you that the first night. Some folks can't."

"No." And I see it now, the whole shape of it, worse and sadder than the curse I carried it as. "It wasn't that she couldn't say it, Loy. It's that she had a *machine* for not saying it. And when it came to me — when it came to the one thing she did to *me* — she didn't say sorry. She did the thing she knew how to do. She — "

I stop. Because the rest of it is the answer and not the question, and the answer is on the wall upstairs in the shape of a man-sized nothing, and I am not going to lay it on this porch with the pintos. But I have it whole now. The thing she is asking me to do to her — eat her, make her never have happened so I can go light — that is the last *instead of sorry,* the biggest one, mother and daughter erasing each other clean so neither one ever has to say the word.

And the cure is so small I could laugh. The cure has been sitting in the bottom of me for nineteen years where the soft ones go to die. The one thing in this whole rotten house that is not *instead of* sorry is *sorry.* The actual word. The useless one. The one that fixes nothing, undoes nothing, brings nobody back, keeps the wound exactly where it is and just — says it. Out loud. To her face, over her body, with the bread set aside and the fire unlit.

I reach for it now, on the porch, to see if it's there. I go down inside myself to the place where it lives and I put my hand out for it the way you reach in the dark for a glass you know is on the nightstand.

It is not there. Or it is there and walled. Nineteen years of not-saying-it has gone hard over the place like scar over a splinter, and I reach and I find the smooth, and I cannot get my fingers around the word, and I close my mouth.

Not tonight. Not yet. Not even now that I know it's the cure. The thing about a wall is it does not come down because you have finally understood you built it.

—

Loy goes near midnight. He stands at the bottom of the steps a long time with the truck door open and the dome light on his wet face and he says, "You don't have to do the throwing-away part. You hear me. You don't have to do the part your people got wrong." And I say, "I hear you, Loy," and I do, and I do not tell him that the part my people got wrong is the only part I know how to do with my hands, that my hands learned the deleting and never learned the carrying, that there may be no carrying left in this house to learn. He drives off crooked and the fog closes over the sound of him and I am alone with what I have left.

Which is this. A loaf cooling in a dead woman's kitchen that looks like a biscuit and is a choice. A mother in the cooler at fifty-one kilograms, the heaviest thing in this house, an account open forty years, the smell of her worst at her tray even cold because the cold can't kill what was never housed. A note in my apron pocket against the place where the bread goes, *the daughter eats the mother, that is the whole of it,* which I carried two months as a curse and now know was a mercy, *be free of me,* my mother trying to do me the last kindness she knew how to do, which is the deletion, which is the murder, because the deletion is the only kindness her people ever taught her to give.

And a green book upstairs in my own hand telling me what I no longer wholly feel. And an old man's gospel, gotten wrong his whole life and right at the worst hour: *carry it, don't throw it away. Remember it for them. That's the mercy. That's more than sorry.*

I go in. I do not go to the cooler and I do not go to the kitchen where the loaf is. I go to the prep room and I crouch at the box under the sink and I take up the green ledger of myself, and under the reasons I cannot fully feel anymore, in my own hand, on a faith with no god under it, I write the thing Loy gave me, so that it will outlast me too, so that when I reach the table tomorrow night with my mind nearly gone I will have it in ink where the bread can't reach:

*The rite was supposed to carry, not delete. To remember for them so they could go light, and the wronged still got to have been real. Somewhere we corrupted it. I will not corrupt it on her. I will carry her instead of eating her. I will keep the wound. I will say the word I have not said since I was 22.*

And then, because I am a keeper of records and the keeper does not lie in the book even to herself, I add the last true line under it, the one that is the whole of the dread, the reason this is not a victory:

*If I can find the word at all. If there is enough of me left tomorrow to know why I came home, and to mean it. I do not know that there is.*

I cap the pen. Tomorrow night she burns. Tonight and one more night is all the time there is to become a woman who can say a word she has spent nineteen years building a self around never saying — guided by a book in my own hand and a lie an old man finally told true, in a house that has smelled for forty years of a sorry nobody ever said.

# Chapter Twenty-Six

## The Last Night With Her

The loaf is on the steel counter under a clean towel, cooling the way the dead cool, from the outside in, and it will be cold all the way through by the hour the county says I have to put my mother in the fire, which is dawn, which is the thing on the other side of this night.

I have nothing to do tonight. That is what I want to tell you first, because it is the strangest thing about it. My whole life in this house has been a thing to do — a body going, a gauge to coax, a name to spell right, an account to write down before the burn. There is always a thing to do here, that is the one true mercy of the trade, it gives the hands somewhere to be while the chest does whatever the chest is doing. And tonight there is nothing. The loaf is baked. The words are in me. The body is in the cooler at thirty-eight degrees being the heaviest thing in the house. Ray knows. Wren is free. Loy has said his piece and gone home to be old in his own bed. There is no body coming, because I called the answering service two days ago and told them the home is not taking transfers, and the man said *for how long,* and I said I would let them know, which was the closest I have come in nineteen years to saying the home is closing, and I could not say it any plainer because saying a thing plainer is how you make it true, and I am not ready for that one to be true yet.

So there is nothing to do. So I do the only thing left, which is the thing I came home swearing I would never do.

I sit with her.

—

I wheel her out of the cooler at full dark. I do it slow, with both hands, the way you do it when there is no one watching and no clock but the one in your own chest, and the cot is light under her — fifty-one kilograms, a hundred and twelve pounds, I logged it on the first night and the number has not changed and it is still nothing, still a woman who weighed a hundred and thirty when she put me on a bus and spent the difference on something across the years between us. I bring her into the prep room under the one good light and I set the brakes and I stand there.

I have stood over a lot of bodies in this room. I am good at it. You learn a stance, a way of holding your face and your hands that lets you be near the dead without the dead getting into you, and I have a stance for everyone, for the abused and the monsters and the good ones and the ones who carry nothing at all, I have a way of standing over each of them. I do not have one for her. I keep reaching for the stance and there is the smooth-walled place where the stance should be. So I just stand, badly, the way a person stands, with my hands not knowing what to do, and I look at my mother's face for the first time in nineteen years with nobody coming and nothing to sign.

And I cannot find it.

I want to be plain about this, because it is the worst thing in the house tonight and it is worse than the loaf and worse than the deadline. I look at my mother's face and I am looking for the face I crossed the mountains to get away from, the cold face, the hinge of a mouth, the eyes that could change the weather of a room, and it is not there, and it is not that death has taken it, death takes a face's animation but it leaves you the architecture, I know this, I have buried two thousand faces and they all keep their bones. Her bones are here. The face is the one in the photograph upstairs, my mother at thirty in front of the sign, holding a baby that is me. The architecture is fine.

It is the rest of it I cannot find. The weight behind it. I look at her and I reach back for *her,* for the kitchen and the silences and the way she would not say a soft word, for the nineteen years of grief I built my whole self on the far side of, and what I get is the *shape* of all of it, the socket, the place a mother goes, and not the mother. I know I hated her. I have the fact of it the way I have the fact of a town I drove through once and could not now describe. I cannot find the hating. I reach for it and there is the smooth wall, and behind the smooth wall there is just my mother's body on a cot, fifty-one kilograms, a stranger I am about to burn.

I have eaten so much of myself that I have started to eat the one thing I came home for.

—

I sit down on the floor.

I do it because my legs do it, the way on the first night the floor of me dropped out at the side door; I do it because the chair is too far and the floor is right here and the cold of the tile is at least a true thing, a thing happening to my actual body in the actual room, and I have learned to be grateful for true things that are happening, they anchor you, they are the gunwale you hold. I sit on the floor with my back against the cabinet under the sink, the cold steel of it coming through my shirt the way it came through the night I read her open account, and I put my hands flat on the tile on either side of me, and I make myself do the thing a keeper of records does when the world and her own head disagree, which is: go to the page.

The green book is up on the desk where I left it. My own. *What I think I had* on the left, *what is there now* on the right — the ledger of myself, the autopsy I have been performing for two months on a body I am still inside. I do not get up for it yet. I sit on the floor a little longer because I am afraid of it, which is a sentence I would not have believed I could write at the start of all this: I am afraid of my own records. Because I know what is in the green book. I know that two nights ago I sat at this desk and read my own reasons for not eating her, the reasons I wrote down *for the day I cannot feel them,* and I could not feel two of the three. I read my own hand telling me why, in ink, in a hand I would know in the dark, and the words were true and I believed them the way you believe a thing a smart person tells you that you cannot check, on their authority, on trust, and the person whose authority I was trusting was *me,* a me from a few weeks ago who still had her reasons whole, writing to a me who has spent them.

That is the thing nobody tells you about losing yourself by the gram. You do not get to keep the part that knows what it lost. You get to keep the part that takes your own word for it.

—

I get up. I get the book. I sit back down on the floor because the floor is where I am tonight, and I open it to the page, and I read the will I made for myself, the only kind of will a woman like me knows how to make.

*I will not eat her. Reasons, for the day I cannot feel them:*

And then the list. Number one, the rite deletes, it does not carry, I have seen what the deleting does, Della happy and Brandy afraid of nothing and a town gone thin and a man named Press becoming a tear — and I read it and I find I have to take the *seen* on faith, because I cannot, tonight, fully see Della anymore, I have the fact of her yellow dress and not the dress, I have the word *Brandy* and a flinch I am told I watched. The proof I wrote the reason on has gone the way of everything else. But the reason is still on the page. The reason does not need me to remember the proof. That is what the page is *for,* I understand it now in a way I did not understand it when I was a child making a record of the world so the world could not — and there the sentence walks off the edge the way it has walked off the edge before, because the *why* of it was eaten months ago, the first time, the original time, and I am left holding the habit with its root pulled, the wall with no memory of why I built it, and I built it, it turns out, against exactly this night.

Number two. Because if I eat her I forgive her, and if I forgive her there was never anything to forgive, and if there was never anything to forgive then nineteen years was nothing, and if those years were nothing then I was nothing, never important enough to be wounded by. I read it. It is the truest thing in the book and I cannot feel a single watt of it. I read *I would rather keep the wound than lose the proof that I was ever real enough to be left,* and it is mine, it is in my hand, and it lands on me like a stranger's confession, like the letters I found in a drawer tied with a shoelace that began *Dear Nona* and that I read and could not place — I have a man like that now, somewhere behind a smooth wall, a man I am told I loved at twenty-five, whose handwriting my own body flinches at though I cannot tell you his name. Reason two reads to me the way those letters read. A true account of a love I no longer have the equipment to feel. The wound it wants me to keep is a wound I can no longer locate. The proof it wants me to protect is gone, was always the point, I am the proof, and the proof is leaking.

And then number three. Which I left blank.

I left it blank two nights ago — no, longer, I left it blank the night I found her open account, the night I stood in the hall and asked the photograph *did you eat him* and would not write the answer until I had the page, because a feeling is a thing the bread takes and a thing on the page is harder for it to reach, or I have to believe it is harder, it is the last religion I have. I left the third reason open the way she left her one account open, the left side reaching, the right side white, because the third reason was the one I could not look at, the answer and not the question.

I have the page now. Loy gave me the page. The ledger gave me the page. I have spent two months and most of myself assembling the page, and the page says: she ate the father so the child would not have to carry him. She did to me, when I was small, the kindest and most unforgivable thing in this whole house — she looked at a man who had done a thing and was leaving and she decided her daughter would not grow up beside the weight of him, and she took him out of the world, cleanly, the way I have watched myself take a hundred people out of the world, and she paid for it out of herself, a price she could never record because to record it was to admit she had broken the trade's one law and used the rite on her own blood, and so she left the account open, and it has been waking heavy in this house for forty years, and the cold I hated her for was the hole where the warmth used to be before she spent it, on me, to spare me, and she could not even tell me, because the telling was one of the things she'd spent.

That is reason three. I have the page now.

And I sit on the floor of the prep room with the green book open on my knees and the cold steel against my back and my mother three feet away under the one good light, and I write it down.

—

I write it slow. My hand is the one inheritance I will cop to having and it does not shake, it has never shaken, it is steady the way hers was steady, and I watch it move across the page making the reason I have not been able to make for two months, and I do not feel the thing the words are about, I have told you, the feeling is gone where the kitchen-smell is gone and the candy is gone and the man at the laundromat is gone, but the hand does not need the feeling. The hand only needs the page. That is the whole of what she gave me when she made me a girl who could not bear a thing not written down — she gave me a way to keep faith with a self I would not be able to feel, an account I could read out to myself across the dark when the creditor and the debtor were the same broke woman. She gave me, in the act of erasing my father, the exact tool I would need, at the end, to refuse to be erased.

I write:

*3. Because she did the same thing to me, out of love, when I was small — she ate him so I would not carry him — and it left a hole in me the shape of a man I cannot grieve, and a wall against the hole that I built into my whole self without knowing why, and a coldness in her I spent nineteen years calling rejection that was the cost of the mercy she did me. And if I eat her, I do to her what she did to him: I take a person out of the world to spare somebody the weight. I become her. The thing I left at twenty-two so I would not become — I become it, at the table, with her on it. And the mercy goes around one more time, mother to daughter to nobody, because there is no one after me, and the line goes out not with a refusal but with one last perfect erasure, and the last thing the Pruitt women ever do is the thing we corrupted the rite into, which is to love each other by deleting each other, which is the genocide of meaning Loy never knew he was teaching me when he handed me the kindest sentence I ever heard.*

*I will not. I would rather carry her heavy for the rest of my life than be light the way she made me light. I would rather keep the hole he left and the cold she paid in and the wound I cannot feel and the proof I am the proof, than set it all down clean and be at peace and be gone. The peace is the murder. I have written it eleven times in this book about other people and now I am writing it about me. The peace is the murder.*

I cap the pen. The reason is whole now, all three, the will complete, the account closed in the one book that is allowed to be honest, and I read it back, and I cannot feel it, and it does not matter that I cannot feel it, because it is on the page, and the page is the witness, and the witness is the woman I was before the trade ate her, testifying to the woman I am tonight, telling her, across the dark, in a hand she would know anywhere: *don't. Whatever it costs. Don't.*

—

I do not know how long I sit there. The retort is cold; there is no clock in here but a battery one over the door that I have never trusted, it runs fast, everything in this house runs fast or runs heavy. The fog has come up against the windows the way it does, and the house is doing its talking, settling and ticking through its 1911 bones, and the smell is in the room, the old one, the one I called the drains on the first night and now know for what it is — my mother's open account, forty years of unhoused weight, the smell of a sin with no name in it, worst at her tray even at thirty-eight degrees because the cold cannot kill what is not in the body. It is in the room with us. It has always been in this house. I grew up breathing it. I am going to spend the rest of my life breathing it, after tomorrow, because I am not going to eat the thing that makes it, and that is a decision I have made on the page with a self I can no longer feel, and I am going to honor it the way you honor a thing a dead person asked of you, on trust, because they were real and they mattered and you owe them the truth.

And somewhere in there — I could not tell you when, there is no when in here — I stop reading and I start talking.

To her. Out loud. In the prep room, to a body, the way I have talked to bodies my whole life, *go on now, go on,* except this is not that, this has no liturgy in it, this is just a woman on the floor in the cold talking to her mother, finally, nineteen years and a night too late, with the one window in which it was ever going to be possible, which is the window where one of us is dead and the other is nearly gone and there is nothing left to lose by it.

I do not say I am sorry. I want to put that down plainly, because I know it is the thing this book has been walking toward, I am not a fool, I have watched it coming the way you watch weather come up the hollow. I do not say it tonight. I cannot. I reach for it the way you reach for a step in the dark and there is the place where the step should be and it is not a wall this time, it is not the smooth socket of a thing the bread took, it is something else, it is a step I have refused to take for so long that the muscle for it has wasted, a thing I *could* do and have not done since I was twenty-two, and there is a difference between a thing you have lost and a thing you have refused, and tonight, for the first time, I can feel that difference, I can feel that the *sorry* is not gone, it is *kept,* it is somewhere in me with the lights off, waiting, and I am not ready, and I will not say it before its hour, because if I have learned one thing in this house it is that a word said too soon is just a coin across a counter and a word said in its hour is a tooth pulled out of your own jaw, and I am saving the tooth.

So I do not say that. I say other things. I say:

"You should have told me." Quiet. To the architecture of her face. "Not at the end. Not in a note. When I was twelve and asking why there's no picture of a daddy, you should have said, there isn't one, because I took him, because he did a thing, and I would rather you grow up beside a hole than beside a wound. I'd have hated you for it. But I'd have hated you for a *thing,* and a thing you can stand next to. You let me hate you for the smell of the work. You let me drive eleven hours every year not coming home over the *smell.* And the smell was you, mama. The smell was the bill coming due on the mercy you did me. I have been holding my nose against my own self all my life and calling it you."

The face does not change. The dead don't. That is the deal, that has always been the deal, you do the talking and they do the staying-still, and there is a kind of honesty in it you cannot get anywhere else, because the dead cannot forgive you and they cannot tell you it is all right and they cannot make it easier, all they can do is be the thing you wronged, held still under a light, while you say the true thing at last into a face that will not let you off.

"I'm not going to eat you," I tell her. "I know that's what the note's for. I know now. I had it wrong for two months, I had you down as cruel, *make my disgusted girl swallow me,* and it took me till tonight to turn it over and read the other side, which is — you wanted me free of you. You wanted me to set it all down. You spent your whole self sending people on light and at the end the only person you had left to be merciful to was me, and the only mercy you knew how to give was the rotten one, the deletion, *be free of me, daughter, even though it costs you yourself,* because you would rather I forgot you ever happened than keep carrying what happened to us. That's the kindest thing you ever did and it's the same as the worst thing you ever did and I cannot tell them apart and I have stopped trying. But I'm not going to take it. I'm sorry —"

And I stop. Because I have come up against the word sideways, in the gap, the way the worst things come out, and it is not its hour, and I make myself stop, I close my mouth on it like closing a drawer, and what I say instead, into the quiet, is the only thing I have that is true and not the word:

"I'm going to keep you. Heavy. The hard way. I'm going to let you wake heavy and I'm going to carry you the rest of my life, the smell and the weight and the open account and all of it, because that's the only way I know to forgive you that doesn't also kill you. Loy thinks the eater carries. Loy's been wrong his whole sweet life. But he's wrong about the *family,* not about the *mercy* — the mercy was always supposed to be the carrying. Somewhere we turned carrying into deleting and called it the same word, and you and Mam and all the way back to a woman in Wales who's just ink now, you all did the deleting and called it carrying, and I am the last one, and I am going to do the carrying and call it nothing, because it doesn't get a pretty name. It just gets done. By me. Forever."

—

There is a thing I keep with me through this, and I want to set it down, because it is the warmth, and the warmth is the only reason any of this is bearable, and I have learned the hard way in this book that you have to put the warmth on the page or the reader has no reason to grieve the ruin.

The thing is Wren, walking out into the day, which she did this afternoon, before all this, before the dark.

She came by to drop a key she didn't need to drop — she has had a key since before I came home, she was the one keeping this place from rotting while my mother emptied out — and she stood in the kitchen with her chipped black polish and her whole feral nineteen years and she told me she got into the cosmetology program over in Bluefield, the real one, the one with the financial aid she finally figured out how to fill out the form for, and she is leaving in three weeks, and she said it fast and embarrassed the way she says everything that matters to her, like if she says it quick enough it won't count as hoping. And I said good. I said it like a fact, because that is how she can stand to be loved, in facts, like me. I said *good, that's good, Wren,* and I did not say the rest of it, which is that I have spent this whole trade with a door in me that opens onto a world where I save myself by handing all of this to her — the ledger, the bread, the words, the whole century of it — because the line takes by the eating and not strictly by the blood, and she could be made the keeper, and *& Daughters* could be true at last, and I would be free.

And I am not going to. I decided it weeks ago and I have decided it again every day since and I decided it once more this afternoon in the kitchen watching her be embarrassed about a future, and the door does not even swing anymore, it is bolted, she is the reason it is bolted, you cannot feed the heartbeat of the thing to the thing. The line ends with me. *& Daughters* goes out a lie on a sign over an empty house, the daughters that never were, and the dead of Caudle's Mercy are going to have to be grieved honestly from now on, the hard human way, wound and all, the last bodies burning heavy, the town carrying its own dead, the mercy dead and the truth alive — and the price of all that, the whole price, is that a girl with chipped polish gets in a car in three weeks and drives over the mountain to a stupid bright ordinary life where nobody ever teaches her to lay bread on a breastbone, and I get to watch the one good thing my choice buys walk out into the world un-eaten, un-erased, free.

I would do the whole night for that. I want to be clear. I would carry my mother heavy for fifty years for that one thing.

And Ray. Who came two days ago, who I finally gave the truth to, all of it, the road and the girl and the men I have eaten and the way each one took a piece of his thirty years down its throat — and who did not run, and did not call it madness, and did not ask me to fix it or undo it, because we both know there is no undoing, the ledger only runs one direction. He just sat at my mother's table and took it, the whole impossible weight of it, and at the end he said the thing that is going to get me through the rest of my life, which is: *then we keep it. The both of us. You set it down all you want, you go to the Ruby Tuesday, you watch the ballgame — but you don't lose it. I'll keep Dana and you keep your dead and neither one of us lets the world have the last say on whether they were real.* Two keepers of records at a kitchen table, choosing the wound on purpose, the only two people in the county who hold a truth no one else can see. He is going to keep his unsolvable girl open forever, on purpose, now. And I am going to keep mine. And we are going to be lonely together, which is the most a person like me ever dared to want and more than I thought I would get.

—

Dawn is a grey thing that does not arrive so much as leak, the way it does in the hollow, the fog going from black to the color of dishwater without ever once being a sunrise. I feel it before I see it, the window going from a mirror of the one good light to a square of wet grey, and I know the night is over, and I know what the morning holds, and I get up off the floor where I have spent the last night with my mother that I am ever going to get, the closest the two of us have been in nineteen years, one of us dead and one of us nearly gone, and I put my hand flat on the cold steel of the cot rail, and I look at her face one more time, the architecture of it, the stranger's face I am about to forgive and burn and carry, and I cannot feel a single thing, and on the desk behind me the green book lies open to a will written in a hand I would know in the dark, three reasons whole at last, telling me who I was and what I owe and what the mercy was supposed to mean, holding the line my own head can no longer hold.

The loaf is cold under its towel. The bread that would erase her, made in her kitchen, from her recipe, by my hands, the most loaded thing I have ever baked, sitting there ready, the corrupted sacrament, the poison, the choice. The fire is cold and the deadline is at the door and the words are in me and the bread is on the counter and my mother is on the cot and my reasons are on the page and my memory is nearly gone and the one thing left undone is the smallest thing in the language, the tooth in my own jaw, the word I have not said since I was twenty-two and have kept, all night, with the lights off, for its hour.

Its hour is the next thing that happens.

I pick up the bread.

# Chapter Twenty-Seven

## The Bread on the Sternum

I pick up the bread and it is the wrong weight in my hand.

That is the first thing, and I want it down before anything else, because it is the kind of thing only I would notice and the kind of thing the whole night is going to turn on. I have made this loaf a hundred times in two months. I know its heft the way I know the heft of the cot scale and the heft of the green book and the heft of my own two hands empty at the end of a shift. I broke the morsel off last night with the rest cooling under the towel, a thumb of it, a bite, the size the recipe wants, *thin as you can, it must be plain,* and I set it on a square of wax paper by the head of the cot so it would be there when the hour came. And the hour has come, the grey is leaking at the window, the deadline is a thing I can feel now like a hand at the back of my neck, and I pick the morsel up off the wax paper and it weighs nothing.

It should weigh something. Bread weighs something. I have logged caudle-bread on the gram scale, fifty-six grams the night I did Carl, I have the number, I keep the number, I keep all the numbers. This weighs the way a thing weighs when your hand has gone wrong and not the thing. I close my fingers on it and there is the soft give of the crumb and under the give there is nothing, there is the absence of a fact my palm should have, and I understand, standing over my mother at first light with the bread in my hand, that I have eaten the part of me that knew what bread weighs.

I count, to steady. There are eleven pipe joints over the carriage-house door, but I am not in the carriage house, I am in the prep room, and there is nothing in here that comes to eleven. I count the things that are at hand. The cot. The one good light. The green book on the steel table. The loaf under its towel. My mother. The bread in my hand. Six. It is not a number I have a feeling about. It does not help. It has not helped in a long while and I have stopped being able to tell you the day it stopped.

So I do not count anymore. I lay the bread on the sternum.

—

It goes where it goes, where it has always gone, over the breastbone, where the weight sits. I do not have to think about the placing; my hands have the placing the way they have the recipe, learned somewhere I cannot get back to, by someone I cannot name, and tonight that does not frighten me the way it used to, tonight it is almost a mercy, that the hands know what the rest of me is losing. The morsel sits on the flat bone over her heart, pale on the white of her, and I straighten it with one finger so it lies square, because if I am going to do this I am going to do it right, that is the floor below which I do not go, I do this right or I do not do it, and I have spent the whole night not deciding which.

She is cold. Thirty-eight degrees of cooler have gone all the way into her over eleven days and the cold comes up off her like a held breath, and that is the thing about a cold body, the rite is slower on a cold body, the bread cannot draw the warmth up because there is no warmth, so it draws the other thing, the long way, by the count Esther learned by feel and never wrote down, *you'll know it when the bread goes heavy in the air over him,* and I stand and I wait for the bread to go heavy in the air over her.

I say the words.

I say them low, the way Loy showed me, the way his aunt showed him, back and back to a woman in Wales who is just a column of ink now, and I am not going to put them on this page, I have never put them on this page, they are not mine to hand to anyone, they go past God to the dead direct, but I will tell you that my voice does a thing in the middle of them I have never heard it do. It is steady at the start. *What lies heavy on thee.* Steady. My voice is the one inheritance I cop to, steady as her hand, steady as the scale. And then I get to the taking, the part where the eater names herself and reaches under the weight to lift it, *I take to me,* and my voice is not steady there, it goes thin, it goes to almost nothing, the way the bread went to almost nothing in my hand, because the words have never once meant *her* before and now they mean her and there is no procedure for that, there is no stance, I have a stance for the abused and the monsters and the good ones and the ones that carry nothing and I have no stance for my mother, and the words come apart in the middle like wet paper and I have to stop and start the line over to get it out.

I get it out. *Go thou light.*

And then the bread starts to draw, and the night ends, the night I have been telling you about, the night that was bearable because there were things to do in it. The drawing is the thing there is no doing in. The drawing is just standing here while it comes up.

—

Here is where I have to stop telling it the way I tell it.

I have a way of telling. I have had it a long time. I read my own life out like a chart at the foot of a bed, the temperature and the times and the grams, and I do not flinch, and I have never once been caught, and the not-flinching is the whole of how I have stayed a person across nineteen years and two thousand bodies and a mother in the ground of the cooler. It is a wall. I know it is a wall. Everyone I have let close enough to matter has known it was a wall — Wren knew, Ray knew, Loy knew before they had words for it, *she'd just go quiet and do the thing, just like her mama* — and I built it so well that I could stand at this very table and lay bread on this very woman and tell you the weight of the crumb to the gram.

And the wall is what comes up off her first. Before the sin. Before any of it. What draws up into the bread first, the top layer of her, the most recent, is *me.* It is the wall, my wall, hers, the same wall, the one she had and I have, the going-quiet, the doing-the-thing, the steady hand over the screaming, and I feel it lift out of her into the crumb and I feel my own come loose at the same time, sympathetically, the way one tuning fork takes up the note of another in the same room, and the going-quiet that has held my whole life lets go, and there is nothing behind it but a woman at a table in the cold with her dead mother and a piece of bread on her breastbone and the truth coming up.

I am not going to be able to be dry about the rest of this. I want to warn you, because I have trained you, I know I have, I have spent this whole book teaching you to dread the place where I stop being able to joke, where the count fails and the procedure runs out and there is no Latin name for the bone and no gram for the grief, and you have learned it, you can feel it coming the way you feel weather come up the hollow, and you are right. It is here. The dry is over. I do not have it anymore. The bread took it.

So. Plainly. What comes up.

—

It comes up the way it came up off Carl and off Verlie and off the monster and all of them, not a vision, not a voice, colder and quieter and worse, a *knowing,* a thing I get to know the way you know a smell in a dark room, all at once, whole. Except it has never been her before. And the knowing of her is not the shape I braced for. I braced for the cold. I have hated the cold of her for nineteen years; I came home with the cold all sandbagged and ready, a river I knew was coming. And what comes up out of my mother into the bread is not cold.

It is the man.

It is the father-shaped hole, and it is not a hole from the inside, that is the thing, that is the thing I did not know until the bread told me, a hole is only a hole from the outside, from where I have always stood, the empty hall, the no-picture, the blank where a daddy goes. From the inside, from her, it is a man. There was a man. The bread gives me the weight of him the way the bread gives me the weight of a sin, and he had a weight, he was real, he had a way of setting a cup down and a way of being in a doorway, and he did a thing — I do not get the thing, she did not keep the thing, she spent the thing, that was the whole point — I get only the *leaving,* the shape of a leaving, a door and a road and a child too small to keep, and I get what she did next, I get it from the inside now, and I have to put my hand flat on the steel of the cot rail to take it.

She looked at the weight of him. She looked at the leaving, which was going to land on a small girl and stay on her the rest of her life, the way a father's leaving stays, a wound you grow up inside, a thing that makes a person — and she could not bear it, she could not bear to watch it land, and she had the one tool in the world that could keep it from landing, the rotten beautiful tool, the deletion, and she used it. On him. On her own. She broke the trade's one law, the law against the keeper's own blood, the law every Pruitt woman back to the ink had kept, and she broke it for me, to spare me a wound, and she took a whole man out of the world and out of my history so that I would grow up beside a hole instead of beside a grave.

And the bread is drawing it up, all of it, the breaking of the law and the man and the leaving and the long forty-year bill of it, and tangled all through it, inseparable, the way you cannot separate the sugar from the water once it is in, is the *love.*

—

That is the part the rite cannot tell apart. I learned this on Harlan Voss, the old man whose only sin was a love the world called shame, I learned that the bread does not know sin from love, it only knows weight, and a love can weigh as much as a murder, and the bread will take whichever sits heavy and never ask which it was. And here it is again, on her, at the end, the worst possible time to have learned it: the sin and the love are the same weight on the same bone, they came up together, they will go down together, and if I eat this I do not delete a cruelty, I delete a mercy, I delete the most monstrous loving thing anyone in this rotten house ever did, I delete the man and the love and the breaking-of-the-law-for-me, and I delete, with it, the hole, the wound, the wall, the whole me that the wound made — and I am standing at the very lip of it, the bread heavy in the air over her now, ready, *go on now go on* the only thing left to say and then the swallow, and I cannot, I cannot find the reason not to.

I reach for it. The reason. I want you to watch me reach for it because it is the thing the whole night has been for. I reach back for why I will not eat her, and there are three reasons, I wrote them, I wrote them down for exactly this, for the day I could not feel them, I have the green book three feet away with the three reasons whole at last in my own hand — but I am not at the book, I am at her, I am at the lip, and the bread is ready, and reaching is faster than walking to a book, and I reach inside myself for the reason the way you reach in the dark for the light switch you have flipped ten thousand times.

And there is the smooth wall where the reason was.

I knew the wall would be there. I have known for weeks. I sat on this floor last night and read my own hand and could not feel two of the three, I told you, I warned myself, the creditor and the debtor the same broke woman. But knowing a step is gone and stepping on it anyway are not the same, and I reach for *why not* and my foot goes through the place where the floor was, and what is there is the shape of nineteen years and not the years, the socket where the wound goes and not the wound, the fact that I was wronged and not the being-wronged, I have it the way I have a town I drove through once, I cannot describe it, I cannot feel a watt of it, and the bread is heavy in the air over her and the swallow is one motion away and the relief — 

—

The relief.

I have to tell you about the relief now because it is right here and it is the thing that is going to take me if anything does, and I have lied about it by omission this whole book, I have called it obscene and clean and a thing I was ashamed of, and all of that is true and none of it is the whole truth, the whole truth is that I have wanted it. Not the way you want a thing you are tempted by. The way you want a thing you have been starving for since you were twenty-two. The swallow is an unweighting, I have said, a setting-down of a thing carried so long you forgot it had a weight — and the thing I have carried longest, the thing under all the others, the floor of me, is *her.* Is the leaving of her. Is the no-going-home and the eleven-hour drives not taken and the cold I called rejection and the guilt I would not put a name to and the proof, the proof, the only proof I was ever real enough to be left.

And the swallow would take it. All of it. One motion and I set her down and the bill is paid and the books are balanced and the smell goes out of this house for the first time in forty years and I am light, I am free, I am the thing she tried to make me when she ate my father, the lighter emptier child with no wound and no wall and no hole, at peace, finally at peace, and *gone.* And I cannot remember why that is not the most wanted thing in the world. I have eaten the reason. The reason is on a page I am not touching. From here, at the lip, with the wall down and the dry gone and the years a socket I cannot feel — from here it just looks like rest. It looks like the bus. It looks like the one second I was twenty-two and stepped onto a bus and the whole weight of this town came off me clean, and I have spent nineteen years and most of my own mind to get back to a table this woman lay on, and here is the bus again, here is the door of it open, and all I have to do is swallow.

I lift the bread off her sternum.

I lift it the way you lift the last thing. My hand does it. The hand that knows the recipe and the placing and the weight I can no longer feel, the steady hand, her hand, it closes on the morsel and lifts it off the breastbone and the drawing stops, the bread is full now, it is heavy in my fingers with everything she was and everything she did and everything I am about to stop being, and I raise it, and it comes up toward my mouth in the grey light, and I am not refusing, I want to be honest, this is not a woman bravely refusing, this is a woman at the end of her reasons reaching for rest with her mother in her hand, and the bread comes up and my mouth opens and —

and my other hand, the empty one, the one not holding the bread, goes out.

—

It does not go to my mouth. It goes sideways. It goes to the steel table. It goes, while the right hand lifts the bread to my lips and the rest of me reaches for the bus, the left hand goes out across the cold steel without me telling it to, the way the legs put me on the floor last night, the way the hands know the placing, and it finds the green book.

I feel it before I know it. I feel the cloth cover of the county-supply ledger, the same cloth as the cremation logs, the nap of it under my fingers, the corner worn soft from two months of my own thumb, and my hand lies flat on it the way it lay flat on the tile last night, the way it lay flat on my own sternum the night I read my open account, and the hand stops. The hand will not lift toward the mouth. The right hand has the bread an inch from my lips, an inch, I can smell it, it smells of nothing, of clean nothing, the odorless nothing of a thing about to erase — and the left hand is on the book, and the two hands are in a war I am not conducting, and the one that is winning is the one that does not even need me, the one that has been winning since I was a child making a record of the world so the world could not change its mind, the one she gave me when she ate my father and made me, without meaning to, a girl who could not bear a thing not written down.

Because I have been here before. Not at this table. But here. At the lip of forgetting. Every rite, at the swallow, I have stood at the lip of forgetting and gone over, and lost the candy and the kitchen-smell and the man at twenty-five and the reason I keep the logs and I do not even know what else, things I do not know I had, things only the page remembers now. And every time, the page remembered. That is what the page is for. That is what she built into me. *You do not get to keep the part that knows what it lost. You get to keep the part that takes your own word for it.* And my word is on the book under my left hand, three reasons whole, written by a woman who still had her reasons, to this woman, who has spent them, and the hand on the book knows what the mind cannot hold: that there is a reason, that I wrote it, that I would not have written it if it were not true, that the self who wrote it is the only witness I have left to who I was and what I owe — and that I should not swallow.

The right hand is at my mouth. The bread is full and ready and a breath away. I can feel the heat of my own lip on it.

The left hand is flat on the green book, and it does not move, and it will not move, and through the cloth and the cover and the worn-soft corner it is saying the thing I cannot feel and cannot say and have not been able to reach all night, in a hand I would know in the dark, across the dark, to the broke and emptied woman standing here at first light with her mother lifted to her teeth:

*Read it first. Before you swallow. Read what you wrote.*

# Chapter Twenty-Eight

## What I Kept

The bread is at my mouth and my hand will not let me eat it.

That is the whole of it, for a long moment, longer than I can measure, and I have lost the means to measure anyway — the count is gone, the six things on the table did not hold, the number stopped helping me somewhere I cannot find the day of. So there is no clock on this. There is only the two of me. The right hand has the morsel an inch from my open lips, full and heavy with everything she was and did, the man and the leaving and the breaking-of-the-law-for-me and the love that cannot be told apart from any of it, and the left hand is flat on the green book on the steel, and the left hand is the one with the grip.

It does not pull me back. I want that understood. There is no wrench, no hauling-away, no struggle a person would see from the door. From the door it would look like a woman standing over her mother in the grey of first light, bread lifted, perfectly still. It is all inside. The right hand wants the bus. The left hand wants nothing — the left hand is past wanting — it only knows the one thing it has known since I was a child too small to keep, which is that a thing not written down is a thing the world is allowed to change its mind about, and that I have written something down, here, under my palm, against exactly this, and that I should read it before I let the world change its mind about me.

So I lower the right hand. An inch. Two. I do not set the bread down — I am not there yet, I want to be honest, the wanting is still on me like a wet coat — but I take it down off my lips, and the smell of nothing comes away from my mouth, the clean odorless nothing of the thing that erases, and I can think a half-thought past it.

And the half-thought is: *all right. Read it, then. Read it and you'll see there's no reason and you'll eat it after, and at least you'll have been thorough.*

That is the lie I tell myself to get my eyes on the page. I will take it. I have taken worse to get through a night.

—

I open the green book with the heel of the hand that is already on it, because the right one is occupied, holding my mother to be eaten, and I leave it occupied, and I read one-handed in the bad light the way I have read a thousand intakes one-handed with a body waiting.

The reasons are where I put them. Three of them, numbered, in my own hand — not the failing hand of these last weeks but the hand from before, steadier, the hand of a woman who still had the things she was guarding and wrote them down for the woman who wouldn't. I knew this day would come. I did not know it would come like this, with the bread up and the wall down and the dry burned off me and my mother an inch from my teeth. But I knew it would come, because I am the one who keeps the books, and the one who keeps the books knows that the self who writes is lending to a self who will be poorer, and writes accordingly.

*Reason one,* it says. *Della Mott is happy and her daughter flinches at a ghost no one in the room remembers making. That is what the mercy does. It does not lift the weight. It un-makes the one who was wronged and leaves the wrong standing in the body with no name on it. If you eat her you do that to her. You make her innocent and you make her gone.*

I read it and I cannot feel it.

I want to say that plainly, because it is the whole horror of where I am standing. I read my own true sentence about Della and her daughter and it is *information.* It is a fact the way a town I drove through once is a fact. I cannot find Della's yellow dress in me. I know there was a yellow dress; I wrote the dress down somewhere, earlier, in another book; but the woman in it has gone the way the candy went and the kitchen went and the man at twenty-five went, and Brandy's flinch is a word and not a girl. The reason does not reach me. It was supposed to reach me. That is what I built it for. And it lies on the page, true and dead, like a key to a door in a house I no longer live in.

But.

Here is the thing the left hand knows that the right hand and the wanting and the whole emptied rest of me does not. The reason does not have to *reach* me. It only has to be *true.* I wrote it when it was true. I would not have written it if it were not. The woman who set it down had the dress and the daughter and the whole weight of the thing in her, and she spent it into ink so that the woman standing here — this one, this hollow one, with her mother at her mouth — would not have to feel it to *believe* it. That was the whole point of the writing. Not to remember. To take my own word for it when I could no longer remember. *You do not get to keep the part that knows what it lost. You get to keep the part that takes your own word for it.* She built that into me when she ate my father. She made a girl who could not bear an unrecorded thing, and I have hated the compulsion my whole life, the logs and the numbers and the notebook that is just mine, the religion with no god in it — and here, at the lip, it is the only thing in the building that is not for sale.

I read *reason one* again. I still cannot feel it. And I believe it anyway, on my own word, the way you believe the scale when your hands have gone wrong, and something in me that is not feeling and is not memory — something older, the thing she gave me without meaning to — leans its whole weight on the believing.

—

*Reason two.* *The paper outlives you. Ray said it. He meant it kind and it is the worst thing anyone has told me. The paper outlives you because you are the thing leaking out from under it. Every rite you are less, and the books are more, and one day the books will be all that is left of who you were, and on that day you had better have written the truth in them, because the truth in the books will be the only you there is.*

That one I can almost feel, because it is happening to me as I read it. I am the leak. I can feel the level of myself dropping even now, even tonight, even without a swallow, the open accounts in me weeping out their weight into the air of this house. And the books are full and square under my hand. And the reason is right: if I swallow, the books I have kept become a list of holes with a hollow woman beside them, and the truth I bled into them dies with the last of me, and then there is no one — not one soul in the turning world — who remembers that Della was wronged and lived, that there was a boy at Bluefield, that a family went off the Front Street bridge while a girl watched from the bank and then spent her whole life pulling other people off it, that Dana was *taken* and did not leave her mother on purpose. I am the only one. The rite has made me the only one, body by body, and it is one swallow from making me no one.

The reason says: don't be no one.

—

*Reason three.*

I wrote it last. It is the newest ink, and the hand is the worst, the hand from this week, the hand that drops the gram and cannot hold the count, and I had to write it as a stranger writing to a stranger because by the time I came to it I could no longer feel the thing it was about. I remember writing it. I remember that I could not feel it and wrote it anyway, *the me that knew is more right than the me that forgets,* which Loy would say is the most Esther thing I have ever done, and he would be right, and that is its own grief for another day.

*Reason three,* it says. *If you eat her, you become her. That is the whole of it. Not a curse. A fact. She had a wound she could not carry and a tool that could un-make it, and she used the tool, on her own blood, and it cost her everything warm in her and she did it anyway because she could not bear to watch the wound land on someone she loved. That is you, right now, with the bread up. The wound is hers and it is going to land on you for the rest of your life and you cannot bear to watch it land, and you have the tool, and you are about to use it on your own blood. And if you do, you will have done the exact thing she did, for the exact reason, and you will have proved that the trade was right and that there was never any other way, and the wound will be gone and you will be light and empty and at peace and you will be her. The peace is the murder. The mercy is the thing that makes you her. Do not eat her. Stay the one she could not be.*

—

I read it twice.

I cannot feel it. I told you I wouldn't be able to and I can't. But I read it twice, and the second time I am not reading for the feeling anymore, I have given that up, I am reading the way you read a will, the way you read the last clear word of someone who knew more than you do now — because that is what it is. The woman who wrote reason three knew more than I do. She had more of herself. She stood on higher ground, with her reasons whole, and she looked down the slope at exactly this morning, at this woman, hollowed out and standing at the lip, and she wrote down the truth and left it where the hollow woman would find it. She is the only witness I have left to who I was. And she is telling me, across the dark, in a hand I would know anywhere, that I should not swallow.

And under reason three, on the next line, smaller, she wrote the other thing. Not a reason. The thing the reasons are for. The thing Loy gave back to me three nights ago, weeping in the kitchen, testifying, the old man who turned out to be the only one in the county who remembered what mercy was for. *It was supposed to be that somebody carried it,* he said. *That's what instead of sorry meant. Your people quit carrying it and started throwing it away and called the throwing-away the same name. You don't have to do the throwing-away part, hon. You don't have to.*

And under reason three, in my own worst hand, I had written it down so I would have it tonight, so it would be in the books and not only in me where it could be eaten: *carry it. don't eat it. that is the whole of it.*

—

I look at the ledger box, open on the floor by the sink where I left it, the century of dead Pruitt women stacked in it, the oldest pages cramped and water-spotted and Welsh, and I understand the thing I have been three months and most of my mind coming to understand.

The books were never supposed to balance by subtraction.

That is the lie at the bottom of all of it, the rot that got into the trade somewhere back in the ink, the place where carrying turned into throwing-away and kept the holy name. *The books must balance.* My mother said it. The oldest pages say it. I have heard it as the law of the house since Loy first handed me the box. And I have been reading it wrong the whole time, the way the whole family read it wrong for a hundred years, because there are two ways to balance a book. You can close the account by deleting the debt — by un-making the thing that was owed, the wrong, the wronged, the weight — until the column reads nothing because there is nothing, and the page is clean, and a clean page looks like justice and is a grave. Or you can close it by *recording* it. By writing down, true and whole and forever, what was carried and who carried it and what it cost — so that the account stays *open* and *honest,* the wrong on the left and the price on the right, both real, both kept, neither erased — and the balance is not that they cancel but that they are *both written down and neither one is allowed to disappear.*

One way the books balance because the truth is gone. The other way the books balance because the truth is *kept.*

My mother balanced hers by erasure for forty years and it ate her hollow and at the end she could not even write down the one account that mattered, could not put her own crime in her own column, left it open and bleeding under the sink because to record it was to keep it and she had spent her whole life learning to throw things away.

And she made me. The girl who cannot throw anything away. The girl who writes everything down. She made the one woman in the line who could close the books the *other* way.

—

I take the bread off my mouth.

I have already taken it down to my chest, I have been holding it there one-handed while I read, full and heavy, the weight of her in my fist — and now I take it the rest of the way down, away from my face, away from the wanting, and I look at it. Pale, plain, scorched at one edge the way the recipe makes it, the size the recipe wants, and full now, drawn full, heavy in a way bread is not heavy, heavy with a man I never met and a leaving I cannot picture and a love I will not get to feel and the whole forty-year bill of a mercy that was also the cruelest thing in this rotten house. I have it in my hand. I drew it up. It is real and it is hers and it is in my hand and all I have to do to make it nothing is open my mouth.

I do not open my mouth.

I reach across the cold body, across my mother, and I set it down.

—

I set it on the wax paper. The same square of wax paper I broke it off onto last night, by the head of the cot, so it would be there when the hour came. The hour came. It is here. And I lay the morsel back down on the wax paper beside my mother's head, full and heavy and undeleted, and I take my hand off it, and I straighten — slow, both hands empty now for the first time since the grey came up — and I stand over her in the cold with nothing in my hands at all.

The drawing does not undo. I want that down, because I am the one who keeps the truth in this and I will not soften it even here. The bread is still full. I cannot un-draw it; you cannot eat your way to restoring a thing, the ledger only runs one direction, and that runs the other way too — what is drawn is drawn, what is lifted is lifted, the weight is up out of her and into the crumb and there is no putting it back into a dead woman. So it sits on the wax paper, the weight of her, housed in bread and going nowhere, not in her and not in me and not deleted from the world. Which means.

Which means she will wake heavy.

I make myself think it all the way through, standing there with my empty hands, because I am about to choose it and a thing chosen blind is not chosen. When I burn her now — and I have to burn her, the window is shut, she goes tonight or she does not go legal at all — she goes into the fire with the weight up out of her and unhoused, the way Tug went, the way Cole Ferry went, the way Harlan went with his love unhoused and seeking Emmett. The burn will go wrong. The temp will drop and not recover; there will be the low wet sound under the roar, the throat clearing to speak and never getting the words; the bone will not fully calcine; the smell will come up and flood the house and not air out, the struck-match wet-stove smell of weight that has not been housed, and it will be the worst the house has ever held because what is in her is forty years old and was always the heaviest thing under this roof. And then the weight, unhoused, will go looking for the living kin, because that is what it does, that is the rule, the dead wake heavy and the heaviness seeks whoever loved them most or is simply nearest in the blood — and there is one Pruitt left. There is one. *& Daughters* is a joke two generations old and I am the punchline and the weight of my mother is going to come for me, slow, the way it came for Carla, the bad dreams and the souring and the guilt that was never mine confessed in the street, the long ruin, the following-down — and I am choosing it. I am standing here choosing to be haunted by my own mother for the rest of my life rather than make her clean.

There is no peace in this. I had a peace in my hand a minute ago, the bus, the clean nothing, and I set it down on a square of wax paper next to her head. What I have chosen instead is the weight. Forever. The smell on me already, the smell that was always partly mine, getting worse now, an account I am choosing to leave open in a house full of the dead who paid to have theirs closed. I will not be light. I will be the heaviest thing in Caudle's Mercy, the last sin-eater, who at the end would not eat, carrying her mother unhoused to her own grave the way the folklore always lied that we did — except now it is not a lie, now it is the truth, now I am doing the thing the trade only ever pretended to do, which is *carry.*

I am going to carry her.

—

That is the choice and I have made it and I want to tell you it feels like grace and it does not. It feels like the cold of her coming up off the steel and into my empty hands. It feels like a smell I will wear the rest of my days. It feels like being the one left, the one who remembers, in a town that paid a hundred years to forget — and that is the loneliest thing there is, and I am choosing it on purpose, on the word of a woman I used to be, written in a book, against the wanting of the woman I am now.

But it is mine. The weight is mine and the truth is mine and they are both real and I am keeping them both, the wrong on the left and the price on the right, my mother guilty and my mother loved and neither one erased, the account open and honest and unbalanced in the only way that is not a grave. I will write it down. When this is over, when she is ash and heavy and the smell is in the walls, I will sit at the desk under the one good light and I will open the green book and I will do the thing my mother could not do, the thing she spent her whole self learning not to do — I will put it in her column, *what was done, and the one it was done to, and that she took it so the child would not have to carry him,* and beside it, in the column that has been white for forty years, I will write what it cost. I will close her books the honest way. I will keep her.

And the books will balance. Not because the truth is gone.

Because the truth is kept.

—

She is on the table in front of me, cold, small, smaller than I remember, smaller than the woman who put me on a bus, and the bread sits beside her head on its square of wax paper, full and undeleted, the corrupted mercy laid aside. The fire is not lit. The retort is dark and quiet out in the carriage house with its eleven pipe joints over the door, and I am not at it, I am here, and there is one thing left undone in this room and it is the only thing the rite cannot do, and I have not done it since I was twenty-two.

I look at her face for it.

And I have to tell you that I am not sure, anymore, whose face it is.

I know it is hers. The intake says so. The toe tag said so, and I lifted the sheet and confirmed it against the tag because that is procedure, eleven days ago, when I still had more of myself than I have now. I know in the books that this is Esther M. Pruitt, my mother, fifty-one kilograms, daughter on the line that asks for relationship to the deceased. But the face — I reach for the face the way I reached for the reasons, and it is going, it has been going all this long night, the long night took it the way the swallow would have, only slower, only for free, and what is in front of me at first light is a small cold woman I am told I came out of and I cannot find her in me, I cannot find the kitchen or the not-smiling or the hand that was steady like mine, I have spent it, I have spent nearly all of it getting to this table, and I am about to do the most human thing I have ever done over a face I can barely hold.

I do it anyway. Because the record says she was real. Because the record says she was my mother and she ate a man off a warm chair so I would not have to carry him and it cost her every soft word she had and she could not say so, not in nineteen years, not ever, and she left me a note that read like a curse and was a door, and she made me, on purpose, the one who could refuse the thing she could not. The record says all of that. I wrote it down. I believe my own word. I do not have to feel her to keep faith with her. That is what the keeping was always for.

I lean down over the cold sternum where the bread is no longer.

I open my mouth.

And this time there is nothing in my hand to eat, and the thing that comes up out of me is not the swallow and is not a word I have let past my teeth since I was twenty-two, and it is right there, it is in my throat, the smallest most useless word in the language, the one that fixes nothing and keeps everything, and I have it, I have it at last, after all of it, and I open my mouth over my mother to —

# Chapter Twenty-Nine

## Sorry

— and nothing comes.

The mouth is open. The throat is ready. The word is right there, the way I told you, in the place a word sits the half-second before it is a word, behind the teeth, on the tongue, ready, and I open over my mother's cold sternum where the bread is no longer, and I push the air up to carry it, and—

Nothing.

Not gone. I want that down first, before anything, because the difference is the whole of it and I am the one who keeps the difference. Not gone the way the candy is gone, the kitchen, the man at twenty-five, the face I am looking at right now and cannot place. Not eaten. There is no smooth-walled socket here, no hole I can read by feeling its edge. The word is *there.* I can feel the shape of it sitting under the breastbone where the weight sits, full and heavy, the heaviest small thing in the language, and I cannot get it up my own throat.

Nineteen years is a long way for a word to climb.

—

I have not said it since I was twenty-two.

I know that the way I know the weight of a body before the scale reads, the way I knew the file was thin before I could say what was missing. I have built my whole self around the not-saying of it. I made a religion of memory and a wall of procedure and a life eleven hours from this house so I would never be in a doorway where it was wanted, and the one time it was wanted, in a county bed, I was the one they could not reach, and then finally reached, and I drove up to bury her and sell the building and never once meant to be standing here in the grey with my mouth open over her, trying.

The body does not want to do it. I am asking it to do a thing it has spent nineteen years learning not to do, the way Della learned to close a cabinet so it would not click, the way Brandy learned it in the highchair before she had words — I learned the not-saying that deep, that early, in some room I cannot find anymore, and the muscle that would carry the word has set hard as scar.

So this is what it costs. Not memory. Not a piece of myself subtracted clean. The other thing. The thing the rite was invented to get around, the thing my people corrupted into a deletion because *this*—this here, this open mouth and the air that won't carry—this is so much harder.

I stand over her and I make myself do the hard thing instead of the clean one.

—

She is so small.

I keep coming back to it because it is the one fact I can hold, the way you hold a rail. Fifty-one kilograms. A hundred and twelve pounds. A woman who weighed a hundred and thirty when she put me on a bus and spent the difference on something across the years between, and I know now what the something was, I have the cassette and the second ledger and the dates that line up like a confession—she spent it on the town, debt by debt, and she spent it on me, on a man off a warm chair so I would not grow up his, and it cost her every soft word she had and she could not tell me, not in nineteen years, not ever, and she left me a note that read like a curse and was a door.

I look at the face that goes with all of that and it is a stranger's.

I have to say that plainly because I will not soften it, not here, not at the one place in my life I came back to be honest in. I have paid away so much of her getting to this table that the face under me at first light is a small cold woman I am told I came out of and I cannot find her in me. I reach the way I reached for the reasons and there is the going-out feeling, the level dropping, the long night took her the way the swallow would have, only slower, only for free—I cannot find the kitchen, I lost the kitchen for a monster; I cannot find the not-smiling, I do not know which rite took the not-smiling; I cannot find her hands, which were steady like mine, the one inheritance I'll cop to, and even that is a thing I read in a book now, in my own hand, *her hands were steady,* a sentence, a fact the way a town I drove through once is a fact.

I am about to do the most human thing I have ever done over a face I cannot hold.

—

And here is the thing.

I do it anyway.

Because the record says she was real. I have it written down. Not the feeling—the feeling is spent, I bled it into ink years and bodies ago, the woman who wrote it had more of herself and she wrote it for this woman, this hollow one, who would not be able to feel it and would have to take her own word. I take my own word. That is what the keeping was always for. I built a wall against erasure my whole life and hated it, the logs and the numbers and the notebook that is just mine, the religion with no god in it, and she built that wall into me on purpose, the morning she ate my father—she made a girl who could not bear an unrecorded thing—and the wall is the only thing in the building that is not for sale, and it is holding the line my memory cannot.

The record says: this is Esther M. Pruitt. My mother. The record says she ate a man off a warm chair so I would not have to carry him, and it cost her every soft word she had, and she could not say so. The record says she was guilty and she was loved and both are true and neither one is allowed to disappear. The record says I owe her the truth.

I do not have to feel her to keep faith with her.

That is the whole of it. That is the thing it took me three months and most of my mind to learn. You can forgive a stranger. You can stand over a face you cannot place and keep faith with it on the word of a self you can barely remember being, *because the record says they were real and they mattered and you owe them the truth*—and that is not less than feeling. It is more. It is the part of love that is left when everything warm has been spent. It is the part that does not need to be paid for in memory because it was never made of memory. It was made of keeping.

—

I put my hand on her sternum where the bread is no longer.

The cold of her comes up into my palm, the steel-cold of eleven days and a county cooler, and I let it. I have laid the bread on a hundred sternums in this room, *where the weight sits,* and drawn the weight up and eaten it and felt the obscene lightness, the bus, the clean nothing—and I am laying my own bare hand here instead, on the place where it sits heaviest, and I am not drawing anything. I am not taking anything off her. I am leaving it on her, all of it, the man and the leaving and the forty years and the soft words she could not say, leaving it where it is and putting my hand on top of it, the way you put your hand on a thing to say *I see it. I am not going to take it away. I am going to stay here with it.*

The bread sits beside her head on its square of wax paper, full and undeleted, the corrupted mercy laid down. The fire is not lit. The retort is dark out in the carriage house with its eleven pipe joints over the door, and I am not at it, I am here, with my hand on the cold and the word stuck under the bone.

I lean down.

I am not sure whose face it is. I have told you. It does not matter now. It mattered when there was a rite to perform and a person to be sure of; it does not matter for this, because this is the one thing you can do for a stranger, the one mercy that does not need you to remember them—you only have to know, on the record, that they were real and you wronged them and they are owed.

I lean down over my mother, over the steady hands I read about and the not-smiling I cannot find and the small cold weight of her, and I push the air up one more time past the scar, past nineteen years, past the muscle that set hard learning not to, past the wall that worked, the wall that got me out, the wall that made a self—I push it up the long way it has to climb, and this time I do not ask the word to come.

I make it.

—

"I'm sorry."

—

It is the smallest sound the room has held all night.

Smaller than the bread breaking off the loaf. Smaller than the cot wheel on the tile. Two words, barely air, said low over a cold body in the grey, and they do not echo, there is nothing in a prep room for a sound to come back off of, the drains and the steel and the cooler eat it the way they eat everything, and it is gone the instant it is out of me, the way breath is gone.

And nothing happens.

I want that down too, because it is the truth and the whole point. The retort does not light. The smell does not lift—it is on me already, the smell of the open account, getting worse, the struck-match wet-stove smell of a weight that is going to stay in the world unhoused because I would not eat it, and the word does not touch it, the word does not house it, the word is not the bread. Nothing in the room changes. Esther is still dead. The father is still a man-sized nothing where a grief should be, and he stays that way, I cannot eat the erasure that ate him, the ledger only runs one direction. Kayla is still off the bridge in 1996 and there is still no baby. Coreen still never had a son. Della is still happy in a yellow dress I can no longer picture, and Brandy still flinches at a ghost no one in the room remembers making, and the boy at Bluefield is still gone, and Dana is still under the dragline spoil past Number Nine, taken, not run off, and her mother still does not get to stop hurting because the only peace I could give her is a deletion and I will not give it, not anymore, not to anyone.
