The word fixes nothing. It is the most useless thing in the language. It does not lift one ounce of weight off one body in this county. It un-makes nothing, un-saves nobody, raises no one, mends no marriage, returns no boy, gives no mother her peace.

That is exactly why it is the only mercy that does not kill.

—

The bread would have fixed everything.

That is the obscenity I have lived inside since the first swallow, since Carl, since the lightness came up where the weight should have come down and I was ashamed of it a full second before I was afraid of it. The bread fixes everything. You lay it on the breastbone and you say the old words and you eat, and the wrong un-happens, and the wronged un-happen with it, and the widow is happy and the file is thin and the page is clean, and a clean page looks like justice and is a grave. The mercy that works is the murder. The whole rotten genius of my family is that we found the one forgiveness that actually takes the pain away, and it takes it away by taking away the one who was pained, and we called it *instead of sorry,* and we believed it, a hundred years of us, ink and ink and ink, because *sorry* doesn't work and the bread does.

*Sorry* doesn't work.

I am standing here in the grey having finally said it, the thing I would not say for nineteen years, the thing the whole novel of my life was built to get to, and I am telling you it does not work. She is still dead. I still cannot see her face. Nothing is fixed. If I wanted it fixed I had the fix in my right hand an hour ago and I set it down on a square of wax paper next to her head.

I did not want it fixed.

I wanted it *true.*

There is a difference and it is the only difference there has ever been, the difference my family spent a century erasing, the difference between a book that balances because the debt is gone and a book that balances because the debt is *written down.* The bread closes the account by deleting it. *Sorry* leaves it open—open and honest, the wrong on the left and the price on the right, both real, both kept, the one I wronged and the wronging of her both standing in the world with my name on the line that asks who carried it—and that is not a failure of the word. That is the word doing the only holy thing it can do, which is to refuse to make the pain stop, because making the pain stop is making the person stop, and I am done, I am done, I will not make one more person stop.

—

I keep my hand on her.

I do not take it off. I want to make that last, the hand on the cold sternum, because the saying was one second and the staying is the rest of it, the staying is the whole carry, and a word said and then walked away from is just another deletion with better manners. So I stay. I stand bent over my mother in the first grey with my bare hand flat on the place where the weight sits, and I do not draw it up, and I do not eat it, and I do not leave, and the cold of her comes up my arm and into me and I let it come, because that is what carrying is. It is letting the cold come up and not setting it down.

I said it to her. *I'm sorry.* And I will say it again, I expect, for the rest of my life, to a face I will go on losing, on the word of a record I will go on keeping, because that is the shape of the thing now—I am the one who stayed, the last Pruitt, the sin-eater who at the end would not eat, who chose to carry her own mother unhoused to her own grave the way the folklore always lied that we did, except now it is not a lie, now it is the only true thing in the house. I am going to carry her. The weight is up out of her and into nothing—not the crumb, I did not finish the rite, not me, I refused—just up, unhoused, the heaviest thing in Caudle's Mercy, and it is going to come for me, slow, the way it came for Carla, the bad dreams and the souring and the guilt that was never mine confessed in some street, the long ruin, and I am standing here with my hand on her saying *yes, all right, come, I see you, I am not going to take you away.*

That is the sorry. The word is just the door of it. The carry is the room behind the door, and the room has no other exit, and I have walked into it on purpose, with my eyes open, on the word of a woman I used to be.

—

There is one more thing and I make myself look at it, because I am the one who keeps the truth in this and I will not soften it even here, especially here.

I am not at peace.

I had a peace an hour ago. It was in my right hand. It was clean and obscene and it tasted of nothing and it would have set me down light as a girl, twenty-two, just stepped onto a bus, and I set it down on a square of wax paper. What I have instead is this: a cold under my palm, a smell I will wear the rest of my days, a mother I can barely see, a town that paid a hundred years to forget and a woman left in it who would not, who could not, whom her own dead mother *built* so she could not. The loneliest thing there is, and I chose it on purpose, against the wanting, for the truth.

But it is mine. The weight is mine and the wound is mine and the word is mine and I gave it, finally, late, useless, true—and they are all real, every one, and I am keeping every one, the wrong and the price and the love and the harm and my mother guilty and my mother forgiven and neither one erased, the account open and honest and unbalanced in the only way that is not a grave.

And the books will balance. Not because the truth is gone.

Because I said it. Because I kept it. Because I am still here, bent over her in the grey with my hand on the cold and the smell coming up, the last keeper, not of the mercy—the mercy is dead, I killed it, I let it die in my own right hand—the keeper of the truth, which is the only thing the fire was ever going to forget, and the only thing I am not going to let it.

I straighten.

I take my hand off her, slow, and the cold comes off my palm and into the air, and I leave it there, the cold and the weight and the word, all of it staying, none of it taken—and I look at her one more time, the small cold stranger I came out of, the face I cannot hold, the mother I am keeping anyway, and I open my mouth, and I find I can do it now, the second time is not nineteen years up a scarred throat, the second time is just a word, and I say it again, low, to be sure she has it, in case the dead can keep a thing the way I keep a thing:

"I'm sorry."

And then there is nothing left to say, and I go out to light the fire.

# Chapter Thirty

## & Daughters

The Power-Pak II runs hot at eighteen hundred and sulks below sixteen, and I have spent my whole life learning the difference, and I light it now for the last body it will ever take, and the body is my mother.

I do it the way I do everything. I check the gaskets. I count the pipe joints over the door, and there are eleven, and it does not help, and I count them anyway because a thing you do without help is sometimes the only thing left that is yours. I bring her in on the cot from the prep room, fifty-one kilograms, a hundred and twelve pounds, the smallest body the cooler held all season and the heaviest thing in Caudle's Mercy, and I do not let the contradiction stop my hands. I have laid bread on a hundred sternums in this building. I am laying nothing on hers. The wax paper is still beside her head with the morsel on it, gone hard, the corrupted mercy I would not eat, and I leave it there, and I roll her to the charge door, and I open the door, and the heat of it comes out and stands in the cold room like a thing waking.

I send her in un-eaten.

—

She does not go up like paper.

I knew she wouldn't. I have heard the difference enough times now to write it in the book and I have written it in the book — *un-eaten, heavy, burns cool, temp will not hold, a sound under the roar* — but knowing a thing and standing at the panel while it happens to your mother are two different counties, and I am in the second one now, watching the needle.

Eighteen hundred, climbing eager the way an eaten body climbs, and then it stops climbing. It hangs. It comes down. Sixteen, and I feed her gas and she takes it and does not rise, the way Tug would not rise, the way Cole Ferry would not rise, the way the heavy ones go — fifteen-forty, like a stone dropped in a creek, the cold of the weight pulling the fire down into itself. And under the roar there is the sound. Not Tug's. Tug was a man clearing his throat to speak and never getting to the words, *hk, hk,* an hour of it. Hers is lower and longer and more even, a sound with no consonant in it at all, a held thing, the sound a house makes settling onto its own bones in the night, the sound I heard the first hour I came home and blamed on the drains. It was never the drains. It was this, in advance, for forty years, and I am hearing it close the loop.

The smell comes up through the building before she is half gone.

Struck match held too long. The inside of a wet stove. The third thing I have never had a shelf for, that I have a shelf for now, that I built the shelf for out of my own bones across three months — the smell of a weight that is not going to be housed, that I have refused to house, that I am sending into the world on purpose with my name on the line that asks who carried it. I do not open the windows. I have opened windows for other people's heavy dead, all night, in the fog, and it does not work, the smoke goes clean and the weight stays, so I do not bother lying to the house tonight. I let it fill. I let it get into the curtains and the casket showroom and the upstairs hall where there is no photograph of a father, and I stand at the panel in it and I keep her vigil, two hours and then a third, the book's last long clock, and I do not leave, because leaving a body you would not house is a deletion with better manners, and I am done with those.

—

I will tell you what I am not.

I am not at peace. I want that down because I am the one who keeps the truth in this house and there is no one left to keep it but me. The peace was in my right hand and I set it on a square of wax paper and it is going hard beside the charge door while she burns, and what I have instead of it is a cold I will wear the rest of my life and a smell I will wear under that, and a mother I am burning into a weight that is going to come looking for the only kin she has left, which is me. Esther wakes heavy tonight. There is no one else for it to find. I have read what it does. I read it in the oldest hand in the book, the cramped Welsh one, the women who would not eat and paid for the not-eating: the bad dreams, the souring, the guilt that is not yours confessed to strangers in the street, and then, in the worst entries, the keeper *follows the dead down within the year.* I watched a smaller version of it crawl up Carla Ammons two years sober until her careful hands shook. I am inviting the whole of it into myself on purpose. I am choosing to be haunted by my mother rather than to forget her.

That is the trade I made. Nobody made it for me. The note read like a curse and was a door, and the door opened onto this room, and I walked into it with my eyes open, on the word of a woman I can barely remember being.

The needle holds at fifteen-forty. She is taking her time. She was not a large woman and she is taking her time, and I let her.

—

Wren comes at first light, because of course she does, because the only thing in this county faster than bad news is a girl who knows you didn't sleep.

She lets herself in the side door with the gas-station bag and she stops in the hall when the smell hits her, and her face does a thing, and she does not say anything stupid. She is nineteen and she has learned, in three months in this building, not to say anything stupid in front of a smell like that, which is more than most grown people in this county ever learn.

"It's heavy," she says. Not a question.

"It's heavy."

She comes the rest of the way in. She sets the bag on the panel ledge — taquitos, two coffees, the Marathon kind that tastes like the bottom of the pot, our breakfast since the start — and she looks at the needle hanging at fifteen-forty, and she looks at me, and she has put it together, because she is not slow, she has never once been slow, that is the whole trouble with her.

"You didn't do the thing," she says. "With the bread. You didn't do it to her."

"No."

"You did it to everybody else."

"I did it to everybody else."

She is quiet a second. Then, in the flat half-feral voice that has made me laugh in a room full of the dead more times than I will ever tell her: "Well. Reckon that tracks. You'd let everybody in the county off easy and make your own self carry the worst one out the long way. That's about the most *you* thing I ever heard of."

And it is funny, the way the worst things in this trade are always a little funny, and I feel it pull at the corner of my mouth, the old disease, the laugh that lives next to the dead, and I let it. It is the first thing that has pulled at the corner of my mouth in days. The wall does not come back up all the way — it never will again, I learned that this week — but there is a relief valve in it I forgot was there, and her name is Wren, and she has never once charged me for it.

"Eat your taquito," I say.

"You eat yours."

We eat them on the panel ledge while my mother burns heavy below us, and it is the warmest I have been all night, and it is obscene, and it is the truest thing in the house, and both of those are allowed to be so at once now. I learned that this week too.

—

I do not make her the keeper.

I want to be plain about it, because it is the second body I am laying to rest tonight and the books must show it. There is a recipe in the oldest hand for the making of an apprentice — *she must be willed it by the keeper, and she must eat her first off the keeper's own hand, and then the burden passes and the first is freed* — and I have read it enough times to know that it is the only door out of this room that does not cost me everything. I could will it to Wren. I could lay a sin on a body and put the bread in her hand and say the words over her and walk out of this building light as a girl who has just stepped onto a bus, freed, the way the recipe promises, and she would do it, she would do it for me, that is exactly the trouble, that is exactly why I will not.

So I tell her she is fired.

I tell her there is no more work here. The home is closing. The retort runs once more, tonight, for my mother, and then it goes cold and stays cold, and the cooler gets emptied and the showroom gets sold for whatever six caskets and a poisoned-creek view will bring, and Pruitt & Daughters takes its dead sign down off the porch and is done. The town's hundred-year mercy ends in this carriage house at dawn, with me, and I am not handing it to a nineteen-year-old with chipped black polish and a future. The dead of Caudle's Mercy are going to have to be grieved the hard way from here out, the honest way, wound and all. The last bodies will burn heavy. People are going to have to carry their own dead, the way the rest of the world does, the way it was always supposed to be before my family found the one forgiveness that works and learned that the way it works is murder.

Wren takes it the way Wren takes everything, sideways and unafraid.

"You're firing me," she says.

"I'm firing you."

"Three weeks before I leave anyway."

"You start the program in three weeks. Bluefield. The cosmetology thing." I have it written in the book downstairs, in my own hand, because there are things I cannot afford to lose the way I have lost so much else — *Wren — Bluefield — three weeks — she gets out.* I read it this morning to be sure it was still true. It was still true. "You don't spend your last three weeks in a funeral home that smells like this. Go home. Pack. Fight with your aunt. Whatever it is you do."

"Dakota texted me *u up* at four in the morning," she says, "from the jail tablet," and I laugh again, a real one this time, low and tired and real, and she grins, because she did it on purpose, because she knows the sound of me being all right is the only present she has the money for, and she has always paid generously in the one currency she's got.

"Go to school, Wren," I say. "Do hair. Make people pretty for the parts of their lives that aren't this. Somebody in this county ought to get to."

She looks at the needle one more time. Fifteen-forty, holding. My mother, taking her time.

"You sure," she says, and it is not about the job.

"I'm sure."

And she goes — but at the door she stops, the way Loy stopped the first night, the way the people who love you in this county always stop at the door with the thing they couldn't get out at the table. She does not turn around.

"You're the only one ever taught me not to be scared of the dead," she says. "I just want you to know I figured out it was the live ones the whole time. The quiet ones. You were right." A beat. "Don't get too quiet."

Then she is gone into the wet morning, un-eaten, un-erased, free, given the rest of her stupid bright life by the one choice I made that cost me everything and bought exactly one clean thing, and the clean thing is her, walking out, and I watch her go from the window until the fog takes her, and it is the only part of this dawn that does not hurt.

—

Ray comes at full light with two coffees, which is a thing he does now, the way I have a notebook that is just mine, the way he tells a story twice — the little religions of people who keep records against a world that won't stay true.

He stands in the smell and does not flinch from it. He knows what it is. I told him the shape of all of it, weeks ago, in this room, everything but the one girl under the dragline spoil past Number Nine, which is his to find or not find and not mine to hand him, and he did not run and he did not call it madness and he did not try to fix it, which is the rarest thing a man ever did in my presence. He looked at the worst true thing I have and he chose to keep standing next to it.

"You did it, then," he says, meaning the not-doing. "Burned her heavy."

"I burned her heavy."

He nods slow. He sets a coffee on the ledge next to Wren's cold one. "She's gonna come for you. The weight. You said."

"She's going to come for me."

"And you're letting her."

"I'm letting her."

He is quiet, looking at the panel, a man who has spent thirty years in rooms learning that the loudest line is the one nobody says. Then: "Carol's still in my front closet. Eleven years. Camel coat on the rail in front of her like I'm fixing to take her somewhere." He says it to the needle, not to me. "You could've made me forget her. You told me that. The grief, even. Made it not hurt." He turns the coffee a quarter-turn on the ledge, the way a man does with his hands when the rest of him is busy. "I'm glad you didn't. The hurting's the part of her I've still got. You take the hurt, you take the her."

"I know," I say. "That's the whole of it."

"Yeah." He almost smiles. It is the saddest, kindest thing in the county. "I'm keeping the Coyle case open."

"There's no case left, Ray. You said. The file's near empty. The confession won't hold still on the page. I did that to it. I ate the man who—"

"I know what you did." Not hard. Patient, the way he is. "I'm keeping it open anyway. On purpose now. Not 'cause I think I'll close it. 'Cause there was a true version of what happened to that girl, and it's sitting in your head and a little of it's sitting in mine, and somebody in this world ought to go on holding it even after the paper quits saying so." He looks at me then, full on, two keepers of records in a room that smells of a weight nobody will house, the only two people in the county who know the dead were ever wronged. "The paper outlives you. But it don't outlive you by much. So while we're here." He lifts the coffee, a quarter-inch, a toast to nothing, to everything. "We hold it. The both of us. The true version. However long we get."

"However long we get," I say.

And we drink the bad coffee in the smell of my burning mother, two grown people who stopped expecting to be understood and got it anyway, late, in a carriage house, at the end of a line, and it is not romance and it is not rescue. It is the other thing. It is being known. It is the warmth that is left in the ruin when everything that could be erased has been, and I will take it, I will take every minute of it I get, because it is real and it is mine and no bread is ever going to touch it now.

—

The needle drops at last. She is done. I run the cool-down and I open the chamber and I do the thing I have done a thousand times, the raking, the gathering, the long patient work of the dead, and her remains are not light. An eaten body comes out clean and small, four pounds, paper-ash, the calcined nothing of an erased thing. Hers come out at a little over six. The bone did not fully give. The heavy ones never do — the fire could not get all the way down into a thing the world still has to reckon with, and so there is more of her left than there is of the ones I let off easy, and I find that I am glad of it, the extra ounces, the bone that would not surrender, the weight that stayed weight. *The books must balance.* They are going to balance by this: by what is left, by what I kept, by the smell in the curtains and the cold in my hand and the six-and-some pounds of a woman who did not get deleted.

I weigh her twice, because I always weigh twice, because a thing not written down is a thing the world is allowed to change its mind about, and I have spent my whole life — I know now why, I have it on a tape, my mother built it into me the morning she ate my father so I would not grow up his — I have spent my whole life refusing to let it. I write the number in the book. I spell her name right. *PRUITT, ESTHER M.* I leave the right-hand column open. I do not write a price in it, because I did not pay one, because I would not eat her, and an honest ledger keeps the wound, and I am keeping it. The account stays open. The books balance anyway. Not because the debt is gone.

Because it is written down.

—

I take her out to the porch at the end, in the temporary container with her name spelled right, and I stand under the sign.

*Pruitt & Daughters.* The paint went to ghost-letters before I was born. There was never more than one daughter, two generations running; the *s* on the end has been a lie the whole time, a hope some Pruitt woman painted up there once and the rest of us kept not scraping off, the way you keep a promise you already know is broken because taking it down would make the breaking final. It is final now. I am the last one. The line ends on this porch this morning, with me, and there will be no daughter to eat me off a table, no keeper to come behind, no hand to put bread on my sternum where the weight sits, which means that when my time comes I will burn heavy too, un-eaten, like her, because there is no one left to do otherwise and I would not let them if there were. We will be the two heavy ones. The mother and the daughter, both carried, neither erased, the only true accounts in a hundred years of a book that balanced by deletion. I think she would — I do not know what she would. I cannot find her face to ask it. I have only the record, and the record says she left me a door and called it a curse so I would walk through it free, and I did not walk through it free, I walked through the other one, the hard one, the one that keeps her, and I have to believe on the word of the record that she would understand. The record is all I have of her now. The record will have to be enough, because it is going to have to be enough for the rest of my life, and that is what keeping faith is — to forgive a face you cannot hold, on the word of a self you can barely remember being, because the record says she was real and she mattered and you owe her the truth.

The fog is in the hollow the way it is every morning till noon. The creek the mine poisoned runs below the porch, and I do not have the word for the color it runs anymore, I lost that one, I do not remember when, but I can see it down there, moving, going somewhere, the way water does whether or not you have a word for it.

I have her in my hands. She is not light.

I have not said it since I was twenty-two, and then I said it, late, useless, true, over a face I could not find, the smallest most defiant word there is, the one that fixes nothing and houses no weight and lifts no ounce off any body in this county and is the only mercy that does not kill — and I am going to go on saying it, I expect, for the rest of my life, to her, to the dead, to the open account I am carrying out the long way to my own grave. Not *instead of* anything. Not anymore. My family said it instead of it ever having happened, instead of mattering, instead of the dead getting to have been real. I am done saying it instead of. There is nothing left to put it in front of.

There is only the thing itself, small and whole and useless and kept.

I stand on the porch under the lying sign with my mother heavy in my hands and the smell of her in the only world that remembers it, and I say it again, low, to be sure she has it, in case the dead can keep a thing the way I keep a thing:

"I'm sorry."

Then I carry her down the steps into the fog, where the work is now, where the work is going to be for as long as I last — the carrying, the keeping, the long unhoused weight of a truth nobody else will hold — and I do not set her down.

I do not set her down.

I do not set her down.
